About the Author

Author’s Note

Thank you for purchasing 300 Practical English Words and Phrases. This is the third book in the Practical English series, and it is intended as a reference for English learners, teachers, and those who are simply passionate about language. It is meant for self-study, but it can also be used as a classroom resource.

For those who have read my first two books, 100 Practical English Phrasal Verbs and 200 Practical English Idioms, you will be familiar with the structure of this book. However, you will also find some new attributes which enhance the learning experience.

What’s new?

In addition to providing definitions, examples, and common sentences, I have added usage explanations, collocations, related phrases, and practice questions. I decided to add these elements in the middle of the writing process because I realized that I was frequently asking myself usage questions about the words and phrases that I was writing about—and if I was asking questions, then students would definitely ask questions as well. My goal then became to anticipate the questions that students would have, and to answer the questions before students could ask them. In short, I wanted to provide students with as much clarity and context as possible, so they could use the language in this book with absolute confidence.

Because of the additions mentioned above, this book is more detail-heavy than my previous efforts. It requires patience and careful study, but I feel it is a richer experience that will reward students who take the time to absorb its nuances.

As always, my goal is to provide language that people will actually use. This means that as you turn these pages, you will not find words and phrases that have fallen out of use. Instead, you will find common words, phrases, and standalone responses with at least three contemporary examples each to give you language you can start using right now.

To conclude, I have received a lot of incredibly positive feedback about my first two books, and I am grateful to everyone who has trusted me and my work. With that in mind, I feel this is my best and most complete book. I hope you will too.

Until next time, I wish you success in your studies.

Alex Makar

March 31st, 2022

Introduction

How to use this book

This book is intended to increase your vocabulary and improve your conversation skills. You can use it as a reference for learning words and phrases that you have read or heard in daily life (scan the index for this approach), or as a guide for learning a few phrases at a time. Like my previous book, I recommend learning three to five new phrases per week. If you feel you can learn more than that, by all means, do it! Just pick a section or group of words and/or phrases that spark your interest, and start reading.

About the organization of this book

The organization of this book is a mix of grammatical categories and usage categories. Do you want to learn about common filler and transition words such as “so,” “well,” and “anyway”? You can do that. Do you want to learn about adjective phrases and idioms? You can do that. How about common English commands? You can do that, too. My goal was to sort the book in a way that made sense for most English learners.

Having said that, there are some categorical imperfections. For example, many of the phrases in the prepositions category could be added to the adverbs or adjectives categories. There are also phrases with multiple usages that sometimes function as adjectives, and sometimes function as adverbs. In the end, I decided on a categorization that looks clean to the human eye. This means that all “on” phrases are in the prepositions section, regardless of their function as a true preposition, an adjective, or an adverb. I have noted the relevant grammatical function where necessary.

About the use of “one,” “you,” and other pronouns in this book

For definitions, in most cases I have opted to use the word “one” when referring to a subject, and “one’s” when referring to a possessive adjective. This is because “one” and “one’s” encompass all human subjects and possessive adjectives. If you are not familiar with this formal usage of “one” and “one’s,” here is a chart to show what they cover:

one

one’s

I

my

you

your

he

his

she

her

we

our

they

their

I chose to do this because seeing the word “you” in definitions makes learners think of a phrase and its definition from only one perspective. In my opinion, it is better to consider multiple perspectives. This expands our minds by forcing us to think of “he/she/we/they” examples in addition to “you” and “I” examples. This is important when you are—or one is—learning a language.

However, there are some situations, such as when discussing commands, where I have used “you” in the definition—because the subject of a command is always the second person singular or plural. Using “one” multiple times in some definitions can also become confusing, so I have used “you” where I feel it makes sense.

Regarding pronouns in the explanation portion of each entry, I have used the singular “they” instead of writing “he/she” every time. This is much simpler and easier to read. Note this example from the second usage of “Bring it in”:

“When it is said, the speaker usually has their arms in an open posture, inviting the person or group they are addressing to hug them.”

I feel this is more pleasant to read than “When it is said, the speaker usually has his/her arms in an open posture, inviting the person or group he/she is addressing to hug him/her.”

I have also used a variety of pronouns (ex: “He/She/We”) in some parts of the book to remind students that many scenarios are possible. Just remember that if you see “I/You/We” at the beginning of an example, it does not mean those are the only options. You are encouraged to try multiple subjects.

About the “related phrases” sections in this book

While this book covers 300 words and phrases, there are of course many others that exist. This is why I have not only included collocations—which are words that commonly go together with the 300 entries in this book—but also related phrases. I have included the related phrases component to encourage learners to continue studying independently and learning vocabulary that is outside of this book. If you are not familiar with one or more of the related phrases that I have listed, they can be found with an online search. You got this!

About the tone of this book

I want my books to feel different than standard reference books. I want them to feel informative and useful, but I also want them to feel fun and approachable. With that in mind, I have tried to make my explanations as clear as possible, but I have also tried to maintain a conversational tone throughout this book. If you see me using contractions in my explanations sometimes, this is why. In short, I want to have a conversation with my readers.

About the difficulty level of this book

This book is meant for upper intermediate and advanced English learners. If you are familiar with the CEFR scale, this book is ideal for B2/C1 students. However, students at other levels can still benefit from this book. By studying clear definitions and useful common sentences, an intermediate student (B1) can also improve their spoken and written English skills. Use your discretion.

What’s the best way to learn new vocabulary?

The best way to learn vocabulary is through context, experimentation, and regular revision. This means seeing or hearing a new phrase in a book, movie, real life conversation, or other scenario, finding out what it means, and then trying to use it yourself—and to use it on a regular basis so you can commit it to memory.

You can also continue context-based learning by reading and studying a book like this one. This book promotes context-based learning in four ways:

1.By giving a detailed explanation of each word, phrase, or sentence, including usage nuances and common contexts.

2.By providing multiple examples of each word, phrase, or sentence, so that learners can see the language used in a variety of relevant contexts.

3.By including at least one common sentence per word, phrase, or sentence, which learners can start practicing immediately.

4.By including common variations and extensions, as well as similar and opposite words, phrases, or sentences.

In the end, you must find the approach that works best for you. I wish you success in your studies, and I hope you discover the many benefits this book has to offer!

COMMANDS, SUGGESTIONS, AND ENCOURAGEMENT

This chapter features imperative sentences you can use to give people commands, suggestions, or encouragement. Practice saying the sentences as they are written and do the practice questions to further test your understanding and practice your conversation skills. Please note that some of the entries in this chapter do not include a common sentences component, as the entries are the common sentences.

“Bring it in.”

1.used for encouraging someone to bring something inside
Common variation: “Bring them in” (for plural objects)

Examples: “Should I leave my bag outside?” “No. Bring it in.”
“Those groceries look heavy. I’ll help you bring them in.”
“I think you would enjoy that book. I have a copy at home. I’ll bring it in tomorrow.” (I’ll bring it from my home to our workplace.)

This is the most literal usage of this phrase. When you say “Bring it in,” you are encouraging someone to bring something that is outside into a building/a room/etc. You can modify this to “Bring them in” for plural objects.

Usage notes: Someone can also use “Bring it in” to talk about bringing something from their home to their place of work, as in example 3.

Practice: Who normally brings in the groceries in your home?

2.“Give me a hug”; come here and hug me or hug the group

Examples: “It was so good to see all of you again. I’m going to miss you. Bring it in!”
“I’m sorry.” “It’s okay. I forgive you. Bring it in.”
“I’ve had a terrible day.” “Forget about it. Come here and bring it in.”

This encouraging command is only made to a person or group of people whom you are familiar with. When it is said, the speaker usually has their arms in an open posture, inviting the person or group they are addressing to hug them. The “it” in the context of “Bring it in” can be understood as “Bring the love in” or “Bring your body into my arms.” It is a way of inviting someone to make a physical connection with you. Three common contexts for this phrase are goodbyes, reconciliations, and offers of emotional support.

Related phrase: “give [someone] a squeeze”

Practice: When was the last time you hugged someone? What can you say next time you want to hug someone?

3.get closer to each other; gather in closer proximity

Examples: “Bring it in, everybody! Cheers to the new year!”
“Bring it in, team! Let’s talk about our strategy for the second half!”
“Mom, you’re not in the picture. Bring it in.” (Context: taking a selfie with one’s mom)

Common sentence: “Bring it in, everybody/team/guys!”

This is used to encourage people to gather together in closer proximity. Some common contexts for this usage include a sports coach telling their team to make a circle around them, or someone who is taking a group photo who wants everyone to get closer together.

Related phrase: “huddle up”

Practice: Imagine you take a group picture of your friends and/or family. What do you say if you want them to get closer together?

“Check it out.”

1.“Look at this/that”; give your attention to the person or thing I am looking at, listening to, etc.
Common variations: “Check this out”; “Check that out.” (Usage depends on one’s proximity to the person/place/thing/etc.)
Similar, see also: “take a look,” “have a look”

Examples: “Check it out. There’s a sale at the electronics store.”
“Check that out. There’s a kitten sleeping on that woman’s head.”
“Hey, check it out. This is Gina’s new boyfriend.” (Your friend is showing you a picture of Gina’s new boyfriend.)

Use this imperative when you want to direct someone’s attention to something. It can be a picture, a video, a person, or anything that catches your interest and that you want the other person to see.

Practice: Look at something around you and say “Check it out.” What is interesting about what you are looking at?

2.try this; experience this

Examples: “Wow, this is a really cool song. Check it out.” (Or, “Check out this song.”)
“I think you’ll really like this soup. Check it out.”
“The MOMA in New York is an incredible art gallery. You should check it out.”

You can encourage someone to try or sample something new, such as a piece of media (ex: a song), a physical place (ex: an art gallery), or an event (ex: a concert). You can use “Check it out” in person or in writing. For example, you can send someone an interesting picture from your phone and type “Check it out.” This is a way of saying “Hey, look at this.”

Related sentences: “Take a look at this/that.” “Try it/this/that out.”

Practice: Have you checked out any new restaurants, stores, or places recently?

“Don’t worry about it.”

1.do not concern yourself with it; you do not need to worry about it

Examples: “Oops. I forgot to clean my plate.” “Don’t worry about it. I’ll do it.”
“Do you want to sit? I can get an extra chair.” “Don’t worry about it. I’ve been sitting all day. I can stand.”
“Here’s the five dollars that I owe you.” “Don’t worry about it. I gave it to you because I wanted to. I’m sure you would do the same for me.”

If someone says “Don’t worry about it,” they mean the situation is not major to them, or that they will handle the situation. It’s similar to saying “It’s not a problem,” or “It’s not a big deal.” b

Related sentences: “It’s not a big deal.” “It’s not a problem.” “No worries.”

Practice: If you paid for a friend’s meal and they offered to pay you back later, would you say “Okay” or “Don’t worry about it”?

2.a substitute phrase for “You’re welcome”

Examples: “Thanks for your help yesterday.” “Oh, don’t worry about it.”
“Thank you for helping Jill with her homework.” “Don’t worry about it.”
“Thanks for lending me those ten dollars.” “Don’t worry about it.”

This is one of many casual ways to say “You’re welcome” after someone has thanked you. Use this with people you are familiar with and comfortable with.

Related sentences: “Don’t mention it.” “It was nothing.” “No problem.” “No big deal.” “Not at all.”

Practice: Someone in your family says “Thanks for your help yesterday.” How do you respond?

“Go for it!”

1.attempt what you want to achieve; try hard to get what you want; do it

Examples: “That’s a great idea for a website. Go for it!”
“This job sounds perfect for you. You should go for it!”
“You think you can get a higher score? Go for it! I’d like to see you try.”

This can be used in three different ways:
1) genuine encouragement (“That sounds great! Go for it!”)
2) a sarcastic response (“You think you can do better? Go for it.”)
3) a suggestion (“It sounds like a good opportunity. He should go for it.”)

This phrase is often used to encourage people to take advantage of opportunities, such as job postings. The word “it” refers to the thing the person wants to achieve. If there is more than one option, you can specify which option you think the person should “go for.” Note the examples: “Go for the yellow one”; “If I were you, I’d go for the second job.”

Related sentences: “Get after it.” “Give it a go.”

Practice: When was the last time you really went for something? (This means you put all of your effort and energy into it. Ex: “The last time I really went for something was when I applied for my current job.”)

“How about…?”

1.used for making a suggestion or offer
Similar, see also: “Why don’t…?”

Examples: “We need to call someone who’s good at math.” “How about Tanya?
“How about a game of chess?” (I want to play chess with you.)
“What do you want to do?” “How about watching a movie?” (Or, “How about we watch a movie?”)

Common sentence: “How about no?” (This is a sarcastic or angry response you can use when someone suggests doing something that you are not interested in. “How about we go to the park?” “How about no?”)

You can use “How about” in a couple of different ways. It can be used as a replacement for “I think we should do this/that.” It can also be used as a replacement for “Here is one idea we can consider.” Only the speaker really knows the intended usage when they say it. This means the speaker can try to persuade others to choose their preferred option, but they can make it sound like it’s “just an idea.”

Grammar note: Follow “How about” with a subject or verb+ing. Note the examples: “How about we go to the beach?” “How about going to the beach?”

Usage note: Do not confuse “How about” with “What about.” “What about” is used to mention a person, object, or potential problem. For example: “Let’s invite Tim and Lisa.” “What about Rob?” “Oh, right. We should invite him too.” In casual speech, “What about” is sometimes accepted to make suggestions in the same way as “How about,” but it should be avoided.

Related phrases: “We could...” “Why don’t we...?”

Practice: Your friend wants to do something tonight. Suggest something.

“Keep…”

1.used for encouraging someone to continue doing or persist in doing something

Examples: “We’re almost there. Keep driving straight.”
“I couldn’t study. My brother kept bothering me.” (He persisted in this behaviour.)
“Why do people like this movie?.” “Keep watching. There’s a twist coming.”

Common sentence: “Keep going.” (Depending on the context, this encourages someone to continue moving in a particular direction, or to continue working or doing something in a particular way.)

Grammar note: “Keep” is followed by a verb+ing for this usage.

Collocations: “Keep going.” “Keep looking.” “Keep trying.” “Keep working.”

Practice: What is something you want to keep doing?

2.used for encouraging someone to stay on a particular path or in a particular area

Examples: “Keep to the road.” (Similar: “Stay on the road.”)
“In London, you need to keep to the left side of the street.”
“Batman usually keeps to the shadows.” (He usually stays in the shadows.)

Common sentence: “Keep to the left/right.” (Or, “Stay on the left/right.”)

Grammar note: For this usage, use “keep to” followed by the path or place to follow or stay in/on.

Practice: If you want to stay safe while driving in the city, which side of the road should you keep to?

“Let’s…”

1.used for encouraging the listener(s) to do a particular activity or to take a particular action together with the speaker

Examples: “I’m tired. Let’s go home.”
“This place is boring. Let’s get out of here.”
“Do you think it’s going to be a good game?” “Let’s wait and see.”

Common sentence: “Let’s go.” (This is used for encouraging someone to leave a place with the speaker; it can also be used to encourage someone to start doing an activity. The second usage is usually said emphatically.)

Usage note: “Let’s” is a contraction of “Let us” and therefore includes the speaker and listener(s) in its suggestion.

Grammar note: “Let’s” is followed by a base verb (ex: “Let’s do it”).

Practice: You and your friend don’t have any money. Use “Let’s” to suggest something you can do that doesn’t involve spending any money.

“Take a look” (also: “Have a look”)

1.look at this/that/it
Common variation: “Take a look at this/that.”
Similar, see also: “Check it out.”

Examples: “There’s something wrong with my laptop. Could you take a look?”
“Hey, Alex. Come here and have a look at this video.”
“The boss sent me an email, but I don’t want to read it right now. I’ll take a look at it tomorrow.”

Common sentence: “Take a look at this”; “Have a look at this.”

This sentence is used to encourage someone to look at or examine something. It can be used to ask someone to help you fix something that you don’t know how to fix (“Could you take a look at this?”). It is also used to say you will assess a situation or problem at a later time (“The kitchen floor is a mess.” “Okay. I’ll have a look at it tomorrow”).

Practice: Do you need to take a look at anything in your home, or is everything in good condition?

“Why don’t…?”

1.used to make a suggestion or offer
Similar, see also: “How about…?”

Examples: “You seem tired. Why don’t you stay here while I go to the store?”
“This isn’t working. Why don’t we try something different?”
“Why don’t we leave early in the morning so we can avoid traffic?”

Common sentence: “Why don’t you stay here while I…?” (+base verb)

Depending on your tone, this phrase can introduce a neutral suggestion or an angry one. “Why don’t we try a different restaurant?” can be said in a gentle way or in an irritated way, for instance.

Grammar note: “Why don’t” is followed by an object and base verb (ex: “Why don’t you call her?”).

Practice: Your friend needs a new job. Use “Why don’t you” to make a helpful suggestion.

“You got this!”

1.you can do this; this is something you are capable of; you will not fail this

Examples: “I’m nervous about my driving exam.” “Are you kidding? You got this!”
“That’s a complicated project. Are you sure you got this?” “I got this.”
“Do you need help installing your printer?” “No thanks. I got this.”

Common sentence: “I/You got this!” (I/You can do this. It won’t be a problem for me/you.)

This sentence is used to encourage someone who is going to attempt to succeed at something, such as an exam, a job interview, a meeting, etc. It can also be used to encourage someone in low-risk situations, such as trying a new cooking recipe or trying to pass a challenging section in a video game. It’s similar to saying “I believe in you.” You can also use this in a question form if you want to ask the person if they feel confident about something. Ex: “You got this?” “Yeah, I got this.” Asking “You got this?” in this way is similar to asking “Can you do this?”

Usage note: Do not confuse “You got this” with “You got it,” which means you know, understand, or understood something. Note the examples: “So, the party starts at 9pm?” “Yep. You got it”; “Is this the right answer?” “That’s right. You got it.”

Practice: Are you a self-confident person, or do you sometimes need to hear someone say “You got this” before you do something?

TIME AND FREQUENCY PHRASES

The time and frequency phrases below are all adverb phrases. You can use the majority of them either at the beginning or the end of a sentence. The only exception in this chapter is “all the time,” which should be used at the end. Read the examples and do the practice questions to increase your confidence with these phrases.

all of a sudden

1.suddenly; without warning

Examples: “I was just minding my own business when all of a sudden, a person on the street started yelling at me.”
“We were looking forward to life returning to normal after the pandemic. All of a sudden, a new variant forced governments to close businesses again.”
“All of a sudden, everything changed.”

Common sentence: “Why are you so happy all of a sudden?”

This phrase is often used when you are telling a story. The story can be something that happened to you or someone else, or it can be a fiction. When used effectively, it can add an exciting element or twist to your story.

Grammar note: “All of a sudden” is typically used at the beginning of a new sentence. However, you can use it in the middle if you add the word “when” in front of it, or after a coordinating conjunction, usually “and.” For instance: “I was taking a shower when all of a sudden, I heard a huge crash.” “I was thinking about the problem, and all of a sudden, I thought of a solution.”

Related phrases: “in an instant,” “out of the blue”

Practice: Have you ever missed a friend or family member all of a sudden?

all the time

1.very often or constantly; on a regular basis; very frequently

Examples: “We talk all the time.” (We communicate with each other very often.)
“Don’t watch that news channel. They lie all the time.”
“Maybe he’s depressed. He says he feels tired all the time.”

Common sentence: “He/She does it all the time!” (Use this when you want to say that someone has a habit of doing something on a regular basis. This can be a good thing or a bad thing.)

This phrase can be used at the end of a sentence, or as a standalone response. For example: “How often do you pay for things with your credit card?” “All the time.”

Usage note: You can reassure someone that something is safe to do by saying “I do it all the time.”

Related phrases: “day and night,” “twenty-four seven,” “week in, week out”

Practice: What is something you think about all the time?

at times

1.on some occasions or in some moments; sometimes
Similar, see also: “from time to time,” “now and then,” “once in a while”
Do not confuse with, see also: “at all times” (consistently/always)

Examples: “At times, I feel like you don’t really listen to me.”
“Everyone gets a little sad at times.”
“At times, I wasn’t sure if you were going to succeed.”

Common sentence: “At times, I’m not so sure.” (I have certain moments when I’m not very certain about this point/opinion/decision/etc.)

This phrase is not a consistent synonym for “sometimes.” For example, “You should call me at times” doesn’t have the same feeling or regularity of usage as “You should call me sometimes.” However, “Sometimes, I don’t want to talk to anyone” and “At times, I don’t want to talk to anyone” are both relatively common. “At times” focuses on specific occasions and moments, while “sometimes” has a more general feeling of time. “At times” is often used to express doubt or worry, as in examples 1 and 3, and the common sentence above.

Usage note: This phrase can be used at the beginning or at the end of a sentence, but it is more effective when used at the beginning.

Collocations: “feel [a feeling or emotion adjective] at times,” “not be sure [of something or someone] at times”

Practice: What is something you worry about at times?

from time to time

1.sometimes; occasionally; without consistent regularity
Similar, see also: “at times,” “now and then,” “once in a while”

Examples: “From time to time, I like to have a glass of wine with dinner.”
“We meet each other for coffee from time to time.”
“After you move to Chicago, promise me you’ll call me from time to time.”

Common sentence: “We message each other from time to time”; “We talk from time to time.” (This is a common response to someone who asks if you still have contact with someone you both know. For example: “Do you still talk to Denise?” “Yeah. I text her from time to time.”)

In addition to being either at the beginning or at the end of a sentence, “from time to time” can be used as a standalone response. For example: “Do you still go to the gym?” “From time to time.”

Collocations: “call [someone] from time to time,” “do [something] from time to time,” “go [somewhere] from time to time,” “see [someone] from time to time,” “talk from time to time”

Practice: Who is someone you talk to from time to time?

now and then

1.sometimes; occasionally; without consistent regularity
Common variation: “every now and then”
Similar, see also: “at times,” “from time to time,” “once in a while”

Examples: “Now and then, we get together for coffee.”
“Do you still play the piano?” “Now and then.”
“When we were kids, we used to go to soccer games every now and then.”

Common sentence: “We talk (every) now and then.” (We talk sometimes, without any consistent regularity.)

This phrase can be used as a standalone response, or at the start or end of a sentence. Its common variation, “every now and then,” has the same meaning and impact when it is used.

Practice: What’s a place you visit now and then?

once again

1.again; one more time; as usual (in some contexts—see explanation below)

Examples: “If Juventus wins this match, they will be champions once again!”
“Once again, my wi-fi has failed me.” (This is a regular occurrence.)
“I’d like to see them in concert once again.”

Common sentence: “Once again, everything is back to normal.” (This can be said after a period of confusion or disorganization.)

This phrase can be used at the beginning of a sentence, the end of a sentence, or in the middle of a sentence before a main verb. For example: “As they entered the dark room, they once again felt scared.” This phrase can also have a similar feeling to “as usual” in some contexts. For instance: “Once again, you have lied to me and disappointed me.” In this case, the speaker is clearly frustrated with the listener, and there is a sense that the listener often lies and disappoints the speaker.

Usage note: “Once again” is similar to “once more” in many contexts, but the focus with “once more” is on doing something just one extra time. Because of this, it can be used with adverb phrases such as “at least” and “at most,” while “once again” can’t. For example: “I’d like you to try it once more at least”; “We’ll need to do it once more at most.” The phrase “once again” cannot be used in these situations.

Practice: Express disappointment by starting a sentence with “Once again.” (Ex: “Once again, I have to clean up the house by myself.”)

once in a while

1.sometimes; occasionally; without consistent regularity
Common variation: “every once in a while”
Similar, see also: “at times,” “from time to time,” “now and then”

Examples: “Once in a while, my roommate orders a pizza and eats the whole thing by himself. I swear!” (“I swear” = “I promise” and/or “I’m being serious”)
“When I first got my driver’s license, I got some speeding tickets, but only once in a while.”
“Do you still talk to each other?” “Once in a while. We’re both busy people.”

Common sentence: “Call me once in a while.” (This is an encouraging sentence to a friend whom you want to contact you more regularly. Make sure you are familiar with the person before saying something so personal.)

This phrase can be used as a standalone response, or at the start or end of a sentence. Its common variation, “every once in a while,” has the same meaning and impact when used.

Practice: What is a shirt you only wear once in a while?

NOUN PHRASES

The phrases in this chapter function as nouns. Many of them are idiomatic. The vast majority of them can be used in the subject or object position of a sentence. Any exceptions are noted.

a blast from the past

1.something one encounters in the present that was part of one’s distant past, such as a song, movie, toy, etc.; something or someone who surprises you because you had almost forgotten about them
Common extension: “a real blast from the past”

Examples: “Do you remember our old math teacher, Mr. Carter?” “I haven’t thought about him in years! What a blast from the past.”
“I used to listen to this album all the time as a teenager. Talk about a blast from the past.” (The phrase “talk about” followed by a noun or noun phrase is used to emphasize the noun or noun phrase.)
“I had totally forgotten about that movie. Wow. What a blast from the past.”

Common sentence: “That’s a (real) blast from the past.” (That’s something I haven’t thought about in a long time. This can refer to a person, an event, a movie, a song, etc.)

This phrase is usually used in a light-hearted (meaning in a cheerful and/or entertaining) way. If you hear a song on the radio that you haven’t thought about for a long time, for example, you might say “Wow! This is a real blast from the past!” You can also use this phrase to talk about something modern that is actually retro and that is trying to be a tribute to something from the past. This means it “feels” old or that it reminds you of something similar that is older. A new video game that uses an older graphics style could be considered “a blast from the past,” for example.

Practice: Have you experienced any blasts from the past recently (either by hearing an old song or hearing news about someone you knew a long time ago)?

a far cry from [somewhere]

1.a long distance from one place to another; a long way

Examples: “Mumbai is a far cry from Berlin.”
“Are you sure you want you work there? It’s a far cry from where you live.”
“She moved to Monaco, which is a far cry from her hometown.”

Common sentence: “That’s a far cry from here.” (That place is very far from here.)

This is the most literal version of this idiomatic phrase. It simply means that one place is very far from another place, or that you have to “cry” (meaning yell or shout) loudly to communicate between two places. Think of a person standing on a mountain trying to call to a friend who is standing on another mountain that is far away. The two friends are a far cry from each other. This phrase can also be used to refer to figurative distances. For example: “My French skills are a far cry from where I’d like them to be.”

Related phrases: “a good ways away from,” “far removed from”

Practice: What is a major city that is a far cry from where you live?

2.very different; less than or not what you expected, usually in a negative way

Examples: “This is the finished product? It’s a far cry from what you promised me.”
“We chose to buy a house in a different neighborhood because the first place we looked at was a far cry from what we could afford.”
“I can’t believe she married Justin. He’s a far cry from an ideal husband.”

Common sentence: “It’s a far cry from what I’m used to.” (It’s very different from what I’m familiar with.)

If something is a far cry from something else, it’s usually not as good as you wanted it to be, or simply not what you expected it to be—it’s very different. This phrase can simply mean that something is very different or far apart from something else (“Heavy metal music is a far cry from jazz music”), but it can also be used to show your disappointment with something (“This is a far cry from what I expected”).

Collocations: “a far cry from one’s expectations,” “a far cry from what one is used to”

Related phrases: “a long way away from,” “far off from”

Practice: Have you ever purchased something that was a far cry from what you expected?

a free-for-all

1.a contest, game, or situation that is open to everybody and that has no rules; a chaotic situation where people are competing for something

Examples: “If you want any birthday cake, you should hurry up and go to the kitchen. It’s a free-for-all in there!”
“We didn’t send our kids to that daycare because it’s just a free-for-all. The kids have zero structure and just do whatever they want all day.”
“I wish you had seen the store on Christmas Eve. It was a free-for-all! People were pushing each other all over the place just to buy gifts.”

Common sentence: “It’s a free-for-all.” (It’s a wild, chaotic situation with no rules and no structure.)

Some common contexts for this phrase include: people who are competing for products at a store or market, a game where anyone can join, a situation where a group of people are waiting for something but there is no ordered line. For example: “I hate going to buffet restaurants. They’re just one big free-for-all. I don’t like walking around with a plate and bumping into people just so I can eat.” Think of the phrase like this: Things are “free” (open) for “all” (everyone).

Practice: Are there any stores or markets that feel like a free-for-all in your area? (This does not mean things are free, but that the places are chaotic.)

case in point

1.referring to a particular example or instance of what is being discussed; an example to show that what you are saying is true

Examples: “Max isn’t a good worker. Case in point, he always arrives late.”
“The environment is getting worse. The quickly-changing climate is a good case in point.” (It is a good example of my main argument.)
Dad: “Your mother and I think you’re too careless with your money.”
Mom: “Case in point, you have bought two cell phones in six months.”

Common sentence: “Case in point.” (Used as a standalone response to say “That is a good example of what we are discussing or trying to say.”)

Use this phrase to point to an example of what you are discussing. It’s similar to saying “An example of my point.” This phrase is usually used at the beginning of a new sentence, but it can be put at the end as well, as in example two. It can also be used as a standalone response to say “See? That’s a perfect example of what I’m talking about.” For instance:
A: “We shouldn’t hire him.”
B: “You’re probably right. For one thing, his qualifications are weak.”
A: “Case in point.”

Related phrases: “for example,” “for instance,” “to illustrate”

Practice: Finish the idea: “She’s a responsible person. Case in point,...”

food for thought

1.something to think about; an idea for someone to consider

Examples: “Here’s some food for thought. What if we stay at my cousin’s place instead of at a hotel?”
“I’m worried that if we don’t make a decision today, the boss might get really angry and punish the entire office. Just some food for thought.”
A: “In general, do you think poor people or rich people are happier?”
B: “That’s some food for thought.”

Common sentence: “Just some food for thought.” (This is just something for us to consider or think about.)

This phrase means “Here is something to think about.” It is very often used in the sentence “Here’s some food for thought,” followed by the thing you want the other person to consider/think about. It can be used as a standalone phrase after you make a point. In this case, it is often expanded to “Just some food for thought.” For example: “If you vote for him, what will the consequences be for your kids? Just some food for thought.”

You can use this phrase to show that you are not being aggressive in a discussion because you just want someone to consider a particular idea. However, depending on your tone and what you said prior, it can be used to show that you think your ideas are superior.

Related phrases: “something to consider,” “something to think about”

Practice: What has recently given you some food for thought (a news article, a personal situation, a book, etc.)?

one of a kind

1.something or someone who is unique or very different; the only one like it/him/her/them; unlike anything or anyone else of its kind/type

Examples: “Be careful with that antique plate! It’s one of a kind.”
“I’m so glad I met you. You truly are one of a kind.”
“There’s no city in the world like New York. It’s one of a kind.”

Common sentence: “It’s one of a kind”; “You’re/He’s/She’s one of a kind.” (There is nothing in the world like it/you/him/her.)

This phrase means that someone or something is incredibly special because it or they are unique. “One of a kind” is usually used in a positive or neutral way, but it can be used in a negative way, too. For instance, “Your mom is one of a kind” can have a positive or negative meaning depending on the tone of your voice and the context of the discussion.

Related words and phrases: “one and only,” “incomparable,” “unique”

Practice: Do you own anything that is one of a kind?

one on one

1.one person to one person; an encounter between just two people

Examples: “When was the last time you spoke to him one on one?”
“All of their courses are one on one.”
“She enjoys combat sports because they’re one on one.”

Common sentence: “We spoke one on one.” (We spoke privately.)

Some common contexts for this phrase include classes, games, and private conversations and meetings. Another way to say “We spoke one on one” is “We spoke privately. Just the two of us.”

Grammar note: You can use the hyphenated form “one-on-one” when you’re using this as an adjective in front of a noun or noun phrase. For example: “They offer one-on-one classes.”

Collocations: “meet one on one,” “speak one on one,” “talk one on one”

Related phrases: “face to face,” “man to man,” “person to person”

Practice: Do you prefer group meetings or one-on-one meetings?

peace of mind

1.feeling calm and not worried; the state of freedom from anxiety and worry
Similar, see also: “at peace”

Examples: “If you want peace of mind about your online security, you should pay for a good antivirus program.”
“I’ve been waiting for the government to make a decision for two months. I want to know for my own peace of mind, so I know what I have to do next.”
“She constantly worries about her brother’s health. She doesn’t have peace of mind.”

Common sentence: “I just want peace of mind.” (This can be said about numerous situations that you are worried about or want finality on.)

This phrase is often used by insurance and home security companies. For example: “What if your house is flooded tomorrow? With our premium home insurance plan, you can have peace of mind.” Peace of mind is very often something you “want,” “need,” or “have.” Something or someone can also “give” you peace of mind.

If you have peace of mind, you are free from worry—your mind and your body can relax. You can have peace of mind about certain things in your life, but not in others. For example: “I’m glad my kids are staying with their grandparents today. It gives me peace of mind that they won’t get into any trouble. I’m still worried about where they’ll go to school next year, though.”

Sometimes, “peace of mind” simply means that someone or some entity (such as a company, a bank, a government office, etc.) finally makes a decision that affects you, so you can stop worrying about the decision. Even if the decision is not what you wanted (a company doesn’t hire you, for example), you have peace of mind because you are no longer wondering about and waiting for the decision.

Related phrase: “inner peace”

Practice: Do you currently have peace of mind? Why or why not?

point of view

1.a perspective; a particular way of seeing things

Examples: “I’ve never heard that point of view before. Could you expand on it?”
“It sounds like we have a lot of different opinions. Jeff, what’s your point of view on the matter?”
“From my point of view, the book does a poor job of explaining the value of art to society.”

Common sentence: “From my point of view,...” (followed by your opinion)

Your point of view is how you see things. Our points of view are affected by our past experiences, our current knowledge of a situation, our internal biases, and anything else that affects our beliefs and opinions about a particular topic. In civil conversation, it is considered polite to respect another person’s point of view even if you disagree with it. (There are of course limits to this in any society.)

People “have” a point of view. You can also ask someone what their point of view “is” (“What’s your point of view?”). This is basically asking someone’s opinion about a particular topic. Points of view are discussed in any situation where people gather to reach a consensus or to increase their understanding of a topic.

Related phrase: “the way one sees it” (ex: ”The way I see it, we have no other option.”)

Practice: From your point of view, who’s one of the most important people in the world today?

trial by fire

1.a test to show how someone responds in high pressure situations; a high pressure situation that reveals how a person acts under stress

Examples: “My boss gave me a really hard project last week. It was a real trial by fire.”
“Compared to a bachelor’s degree, a master’s degree is like a trial by fire.”
“When we had our first child, it felt like trial by fire. There were so many things we didn’t know.”

Common sentence: “It’s trial by fire.” (It’s a difficult, high pressure situation that reveals something about one’s character.)

This phrase comes from the medieval practice of determining if someone was guilty of a crime, by having them complete a difficult task, such as walking barefoot through a fire. You can “pass” a trial by fire. This phrase is similar to “baptism by fire,” which refers to learning something new that is difficult because of a person’s lack of experience.

Related phrase: “a sink or swim environment” (ex: “That company doesn’t offer a lot of support. It’s a sink or swim environment”), “a sink or swim situation”

Practice: Is trial by fire a good way to learn something?

word of mouth

1.oral communication; the verbal passing of information, especially when it refers to public opinions about the quality of something (ex: a product); the passing of oral information to promote something

Examples: “How did you hear about this?” “Through word of mouth.” (It’s also possible to simply say “word of mouth” in this case. In both cases, the person is saying they heard it directly from another person.)
“The word of mouth on her new book is really positive.”
“If you make a good product, people will promote it for you through word of mouth.”

Common sentence: “I heard it through word of mouth.”

This phrase is often used in marketing and promotion. Many people believe that even in the technological age, “word of mouth” is still the most powerful form of advertising. This means that if you like a product, you will tell your friends, who will tell their friends, and so on. The word of mouth on something can be positive, negative, strong, or weak. For instance, if it seems like people aren’t talking about something (ex: a new movie), you could say “The word of mouth on that movie isn’t really strong.”

Usage note: This phrase is also often preceded by “through.” This refers to how something is communicated—in this case, through word of mouth.

Collocations: “positive word of mouth,” “strong word of mouth,” “through word of mouth”

Related words: “buzz,” “hype” (ex: “There’s a lot of positive buzz about it.”)

Practice: Do you believe that digital advertising or word-of-mouth advertising is more effective?

ADJECTIVE PHRASES

Just like an adjective, an adjective phrase describes or gives more information about a noun. Many noun phrases can become adjective phrases. For example, you can say “That statue is one of a kind” (see: “one of a kind” in noun phrases), or “That’s a one-of-a-kind statue.” Just like adjectives, adjective phrases can be used in the present, past, and future. They are also often preceded by a form of the verb “to be.” For example: “You are better off alone” (see: “better off” below).

be used to

1.to be accustomed to something or someone; to feel comfortable with or familiar with something or someone; to feel like you have accepted something or someone
Do not confuse with, see also: “used to,” “get used to”

Examples: “I’m not used to waking up early.” (I usually sleep late.)
“He always yells at me, but I’m used to it.” “You shouldn’t be!”
“Before I met you, I wasn’t used to sharing my feelings with someone.”

Common sentence: “I’m used to it.” (I’m accustomed to it.)

You can be used to something or someone, but this phrase most often refers to places, things, habits, and activities. For instance, you can be used to cold weather, living in the city, pineapple on pizza, or sleeping on the floor. The negative form is “be not used to.” For example: “I’m not used to my job yet.” This means you are still learning to feel comfortable at your job.

Another common context is when you have become comfortable with a bad situation. For example: “I’m used to working for free”; “He’s used to bad news. He has had a hard life”; “We were used to poor treatment.”

Grammar note 1: “Be used to” can be followed by numerous things, including nouns (apartments, football), noun phrases (my dad’s cooking), gerunds (hiking, watching), and more. For example: “She’s used to going to bed early.”

Grammar note 2: “Be used to” is most often used in the past simple, present simple, and future simple tenses: “I was used to it”; “I’m used to it”; “I will be used to it.” However, it is more common to hear “I will get used to it” for the future tense (see “get used to”).

Collocations: “be used to a job,” “be used to a person,” “be used to a place,” “be used to doing [something],” “be used to living [somewhere]”

Related phrase: “be no stranger to [something or someone]”

Practice: What is something you still aren’t used to?

better off

1.to be in a better position (compared to another point in time or situation) Opposite, see also: “worse off”

Examples: “You’re better off not responding to that email.” (It’s better for you if you don’t respond to that email.)
“Who is better off? People with kids or people without kids?”
“You will never be better off unless you make some serious changes.”

Common sentence: “You’re better off.” (You’re in a better situation now, or you will be in a better situation if you choose this particular option. Ex: “You’re better off not talking to them.”)

While this phrase often refers to your financial position in life, you can be better off physically, mentally, emotionally, etc. This phrase simply means you were, you are, or you will be in a better position. A common context for this phrase is encouraging someone after they have ended a relationship. In particular, it’s common to say “You’re better off without him/her/them,” which means “You are in a better position because you are not with that person anymore. They were not good for you.”

Grammar note: Because this is such a versatile phrase, it can be followed by an adverb (“You’re better off here”), a present participle (“She’s better off quitting her job”), or a preposition (“I was better off in Paris”).

Collocation: “better off dead” (often said to exaggerate one’s bad situation)

Related phrases: “in a better position,” “in a better spot,” “in a good position,” “in a good spot”

Practice: Are you better off now than you were five years ago?

curious about

1.to have interest in something or someone in particular

Examples: “I’m curious about my test score. I’ll get it tomorrow.”
“Aren’t you curious about the next step?”
“What I’m really curious about is what her reaction is going to be.”

Common sentence: “What are you so curious about?” (Why are you so interested in this topic/situation/problem/etc.?)

Grammar note 1: While it’s possible to follow this phrase with a gerund (“We were curious about meeting you”), it’s more common to follow it with a noun (“They were really curious about physics”), noun phrase (“I’m curious about the next episode”), or an object pronoun (“She’s curious about you/it/him”).

Grammar note 2: You can also follow “curious” and other adjectives with to+base verb. For example: “I’m curious to hear more details.”

Practice: What is a topic you’ve been curious about lately?

get used to

1.to become accustomed to something or someone; to become comfortable with or familiar with something or someone; to go through the process of accepting something or someone
Do not confuse with, see also: “used to,” “be used to”

Examples: “Is it always so busy here?” “Don’t worry. You’ll get used to it.”
“My new job is difficult, but I’m getting used to it.”
“How’s life in Colombia? Have you gotten used to the weather?”

Common sentence: “You’ll get used to it.” (Don’t worry. You’ll become accustomed to the situation after some time and experience.)

While “be used to” means you are already accustomed to someone or something, “get used to” refers to the process of reaching that state. It is usually used with new habits or new realities in our lives. It is often used in the present continuous to state that we are still in the process of becoming accustomed to something new. For example: “I just moved from Toronto to London. I’m getting used to driving on the left side of the road.”

Grammar note 1: If you follow “get used to” with an activity, it can be a noun (videogames, football), or a gerund (hiking, running, watching). For example: “How long did it take you to get used to eating spicy food?”

Grammar note 2: “Get used to” can be used in all of the tenses: “I had gotten used to it”; “I had been getting used to it”; “I was getting used to it”; “I got used to it”; “I’m getting used to it”; “I have gotten used to it”; “I get used to it”; “I will get used to it”; “I will have gotten used to it”; “I will be getting used to it”; “I will have been getting used to it.”

Practice: What is something that took you a long time to get used to? (Ex: “It took me a long time to get used to my old job.”)

hands-on

1.involving direct action and/or involvement with something; not just talking about something in theory, but becoming directly involved with it; to actually work with something directly instead of just thinking or talking about it

Examples: “If you want to play piano, you need hands-on experience. You can’t just sit and watch online videos about music theory.”
“My English teacher is very hands-on. He points out my mistakes and shows me exactly how to fix them.”
“I took a radiology course. The first term was all theory, but in the second term, we got a lot of hands-on training.”

Common sentence: “Do you have any hands-on experience?”

If someone is “hands-on,” they like to become directly involved in a job, a process, a project, etc. A teacher can be hands-on. You can have a hands-on experience with something, or you can use a hands-on approach to a problem, situation, or role. For example, a parent can have “a hands-on approach to parenting” where they try to teach their child directly by helping them with their homework, paying attention to the friends their child has, etc. The opposite of this is a hands-off approach, where the parent gives their child more space to make their own decisions, mistakes, etc.

Collocations: “a hands-on approach,” “hands-on experience,” “hands-on training”

Practice: What are the benefits of hands-on training?

keen on [something or someone]

1.to be interested in something or someone; to enjoy a particular activity; to be interested in someone romantically or to find them attractive

Examples: “You should ask her out for coffee. I think she’s really keen on you.”
“If you want to play board games with someone, ask Christian. He’s really keen on that stuff.”
“Do you want to see the new Christopher Nolan movie?” “I don’t think so. I wasn’t too keen on his last film.”

Common sentence: “I wasn’t too keen on it.” (I wasn’t a fan of it; I didn’t enjoy it that much.)

This phrase is used to say you really enjoy something, or that you’re very interested in something. It can be a personal passion. For instance, you can be keen on a particular sport, an activity or hobby, a movie genre, a particular cuisine, etc. This phrase is often emphasized with the extension “really keen on.” The negative of this phrase is “not keen on.” The negative form is often modified as “not so keen on” or “not too keen on.”

In regard to people, if you are keen on someone, you are attracted to them and/or romantically interested in them.

Collocations: “(not) really keen on,” “(not) very keen on,” “not so keen on”

Related phrase: “be big on [someone or something],” “be into [someone or something]”

Practice: What hobbies and activities were you keen on when you were a child?

open to

1.to be receptive to or accepting of something or someone; to entertain the possibility of something happening; to allow for the possibility of something

Examples: “I don’t believe that’s true, but I’m open to having my mind changed.”
“She said she’s not sure about your plan, but she’s open to the idea.”
“Are you open to doing something active this weekend?”

Common sentence: “I’m open to it.” (I am receptive to the idea/decision/possibility/belief/etc.)

Another way to say you are open to something is “I’m keeping an open mind.” This means you are not making any immediate judgments, and you are open to having your opinions changed, or you’re allowing for the possibility of being pleasantly surprised by something. People are often open to things because they are curious about them, or they just want to give someone or something a chance.

Grammar note: “Open to” can be followed by a gerund, noun, noun phrase, adverb, or pronoun. (Ex: “I’m open to that”; “I’m open to being proven wrong.”)

Practice: What is an activity or experience you would be open to trying?

out and about

1.to be out of your home and doing things, especially after not being able to do so due to injury, illness, depression, etc.; to be engaged in activities outside of your home, especially in social contexts
Do not confuse with, see also: “up and about”

Examples: “I wish I could be out and about with you guys, but I hurt my ankle today.”
“I saw Tim at the hockey game tonight. It was nice to see him out and about after his divorce.”
“I’ll come with you to the store. I’ve been home all day, so it will be good for me to get out and about.”

Common sentence: “He’s/She’s out and about somewhere.” (I don’t know where he/she is, but I know he/she is out somewhere.)

If you’re out and about, you’re staying active outside of your home or office. If you have been indoors a lot, you can say “I need to get out and about.” If someone asks you where someone is, and you know the person is out somewhere, but you don’t know the exact location, you can say “I’m sure they’re out and about somewhere.”

Grammar note: You can follow this adjective phrase with a gerund to tell people what the subject is doing while they are out and about. For example: “He’s out and about looking for a job.” This means he is probably visiting companies directly and submitting his resume to them.

Collocations: “be out and about,” “get out and about”

Practice: Are you usually out and about on weekends, or do you usually stay at home?

out of the question

1.not something to be considered; impossible; something that is not an option or possibility (usually because someone is strongly against it)

Examples: “We should give our customers free samples.” “That’s out of the question.”
“Is it out of the question to ask for an increase in my salary this year?”
“You want to marry my daughter? Out of the question!”

Common sentence: “That’s out of the question.” (That’s not something that is possible, and/or that’s not something I want to consider as an option.)

If something is out of the question, it is not something a person wants to hear about or think about as an option. This phrase is typically used after someone makes a suggestion, a request, or has an idea that is rejected by someone else. For example: “Can I borrow your new car?” “Sorry, but that’s out of the question.” This phrase gives someone a strong “No” response.

Grammar note: “Out of the question” is most often preceded by a form of the verb “to be.” While it can be used as a quick and strong standalone response, its two most common uses are “That’s out of the question” and “It’s out of the question.”

Related phrase: “No way, no how,” “out of the realm of possibility”

Practice: What is an activity that is out of the question for you (because of personal preference, ability, or beliefs)?

par for the course

1.what is expected; normal; common; usual

Examples: “Your sister called and said she can’t help you.” “That’s par for the course.”
“The government rejected your file.” “Well, that’s just great. It’s par for the course, though.” (They expected their file to be rejected.)
“Trisha’s been late three times this week.” “That’s just par for the course, isn’t it?” (Are you surprised? She’s usually late for work.)

Common sentence: “That’s (just) par for the course.” (That’s what usually happens or what I expected to happen. I am not surprised at all.)

Use this phrase to show that you are not surprised by a piece of news that someone gives you, or to say “See? This is what you can expect from this person. Don’t act surprised when they do what they have always done.” For example: “The judge said there wasn’t enough proof to convict the thief even though there was video evidence.” “That’s par for the course in our justice system.”

This phrase comes from the game of golf, where you are expected to get the ball in the hole in a certain number of turns. If you get the ball in the expected number of turns, you “hit par.” Therefore, if you hit par for the course—meaning you hit par on all 18 holes of golf—you do what you are expected to do on that course.

Grammar note: “Par for the course” can be used as a quick standalone response to someone, but it’s most often used in the sentence “That’s par for the course.”

Related phrases: “as expected,” “not expect anything less”

Practice: What is a recent news story that made you feel like a person’s actions were par for the course? (Ex: “Lying to the public is just par for the course for him/her.”)

rife with

1.full of or abundant with something (usually with a negative meaning); full of something bad, unpleasant, or undesirable

Examples: “This app is rife with glitches.”
“The police department has been rife with corruption for many years.”
“Their relationship was rife with problems. It’s a good thing they broke up.”

Common sentence: “It’s rife with problems.” (It’s full of problems.)

This phrase is used to indicate that something has a large amount of something in it. For instance: “This meal is rife with salt.” However, today, “rife with” is typically used to talk about the negative aspects of something. For instance: “This essay is rife with grammatical mistakes.”

Collocations: “rife with errors,” “rife with mistakes,” “rife with problems,” “rife with disease,” “rife with corruption,” “rife with controversy”

Related phrases: “filled with,” “full of,” “loaded with,” “packed with”

Practice: What is something that is rife with problems right now?

spoken for

1.reserved for someone; already belonging to or promised to someone else

Examples: “Excuse me. Is this seat spoken for?”
“Can I reserve room 201?” “I’m sorry, sir. That room is already spoken for.”
“I really like Daniel. Do you know if he’s already spoken for?”

Common sentence: “It’s (already) spoken for.” (Someone else has already reserved it or expressed their interest in it.)

This phrase can be used to talk about objects, places, or people. In a shop, a customer might ask for a specific item, but the owner might say “I’m sorry. This one is already spoken for.” This means the item is being reserved for someone else because they have already expressed (“spoken”) their desire to purchase it, and they have already paid for it as well. In a movie theatre, you can say “These spoken for” to someone who wants to take two or more seats that are already reserved beside you. In this context, however, it is more common to say “I’m sorry. These are reserved” or “These are taken.”

Usage note: A person can be “spoken for” as well, but this is an old-fashioned usage. A man might say “She is spoken for” to mean that his girlfriend or wife is not single. They are already “reserved” for them. “He/She is already taken” is another way of saying this.

Collocation: “already spoken for”

Related words: “booked,” “reserved,” “taken”

Practice: What would you do if you tried to rent a room at a hotel, but all of the rooms were spoken for?

uncalled for

1.said or done without provocation or justification; not called for or needed; unnecessary; needlessly and/or excessively harsh or critical

Examples: “I think you would be a bad leader.” “And I think that opinion is uncalled for.”
“Why did you call your sister lazy?” “Sorry. I know that was uncalled for.”
“Violence is almost always uncalled for.”

Common sentence: “That was uncalled for.” (That was unnecessary and unjustified.)

If someone says “That was uncalled for,” they mean “No one asked you to say or do that, and you should have just said or done nothing instead.” Words or actions that are uncalled for usually feel excessively harsh or critical. They are unwanted and unnecessary. A person who says or does things which are uncalled for does not respect social rules. For example, if a boxer is on the ground, but their opponent gives them an extra punch, the commentator might say “That was uncalled for! He was already down!”

Grammar note: This phrase is usually used in the past, but it can be used in the present as well, usually in this form: “That’s uncalled for.”

Related phrases: “a bit (too) much,” “below the belt,” “out of order”

Practice: What types of public behaviours are uncalled for?

up and about

1.not in bed anymore (after sleeping or resting due to an illness, injury, or operation)
Do not confuse with, see also: “out and about”

Examples: “Is dad awake yet?” “Yeah, I think he’s up and about in the kitchen.”
“After the operation, it took a week before she was up and about again.”
“I’m going to call Peter. He’s usually up and about around this time.” (He’s usually awake and doing things around this time.)

Common sentence: “He’s/She’s up and about somewhere.” (He’s/She’s awake and somewhere in the house/area/etc.)

If you’re up and about, you’re out of bed and active. The first context for this phrase is that a person is no longer sleeping (“I’m usually up and about at 7 o’clock”) or they are not sleeping yet (“Is she still up and about, or has she gone to bed?”). The second context for this phrase is that a person has had an illness, injury, or operation. For example, a doctor might tell a patient, “You should be up and about in two or three days” after an operation. This means the person will be mobile and able to leave their bed to do regular activities in two or three days.

Grammar note: Follow “up and about” with a verb+ing to say what someone is doing. For example: “He’s up and about cleaning his room.” This means he is awake, out of bed, and cleaning his room.

Related phrase: “out of bed”

Practice: How long does it usually take you to get up and about after having a cold?

up for grabs

1.available and able to be obtained by anyone; something that is able to be won (as in a contest)

Examples: “This week, seven million dollars are up for grabs in the lottery.”
“Is that tablet still up for grabs, or did someone already win it?”
“The President quit last week, so his job is up for grabs.”

Common sentence: “It’s up for grabs.” (It’s available to be won or taken by anyone who wants it, or who wants to try to get it.)

To get a better image of this expression, imagine someone throwing a chocolate bar into the air, and a lot of hands reaching up to grab it. Many things can be up for grabs: prizes, jobs, money, or anything else that is available or that suddenly becomes available and is able to be obtained by someone.

Grammar note: This phrase usually comes at the end of a sentence and often follows the structure “X is/are (not) up for grabs.” For example: “If anyone wants it, my camera is up for grabs. I don’t need it anymore.” The negative form is “not up for grabs,” as in “Sorry, but the job isn’t up for grabs anymore.”

Related phrases: “there for the taking,” “yours for the taking”

Practice: Are there any positions up for grabs in your company right now?

worse off

1.to be in a worse position (compared to another point in time or situation) Opposite, see also: “better off”

Examples: “You’ll be worse off if you leave the group.”
“Always remember that you were worse off five years ago.”
“He’s worse off now than he’s ever been.”

Common sentence: “I’m worse off not doing anything.” (I’m going to be in a worse position if I do nothing. I need to do something.)

While this phrase often refers to your financial position in life, you can be worse off physically, mentally, emotionally, etc. This phrase simply means you were, you are, or you will be in a worse position.

Grammar note: Because this is such a versatile phrase, it can be followed by an adverb (“I was worse off there”), a present participle (“You’re worse off not doing anything”), or a preposition (“I was worse off with him”).

Related phrases: “in a bad position,” “in a bad spot,” “in a worse position,” “in a worse spot”

Practice: Think of a time when you were worse off. What happened to change your situation?

ADVERB PHRASES

Just like an adverb, adverb phrases can modify verbs, adjectives, other adverbs, or entire sentences. The important thing is to know how the phrase works in the context of a sentence. Read the examples and explanations to get a clear idea of the function of each phrase. Perhaps some are similar to phrases in your first language.

about time (“It’s about time...”)

1.this should have happened sooner; something one wanted or expected to happen before now; it is close or past time for this decision/event/etc.

Examples: “It’s about time they got married!”
“Gina got her university degree? It’s about time!”
“It’s about time to eat, isn’t it?”

Common sentence: “It’s about time!” (I am relieved this has finally appened or is finally happening. This should have happened sooner.)

This phrase is almost always used in the common sentence, “It’s about time.” This sentence can be used to show your relief that something has finally happened or is going to happen. Before this point in time, you may have felt frustrated, annoyed, impatient, or just upset that something hadn’t happened yet—or you had stopped expecting it to happen.

You can also use this phrase to give someone life advice or a push in the right direction. For example: “It’s about time that you learned how to drive”; “It’s about time you got your own apartment.”

Grammar note: “It’s about time” can be followed by the following:

1) A “that” clause with the simple past: “It’s about time that we left.” In this case, the past simple “left” still refers to the present moment, as “It’s about time” is similar to a conditional or an “I wish” situation. You can also omit the “that” in this structure. For example: “It’s about time you got a job.”

2) A “to”-infinitive: “It’s about time to leave.” In this case, you are saying it is time to go. This can be a statement of fact or a suggestion.

Related phrase: “high time” (ex: “It’s high time you grew up.”)

Practice: Finish the sentence: “It’s about time that we...”

about to [do something]

1.to have (or have had) the intention of doing something in the immediate future; to look like something is going to happen (or was going to happen) in the immediate future
Common extension: “just about to”

Examples: “Hey, John! I’m happy to see you! I was just about to call you!”
“The bus is about to leave, so hurry up.” (The bus is leaving very soon.)
“It looks like it’s about to rain.” (The sky is grey and there are clouds.)

Common sentence: “I was just about to _____.” (I was going to do this in the immediate future. Examples: call you, leave, tell you something, email him/her, etc.)

If you are about to do something, you are a moment away from doing it. This phrase is preceded by a “be” verb, and is most often used in the present (“I’m about to start preparing dinner”) and the recent past (“She was just about to make a huge mistake”). These cases are similar to “I’m going to start preparing dinner” and “She was going to make a huge mistake.” These decisions and actions seem(ed) imminent. It’s possible they’re going to happen—as in the case of “I’m about to start preparing dinner,” where the intention is clear that this person will prepare dinner—or maybe they won’t, as in the case of “She was about to make a huge mistake,” where it seems like something stopped her from making a huge mistake.

“Be about to” can also refer to events that seem or seemed imminent, even if they’re happening to objects. For example: “The building is about to collapse”; “My computer is about to shut down”; “Your phone was about to fall off the table, so I grabbed it.”

Related phrase: “on the verge of” (ex: “He’s on the verge of crying.”)

Practice: Finish the idea: “I was just about to...”

after all

1.despite contrary views or claims; despite other considerations and expectations; used to say that something is true despite other things that were said or planned before

Examples: “We shouldn’t eat there. After all, there are cheaper and better restaurants.”
“Why did you call him? You’re not together anymore after all.”
“Are you sure you want to join us? After all, you don’t even like soccer.”

Common sentence: “After all, you didn’t say anything.” (I did something without consulting you because you did not say anything to stop me.)

To simplify the above definitions, “after all” is often used to introduce a point to support something you have just said. For example: “He shouldn’t travel by plane. After all, he is 95 years old.”

Grammar note: “After all” can be used at the beginning or at the end of a sentence: “After all, it wasn’t a good idea” or “It wasn’t a good idea after all.” Avoid the common misspelling, “afterall.”

Practice: Finish the idea: “We shouldn’t go there. After all,...”

2.when everything in considered and in view of all circumstances; after everything
Similar, see also: “in the end”

Examples: “It looks like it’s going to rain after all.”
“We didn’t attend the wedding after all.”
“I’m leaving. Coming here wasn’t a good idea after all.”
“It’s a small world after all.” (Context: popular lyrics from a popular song)

Common sentence: “It was true after all.” (Despite contrary claims, and in view of all circumstances, it was actually true.)

Grammar note: In this case, “after all” is typically used at the end of a sentence. For example: “It was a great day after all.”

Collocations: “be right after all,” “be wrong after all”

Practice: Have you ever thought you were right about something, but you were wrong after all?

all in all

1.after considering everything; after thinking about all of the positives and the negatives; in the final outcome or result; overall

Examples: “I went for a hike and visited a friend. All in all, I had a good day.”
“We survived. All in all, things could have been much worse.”
“I wasn’t a fan of the soundtrack, but I loved the story. All in all, I thought it was an enjoyable film.”

Common sentence: “All in all, I’m glad I did it.” (I’m glad that I made that decision or had that experience.)

Use this phrase to give your final assessment or conclusion about something. It can be about the quality of something such as a film, a game, an album, or a book. It can even be an assessment of an idea, philosophy, or a theory. For instance: “All in all, I think his ideas are very practical.” You can also use it to talk about personal experiences. For example: “All in all, she regrets not selecting a different university.”

Related phrase: “everything taken into account,” “in the grand scheme of things,” “on the whole”

Practice: All in all, how was your last vacation?

as a matter of fact

1.in fact; actually

Examples: “Did she call you?” “As a matter of fact, she did!”
“That store is really old. As a matter of fact, I remember buying my first basketball there when I was just 8 years old.”
“Is the episode almost finished?” “It’s not, as a matter of fact. There are still 25 minutes left.”

Common sentence: “Yes/No, as a matter of fact.” (This is similar to saying “Actually, yes,” or “Actually, no.”)

Use “As a matter of fact” when you want to give a more specific example of what you’re saying, or you just want to provide more details about something. For instance: “Lions are killers. As a matter of fact, a single lion kills around 15 large animals per year for food.”

You can also use this phrase when you want to answer a question, or counter or correct what someone else is saying. For example: “I’ve heard that she’s a bad boss. Is that true?” “It’s not, as a matter of fact. She gave every employee a six-percent raise last year.”

Grammar note: You can use “as a matter of fact” at the beginning or at the end of a sentence. It’s common to use it at the end of a yes/no question. For instance: “Did you see Mount Fuji while you were in Japan?” “Yes, as a matter of fact.”

Related phrases: “believe it or not,” “in actual fact,” “in point of fact”

Practice: Finish the idea: “Tricia loves the beach. As a matter of fact,...”

as far as

1.to the degree or extent

Examples: “Is he interested in joining the team?” “As far as I can tell, he seems interested, but it’s hard to know for sure.”
“As far as your salary is concerned, you don’t have to worry.”
“How’s the new receptionist doing?” “As far as I’ve seen, she’s organized and is handling things well.” (Based on what I’ve seen, she is doing well.)

Common sentence: “As far as I know.” (Based on the degree of my knowledge of the topic/situation/etc., your statement is accurate.)

A common usage of this phrase is “As far as I know.” Another way to understand this usage is: “Based on what I know,” or “This is the extent or degree of my knowledge on this topic.” For example: “As far as I know, our new computers are supposed to be delivered tomorrow.” This is the information I have heard about the delivery of our new computers. I might be right or I might be wrong. I am just telling you what I know. This sentence can also be used as a standalone response. For instance: “Is your cousin doing okay after the accident?” “As far as I know.” This means “Based on the most recent information I have, he is doing well.”

Collocations: “as far as I know,” “as far as I can tell” (similar to “as far as I can see”), “as far as I’ve seen,” “as far as that’s concerned” (informal alternative: “as far as that goes”)

Related phrases: “to the degree that,” “to the extent that”

Practice: As far as you know, what’s one of the best places to live in your area/region?

at the drop of a hat

1.without delay; immediately; without strong provocation or persuasion
Similar, see also: “on the spot”

Examples: “I’m lucky to have such a good brother. If I need help with anything, I just need to call him, and he comes at the drop of a hat.”
“Tam treats him poorly, but he runs to her at the drop of a hat. It’s sad.”
“I don’t like that yoga studio. They cancel classes at the drop of a hat.”

Common sentence: “He’ll/She’ll do it at the drop of a hat.” (They will do it without strong provocation or persuasion. I know them well, and this is the type of person they are.)

If you do something at the drop of a hat, you do it almost immediately—like a reflex. This can be because of your character or because of a strong external motivator. For example: “My boss yells at his employees at the drop of a hat.” In this case, the boss is someone who gets angry easily. Imagine someone dropping their hat, and someone else doing something before the hat touches the floor. This is the basic idea behind this phrase. “At the drop of a hat” could refer to any action, either positive or negative.

Related phrases: “on the spur of the moment,” “without provocation,” “without warning”

Practice: Who is someone you would help at the drop of a hat (meaning if they needed your help, you would help them immediately)?

back and forth

1.backward and forward; from one location to another and back again; from one person to another

Examples: “How do your kids get to and from school every day?” “They travel back and forth by bus.”
“We’ve been messaging each other back and forth for two hours.”
“We saw a couple arguing back and forth at the restaurant.”

Common sentence: “How long does it take you to go back and forth?” (How long does it take you to travel from one point to the other and back again? This is often asked about a person’s travel time to and from work or school.)

You can do many things back and forth. For instance, you can travel back and forth, message back and forth, or argue back and forth with someone. The idea is there is a constant cycle of movement from one point (one place or person) to another. People often talk about how long it takes them to travel to school or work, and back to their home again. For example: “It takes me three hours to travel back and forth between my house and my job.” If you’re anxious or angry about something, and you feel like you need to stand up and walk back and forth to help you process your emotions or just do something physical, you can say “I paced back and forth.”
