Collocations: “argue back and forth,” “be back and forth between,” “drive back and forth,” “go back and forth,” “message back and forth,” “pace back and forth,” “run back and forth,” “travel back and forth”

Related phrases: “backward and forward,” “forward and backward”

Practice: How long does it take you to travel back and forth between your home and your job/school?

by oneself

1.without any help from other people
Common extension: “all by oneself”

Examples: “Your house looks great. Did you do the renovations all by yourself?”
“They cut the tree down by themselves.”
“Do you need any help?” “No, thank you. I can do it by myself.”

Common sentence: “I did it by myself.” (I did it with no assistance.)

If you do something by yourself, you do it with no assistance from anyone. For example: “I painted my apartment by myself.” This phrase is often used to emphasize a person’s independence, or to show that a person is impressed by someone else’s resourcefulness (“Wow. She fixed her car all by herself”).

Collocation: “do [something] by oneself,” “finish [something] by oneself”

Practice: What is the last thing you did by yourself?

2.alone; without anyone else
Similar, see also: “on one’s own”

Examples: “Does he have a roommate?” “No, he lives by himself.”
“Did anyone go to the store with him?” “No, he went by himself.”
“Their parents passed away. They’ve been by themselves since they were teenagers.”

Common sentence: “I went by myself.” (I went alone.)

Collocations: “live by oneself,” “go by oneself,” “be by oneself”

Practice: Have you ever gone to see a movie or a concert by yourself?

day by day

1.in small amounts or stages, every day; incremental changes from one day to the next day
Similar, see also: “little by little,” “step by step”

Examples: “Day by day, she improved her cardiovascular health.”
“The situation is becoming more complicated day by day.”
“Day by day, my life is getting a little better.”

Common sentence: “Take it day by day.” (Do a little bit every day. Don’t get discouraged by the bigger goal—just focus on what you can do today.)

You can use this phrase to discuss both positive and negative things that are slowly changing with the passage of time. For example: “My English is improving day by day”; “The political situation is getting worse day by day.” The idea is that small changes are happening over time.

Collocations: “get better day by day,” “get worse day by day,” “go day by day,” “take it day by day”

Related phrases: “a day at a time,” “one day at a time,” “over time”

Practice: What is something in your life that is improving day by day?

early on

1.in the early stages of something, typically of a process or activity; at or during the beginning stages of something

Examples: “Early on, I wasn’t sure if I would be able to do the job, but now, I can say that I’ve been here for over seven years.”
“When you start something new, things are always harder early on.”
“I think they knew early on that they would be together forever.”

Common sentence: “Early on, I wasn’t sure.” (I had doubts in the early stages of the process/activity/relationship/etc.)

This phrase is not a simple replacement for the word “early.” For example, you cannot say “I got to school early on.”

A similar-looking phrase to “early on” is “earlier on,” which means “before now.” For example: “Earlier on, I told you what I had for breakfast.” This means I spoke with you earlier in the day, and I told you what I had for breakfast. “Earlier on” is a simple replacement for the word “earlier” and can be used in all cases where “earlier” can also be used. This means it can be used in the previous paragraph example above, “I got to school earlier on,” which means “I got to school earlier.”

Collocation: “know early on” (ex: “I knew early on that…” as in “I knew early on that if I wanted to pass the test, I would need to study hard.”)

Related phrases: “in the beginning stages,” “in the early going,” “in the early stages”

Practice: How did you feel about this book early on? How do you feel about it now?

in and of itself

1.intrinsically; by its own nature; an emphatic way to say “by itself”

Examples: “Technology is not bad in and of itself. It’s how you use it that determines its moral value.”
“In and of itself, the pharmaceutical industry isn’t corrupt, but there are people in the industry who use it for their own financial gain.”
“The first book in the series, in and of itself, is a great work of art.”

Common sentence: “It’s not bad in and of itself.” (Intrinsically, it isn’t bad.)

This is a semi-formal phrase that is used for emphasis. You can use it to assess the value or quality of something out of its context. It focuses on the basic nature of something. In this way, the phrase can be used to mean “intrinsically” or “by itself.” While it’s often used in negative sentences (“It’s not a bad thing in and of itself”), it can be used in positive cases as well (“Nature is beautiful in and of itself”).

Grammar note: You can use “in and of itself” at the start, in the middle, or at the end of a sentence. See the examples above. Note the punctuation.

Collocations: “bad in and of itself,” “good in and of itself”

Practice: Do you believe that guns are in and of themselves bad?

little by little

1.by small amounts and degrees; gradually
Similar, see also: “day by day,” “step by step”

Examples: “Little by little, I’m making positive changes in my life.”
“You won’t notice an immediate difference, but if you commit to the program, little by little, you will start seeing the benefits.”
“Little by little, the economy has started to recover.”

Common sentence: “Little by little, things are getting better.” (Gradually and by small amounts and degrees, things are improving.)

This phrase is often used to talk about gradual changes in someone or something. For example: “Little by little, the country is becoming more democratic”; “My health has been improving little by little.”

Grammar note: This phrase is typically used at the beginning or at the end of a sentence, but it can be used in the middle depending on what you want to express. Pay attention to the examples and punctuation above.

Related phrases: “a little at a time,” “in small doses,” “slowly but surely”

Practice: Finish the idea: “Little by little, we’re...”

off the top of one’s head

1.saying something without much thought or investigation (this can be said of something that is known, thought of, or remembered)
Similar, see also: “on the spot”

Examples: “Off the top of your head, can you name all of the countries in Asia?”
“Do you remember Larry’s phone number?” “Not off the top of my head.”
“What’s a good lunch restaurant around here?” “I can name at least 3 off the top of my head.”

Common sentence: “Not off the top of my head.” (I do not have this information readily available in my brain. If you want me to give you a proper answer, I will need time to think of it or to investigate it.)

If you say something off the top of your head, you say it without thinking carefully or having time to investigate things properly. If a person asks if you know something, can think of something, or can remember something off the top of your head, they want a quick answer—now! In the moment! Here are two more examples: “Off the top of your head, do you remember the name of the market we visited in Buffalo?” “Tina is amazing at geography. She can name all the capital cities in the world off the top of her head.”

Usage note: This phrase is often used in the negative standalone response “Not off the top of my head.” This means “I don’t know this information because I haven’t memorized it,” or “I can’t think of this information immediately.” “Not off the top of my head,” means you could find the information if you were given more time to think about it or investigate it.

Collocations: “know [something] off the top of one’s head,” “recite [something] off the top of one’s head,” “remember [something] off the top of one’s head”

Related phrases: “in the spur of the moment,” “off the cuff”

Practice: How many capital cities can you name off the top of your head?

on one’s own

1.independent of anyone else; alone
Similar, see also: “by oneself”

Examples: “She made the decision on her own (with no input from other people).”
“Do you live on your own?”
“You’re on your own.” (You are responsible for yourself and your own survival, and no one is going to help you.)

Common sentence: “You’re on your own.” (See example 3 above. This sentence can also mean that no one shares your situation or your opinion.)

Independence is closely associated with this phrase. It means you are free from outside influences. It can be similar to “by oneself” in meaning you are alone. However, there can be a perceived difference in saying “I’m by myself” (I’m alone) and “I’m on my own” (I’m independent). The second example has this meaning in a very specific context. Specifically, if you need help and someone says “You’re on your own,” this means they will not help you—you are responsible for yourself and your own resources for survival.

“You’re on your own” can also mean “Sorry, I’m not going to support what you just said.” For example, if someone says “I think we need stronger drug laws” in a room with three other people, and everyone stays silent because it’s clear they don’t agree with this position, one of the people could say “I think you’re on your own.” This means no one shares the idea regarding stronger drug laws. “You’re by yourself” is not used in this context.

Grammar note: “On one’s own” can be used as an adjective (“I am on my own”), or an adverb (“I finished the project on my own”), but for the simplicity of having it closer to “by oneself,” it is included in this chapter.

Collocations: “be on one’s own,” “live on one’s own”

Practice: Have you ever lived on your own? What were/are the challenges of living on one’s own?

once and for all

1.for the final time so we never have to deal with this or think about it again; for the last time ever; with finality; finally and completely; forever

Examples: “We need to eliminate this ancient law once and for all.”
“Once and for all, he hopes to prove that he is the superior fighter!”
“I’m telling you once and for all that I have no interest in dating your brother.” (I’m telling you for the last time.)

Common sentence: “Let’s finish this once and for all.” (Let’s do this for the final time ever, so that we have a decisive conclusion.)

This is a slightly dramatic phrase that means to do something for the final time. The “for all” portion implies “for all time.” The idea is that you want to do something so it can be finished and decided forever.

Collocations: “decide [something] once and for all,” “do [something] once and for all,” “end [something] once and for all,” “finish [something] once and for all”

Related phrases: “for good,” “for the last time”

Practice: What is something you wish would end once and for all (ex: war, poverty, world hunger, etc.)?

one by one

1.in an ordered sequence, starting with one then going to the next one

Examples: “Please sit in the waiting room. The doctor will call his patients one by one.”
“Eating the cookies one by one, Lee finished the package in one sitting.”
“The performers stepped onto the stage one by one.”

Common sentence: “Go one by one”; “Do them one by one.” (Do your task in an ordered way, starting with one question/task/etc., then doing the next one, and then the next one, and then…)

If there is a line of people waiting at the bank, but there is only one bank teller, the bank teller must help the people one by one. This means he/she must help one person, then another, then another, and so on, in an orderly way. You can count things one by one, organize things one by one, answer exam questions one by one, or call people one by one. These are just three examples. See the sentences above for more contexts.

Collocations: “address them one by one,” “do them one by one,” “handle them one by one,” “see them one by one,” “talk to them one by one”

Related phrases: “one after another,” “one at a time”

Practice: Do you eat chips/crisps one by one?

right now

1.precisely now; exactly now; at this exact moment; immediately
Do not confuse with, see also: “right away”

Examples: “You need to leave.” “You mean now?” “Right now!”
“I can’t talk right now. Can you call me later?”
“We can’t delay any longer. You need to make a decision right now.”

Common sentence: “I’ll do it right now.” (I will do it immediately.)

This is a more emphatic way to say “now.” You can soften it or give it a more playful tone by using the phrase “right about now,” which means “around this exact time.” This phrase is often used to create comedy in stories or real-life situations. For example, in a story, character A might ask character B: “When is it supposed to start raining?” After character A asks this, it starts raining, and character B says: “Right about now.” Brilliant!

Collocations: “come right now” “do [something] right now,” “go right now,” “leave right now”

Related phrases: “right this instant,” “right this minute,” “this instant”

Practice: What are you thinking about right now?

right away

1.in the immediate future and as quickly as possible; without any hesitation
Do not confuse with, see also: “right now”

Examples: “When she walked into the room, right away, I knew she was the one.”
“I need you to fix this right away.”
“I fell off my bike. Right away, I knew that I had broken my arm.”

Common sentence: “I’ll do it right away.” (I’ll do it immediately and as quickly as possible.)

If you do something right away, you do it almost instantly and as quickly as possible. “Right away” and “right now” share the meaning of doing something immediately, but “right away” can also refer to speed, while “right now” refers to the point in time that something happens or begins to happen.

You can use “right away” as a standalone response. For example: “Could you get me a glass of water?” “Right away.” This means I’m going to get you a glass of water now, and I will get it quickly. You might hear this usage in the service industry, such as at a restaurant. “Right now” isn’t used in this way.

Grammar note: “Right away” is typically used at the end of the sentence, but it can be used in the middle or at the beginning as well.

Collocations: “come right away,” “do [something] right away,” “get [something] right away,” “go right away,” “leave right away”

Related phrases: “straight away,” “without delay”

Practice: When you get a present from someone, do you usually open it right away? What about when you get a package in the mail?

side by side

1.adjacent to one another; beside one another; next to one another

Examples: “When we were kids, we sat side by side on the bus.”
“Citizens worked side by side with doctors to help the wounded.”
“In that neighbourhood, people from all over the world have lived side by side with each other for over fifty years.”

Common sentence: “They were side by side.” (Regarding the physical positions of at least two people or objects, or about the collaboration or support that at least two people or groups had.)

If two things or people are side by side, they are beside each other. This phrase can be used literally (“Stand side by side for the family picture!”) or figuratively. For example: “I will stand side by side with you no matter what.” This means “I will support you and be by your side in all situations.”

Collocations: “be side by side,” “stand side by side with [someone]”

Related phrases: “next to [someone or something],” “stand shoulder to shoulder with [someone]”

Practice: Who is someone you would stand side by side with during good times and bad times?

step by step

1.progressively, from one stage to the next; to do something in a specific order
Similar, see also: “little by little,” “day by day,” “one step at a time”

Examples: “Are you sure the bed is supposed to look like that? Did you follow the instructions step by step?”
“It’s a great course. It goes step by step and never makes you feel like you’re doing something that is too difficult.”
“You’re trying to do too much, too soon. Remember that it’s a process. You have to go step by step.”

Common sentence: “Go step by step.” (Don’t rush. Trust the process. Go one step at a time.)

Doing something step by step means you are doing it in a slow and steady way—you are following a process. Think about how able-bodied people walk: by taking one step with one foot, another step with the other foot, and so on. It is the same idea when you do something step by step. For example: “If you follow the instructions step by step, you shouldn’t have any problems.” This phrase is often used to encourage others or to discuss instructions for something.

Grammar note: You can use “step by step” as an adjective before a noun by hyphenating it: “It’s a step-by-step process.”

Collocations: “a step-by-step process,” “go step by step”

Related phrases: “one day at a time,” “one step at a time”

Practice: What is something that needs to be learned step by step? Are you good at following instructions and going step by step?

to an extent

1.to a certain degree
Common variations: “to a certain extent,” “to some extent”
Similar, see also: “more or less”

Examples: “I agree with you to an extent, but I think your conclusion is flawed.”
“Was it worth the money?” “To an extent.”
“I think that’s true to an extent, but it doesn’t address the economic factors that create those conditions.”

Common sentence: “I agree to an extent.” (I agree with you and/or your point to a certain degree, but I don’t agree 100%.)

Use this phrase to mean there is some truth to what someone is saying. It’s similar to saying “You are partially right, but not exactly.” It can also be used as a standalone response. For example: “Are you saying that people with pets are better people?!” “To an extent.” This is a slightly humorous example, but it shows the phrase in action. The most common context for this phrase is an argument. See examples 1 and 3 above for a better idea of this usage.

Collocations: “agree to an extent,” “be true to an extent”

Related phrases: “to a certain degree,” “to a point,” “up to a point”

Practice: Who is someone that you trust to an extent (but not completely)?

word for word

1.exactly as spoken or written; to state what someone stated exactly as they stated it (in word or in print); verbatim

Examples: “Tell him I never want him to return. Repeat my message word for word.”
“Actors need to memorize their dialogue word for word.”
“She says she copied the definition word for word from the dictionary.”

Common sentence: “I memorized it word for word.” (I memorized it exactly as it was said or written.)

You can repeat, say, copy, or write something word for word. This means you repeat something exactly as it was stated. For example: “I know the dialogue in that movie word for word.” This means you can repeat the entire movie from your memory because you have seen it so many times.

Grammar note: You can use “word for word” as an adjective by hyphenating it. For example: “It’s hard to do a word-for-word translation of that text.”

Collocations: “copy [something] word for word,” “know [something] word for word,” “memorize [something] word for word,” “repeat [something] word for word,” “say [something] word for word,” “translate [something] word for word,” “write [something] word for word”

Practice: Do you know any song lyrics word for word?

VERB PHRASES

This chapter focuses on common verb phrases, which include common verb and preposition combinations, idioms, and phrasal verbs. The function of each verb phrase has been noted when necessary. Remember that verbs are action words that describe what a subject is doing. Verb phrases function in the same way.

account for [something or someone]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to explain or show where something or someone is; to give an acceptable explanation for something

Examples: “This report doesn’t account for the $2,000 we lost last month.” (The report does not list this amount—it does not show or explain where it is.)
“I appreciate your apology, but it still doesn’t account for what you did.” (It does not explain the severity of your mistake. Your apology is not enough.)
“This news story doesn’t account for the political situation in the country.”

Common sentence: “How do you account for __________?” (How do you explain this?)

When you account for something, you are able to show where it is and/or explain what happened to it. This phrase is often used to discuss money that is missing (usually related to big business or politics), but it can be used in other contexts as well. For example: “Brock says he was at the arena. Is that true?” “I can account for him. I saw him there.” This means I can show or explain his location during a particular time because I was there, too.

Related phrase: “give an explanation for [someone or something]”

Practice: Finish the idea: “Your explanation doesn’t account for...”

2.to consider or include something or someone in a calculation
Similar, see also: “keep in mind”

Examples: “We need to account for three hundred people at the wedding.”
“When you make your report, make sure it accounts for last month’s sales in addition to this month’s sales.”
“We need to account for possible mistakes.” (We need to be prepared for possible mistakes and include those possibilities in our plan.)

Common sentence: “Have you accounted for __________?” (Have you included this or considered it in your calculation?)

Collocations: “account for potential mistakes,” “account for [someone]”

Practice: What do you need to account for if you are planning a wedding?

act on [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to take action as a result of receiving orders, new information, advice, etc.

Examples: “I need you to act on this project starting today.” (I need you to take action.)
“Are we going to act on this?” (Are we going to take action on this?)
“He attacked me, so I hit him. I acted on instinct.”

Common sentence: “Are you going to act on it?” (Are you going to do something because of it, or deal with it in some way?)

If you act on something, you take action because of it or you take action to deal with the thing you are “acting on.” In the first case, you can act on someone’s advice, for instance. For example: “I’m going to act on your advice and send him an email” (I’m going to follow your advice). In the second case, you can take action on something like a work project. See the first example above for this usage.

Collocations: “act on instinct,” “act on [someone’s] behalf,” “act on [someone’s] orders”

Practice: Do you usually act on your instincts?

add on [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, separable)

1.to attach something to something else, or to make it part of it; to include something as part of something else

Examples: “They’re going to add on another room to their house.”
“If you buy our home insurance package, car insurance comes added on.”
“So far, you have ordered three spaghetti meals. Would you like to add on anything else?” (Would you like to add anything else to your order?)

Common sentence: “You can add it on later.” (You can choose to attach it or include it later.)

If you add something on, you attach it to something else, or include it with something else. For example, a company can choose to add on an extra week of vacation to an employee’s contract. Stores can ask their customers if they want to add on something to a purchase, usually a legal contract such as a warranty. For instance: “Would you like to add on our guaranteed replacement plan to your laptop purchase?” Here is one more example of a common usage of this phrasal verb in the sales industry: “If you buy the car today, I’ll add on free oil changes for one year.” This means the oil changes will be attached or included with the purchase of the car.

Grammar note: You add on something to something else. “We’re thinking about adding on an extension to our house.”

Practice: What do tech stores usually try to add on when you buy a new phone or computer?

ask for it

idiomatic phrase

1.to invite punishment as a result of one’s behaviour; to act in a way that makes people feel like one deserves retribution/punishment
Similar, see also: “ask for trouble”

Examples: “Why are you teasing me all the time? You’re really asking for it.”
“You punched him?!” “Yeah. He was asking for it!”
“Stop tickling me! I mean it! Okay, that’s it. You asked for it!” (The person might tickle their friend/child/parent/partner/etc. in return.)

Common sentence: “You’re asking for it.” (Stop behaving like that because you are inviting punishment. The context can be serious or a joke.)

This idiomatic meaning of “ask for it” is common in situations where someone acts in a way that makes people feel like the person deserves negative consequences for their behaviour. For example, if one of your male friends is singing loudly at a party and making people around him feel annoyed, and someone else at the party steps on your friend’s foot because he wouldn’t stop singing, you might say “He was asking for it.” The “it” refers to a negative consequence. That means he was inviting punishment for his obnoxious behaviour—he deserved what he received.

This phrase can be used in a playful way. If someone is bothering you in a way that doesn’t actually bother you, you can say “You’re really asking for it” in a joking way to mean that your friend is going to be in trouble or face punishment if they don’t stop bothering you.

Unfortunately, this phrase is sometimes used by misogynistic groups when discussing the sexual assault of a woman. “She was asking for it” is a bad argument made to justify sexual assault, as it implies that a woman’s appearance invites assault, and that a man is not responsible for his actions.

Grammar note: This phrase is usually used in the past or present continuous tenses. For example: “You’re asking for it”; “You were asking for it”; “I was asking for it,” etc. It is also often used in the past simple (“You asked for it!”) to say “That’s enough. I’m going to punish you now.”

Practice: Finish the idea: “You’re asking for it if you don’t stop...” (+verb+ing)

ask for trouble

idiomatic phrase

1.to behave in a way that invites trouble; to do or say something that might result in negative consequences
Similar, see also: “ask for it”

Examples: “You stole money from your parents? That’s asking for trouble.”
“She had some ice cream even though she’s lactose intolerant. She was asking for trouble and spent half an hour in the bathroom.”
“You’re going to lift 200 pounds? Are you sure that’s not asking for trouble?”

Common sentence: “He’s/She’s/They’re asking for trouble.”

You don’t want to ask for trouble! If you ask for trouble, you say or do (or plan to say or do) something that might cause problems for you. This can be in major ways or small ways. For example, if you have a weak stomach and you eat spicy food, you’re probably asking for trouble. You’re also asking for trouble if you drive too fast in a police zone.

Grammar note: This phrase is most often used in the present and past continuous tenses: “You’re asking for trouble” or “We were asking for trouble,” for example. “That’s asking for trouble” is also common. The phrase is also used in the past simple, as in “He asked for trouble, and he got it.”

Practice: Would you tell someone if you thought they were behaving unwisely and asking for trouble?

bank on [something or someone]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to rely on something or someone 100%; to expect that something will happen, even though it might not; to trust something or someone 100%
Similar, see also: “depend on,” “rely on”

Examples: “They’re not going to win. You can bank on it.”
“Will dinner be ready soon?” “Don’t bank on it.”
“She was banking on getting a promotion at her job, but it didn’t happen.”

Common sentence: “Don’t bank on it.” (Don’t expect it to happen. Don’t have false hope. Don’t invest money or emotional energy in this outcome.)

“You can bank on it!” This means you can trust and be confident in “it.” For example: “Manchester United are going to beat Burnley tonight. You can bank on it.” This means you can bet money from the bank on this game because I am so certain the outcome will happen—even though there is a chance it won’t. This is a common phrase when we want to make a prediction about something and seem confident about it. With this in mind, this sentence is commonly used when you feel confident about the outcome of a sporting event.

In general, this phrase is used when we feel very good about the chances of a future outcome, or when we want to reassure someone that we know what we are talking about and that they can trust us. For example: “You can always bank on my friendship. I’ll always be here for you.”

Practice: What is a current sports team you can usually bank on?

base on

phrasal verb (transitive, separable)

1.to model something after something else (usually refers to a work of art, such as a book, a movie, a TV series, a painting, etc.)

Examples: “This book is based on a true story.”
“She bases all of her work on the female experience.”
“Did you know the Netflix series The Witcher is based on a series of books?”

Common sentence: “It’s based on a true story.” (It is modeled on something that really happened. It is a true story.)

Many movies are based on books. Something can be based on a true story. Someone might say they want to write a story and base it on your life. In all of these cases, a story or point of inspiration or reference is used as a model for another creation. For example: “The book is based on the author’s personal experience.”

Grammar note: While you can use “base on” in its regular form, as in “I will base the book on the Vietnam war,” it is more common to use the form “be based on,” often in the present or past simple. For instance: “The painting is based on the artist’s personal experience” or “I saw the movie that was based on that book.” “Be based on” is inseparable.

Collocations: “be based on a book,” “be based on a true story,” “be based on [someone’s] life,” “be based on true events”

Practice: How many movies or shows can you name that were based on books or true events?

2.base [something] on [something]. to use something as the basis of a belief, feeling, argument, decision, theory, etc.; to use something (facts, ideas, etc.) as a starting point and/or reason for a decision, belief, calculation, conclusion, etc.

Examples: “His beliefs are based on his family’s religion.”
“I think she’s lying.” “That’s a big accusation. What do you base it on?”
“If you’re going to write that article, you need to base it on solid facts.”

Common sentence: “What do you base that on?” (Why do you think that? What is the reason for your position/belief/etc.?)

“What do you base that on?” You might ask this question if you want to understand the source of someone’s ideas. The person might respond, “I base it on scientific evidence.” The person is explaining the source of their belief or argument—the base or foundation of their knowledge.

Grammar note: As in usage 1, it is more common to use the form “be based on” instead of simply “base on.” Note the example sentences above.

Collocations: “based on personal experience,” “based on the fact that...,” “based on the information available,” “based on what one knows,” “be based on facts”

Practice: What are your personal beliefs based on?

bring about [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, separable)

1.to cause something to happen; to be the catalyst for something; to create a consequence, effect, or result; to cause change in a situation
Similar, see also: “come about”

Examples: “If these policies work, they could bring about an age of great prosperity.”
“What brought about the change in your attitude/perspective/etc.?”
“He wasn’t the one who brought about these disastrous social conditions.”

Common sentence: “What brought it about?” (What caused it to happen?)

A person can bring about positive change or negative change. A political election can bring about stability or chaos. Your beliefs and opinions can also be brought about by something. In all of these cases, the thing or person who brings about something causes something to happen. In many cases, you can substitute the phrase “bring about” with “bring” or “cause” in your mind for a similar meaning. For instance: “Gandhi helped to bring about Indian independence.” This means Gandhi helped to bring independence to India, or he helped to cause the situation.

Grammar note: The common structure for this phrasal verb is: the agent of the change + bring about + the result. This phrase is common in all tenses. For instance: “Her charity has brought about hope to many people” (present perfect) or “Streaming platforms are bringing about a revolution in how people watch entertainment” (present continuous).

Collocations: “bring about change,” “bring about peace,” “bring about disaster,” “bring about hope,” “bring about a new era”

Practice: What is something we can do to bring about a better world?

chime in

phrasal verb (intransitive)

1.to give or offer your opinion in a group discussion

Examples: “Thanks for chiming in, but I don’t think your point is relevant.”
“If I may just chime in for a minute…” (Say this when you want to enter the discussion or debate.)
“Ron always has to chime in with his opinion even when no one asks for it.”

Common sentence: “Do you want to chime in on this?” (Do you want to offer your opinion on what we are discussing?)

If you chime in during a discussion or debate, you enter the discussion or debate and offer your opinion, in the same way a bell chimes and attracts attention. This phrase is common in informal meetings or discussions between friends and colleagues. People usually chime in with suggestions, ideas, opinions, etc. For instance, “Vera, would you like to chime in?” is an invitation for Vera to speak and to offer her thoughts on what is being discussed.

Grammar note: While “chime in” by itself is intransitive, which means it is not followed by an object, it can be extended to become transitive with two words: “with” and “on.” Note the examples: “He chimed in with a great suggestion”; “They are conducting a public survey because they want regular people to chime in on the debate.”

Collocations: “chime in on [something],” “chime in with an idea,”

Related phrases: “have one’s say,” “put in one’s two cents,” “voice one’s opinion”

Practice: Do you always try to chime in when you’re involved in a discussion?

chip in

phrasal verb (transitive or intransitive, separable but usually not separated)

1.to donate or offer one’s time or money to a group cause; to voluntarily contribute to a group effort
Similar, see also: “pitch in”

Examples: “Sorry, I can only chip in 5 dollars.” (I can only give 5 dollars to the cause.)
“Everyone is chipping in to buy Brenda a new backpack.”
“If we all chip in, we can finish this project by this afternoon.”

Common sentence: “Thanks for chipping in.” (Thanks for your contribution to the group effort/project/etc.)

Chipping in is a voluntary action. The most common usage is to give money to a group cause. For example, if a woman at your workplace has a baby, your manager might ask employees to chip in money to help buy a gift for the new mother: “Everyone is chipping in to buy Brenda a gift.”

You can also chip in to a cause with your time and energy. This means you contribute to a group effort. For example: “We all chipped in to clean up the park this past weekend.” See the examples above for more contexts.

Grammar note: The similar phrase above, “pitch in,” is typically used to discuss contributions of one’s time or energy. Between the two, “chip in” is more common for financial contributions, though it is also equally used for time and energy contributions.

Collocation: “chip in (a monetary value)”

Related phrases: “cough up,” “shell out,” “make a contribution”

Practice: Think of a time you chipped in for something. What was it?

come about

phrasal verb (intransitive)

1.to come into existence; to be the result of something or someone; to happen because of something or someone
Similar, see also: “bring about”

Examples: “Disaster came about when the two nations declared war on one another.”
“You mentioned that your last movie was very difficult to make. Could you tell us how it came about?”
“How did it come about that people became so addicted to their phones?” (How did this situation happen?)

Common sentence: “How did it come about?” (How did it come into existence?)

“How do islands come about?” This question means “How are islands formed or how do they begin existing?” Here is an example answer: “They come about as a result of underwater volcanoes.” This means islands are formed as a result of underwater volcanoes.

What is the difference between “come about” and “bring about?” Here are two sentences that illustrate the difference: “Their government brought about war”; “War came about because of their government.” As you can see, “bring about” focuses on the cause of something, while “come about” focuses on the result.

Grammar note: To mention the cause of what came about, it is common to use “because of” or “as a result of” after “come about.” For example: “The company came about as a result of two women.” This means the company came into existence because of two women. Note the structures that are possible with this phrasal verb by examining the examples above.

Practice: Finish the idea: “This was brought about by...”

count on [someone]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to rely or depend on someone; to trust that someone will act in the way you expect them to act
Same, see also: “depend on,” “rely on”

Examples: “I’m worried that I can’t count on you. You disappointed me twice today.”
“Don’t count on Jim if you want the job done correctly.”
“I want you to deliver this to the dean’s office. Can I count on you to do it?”

Common sentence: “You can count on me.” (You can depend on me. You can trust me to do what I say I will do.)

If you can count on someone, they are probably a very dependable and/or reliable person. You know what to expect from them and you can trust them to do what they say they will do. Friends count on each other. Children count on their parents. Companies count on their employees, and vice versa.

Practice: Who can you count on in your life?

2.count on [something]. (transitive, inseparable) to expect something to happen; to feel very confident about a future event/occurrence/prediction

Examples: “You can always count on rain in this city.”
“They’re going to lose. You can count on it.”
“I’ll buy a house after I win the lottery.” “You shouldn’t count on that.”

Common sentence: “Don’t count on it.” (Don’t expect it to happen.)

This phrase is used when you are certain or almost certain about something. For instance: “If you live in Canada, you can count on snow in February.” This means you can expect it. You can use the standalone response “Don’t count on it” to tell someone that they shouldn’t expect a particular result or occurrence. For example: “Do you think we could ever be together again?” “Don’t count on it.” The second person is telling the first person that their romantic relationship is finished and that the first person should not expect the relationship to start again.

Practice: Can you count on the weather in your area? (Is it usually predictable?)

deal with [something or someone]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to handle something or someone; to manage a situation or a person; to do something about a person or a situation that is causing problems

Examples: “Are you sure it won’t be a problem?” “Don’t worry. I’ll deal with it.”
“You’re going to deal with a lot of stress if you accept this promotion.”
“It was a difficult period in my life, but I dealt with it.”

Common sentence: “I’ll deal with it.” (I’ll manage or handle it; I’ll take responsibility for solving this problem/issue.)

How you deal with people and with situations determines part of your character. Do you get angry at the smallest thing? Are you calm or do you freeze in stressful situations? If you work at a job where you have to deal with people, how do you manage difficult customers or clients? All of these situations refer to how you deal with things or people—how you handle or manage them. For example: “I’m dealing with a lot of financial problems” means you currently have financial problems that you are trying to manage. “He’s dealing with the death of his grandmother” means his grandmother died and he is trying to manage his emotions after her death.

A specific context of this phrasal verb means to handle or manage someone or something that is causing you problems. “I’ll deal with him” means you will handle the situation concerning “him.” Perhaps he works with you and hasn’t been doing his job properly lately, and you need to have a serious conversation with him to discuss his poor performance.

Collocations: “deal with stress,” “deal with problems,” “deal with loss,” “deal with success,” “deal with challenges,” “deal with uncertainty,” “deal with questions,” “deal with emergencies,” “deal with situations,” “deal with people,” “deal with setbacks,” “deal with complaints”

Related phrases: “come to terms with,” “contend with,” “grapple with”

Practice: How do you deal with stress?

depend on [someone or something]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to be determined by something or someone; to be decided by something or someone

Examples: “The picnic will depend on the weather.”
“When are you going to get here?” “I’m not sure. It depends on traffic.”
“I want to see you all tomorrow, but it depends on how I feel.”

Common sentence: “It depends on you.” (The outcome or decision will be determined or decided by your choice/situation/health/actions/etc.)

If something depends on something else, it needs that thing to act in a certain way for the desired outcome. For example: “My future depends on the bank’s decision.” This means you are waiting for an important decision from the bank that will determine something important about your future.

A common reduced sentence that is related to this phrasal verb is “That depends” or “It depends.” Here is an example of its usage: “Are you coming to the party?” “That depends.” “On what?” “Will Mario be there?” This means your decision will be influenced by Mario’s attendance at the party.

Another common but dramatic sentence is “My/Your/His/Her life depends on it.” This means one’s life or death is determined or decided by something or someone. As mentioned, you can use this to add some drama to your words.

Practice: Finish the idea: “The result depends on...”

2.to need something or someone; to be reliant or dependent on something or someone
Same, see also: “count on,” “rely on”

Examples: “She depends on her parents for money.”
“They depend on government assistance to survive.”
“I depend on my car to get to work every morning.”

Common sentence: “I don’t want to depend on anyone.” (I want to be self-sufficient and independent.)

Newborn babies depend on their parents; they need them to survive. This is one example of this usage. If the person or thing you depend on isn’t present or is compromised, your life could suddenly be different. “Don’t depend on anyone” is a popular saying which means “Be independent.” This can also be used to mean “Don’t trust anyone.” (See usage 3.)

Practice: Who did you depend on for money when you were a kid (a child)?

3.to trust someone or something; to be sure or confident about someone or something
Same, see also: “count on,” “rely on”

Examples: “You can’t depend on anyone these days.”
“I need someone to mail this letter.” “I’ll do it. You can depend on me.”
“You can’t depend on giant corporations to do the right thing.”

Common sentence: “You can depend on him/her/me.” (You can feel confident about him/her/me. You can trust him/her/me to act in the way you hope or expect.)

This meaning is more about trust and having confidence in something or someone. “You can depend on me” means “You can trust me.” In the same way, “You can depend on it” means “You can have confidence in it” or “You can trust it to act in a predictable way.”

Related phrases: “believe in,” “have faith in”

Practice: Which websites or apps do you depend on to give you accurate information?

dwell on [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to think and/or talk about something for a long time (in a way that is often unhealthy for someone); to focus on a particular point for a long time

Examples: “I think you’re dwelling on the wrong part of what I’m saying.”
“You shouldn’t dwell on the past. You need to accept it and move forward.”
“We’ve dwelled on this question for long enough. Let’s move on.”

Common sentence: “Don’t dwell on it.” (Don’t think about it too much or for too long. Don’t devote too much mental energy to it.)

“Don’t dwell on the past” means “Don’t think too much about the past because it is unhealthy to do so.” When we dwell on things, we focus on them for an extended period of time. “To dwell” means to live or stay in a place. “A dwelling” is a home. In the same way, if we dwell on something, we let it get comfortable living in our minds. You can dwell on someone as well (“Stop dwelling on him. He’s not worth it”), but this phrasal verb is more commonly used to talk about things. While there are positive uses of this phrasal verb, it is usually used negatively or as a warning to someone.

Collocations: “dwell on a subject,” “dwell on a question,” “dwell on an idea,” “dwell on one’s mistakes,” “dwell on the past”

Related phrases: “keep thinking about,” “linger on,” “mull over”

Practice: Why isn’t it healthy to dwell on your mistakes? Do you ever do this?

embark on [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to begin a course of action or a journey, usually one that will be long, demanding, and/or significant

Examples: “Zita has decided to embark on a new career.”
“We’re embarking on a Caribbean vacation next month.”
“The company is embarking on a massive project starting next week.”

Common sentence: “We’re embarking on an adventure.” (We’re starting an exciting time/project/journey/etc.)

The word “embark” means to board a boat, a plane, or other vehicle. Consequently, the idea of beginning a long journey is implied in the phrasal verb “embark on.” With this in mind, it is a slightly formal phrase that can be used to give more importance or drama to your words. For example: “We are going to embark on a long journey. Who’s with me?” The long journey might be a work project or an actual physical journey. It can also be used more neutrally, as in “I’m embarking on a new career” or to discuss your travel plans, as in “We’re embarking on our trip to Brazil tomorrow.”

Collocations: “embark on a journey,” “embark on an adventure,” “embark on a quest,” “embark on a career,” “embark on a trip,” “embark on a vacation”

Practice: How did you decide to embark on your current path (career, education, life decisions, etc.)?

fall by the wayside

idiomatic phrase

1.to be put into a position of disuse and neglect; to be ignored or pushed away, either consciously or as a result of circumstances; to fail to continue existing as before, or to fail to continue doing something; to drop

Examples: “After I lost my job, my motivation fell by the wayside.”
“You can have your phone back if you promise not to let your schoolwork fall by the wayside.”
“Ten players started the game, but by the end, two fell by the wayside.”

Common sentence: “It fell by the wayside.” (It stopped existing in the same way. It became neglected and ignored.)

“The wayside” refers to the edge of a road. If something falls by the wayside, it is not on the main part of the road anymore. If your schoolwork falls by the wayside, it is pushed away or neglected. You can consciously allow something to fall by the wayside, or it can be the result of outside factors. For example: “After I got sick, my schoolwork fell by the wayside.” This means I became sick and I had to ignore my schoolwork as a result.

A routine or habit can also fall by the wayside. For instance: “She normally eats healthy, but after taking that stressful job, her diet fell by the wayside”; “After we had our first child, going to the gym fell by the wayside.”

Related phrases: “go down the tube,” “grind to a halt”

Practice: Has your motivation ever fallen by the wayside? If yes, what happened? How did this affect your life and the people around you?

fall for [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to be tricked, deceived, and/or fooled by something; to believe an illusion, lie, or trick; to be deceived by something

Examples: “Don’t fall for his promises. He can’t be trusted.”
“Have you ever fallen for an email scam?”
“She’s a really good magician! I fell for all of her illusions.”

Common sentence: “Don’t fall for it.” (Don’t believe the lie. Don’t be tricked or fooled by this.)

“I can’t believe you fell for that!” This means “I can’t believe you believed that!” If you fall for something, you believe a lie or deception. Two common sentences with this phrase are “Don’t fall for it” (Don’t allow yourself to be tricked by it), and “I fell for it” (I believed the lie/trick/deception).

Collocations: “fall for a scam,” “fall for a lie,” “fall for a trick,” “fall for a promise,” “fall for [someone’s] promises,” “fall for an illusion”

Practice: Have you ever fallen for an email or telephone scam?

2.fall for [someone]. (transitive, inseparable) to be infatuated with and/or to have romantic feelings for someone; to begin having feelings of romantic love for someone
Common variation: “fall hard for [someone]”

Examples: “When I met my husband, I fell for him almost instantly.”
“She’s really sweet.” “Wait. Are you falling for her?” (Are you starting to have romantic feelings for her? Are you falling in love with her?)
“I fell hard for her after our first date.”

Common sentence: “Are you falling for her/him/them?” (Are you falling in love with him/her/them? Are you starting to have feelings of romantic love?)

This phrase is closely linked to the phrase “fall in love.” This means you start having deep romantic feelings for someone. It is often used in the simple past (“I fell for her”) and the present continuous (“I’m falling for you”).

Related phrases: “be infatuated with,” “fall head over heels [for someone]”

Practice: Have you ever fallen for someone the first time you saw them?

fall short [of something]

phrasal verb (transitive or intransitive, inseparable)

1.not to meet expectations; to get close to a desired point, but fail near the end; not to reach a desired point/goal/etc.
Common extension: “just fall short [of something]”

Examples: “The election results are official. The ruling party fell short of a majority.”
“I was expecting a lot more from that film. It fell short of my expectations.”
“He won the race and just fell short of the world record!”

Common sentence: “He’s/She’s/They’re going to fall short.” (They are not going to succeed. They will get close to success, but won’t achieve it.)

If someone falls short of your expectations, they do not satisfy or meet them. Maybe they get close to what you expected, but they still fail in the end. If you try to jump from one rock to another, but you do not reach the other rock, someone could say “He/She fell short of the rock/the goal.”

Collocations: “fall short of a goal,” “fall short of expectations,” “fall short of success,” “fall short of victory,” “fall short of winning”

Related phrases: “fail to live up to expectations,” “not be up to snuff”

Practice: What is something that fell short of your expectations? (Also possible: something that didn’t live up to your expectations.)

fiddle with [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to handle something with one’s hands or fingers in an anxious or reflexive way

Examples: “Stop fiddling with your keys. It’s annoying.”
“When he’s nervous before a test, he fiddles with his eraser.”
“She always fiddles with her hair.”

Common sentence: “Stop fiddling with that.” (Stop your anxious or reflexive behaviour. Stop playing with that thing.)

If you are waiting for a test to start, you might play with your pen or pencil in your hands. In this case, you are fiddling with your pen or pencil. We might fiddle with things when we feel anxious or bored, or because it is just one of our habits. For example: “Deborah always fiddles with her necklace.” This means she plays with it in some way, maybe while she is reading her emails or waiting for something.

Practice: Did you fiddle with pens or pencils when you were in school?

2.to try to fix something by experimenting with different strategies (pushing different buttons, etc.) but actually not being sure how to fix it
Common extension: “fiddle around with”

Examples: “Don’t fiddle with the engine! It’s better to take your car to a mechanic.”
“I fiddled with the oven for 30 minutes, but I couldn’t find the problem.”
“Hey, could you help me? I was fiddling with the settings, and I think I accidentally changed the language on my phone to Korean.”

Common sentence: “I tried fiddling with it.” (I tried to fix it, but I wasn’t successful. I think I need someone else to help me with this issue.)

“How did you fix your laptop screen?” “I just fiddled with the settings.” In this case, the person fiddled (or played) with the settings to fix their laptop screen. They discovered the solution by exploring and trying different options, settings, etc. This phrase is usually used with machines, but it can be used to talk about digital settings too, as in the example above.

Related phrases: “fiddle with a knob,” “fiddle with a lever,” “fiddle with a switch,” “fiddle with the settings”

Practice: Have you ever had to fiddle with the settings on your computer or phone because something was wrong?

3.to change something in a way that is harmful, foolish, or annoying
Common extension: “fiddle around with”

Examples: “Someone has been fiddling with the TV settings. The colours look strange.”
“Who has been fiddling with the temperature in my office?”
“Someone fiddled with this machine because they wanted to sabotage it.”

Common sentence: “Someone has been fiddling with this.” (Someone has been playing with this. It’s possible they wanted to sabotage/break it.)

“I don’t know why the writers fiddled with the series so much.” This means the writers of a TV series made some changes that were harmful, foolish, or annoying for viewers. The idea of using one’s hands and fingers to “play” with something is also present in this usage of “fiddle with.”

Related phrases: “fiddle with a knob,” “fiddle with a lever,” “fiddle with a switch,” “fiddle with the settings”

Practice: Have you ever caught someone fiddling with something because they were trying to sabotage it?

fill in [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, separable)

1.to complete a form, application, or other document that requires information

Examples: “Please fill in this form and give it back to me when you’re done.”
“You have to fill in a lot of boxes to sign up for this website.”
“Are you sure you filled it in correctly?”

Common sentence: “You forgot to fill something in.” (You forgot to include a particular piece of information on this form/document/website/etc.)

You can fill in a job application, a school admissions form, or a government document. This usage has also transferred to digital documents. For example: “You forgot to fill in your home address.” This means you did not complete the box on the webpage that asks for your home address.

Grammar note: This usage is often interchangeable with “fill out” in American English, while “fill in” is preferred in many current and former British regions.

Collocations: “fill in an application,” “fill in a form,” “fill in a box,” “fill in a page,” “fill in a sign-up page,” “fill in an order form”

Practice: When was the last time you had to fill in a form (paper or online)?

2.to fill a shape or space until it is full (common context: colouring)

Examples: “I would like you to draw a circle and fill it in with a colour of your choice.”
“Don’t forget to fill in the background. I don’t want to see any white space.”
“You should fill in that hole in your garden with some fresh soil.”

Common sentence: “Make sure to fill it in.” (Fill it completely.)

If you colour a picture, you probably try to fill in the spaces between the lines until those spaces are solid colours with no visible white spaces. You can also fill in an empty space, such as a hole in the ground with cement, sand, etc.

Collocations: “fill in a hole,” “fill in a shape,” “fill in a space”

Practice: Finish the idea: “Make sure you fill in...”

3.fill [someone] in [on something]. (transitive, separable) to make someone aware of a situation; to update someone on the most recent news about a relevant situation/project/news story/etc.

Examples: “You’re late, Dennis. Who wants to fill him in on what has happened?”
“Why does everyone look so sad? Can someone fill me in?”
“That’s good to know. Thanks for filling me in.”

Common sentence: “I’ll fill you in.” (I’ll give you the information you need to know, so you know as much as everyone else.)

If you need to be filled in on something, you don’t have the latest information. You need an update from someone, so you know what everyone else knows. For example: “What’s the latest news on the company’s decision?” “Sit down. I’ll fill you in.” This means the person will “fill” the information gaps you have about the company’s decision, so you know what that person already knows, and what maybe others know as well.

Grammar note: “Fill [someone] in” can stand alone. However, if you would like to specify the information the person needs, use “fill [someone] in on,” as in “Could you fill me in on the details?”

Related phrase: “bring [someone] up to speed”

Practice: Finish the idea: “I need to bring you up to speed on...”

fit in

phrasal verb (intransitive)

1.to be accepted as part of a group

Examples: “Jack’s trying really hard to fit in at his new school.”
“It took 10 years for us to feel like we fit in with this community.”
“You dance ballet? You’ll fit in here just fine. I dance ballet, too.”

Common sentence: “Is he/she having trouble fitting in?” (Is he/she having a difficult time being accepted by the group?)

Someone can fit in with a social group, either large or small. You can fit in at a new school or with a large community. Someone might also not fit in. For example: “She doesn’t fit in because of her strange beliefs.”

Related phrase: “be a bad fit,” “be a good fit”

Practice: Was it easy for you to fit in with the other kids at school?

2.fit in [someone or something]. (transitive, separable) to have space in one’s schedule and/or life for someone or something

Examples: “I’d like to see you next week. Can you fit me into your schedule?”
“Mr. Burns is very busy, but I think he can fit you in next week.”
“He fits in three trips to the gym every week.”

Common sentence: “I can fit you in next week.” (I have time on my calendar for you next week. This might be said in a professional situation.)

If you’re a busy person, your schedule is probably full a lot of the time. With that in mind, you have to find specific periods of time to “fit” people and things into your life. Here are two examples: “I can fit you into my schedule on Tuesday afternoon”; “Find time to fit in what you love into each day.”

Collocations: “fit [someone] into one’s schedule,” “fit [someone] into one’s calendar,” “fit [someone] in tomorrow/on Monday/next week/etc.”

Related phrase: “squeeze [someone] in”

Practice: Could you squeeze in a trip to the grocery store today? (Do you have time for this today?)

3.fit in [someone]; fit in(to) [something]. (transitive or intransitive, separable) to have physical or figurative space for someone or something

Examples: “She ended the relationship because he didn’t fit into her life anymore.”
“How many people can fit into your car?”
“You can fit a lot of things into that bag. It’s huge!”

Common sentence: “It won’t fit in.” (It won’t fit. There isn’t enough space.)

Fitting in is all about space. In this case, we are talking about physically or figuratively fitting in. How much stuff can you fit into your bag? How many people can you fit into your life? These are the ideas behind this meaning. This phrasal verb is often used as “fit into.”

Practice: How many people can fit into your room/car/house/etc.?

follow up [with someone]

phrasal verb (transitive or intransitive, inseparable)

1.to contact someone a subsequent time because you want to give them more information, or because you need more information from them

Examples: “The doctor said he would follow up with you next week.”
“Dear Jack, I’m following up to ask if you are still interested in our products.”
“The client hasn’t responded to our last email. Do you want me to follow up with him again?”

Common sentence: “Did you follow up?” (Did you contact them again?)

“Let’s follow up with each other next week.” This means we will speak again next week to give each other new information. When you follow up with someone, you contact them about a topic you have previously discussed.

In the sales industry, it is common for businesses to follow up with a potential customer if the customer showed some interest in a product but hasn’t contacted the business in a few days to confirm their interest in actually purchasing the product. For example: “Do you remember Luke from last week? He seemed pretty interested in a new car, but he hasn’t returned our last call. Could you follow up with him today?” This means “Could you contact him today and ask if he’s still interested in buying a car from us?”

Grammar note: You can turn “follow up” into an adjective by hyphenating it, as in “Let’s schedule a follow-up appointment next month.”

Collocations: “follow up immediately,” “follow up today/tomorrow/etc.”

Related phrases: “check up on [something or someone],” “get back to [someone]”

Practice: If someone doesn’t respond to a message from you, how long do you usually wait before you follow up?

2.follow up [on something]. (transitive or intransitive, inseparable) to get the latest information on something by contacting a relevant person; to contact someone again to discuss a topic you have previously discussed

Examples: “Did you follow up with the accounting department about the sales report?”
“I’m going to follow up on the appointment.”
“Make sure she follows up with you on the marketing project this week.”

Common sentence: “Did you follow up on it?” (Did you contact someone again or organize another meeting to take the next step in the process?)

You can follow up with someone or on something. This is similar to usage 1, but it focuses on the topic previously discussed instead of the person. For example: “We need to follow up on last week’s meeting.” This means we need to continue the discussion we started last week. Note examples 1 and 3, which use “follow up with” and “follow up on” in the same sentence.

Do you currently have to follow up on anything with someone?

3.follow up [something] [with something]. (transitive, separable) to follow something with another action; to do something in addition to something else, usually to achieve a desired result or to make a desired result more probable

Examples: “I like to follow up dinner with something sweet.”
“It’s common to follow up a job interview with a thank you email.”
“If you don’t follow up strenuous exercise with rest, you’re doing it wrong.”

Common sentence: “What did you follow it up with?” (What did you do or say after your first action or words?)

If you enjoy having a cup of tea after your lunch, you can say “I usually follow up my lunch with a cup of tea.” This means I eat my lunch first, then I have my tea. This usage means that one action is followed by another action.

You can also follow up something you said. For example: “She said he was lazy, then followed up by calling him selfish.” In this case, first she called him lazy, and then selfish. This usage is often followed by “with” or “by.”

Collocations: “follow up an email,” “follow up an interview”

Practice: Should you always follow up an interview with a thank you email?

get back to [someone]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to reply to someone; to respond to a person’s message (text, email, letter, phone call, voicemail, etc.)
Common extensions: “get back to [someone] about/with/on [something]”

Examples: “I’ll get back to you.” (I will respond/reply to you later.)
“Could you please get back to me when you have a chance?”
“Thank you for getting back to me so quickly.”

Common sentence: “Can I get back to you (on that)?” (I need more time to respond. May I respond later?)

If a person gets back to you, they respond to you. We get back to someone when they are waiting for us to answer a question, make a decision, or because they would like to have a conversation with us. Sometimes, we do not get back to people immediately because we are busy or we need time to assess a situation, make a decision, or to formulate a proper response.

Usage note: This phrase is often used in emails and conversational exchanges in the workplace. The 3 examples above are very common in this context. Here are 3 more common examples: “I apologize for not getting back to you sooner”; “Has he/she gotten back to you yet?”; “Please get back to me as soon as possible.” (Or, “Please get back to me at your earliest convenience” if you would like to be more polite.)

Collocations: “get back to [someone] about this/that,” “get back to [someone] on this/that,” “get back to [someone] with a response,” “get back to [someone] with an answer,” “get back to [someone] with more details,” “get back to [someone] with more information”

Related phrases: “return [someone’s] call/text/etc.,” “write back to [someone]”

Practice: Are you currently waiting for someone to get back to you about something?

get by

phrasal verb (intransitive, inseparable)

1.to do enough or to have enough to survive; to survive

Examples: “I don’t know how I got by in high school. I was such a bad student.”
“He’s a single father and he’s barely getting by.” (He’s always close to not having enough money to continue living with his child/children.)
“How are you getting by these days?” (How are you living and surviving? Similar to “How are you doing these days?” but only for familiar people.)

Common sentence: “I’m just getting by.” (I’m doing just enough to survive. This could refer to one’s job, a school program, etc., or life in general.)

This phrase often refers to a person’s financial situation (“I make enough money to get by”), but it is used in other contexts as well. For example: “She got by with a really old computer and managed to finish her degree.” In both cases, we are concerned with doing or having enough to keep moving forward in life or in a particular area of one’s life. “Most people are just trying to get by” means “Most people are just trying to survive—to do enough to continue living.”

Practice: How much money do you need to get by each week?

2.to do the minimum that is required to survive or succeed

Examples: “He doesn’t mind just getting by.” (He’s okay only doing the minimum.)
“You can do more than just get by.” (I know you are capable of doing more.)
“They seem happy just getting by.”

Common sentence: “Are you happy/satisfied just getting by?” (Are you okay just doing the minimum?)

This is similar to the first usage, but it focuses more on the effort a person uses in his/her life. If you say “He does just enough to get by,” you mean he does not try very hard and only does the minimum he needs to do at his job, school, etc. The phrases “do just enough” and “just get by” are common in this usage. In the case of “just get by,” it is often preceded by an adjective such as happy, satisfied, content, or okay. See the examples above.

Collocations: “content to (just) get by,” “happy to (just) get by”

Practice: Are you satisfied with just getting by?

get something [from someone or something]

1.to obtain or receive something from someone or something

Examples: “I got a present from my aunt.”
“Where did you get that from?”
“The dress I got from him was very elegant.”

Common sentence: “I got it from _____.” (This is who or where I received it from.)

If someone gives you something, you “get it” from them. This simply means you receive it from them. You can also find (or obtain) something, and someone might ask you “Where did you get that?” or “Where did you get that from?” You can also get something from a place, typically a store.

You can also “get” a virus or physical illness from someone. For example: “She got hepatitis from her boyfriend.”

Practice: What was the last gift you got from someone?

2.get something [from something]. phrasal verb (transitive, often separated) to receive a particular benefit from someone or something
Common variation: “get [something] out of [something]”

Examples: “What do you get from reading those books?” “Escapism.”
“He gets a feeling of satisfaction from helping others.”
“She got a strong sense of accomplishment from her job.”

Common sentence: “What do you get from it?” (What benefits do you receive from that situation, relationship, or activity?)

In this usage, you can receive something physical or something more abstract. Things can make us feel comfortable, secure, or warm. Note the examples for the types of benefits one can receive.

Grammar note: Make sure to follow “from” with a gerund if you wish to follow it with an activity, as in “I get happiness from helping others.” Another common question that uses the common variation listed above is “What do you get out of it?”

Practice: Think about your hobbies. What do you get from them?

get it on

idiomatic phrase

1.to begin doing something, often with enthusiasm (often said as “Let’s get it on!” to encourage yourself and others around you)

Examples: “Okay, it looks like everyone is ready. Let’s get it on!”
“I wish the game was today and not tomorrow. I’m ready to get it on now!”
“I’ve been waiting to do this presentation for over a week. I’m ready to just get it on.”

Common sentence: “Let’s get it on!” (Let’s start! Let’s go!)

The encouraging phrase “Let’s get it on!” is similar to “Let’s go!” It invites others to get excited and ready for an activity. A common context for this usage is at the start of a mixed martial arts fight. The referee sometimes says “Let’s get it on!” to start the match. Another similar sentence to encourage others to begin an activity is “Let’s get this thing going/started!”

Related sentence: “Let’s do this thing.”

Practice: You want to motivate your team before a big game. What do you say?

2.(slang) to have sexual intercourse

Examples: “Have you gotten it on yet?” (Have you and your partner had sex yet?)
“You didn’t know? They’ve been getting it on since January.”
“Are you ready to get it on?”

Common sentence: “Let’s get it on.” (An invitation to a romantic partner to have sexual intercourse. Also the name of a popular song with the same meaning.)

This is a colloquial way to refer to sexual intercourse. It should only be used with people you are familiar with. Never use this in a formal setting.

Practice: Are you familiar with the song “Let’s get it on” by Marvin Gaye?

get on with [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to continue doing an activity; to reconvene an activity

Examples: “Sorry for bothering you. I’ll let you get on with your work.”
“Could we get on with the meeting? I need to be home in an hour.”
“You have spent too much time thinking about your mistakes. It’s time for you to get on with your life.” (Also possible: “move on with your life”)

Common sentence: “Get on with it.” (Say or do what you want to say or do. I am impatient and just want this to be finished.)

If someone tells you to “get on with it” in an annoyed way, they want you to continue to the next part of your speech, presentation, point, discussion, etc. People who are bored, impatient, or who think that something (a performance, a presentation, etc.) is moving too slowly might use this imperative. It can also be used if you feel someone or something is taking too long. For example: “We have been waiting to start this meeting for fifteen minutes. Can we just get on with it?” However, it can also be used in a more neutral way, as in example 1 above.

Collocations: “get on with it,” “get on with the show,” “get on with the proceedings,” “get on with one’s life,” “get on with one’s work”

Related phrases: “get a move on,” “get down to business”

Finish the idea: “This is taking too long. Can we...?”

2.get on [with someone]. (transitive or intransitive, inseparable) to have a good relationship with someone; to be on friendly terms with someone (more common in past and current British regions)

Examples: “My cousin and I don’t get on well.”
“Do you get on with your siblings?”
“We haven’t gotten on since our big fight five years ago.”

Common sentence: “We (don’t) get on.” (We have/don’t have a positive relationship; we are/aren’t friendly with each other.)

In American English, this is equivalent to “get along with someone.” In short, it means that you and someone are able to coexist—you are friendly with each other and don’t have any personal problems with each other.

Related phrase: “be compatible with [someone],” “be on friendly terms with [someone],” “get along [with someone]”

Practice: Do you get on well with everyone in your family?

get with [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to be engaged with something; to become alert

Examples: “He still uses a portable CD player. He needs to get with the times.”
“I don’t know what’s wrong with you, but you have to get with it.”
“Get with the program, Brad! We already discussed that topic!”

Common sentence: “Get with it.” (Become engaged with the present moment. Focus on what is being discussed, done, etc. Join us here!)

This phrase has a couple of common uses: “Get with it” and “Get with the times.” “Get with it” means that your mind is not properly engaged with what we are doing or discussing. For example, if someone is playing basketball, but they seem distracted and are playing poorly, one of their teammates might say “Get with it!” to mean “Focus on what you’re doing! Join us here in the present! Engage with what is actually happening directly in front of you!” The sentence “Get with the program” has a similar meaning in this case. It says “Pay attention to what everyone else is doing. You need to do the same thing. Wake up!”

“Get with the times” means “You need to change your old thoughts and ways so you can understand and fit with contemporary culture.” For instance, if someone is wearing a pair of pants that stopped being fashionable 30 years ago, they might be told to “get with the times.”

Grammar note: You can separate this phrasal verb by putting an object after “get.” For instance: “You need to get your grandfather with the times.” This usage is not as common as the others listed above.

Collocations: “get with it,” “get with the program,” “get with the times”

Practice: Are you someone who keeps up with technology, or do you need to get with the times in some way?

give up [something]

phrasal verb (transitive or intransitive, separable)

1.to quit doing or trying to do something; to quit a (usually harmful) habit

Examples: “She gave up smoking. She was spending too much money on cigarettes.”
“Don’t give up. You can do it!”
“I’ve been trying to fix this for 2 hours, but nothing is working. I give up.”

Common sentence: “I give up.” (I quit. I’m not going to try anymore.)

If you give up playing piano, you stop trying to learn it. Perhaps you lost interest, didn’t have the time, or didn’t have the money to keep practicing. In all these cases, you quit the activity. You can “give up” something voluntarily or because of outside factors. Finally, the phrase “give up” is often associated with quitting what many people consider to be bad habits or addictions, such as drinking alcohol, smoking, or doing hard drugs.

Usage note: You can also “give up on” something or someone. This means you stop investing your energy and hope in something or someone. For instance, “Please don’t give up on me” means “Please continue to believe in me and trust that I will not disappoint you.” You can also give up on things such as the government, a belief system, a company, a relationship, etc. For example, if you say “They gave up on their friendship,” this means they stopped believing in their friendship and stopped trying to make it work.

Collocations: “give up believing in [something or someone],” “give up drinking (alcohol),” “give up smoking,” “give up trying”

Practice: When is it a good idea to give up trying to do something?

2.give up [something or someone]. (transitive, separable) to freely give something or someone to someone or to an organization/legal body/company/etc., often because you are no longer able to keep it (due to circumstances that can be in or out of your control)

Examples: “They had to give up their house because they couldn’t pay their mortgage.”
“No parent ever wants to give up their children.”
“We gave up the kittens for adoption because we didn’t have space for them in our house.”

Common sentence: “They gave him/her/it up for adoption.” (They gave their child or pet to an adoption agency.)

Monks have to give up their possessions when they enter a monastery. If you don’t have the money to pay for your home, you might have to give your home up to the bank. A person can give up their rights to something (”Never give up your right to free speech”). In all of these cases, someone is forced to give something without a choice. However, you can give up something voluntarily. For instance, a soldier can choose to give up their life for a noble cause.

Collocations: “give a child up for adoption,” “give a pet up for adoption,” “give up one’s home,” “give up one’s life,” “give up one’s possessions,” “give up one’s right to [something]”

Practice: Would you give up all of your possessions for love?

go by [something or someone]

1.to pass something or someone

Examples: “We went by your house, but your car wasn’t in the driveway.”
“Time goes by too quickly.”
“It looks like she’s in a hurry. She just went by five people in three seconds.”

Common sentence: “We went by __________.” (We passed that location.)

This phrase means to move or pass by someone or something. If you take the bus in the city, the bus will go by many cars, buildings, etc. Abstract ideas such as time can also go by.

Usage note: The phrase “pass by” is used more often to mean “We stopped at that location then continued our journey” than “go by.” For example: “Could we pass by the bank before we go to the store?” This means “Could we make a quick stop at the bank before we go to the store?” You can also use the phrase “stop by” in this case. The phrase “go by” typically means to continue moving past a person or place without stopping, though it can be clarified in a situation such as this one: “We went by your place to see if you were home, but the lights were off, so we kept driving.”

Related phrases: “drop by,” “pass by,” “pop by,” “stop by”

Practice: Finish the idea: “Turn left and go by...”
