2.phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable) to base your actions, beliefs, decisions, thoughts, words, etc. on something or someone; to follow specific guidelines, rules, policies, suggestions, instructions, words, etc.

Examples: “I’m just going by what you said.”
“He’s not going by the rules. He’s going by what he thinks is right.”
“If we don’t want to get in trouble, we need to go by the company policy.”

Common sentence: “We need to go by the rules.” (We need to follow the rules.)

If your company has policies that you must follow, you must go by those policies—you must base your actions and decisions on them because if you don’t go by the rules, you might lose your job. You can also go by a person’s words, which means you accept their words and base your decisions on them. “Hey, don’t be mad. I was just going by your advice,” for example. This means you acted by following your friend’s or colleague’s advice.

Collocations: “go by the rules,” “go by what one knows,” “go by what one heard,” “go by one’s instincts,” “go by what [someone] said”

Practice: Finish the idea: “We should go by...”

3.go by [one’s name]. phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable) the name a person prefers to be called or is called in a particular situation

Examples: “My full name is Aleksander, but I go by Alex. It’s faster and easier.”
“Zack goes by at least three different names online.”
“She doesn’t go by her married name anymore.”

Common sentence: “He/She goes by __________.” (He/She is called this name in public situations. He/She prefers to use this name.)

What name do you go by? What you go by is what you want people to call you. For example: “Her full name is Lucinda, but she goes by Lu.” In most cases, we use this phrase to talk about people’s names, but it can be used to talk about the names of places as well. For example: “Didn’t that company go by a different name before?” However, this usage is inconsistent, so it’s usually safer to just say “Didn’t that company have a different name before?”

If you have different online identities, you can use this phrase as well. For example: “She goes by SuperGirl47 on social media.” A person can go by many names in many different places.

Practice: What name do you normally go by?

go on [doing something]

phrasal verb (intransitive, inseparable)

1.to continue or proceed, as if on a journey or part of a process
Similar, see also: “keep on”

Examples: “The teacher told them to stop, but they went on talking.”
“The presentation was too long. It went on for three hours.”
“It doesn’t matter how you feel. Life will go on without you.”

Common sentence: “It went on for __________.” (It continued for this many seconds, minutes, hours, days, weeks, months, years, etc.)

If someone simply says “Go on,” they mean “Continue.” Perhaps they want to hear what you have to say, or they want you to continue doing an activity. “How long did it go on for?” means “How long did it continue or proceed for?” Another common question with this phrase is “How long has this/it been going on for?” This means “How long has this been happening or continuing for?” You can also go on doing an activity. For instance: “He went on driving until he reached his destination.”

If someone “goes on talking,” the meaning can be neutral, but it can also mean they talk excessively. In this case, you could say “He went on and on.” This means he continued talking for a long time in a way that was perhaps annoying or frustrating for his listeners. The emphatic phrase “go on and on” can refer to continuing an activity for an extended period of time.

Collocations: “go on and on,” “go on for (a duration of time),” “go on talking,” “go on trying,” “go on until (a particular time),” “life goes on”

Practice: Finish the idea: “The meeting went on for...”

2.go on to [something] / go on to do or be [something]. to do or become something in the future; to do something after doing something else

Examples: “He finished university and went on to open his own medical practice.”
“After this activity is finished, we will go on to discuss your homework.”
“Stay on this path, and you will go on to do great things.”

Common sentence: “He/She/They went on to __________.” (This is what they did or became in the future.)

If you finish one task and continue to another task, you “go on to the next task.” For example: “After I had my lunch, I went on to my work”; “I went on to finish my work.” This is the immediate future usage of this phrase. It can have a longer time meaning. For example: “Do you remember Jenna from elementary school? Did you know she went on to become an opera singer?” This means I learned that Jenna became an opera singer when she grew older. You can set goals for yourself with this phrase by saying something such as: “I want to go on to become a writer.” This is what I want to do in the future.

Collocations: “go on to be someone,” “go on to become someone,” “go on to the next point,” “go on to the next task”

Related phrase: “move on [to something]”

Practice: When you were a child, what did you want to go on to become?

have to do with [something or someone]

idiomatic phrase

1.to be related to something or someone, or be about something or someone, especially when one does not know the exact details, or does not want to give the exact details immediately

Examples: “What was the meeting about?” “It had to do with budget cuts.”
“What does this have to do with me?” (How does this relate to me?)
“I haven’t read the whole thing, but the article has to do with agriculture.”

Common sentence: “What does that have to do with it?” (What is the relationship between what you said and what we are discussing?)

“What does this have to do with me?” You might ask this if you want to know how a discussion relates to you or how it concerns you. The phrase “have to do with” cannot be taken apart and understood by its individual words. You can try to replace it in your mind with words and phrases like “about,” “related to,” or “concerned with.” For example: “The movie has to do with life in Tibet.” This means the movie is about life in Tibet in some way.

We often use this phrase when we do not have the exact details of something. For example: “I haven’t read the book, but I know it has to do with stoic philosophy.” Another adjacent example of this usage might be: “I don’t know what they’re talking about, but I think it has to do with you.”

Here is a more advanced usage: “Our decision has to do with the fact that we need to spend less money.” This means our decision is related to the fact that we need to spend less money. This can be one of many factors in our decision, or the only factor.

Grammar note: In affirmative sentences, this phrase is often used with the third person subject “it.” For instance: “It has to do with money.” The determiners “This” and “That” are also commonly used. “This has to do with your brother,” for instance, meaning “This is about your brother.”

Collocations: “have to do with [someone],” “have to do with [something],” “have to do with the fact that __________,”

Practice: What does our environment have to do with our health?

hold on [to something or someone]

phrasal verb (intransitive, transitive with “to” added)

1.to hold something or someone; to keep something secure; to keep one’s hand on or around something for safekeeping in order to prevent it from falling, or to support oneself

Examples: “Could you hold on to my phone for a minute?”
“Hold on tight! It’s going to be a bumpy ride!”
“I thought he would let go of the ball after he was tackled, but he held on.”

Common sentence: “Hold on to this.” (Hold this and do not release it.)

In many situations, this is mostly a different way to say the word “hold.” You can hold on to a baseball bat, a person’s hand, or a ball. If you are on a boat with your family and the waves are strong, you might hear someone say “Hold on to your mother.” In a slightly different case, if you are entrusted with keeping someone’s laptop safe and secure while they are on vacation, you are also “holding on” to that person’s laptop until they return.

Collocations: “hold on to [someone’s] hand,” “hold on to [someone’s] things,” “hold on to [something],” “hold on to [someone]”

Practice: Are you able to stand up from a chair without any assistance, or do you have to hold on to something?

2.hold on. (intransitive) to wait or stop (often used as an imperative)

Examples: “Hold on for five minutes, please.”
“Hold on. What are you saying?”
“Just hold on a little longer. We are almost at our destination.”

Common sentence: “Hold on!” (Wait!)

You can tell someone to hold on when you want them to wait somewhere. You can also tell someone to hold on if they are speaking too quickly and you want to clarify what they just said. For example: “Hold on. Do you mean that we only have sixty minutes to clean the house before our guests arrive?”

Collocations: “hold on a minute,” “hold on a sec(ond)”

Practice: Finish the idea: “Hold on. What did you mean when you said...”

3.hold on [for something or someone]. (intransitive) to persevere and manage to be successful despite great difficulties, problems, odds, challenges, etc.

Examples: “It was a close game, but they held on for the win.”
“If you hold on long enough, I’m sure you’ll get that promotion eventually.”
“They held on for their fans, who believed in them all season!”

Common sentence: “Hold on a little longer.” (Keep trying and persevering for a little longer. Don’t quit. It’s almost finished.)

Holding on to something requires energy and effort. In the same way, holding on to achieve a desired result also requires energy and effort. In this case, it means to persevere and to keep going despite difficulties. Ultimately, you are successful! For example: “I’m holding on as long as I can.” This means I’m trying to maintain the condition I need in order to be successful, but it requires continued energy and effort. This usage is often used in sports and athletic competitions, as in example 1 above.

If you are inspired by someone to keep persevering, you can hold on for them. You are holding on for their benefit in this case. See example 3 for this usage.

Collocations: “hold on for the win,” “hold on until the end,” “hold on in the face of adversity,” “hold on for as long as one can,” “hold on a little longer”

Practice: If you wanted to motivate someone to try and persevere just a little longer, what would you say to them?

hone in

phrasal verb (intransitive, inseparable)

1.to focus completely on something or someone; to give all of your attention to something or someone, usually because you want to achieve a specific goal; to move closer to an objective or target
Common extension: “hone in on [something or someone]”

Examples: “We need to hone in on phase 3 of the project this week.”
“We are honed in on the target.” (We are focused on it and ready for action.)
“This week, I really want you to hone in on what you want to do after you graduate high school.” (I want you to focus your attention on this.)

Common sentence: “I really need to hone in on this.” (I really need to focus on this. This can be said because you want someone to leave you alone so you can focus on your work, or to remind yourself of your goal.)

“Hone” is a slightly formal synonym for “sharpen.” If you hone in on something, you sharpen your focus and give all your attention to that one thing. A teacher might say the following to a student: “Your essay is good, but I want you to hone in on your second point and provide more details.” This phrasal verb is often used in military contexts to talk about honing in on a target. “We are honing in on the enemy base,” for example. This means we are getting closer to our target/focus, which is the enemy base.

Collocations: “hone in on the target,” “hone in on one’s work”

Practice: Are you good at honing in on your work when necessary, or are you easily distracted?

keep an eye on [something or someone]

idiomatic phrase

1.to monitor someone or something; to pay close attention to someone or something
Common variation: “keep a close eye on [something or someone]”

Examples: “My dad always kept a close eye on us because we were troublemakers.”
“I’ve been keeping an eye on this online auction. I want to get this item.”
“Could you keep an eye on this for a minute?”

Common sentence: “I’ll keep an eye on it.” (I’ll pay attention to and monitor this object or situation.)

If someone asks you to keep an eye on your backpack while they are in the bathroom, they are asking you to monitor their backpack—to make sure no one takes it. “Could you keep an eye on this until I return?” is a common request. Another common context is when someone is cooking but they need to leave the room for a period of time. They might ask you to “Keep an eye on the stove” or “Keep an eye on the soup.”

If you are suspicious of someone or something, you might also want to keep an eye on them or it. Finally, you can keep an eye on something because you are monitoring it for any changes, as in “That cut looks bad. You should keep an eye on it and see a doctor if it gets any worse.”

Collocations: “keep an eye on it/this/that,” “keep an eye on me/you/him/her/us/them”

Practice: Are you keeping an eye on anyone or anything these days?

keep in mind

idiomatic phrase

1.to remember or to keep something close to your thoughts, especially something that will be useful in the future; to consider something or someone as a possibility or an option
Common variation: “keep [something or someone] in mind”

Examples: “Thanks for the advice. I’ll keep it in mind.”
“If you buy him new shoes, keep in mind that his favourite colour is red.”
“Her words are worth keeping in mind.” (It’s a good idea to remember her words; they might be useful.)

Common sentence: “I’ll keep it in mind.” (I’ll remember this information when I’m in a situation where the information is useful to me.)

If you keep something in mind, you remember it so you can use it later. This phrase is sometimes used as a warning by the speaker to say “Don’t forget,” or “The next thing I say is very important and relevant to the situation.” For example: “I want to ask the boss for a raise.” “I think that’s a great idea because you deserve a raise, but keep in mind that the boss has already given three people raises this week. It’s probably a better idea to wait.”

This phrase is often used when we are giving advice, a suggestion, or information to someone. For example: “When you go skiing, you have to keep the importance of balance in mind.” In this instance, the speaker is giving you some obvious information: You have to remember the importance of balance if you are going skiing.

This phrase can also be used to consider something or someone as an option. For example: “If you need help with something, keep me in mind”; “You should keep Larisa in mind for the new position.”

Collocations: “keep it/that/this in mind,” “keep me/him/her/etc. in mind”

Practice: What should you keep in mind if you’re planning to go to the beach?

keep on

phrasal verb (intransitive)

1.to continue doing something; to do something repeatedly (in a way that can be neutral, positive, or annoying to others)

Examples: “He kept on interrupting the presentation.”
“You need to keep on going to the gym.”
“Last week, a bunch of telemarketers kept on calling me.”

Common sentence: “Keep on going.” (Don’t stop. This encouraging sentence can refer to physical or figurative distances.)

If you do something repeatedly and/or with regularity, whether in an individual series or in a continuous action/motion, you keep on doing it. For example, you can keep on checking your email throughout the day (an individual series), or you can keep on riding your bike (a continuous action/motion). With this phrasal verb, imagining continuous or regular motion is the key. For instance: “He kept on talking even after I told him that I needed to focus on my work.”

Usage note: If someone “keeps going on” about something, they keep mentioning it and talking about it. For example: “Yunhee keeps going on about that restaurant.” This means she won’t stop talking about that specific restaurant. This can be positive or negative depending on the listener.

Collocations: “keep on complaining,” “keep on going,” “keep on interrupting,” “keep on talking,” “keep on trying”

Practice: How can you keep on improving your English skills? (Ex: “I can keep on improving my English skills by...”)

2.keep [someone] on. (transitive, usually separated) to continue employing someone, usually after their contract has expired, or in uncertain circumstances

Examples: “Daniel was a good student worker. We kept him on for three summers.”
“The company’s firing a lot of people. I’m worried they won’t keep me on.”
“They could only afford to keep six people on during the pandemic.”

Common sentence: “They kept him/her/on for __________.” (They employed them for this duration of time—two months, one year, etc.)

If a company keeps you on, they keep you as part of their team—you continue working for them as an employee. You can be a seasonal worker and say “They just kept me on for the holiday season,” meaning they gave you a job during the holidays because they needed more people for a short period of time. While this phrasal verb can be unseparated (“They only kept on four people in the marketing department”), it is usually separated (“I hope they keep you on after your probation”).

Related phrases: “keep [someone] on the payroll,” “retain [someone’s] services”

Practice: Finish the idea: “They kept him on because...”

lean on

1.to place part of one’s body’s weight on something or someone for support
Common variation: “lean against [something or someone]”

Examples: “Stop leaning on that. It’s fragile.”
“Why do you keep leaning on my shoulder?”
“Don’t lean on the door. I just cleaned it.” (Also: “Don’t lean against…”)

Common sentence: “Don’t lean on that.”

“To lean” means to rest or place part of your body weight on or against something or someone. In the case of “lean on,” you are typically putting your weight on top of something or someone for support, though this preposition is also used in cases where you are leaning vertically against something as well. In everyday speech, “He’s leaning on the wall” can have the same meaning as “He’s leaning against the wall,” for instance.

Usage note: For the verb “lean,” you can also “lean back,” “lean forward,” or “lean to the side.” For example, if you take a relaxed posture while you are sitting in a chair, you are leaning back in your seat. A parent or teacher might say “Don’t lean back in your chair” to a child if the child has pushed their chair back on two legs and might be in danger of falling backwards.

Practice: Do you normally lean back in your chair, or do you sit straight?

2.lean on [someone] [for something]. phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable) to rely on someone to accomplish a task or to provide support; to rely on someone more than other people to do what needs to be done
Common variation: “lean heavily on [someone for something]”

Examples: “He’s been leaning on you a lot for emotional support.”
“The boss leans on you so much because she knows you won’t say no.”
“I’m leaning heavily on him this week because I’m sick.”

Common sentence: “He’s/She’s/They’ve been leaning on you a lot lately.” (They have been relying on you to do a lot lately.)

If you lean on someone, you trust them and rely on them for something. Sometimes, it is not fair to lean on someone too much because they can get tired, stressed, or frustrated if we expect them to handle too much. We can also invite someone to lean on us if we want to help them. “I know a lot of people are sick this week, so you can lean on me more for a few days.”

Imagine that the person who is being leaned on is like a piece of wood that is supporting a house; they can help support the house for a few days, but eventually, the house needs other measures of support. In the same way, we should only want to lean on someone for a short period of time unless we feel they can handle the tasks given to them for a long period of time, or we don’t have a choice. For example: “My wife starts work early in the morning, so my family leans on me to prepare breakfast and to drive the kids to school every day.”

Collocations: “lean on [someone] for advice,” “lean on [someone] for help,” “lean on [someone] for support”

Practice: Do you know the song “Lean on me” by Bill Withers?

3.lean on [something]. phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable) to rely on or use something to help you with something; to use something as the basis of your decisions, actions, words, etc.

Examples: “How will you impress her?” “I’m going to lean on my natural charm.”
“He leans on his sense of humour a lot in new social situations, which is a bad idea because he’s not that funny.”
“Mike leans on his past knowledge too much and doesn’t realize that times have changed.”

Common sentence: “Don’t lean on that too much.” (Don’t rely on or use that too much and expect it to be successful.)

If you lean on your sense of humour on a first date, you use your sense of humour and rely on it to get a positive response from your date. We all lean on certain behaviours to try to achieve a desired result. If you have a job interview, you will probably lean on your past work experience and/or your personality to make a good impression on your interviewer. In both cases above, you are using something as the basis for your words and actions.

Collocations: “lean on one’s experience with [something or someone],” “lean on one’s history with [something or someone],” “lean on one’s knowledge of [something or someone]”

Practice: What do you lean on in difficult situations? (Use a collocation.)

let in

phrasal verb (transitive, separable)

1.to allow someone or an animal to enter a place
Common extension: “let [someone] in/into [somewhere]”

Examples: “Who let him in here?” (Who allowed him to enter this place?)
“Could you let the dog in?” (The dog is outside and wants to come inside.)
“Don’t let them into the building.” (Don’t allow them to enter the building.)

Common sentence: “Let him/her/them in.” (Allow them to enter.)

“In” and “into” are often interchangeable in people’s minds when they use the common extension listed above. To specify, “Let me in the house!” and “Let me into the house!” are both possible and are both used. Technically, “in” means to be present inside the house (“I don’t want to be outside! I want to be inside!”). “Into” refers to the action of crossing a line from one place to another, in this case crossing the line (the door) between the outside and inside parts of the house.

Grammar note: “Let [someone] in” is preferred when an adverb of place, such as “here” or “there,” is used. See example 1 above.

Related words and phrases: “admit,” “allow in,” “let [someone] pass”

Practice: Finish the idea: “Don’t let _____ in.”

2.let [someone] in. (transitive, separable, usually separated) to share your intimate thoughts and feelings with someone; to trust someone and allow them to see your vulnerable side

Examples: “I want to help, but you have to let me in.” (You must share your troubles.)
“I’ve let you in, but you still won’t tell me anything about yourself.”
“She has a hard time letting people in.” (She has a hard time trusting others and telling them intimate details about her thoughts and feelings.)

Common sentence: “I can’t help you if you won’t let me in.” (I can’t help you if you don’t tell me what is actually bothering you.)

The idea behind this phrase is that you let people into your life and your heart—you allow them to see who you truly are on the inside. People can have a hard time letting others in. This means it’s difficult for them to talk about their most intimate thoughts and feelings with other people. If someone doesn’t want to let you in, they might be a private person, or they might be afraid of becoming close with others. They might also require more time before they feel comfortable opening up to you.

Related phrase: “open up to [someone]”

Practice: Why do people have a hard time letting others in to discuss personal problems?

let on

phrasal verb (transitive or intransitive, separable; usually intransitive)

1.to give clues, signs, or indications about something (usually something that is meant to be a secret); to make people think that something is true

Examples: “He’s been letting on that he wants a new phone for his birthday.”
“Her neighbours are letting on that she’s too loud.” (They have probably given indications to her that she’s too loud.)
“You haven’t let on that we’re not planning to visit your parents this year, have you?” (You haven’t given any clues to your parents, have you?)

Common sentence: “Don’t let on that anything’s wrong.” (Don’t give any indications that something is wrong.)

This phrase can be used in the affirmative, but it is also often used in the negative. For example: “He’s not letting on that he’s upset.” This means he is not providing any indications or signs that he is upset; he appears to be fine despite what other people have said about his mood. This phrase is also often used in negative imperatives, such as “Don’t let on that anything is wrong.” This means “Don’t give any indications to other people that anything is wrong. Act like everything is okay.”

Grammar note: “Let on” is often followed by a “that” clause. For example: “She let on that she wasn’t happy with her decision.” “Let on” can be used in all tenses, but is often used in the present simple, past simple, present perfect, and continuous forms.

Related words and phrases: “let [something] slip,” “reveal,” “suggest”

Practice: Are you good at keeping secrets, or do you usually let on that you know something?

listen to [something or someone]

verb+preposition combination

1.to pay attention to a sound (speech, music, etc.); to give your listening attention to something or someone

Examples: “We spent the morning listening to the president’s speech.”
“I saw you dancing. What were you listening to?”
“What kind of music do you listen to?”

Common sentence: “What are you listening to?” (This is often said to someone with headphones or earphones on, or to someone who is listening to something that catches your interest.)

This usage simply means paying attention to something you can hear. This is usually a person’s words (”Are you listening to me?” “Yes, I’m listening to you”), but it can be a song, an online video, a news report, or anything that requires the ability to hear and follow speech or other sounds, such as music or the sound of running water.

Related phrase: “catch [someone’s] words”

Practice: What kind of music have you been listening to lately?

2.to follow what someone or something says, such as a command, a policy, a set of rules, etc.; to give thoughtful attention to what someone is saying

Examples: “We’re not going to listen to you!” (We’re not going to give our attention to you, or we’re not going to follow your rules.)
“He refused to listen to her tearful pleas.”
“Could you put down the phone and just listen to me?”

Common sentence: “Don’t listen to him/her/them.” (Do not follow or believe what they say.)

Listening requires active attention with your ears and your mind. Parents might ask their children, “Are you listening to me?” or “Have you heard a single word I’ve said?”. The verb “hear” refers to the physical ability to receive sound by your ear, but it can sometimes be used in a similar sense to “listen,” as in the example above. In that example, if a child chooses to ignore what their parents say, it can be said that they “don’t hear” what their parents say. It can also be said that they “don’t listen” to their parents.

Grammar note: While the verb “hear” doesn’t require a preposition when you want to follow it with an object (“I heard you”), the verb “listen” does (“She doesn’t listen to her mom”).

Related phrase: “pay attention to [someone or something]”

Practice: Did you always listen to your parents when you were a child?

live by [somewhere]

verb+preposition combination

1.to live near or around a place; to live beside a place

Examples: “We live by the beach.”
“Doesn’t she live by the train station?”
“Where do you live by?” (“Where do you live?” is much more common, but you can add “by” if you want to know a little more information.)

Common sentence: “I live by there.” (I live near there.)

This phrase indicates someone’s close proximity to something. Where do you live by? Do you live by a grocery store? A gas station? A bank? A train station? This means you live near a particular landmark, building, etc.

Practice: What do you live by? (Ex: “I live by a train station.”)

2.live by [something]. phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable) to base one’s actions on a set of rules, beliefs, principles, etc.; to follow a set of rules, beliefs, principles, etc.

Examples: “I live by what my father taught me: Have courage and be kind.”
“My grandmother lived by a very strict set of principles.”
“You want some words to live by? Love and be loved.”

Common sentence: “Words to live by.” (Those are words you can use as the basis for a person’s actions. They provide good principles for living.)

You can live by the words of a person, a book, a sacred text, or simply a proverb. For instance: “I live by the motto that discipline is doing what needs to be done even when you don’t want to do it.” The phrase “words to live by” is common in this context, and is the meaning of the word “motto” above.

Grammar note: This phrase is often followed by a noun phrase (“the words of my mother,” “strict principles,” etc.) Don’t forget that a noun phrase can also begin with a question word, such as in example 1 above.

Collocations: “live by one’s beliefs,” “live by one’s faith,” “live by one’s principles,” “live by the belief that…,” “live by the idea that…,” “live by the Torah/the Quran/the Bible/etc.,” “live by the words of [someone],” “live by what [someone] taught you or said”

Practice: What are some words to live by? (Ex: “’Love and be loved’ are words to live by.”)

log in

phrasal verb (transitive or intransitive, separable)

1.to enter the required information to enter a computer’s main user interface or a particular webpage on the internet
Often interchanged with: “log on”
Opposite: “log out,” “log off,” “sign off”
Same: “sign on”

Examples: “Did somebody log in to my computer while I was away?”
“How often do you log on to your social media?”
“I forgot my password. I can’t log in.”

Common sentence: “I haven’t logged in yet.” (This is often said to let other people know that you intend to log in, and for people to be patient.)

If you have a laptop computer that requires a username and a password to access, you need to log in before you can start using your laptop’s operating system. It’s the same for any online account. For example: “You have to create an account to log in to that site.” Because we spend time on the internet and work on computers, this phrase can also be interchanged with “log on” in most cases (or “log on to” for transitive usage).

Grammar note: Use “log in to” or “log into” for transitive usage. Both are accepted in speech and in writing, though “ log in to” is still seen as more correct. For example: “She hasn’t logged in to her email today.” You can also “log someone in” if you want to use the transitive form. For instance: “Could you log me in? Here’s my password.” The negative form is “log out.” Use “log out of” for transitive usage. For example: “Don’t forget to log out of your social media account.”

You can also “be logged in” or “be logged on.” For example: “Are you logged in/on yet?” A friend might ask you this if they are waiting for you to join them in an online environment such as a game, a messaging platform, etc.

Collocations: “log in to an account,” “log in to a system,” “log in to a website”

Practice: How many times per day do you log in to your email?

look for [something or someone]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to search for something or someone

Examples: “Can I help you? Are you looking for something in particular?”
“I have been looking for you everywhere! Where have you been?”
“She was looking for love in all the wrong places.”

Common sentence: “I’m looking for __________.” (I’m trying to find this particular person, place, or thing.)

What are you looking for? You can look for tangible things, such as objects, places, and people, or for intangible things, such as love, justice, or security. In all of these cases, you are trying to find something—you are seeking for or looking for something or someone.

Grammar note: While “look for” is common in most tenses, it is often used in the continuous. For example: “What are you looking for?”; “We were looking for a place to stay.”

Collocations: “look for a new home,” “look for a place to stay,” “look for a reason,” “look for a way in/out,” “look for an answer,” “look for anomalies,” “look for love,” “look for [someone],” “look for [something],” “look for trouble”

Related phrases: “hunt for,” “search for,” “seek out,” “track down”

Practice: Finish the idea: “Could you help me? I’m looking for...”

make a difference

idiomatic phrase

1.to have an effect or to cause a change (usually positive)

Examples: “They can score 3 goals and it won’t make a difference. They’re losing 7-0.”
“He has made a major difference to our sales numbers since being hired.”
“She tried taking pain medication, but it didn’t make a difference.”

Common sentence: “It won’t make a difference.” (It will not cause any change in the situation.)

Good mentors can make a difference in our lives. The right treatment can make a difference when we are recovering from an injury. Studying a book like this one can make a difference to your English speaking skills. As you can see, the phrase “make a difference” is most often used to talk about something or someone who has caused positive change(s) to happen.

Collocations: “make a big difference,” “make a huge difference,” “make a major difference,” “make a massive difference,” “make a significant difference,” “make a slight difference,” “make a small difference”

Related phrase: “make an impact [on someone or something]”

Practice: Who or what has made a significant different in your life?

make sense

idiomatic phrase

1.to be comprehensible; to have a clear meaning or be easily understood
Common colloquial variation: “(not) make a lick of sense”

Examples: “Your argument doesn’t make sense.”
“I think we should do this first.” “Yes. That makes perfect sense to me.”
“Bob, slow down and start again. You’re not making any sense right now.”

Common sentence: “That makes sense.” (That is clear. I am able to understand it, and I think it is logical and sensible. Opposite: “That doesn’t make sense.”)

Things that make sense are usually logical, or as the word says, sensible. A person who is not thinking clearly might not make sense when they are trying to explain something. If something is difficult to understand, you might say “It doesn’t make sense to me.” Or perhaps you know a romantic couple whose union you do not understand. In this case, you might say “I can’t make sense of it.” You can also use this statement if you cannot solve or understand a problem, a text, etc.

Usage note: Here are some other very common uses of this phrase: “Does that make sense?”; “It makes no sense”; “This doesn’t make any sense”; “Am I making sense?”; “Is this making sense to you?”; “That doesn’t make a lick of sense” (colloquial).

Collocations: “make a lot of sense,” “make no sense,” “make perfect sense,” “not make a lot of sense,” “not make any sense”

Related sentence: “That adds up.” (Similar: ”That makes sense.”)

Practice: Who is someone in your life who usually makes perfect sense when they give you advice?

make sure

idiomatic phrase

1.to check something to confirm it is true, correct, happening, or the case; to ensure that something is done

Examples: “Could you make sure the oven is off?”
“Make sure you finish your homework before your friends come over.”
“Are you sure this is the right answer?” “I’ll check it again to make sure.”

Common sentence: “I’ll make sure of it.” (I will ensure that this happens.)

If you make sure that something happens, you accept responsibility for it happening. The word “ensure” is synonymous with this usage. For instance: “I’ll make sure all the doors are locked before I leave the house.” This means you will check all the doors and ensure that they are locked. You have accepted responsibility for ensuring that this task is done. Here is another example involving a tutor and a student: “I’ll make sure she receives all the academic support she needs.” The tutor is making a promise that they will provide all of the academic support the student needs.

Usage note: If someone asks you to do something important, especially when it’s related to the actions of another person, you can respond with “Don’t worry. I’ll make sure of it.” For example: “Make sure your little brother is in bed by 8pm.” “Don’t worry. I’ll make sure of it.”

Collocations: “make sure to do something,” “make sure of something”

Related phrases: “make certain,” “see to it that something happens or is done”

Practice: What do you make sure to do before you go to bed each night?

move on

phrasal verb (intransitive)

1.to continue moving forward; to continue to the next part or stage
Similar, see also: “go on”

Examples: “Could we move on? We don’t have a lot of time left.” (Could we continue? Could we move to the next part of this meeting/presentation/video/etc.?)
“We went to the bank, then we moved on to the theatre.”
“We shouldn’t stop here. We need to move on.” (We need to continue moving forward towards our goal/destination.)

Common sentence: “Let’s move on.” (Let’s continue to the next location, the next point, the next stage, etc.)

This phrase can be used with physical distances and locations (ex: “We moved on from my friend’s house to the coffee shop”) or figurative distances in conversations, presentations, meetings, etc. For instance: “Next, I would like to move on to discussing last month’s sales report.” In both cases, the idea of continuing from one stage of something to next is present. If you say “Let’s move on” during a meeting, you are concerned about the length of the meeting, or you feel the current topic has been adequately discussed. “We have to move on” is a clear indication that time is an issue.

Grammar note: Often extended as “move on with” or “move on to” for transitive usage. For example: “Move on with the meeting”; “Move on to the next point.” This is also a case where “to” is followed by a gerund if you wish to mention an activity (“We moved on to talking about her birthday”).

Collocations: “move on to the next point/section/stage/etc.,” “move on with the discussion/meeting/presentation/etc.,” “move on with it”

Practice: Finish the idea: “We need to move on to...”

2.(intransitive) to continue with one’s life after a difficult event, situation, or moment; to find the strength to continue moving forward after a difficult event, situation, or moment

Examples: “He broke up with you two years ago. You need to move on.”
“The earthquake changed everything. It’s hard to just move on from it.”
“Stop living in the past. It’s time to move on.”

Common sentence: “You need to move on.” (It’s not healthy for you to continue thinking about this. You need to accept what happened, release your anger/pain/etc., and continue with your life.)

If you have experienced the end of an important relationship, you know how difficult it can be to continue with your life. There are personal events that make us feel like life is impossible or that it is very difficult. The phrase “move on” can help us in these cases. It can refer to any difficult situation that you continue to focus on even when it’s not healthy for you. For example: “They fired you. Don’t hold on to your anger. Move on.”

When we talk about “moving on,” we usually talk about something that happened in the past and our need to “move on” from it—to continue living our life in the present. This is not always easy, especially if we are dealing with serious emotional trauma and/or something like a natural disaster, as in example 2 above. In all of these cases, however, we must still try to move on and to focus on doing what we need to do next in our lives.

Grammar note: Often extended as “move on with,” “move on to,” or “move on from” for transitive usage. For example: “Move on with your life”; “Move on to the next stage of your life”; “Move on from him/her/them.”

Collocations: “move on from him/her/them,” “move on from it,” “move on with one’s career,” “move on with one’s life”

Related phrases: “get over something,” “move past something”

Practice: How can we help others to move on after a difficult life event (the end of a relationship, the death of a loved one, etc.)?

part ways [with someone]

idiomatic phrase

1.to end a relationship

Examples: “It wasn’t working, so we parted ways.”
“Don’t part ways if you think there is a chance he still loves you.”
“We had to part ways after she moved away for school.”

Common sentence: “We parted ways.” (We ended our relationship.)

This is a polite way to say you ended a relationship. If you part ways with someone, one person goes one way, and the other person goes another way. You “part” (or “separate”) and go in different directions. “We parted ways” sounds more poetic and somber than the more informal “We broke up.” If you part ways, it’s possible the end of the relationship was mutual and/or sad and difficult for both of you.

Usage note: You can “part on good terms” or “part on bad terms” with someone. This means the parting had either positive or negative emotions at the end. If you parted on bad terms with someone, there were likely hurt and/or angry feelings involved at the end of the relationship.

Grammar note: For transitive usage, use “part ways with,” as in “She parted ways with Henry.” You can also simply say “They parted ways.”

Related phrases: “break up [with someone],” “part on bad/good terms”

Practice: Have you ever parted ways with someone on bad terms?

2.to leave someone; to end a meeting or date with someone and go in different directions

Examples: “Well, this is my bus stop. I have to part ways with you here.”
“I hate to part ways right now, but I have to wake up early tomorrow.”
“I’ll walk with you to the corner, but then I have to part ways with you.”

Common sentence: “I must part ways with you here.” (A performative way to say goodbye to someone.)

This refers to a physical separation of two or more people. It is polite and can sound slightly formal to some people, but it can be neutral as well, as in the examples above. It is sometimes said in a joking way, as if you are giving a dramatic performance. To illustrate: “I had a lovely time, but I must part ways with you now. I bid you adieu.” “I bid you adieu” is another formal sentence meaning “I wish you goodbye,” but it is often said in a joking or flippant way. If you want to be very dramatic and comedic, you could bow as you say this.

Grammar note: For transitive usage, use “part ways with,” as in “I have to part ways with you here.” If you want to indicate the place where you part ways, use the appropriate preposition after “part ways.” “At” is very common in this case, as in “We parted ways at the train station.”

Related words and phrases: “leave [someone] high and dry,” “split”

Practice: Finish the idea: “We parted ways at/on/in/near...”

pass on

phrasal verb (intransitive)

1.to die and continue one’s journey in the afterlife

Examples: “You didn’t hear? Robert passed on last month.”
“She lived with cancer for several years before she passed on.”
“I’m happy he passed on peacefully and that he’s in a better place now.”

Common sentence: “She passed on peacefully.” (She died without pain.)

“He passed on” is a softer way to say “He died.” While this phrase is similar to “pass away,” the received meaning is slightly different. The phrase “pass on” is linked to a belief in an afterlife—the belief that a person’s life (or their soul/spirit) continues living in a different plane of existence after their physical body dies in the mortal world. The phrase “pass away” is also a softer way of saying “die,” but if you say, “He passed away,” the meaning is closer to “He died and is not here anymore,” versus “He passed on,” which is closer to “He died and is continuing his journey in the afterlife.” The sentence “He’s/She’s/They’re in a better place” is also common among people who believe in an afterlife.

Grammar note: The noun form of “pass” in this context is “passing.” We can talk about someone’s death or “someone’s passing.” For example, “Did you hear about Diana’s passing?” or “Have you heard about Diana’s passing?” are common, soft ways to ask if someone has heard about a person’s death.

Collocations: “pass on peacefully,” “pass on to the next life”

Related phrases: “give up the ghost,” “meet one’s maker,” “pass away”

Practice: Finish the idea: “She passed on...” (give a time or use a collocation)

2.pass on [something] to [someone]. (transitive, separable) to give something that is yours to someone else; to give ownership to someone Common variation: “pass down [something to someone]”

Examples: “This recipe was passed on/down to me by my grandmother.”
“This knowledge has been passed on/down from generation to generation.”
“When my kids are a bit older, I’ll pass these toys on to them.”

Common sentence: “It was passed on to me by __________.” (I received this significant object from my mother/father/brother/etc., who owned it before giving it to me.)

A teacher passes on their knowledge to their students. A parent can pass on a secret family recipe to his/her children. A friend can pass on a valuable object to another friend. The idea behind this usage is that someone is giving something to someone else. Often, this usage relies on things being transferred from one generation to the next (parents to children), but it can be used in other situations, as mentioned above. In all of these cases, the thing that is being passed on usually has real or sentimental value to the person who is giving it. You can also use this phrasal verb as a substitute for “pass.” (Ex: “Take a copy of this document and pass the others on to your classmates.”)

Grammar note: The phrase “pass down” is similar, but its received meaning has a direct link to time and different generations of people. “This was passed down to me by my grandmother” means my grandmother had it first and passed it “down” the family line to me.

Collocations: “pass on a book/other object that has meaning to the person giving it,” “pass on a family secret/recipe/story/etc.,” “pass on everything one knows,” “pass on one’s knowledge,” “pass something on to [someone]”

Practice: Has a friend or family member ever passed on something significant to you?

3.pass on [something]. (transitive, inseparable) to decline or reject something; to choose not to accept or involve oneself with something

Examples: “I passed on their job offer. The money wasn’t good enough.”
“Did you respond to Zack’s birthday invitation?” “Yeah, I passed on it.”
“So, is she going to accept his offer?” “I think she’s going to pass on it.”

Common sentence: “I’m going to pass on it.” (I’m going to say “No.” I’m going to decline it. I’m not going to involve myself in it.)

“I’m going to pass on dinner” means “I am not going to have dinner.” You can pass on invitations, requests, or offers. For example: “Do you want to come to the zoo with us tomorrow?” “No, thanks. I’ll pass.” This is an informal response, and you have to be careful who you say it to, and how you say it because it can sound a little rude. In the most recent example, the received meaning could be “I have better things to do” or “I have no interest in going to the zoo.” If you keep a neutral and respectful tone, however, this phrase can just feel like a casual “No.”

Collocations: “pass on an invitation,” “pass on an offer,” “pass on it/that,” “pass on a request”

Related phrases: “opt out of something,” “turn down something”

Practice: What is a good reason to pass on an invitation?

pick up

phrasal verb (transitive, separable)

1.to grasp and lift something or someone from a surface

Examples: “Pick your socks up from the floor, please.”
“Our kids never pick up their toys when they’re done playing with them.”
“Would you mind picking that up for me? My back hurts. Thank you.”

Common sentence: “Pick it up.” (Lift it. Perhaps the person saying this is not happy with the mess you have made by leaving something on the floor.)

Typically, we pick up (or, “lift”) something from the ground, but it can be from any surface. For example: “Pick up your backpack, please. It shouldn’t be on your bed.” We can also pick up people. For example, a small child might say “Mama! Pick me up! I want to touch the ceiling!” If someone asks you to pick up your things because they are on various surfaces in a room, the person is asking you to take your things from all of the surfaces in the room to make the room tidy.

Collocations: “pick something up from the floor,” “pick something up off the floor,” “pick up after oneself,” “pick up a baby,” “pick up one’s feet,” “pick up one’s socks,” “pick up one’s stuff,” “pick up one’s things”

Practice: Do you usually pick up after yourself? (This means if you make a mess, you clean it. For example, you clear the table after eating.)

2.pick up [someone or something]. (transitive, separable) to obtain/get someone or something from a specific location, often by driving a vehicle to get the person or thing
Opposite: “drop off”

Examples: “Wait there. I’ll pick you up in 20 minutes.” (Context: speaking on the phone.)
“I need to pick up some bread from the grocery store on the way home.”
“My mom used to pick us up from school when we were kids.”

Common sentence: “I can pick you up.” (I can drive and get you from your location, and then take you where you or we plan to go.)

If you pick up someone, you go to where they are and transport them to where they need or want to go. For example: “I’m going to pick up Mike at his house, and then we’re going to go to the movie theatre.” You can also use this phrasal verb to talk about obtaining objects from specific places. This is often used when talking about buying things at a store. For example: “I need to pick up some tomatoes from the market.” If you go to a person’s house to acquire something from them, you can also use this phrasal verb, as in “After school, I went to my aunt’s house to pick up a cake.”

Grammar note: This phrase is often extended in one of these ways: “pick [someone or something] up at/on/in a particular time/day/period of time/etc.” or “pick [someone or something] up at/from a particular place.”

Collocations: “pick [someone] up in 15 minutes/an hour/etc.,” “pick up breakfast/lunch/dinner,” “pick up some groceries,” “pick up [someone] from school/work,” “pick up [something] for breakfast/lunch/dinner,” “pick up [someone or something] on the way home,” “pick up [someone or something] on the way to school/work”

Practice: Do you need to pick up anything from the market or grocery store?

3.pick up [something]. (transitive, separable) to learn a new skill or how to do or use something (a language, an instrument, a process at work, etc.)

Examples: “When did you pick up the guitar?”
“I’ve been trying to pick up Italian for over 3 years.”
“It’s not hard to learn. I’m sure you’ll pick it up in no time.” (“In no time” means “very quickly” or “immediately.”)

Common sentence: “You’ll pick it up in no time.” (You’ll learn it quickly.)

If you learned piano while you were in high school, you can say “I picked up piano in high school.” This means you learned it—you acquired the skill of playing piano. This usage is common when talking about learning instruments and languages, but it can be used with other skills as well. For example, if you get a new phone and you don’t know how to use it, a friend of yours might say, “Don’t worry. I’m sure you’ll pick it up in a few days.” This means “I’m sure you’ll learn how to use it in a few days.”

Grammar note: This phrase is often used in the past simple (“She picked up swimming really quickly when she was a kid”) and the future simple (“You’ll pick it up quickly”), but it can be used in almost any tense. Here are a few other examples: “I’ve been trying to pick up cooking for the past three months”; “This new coding language is hard to understand, but I’m picking it up”; “It takes dedication and consistency to pick up any new skill.”

Collocations: “pick something up in no time,” “pick something up over time,” “pick up English/French/Korean/Farsi/any language,” “pick up piano/guitar/any instrument”

Practice: Which languages would you like to pick up eventually?

4.pick up [someone]. (transitive, separable) to successfully flirt with someone; to talk to someone with the intention of getting them romantically or sexually interested in you

Examples: “One of my co-workers tried to pick me up at lunch yesterday.”
“He’s always trying to pick up girls at the mall. It’s sad.”
“That’s a nice bracelet.” “Are you trying to pick me up? I’m not interested.”

Common sentence: “He/She/They tried to pick me up.” (He/She/They tried flirting with me to attract my romantic and/or sexual interest.)

If you try to pick someone up at a social event, a dance club, or other location where people gather, you try to get them interested in you as a romantic partner. Someone’s goal in this situation is usually to persuade the other person to give them his/her phone number. The goal of this practice is usually to have casual romantic or sexual encounters, but it can be for serious relationships as well. If your friend asks you, “Who’s Paul?” and you say “He’s the guy I picked up at my cousin’s wedding,” the received meaning is that the person who picked up Paul isn’t very serious about him, but that they will probably date him and see what happens.

Collocations: “pick [someone] up at the club/the bar/the disco/the theatre/etc.,” “pick up boys,” “pick up girls,” “try to pick [someone] up”

Practice: How would you react if someone tried to pick you up in public?

5.pick up [an illness or disease]. (transitive, separable, usually unseparated) to acquire an illness or disease [from someone, something, somewhere, etc.]

Examples: “Dress warmly or you might pick up a cold.”
“She picked up the flu from her kids.”
“We both picked up Hepatitis C while we were on vacation.”

Common sentence: “I picked up a cold/the flu/etc.” (I acquired this illness. The past simple or the present perfect are both common when we want to tell someone that we have recently contracted a particular illness.)

If your friend has a cold and transmits the cold virus to you, then congratulations, you have picked up a cold from your friend! Any transmittable illness or disease can be “picked up” by someone if they’re not careful. You can also pick up illnesses and diseases in other ways. For instance: “I picked up salmonella after eating some undercooked chicken.” People might ask you “When did you pick it up?” or “How did you pick it up?” Be ready to answer with times and places, as in “I picked it up yesterday” or “I think I picked it up from my husband/wife/uncle/etc.” If you don’t want to use this phrase, you can just say “I got it from my sister,” for instance.

What’s the difference between an illness and a disease? An illness needs to be managed until your body fights it and returns to a normal state (a cold, a flu, etc.). A disease needs to be cured (malaria, Ebola, etc.).

Collocations: “pick up a cold,” “pick up the flu,” “pick up Hepatitis A/B/C,” “pick up malaria,” “pick up Covid,” “pick up chickenpox,” “pick up a headache,” “pick up a stomachache,” “pick up an STD” (a sexually transmitted disease), etc.

Related phrase: “come down with a physical sickness” (ex: “I think I’m coming down with a cold.”)

Practice: When was the last time you picked up a cold? How long did it last?

play it by ear

idiomatic phrase

1.to act by using your instincts and reacting to the situation instead of trying to follow a set plan; to manage something without following a specific plan or instructions; to improvise by reacting to changing circumstances

Examples: “What time is good for you? I’m free at 1pm and 3pm.” “Let’s play it by ear.”
“This project is so confusing, and the boss has given me no guidance. I’m just playing everything by ear.”
“Sorry that I can’t give you a clear answer. Let’s play it by ear, okay?”

Common sentence: “Let’s play it by ear.” (Let’s see how the situation develops and base our decisions on those developments.)

“I would love to have coffee with you tomorrow, but I have a lot of work to do. Can we play it by ear?” This means “Can we see how the situation develops? If I finish my work, we can meet for coffee, but it’s possible I will have too much work and we won’t be able to meet for coffee.” Just like a musician improvises with their instrument, people improvise when they act or when they make plans. It’s similar to saying “Let’s see what happens.”

Grammar note: While the most common idiom is “play it by ear,” the “it” is sometimes replaced with “everything” or “the situation,” as in, “It’s obvious that she doesn’t have a plan. She’s playing everything by ear.”

Usage note: The phrase “make it up as you go along” is also similar if you mean to say that someone is just deciding things in the moment with no plan—they are just inventing things and reacting in the moment. This is similar but not exactly the same as “play it by ear,” which has a received meaning that sounds more responsible.

Related phrases: “see how it goes,” “see what happens,” “take it as it comes,” “take things as they come”

Practice: Is it sometimes necessary to play a situation by ear? Can you think of a time you had to do this?

root for [someone or something]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to cheer for someone or something; to show support for someone or something with one’s voice, actions, etc.

Examples: “This is such a good game! I don’t know who to root for!”
“Good luck on your test today. I’ll be rooting for you.”
“Sorry that your team lost. I was really rooting for you guys.”

Common sentence: “I’m rooting for you.” (I want you to be successful and I want you to know you have my support and my wishes for your success.)

Sports fans root for their favourite teams and players. Friends root for their friends to be successful. In both cases, someone shows vocal support for someone or something. This phrasal verb is most often associated with sports, as in, “They’ve been rooting for Chelsea since they were kids,” which means they have been supporting (or, “cheering for”) Manchester United since they were kids.

This phrasal verb can be used in other cases as well. For example, if you want a romantic relationship between two of your friends to be successful, you can say “I’m rooting for you guys,” which means “I support you and strongly hope that you stay together as a couple.”

Collocations: “nobody to root for,” “root for the home team”

Related phrases: “cheer for,” “cheer on”

Practice: Do you root for any sports teams? Which ones?

rule out [something or someone]

phrasal verb (transitive, separable)

1.to remove something or someone from consideration; not to consider something or someone as a possible explanation or cause of something

Examples: “Do you think Erika could have done this?” “I wouldn’t rule it out.”
“The police have ruled out revenge as a motive for the criminal’s actions.”
“That explanation sounds a little unbelievable, but I’m not ruling it out.”
Common sentence: “I’m not ruling it out.” (I’m not removing that as a possible explanation, cause, or reason.)

This phrase is often used to talk about the possible reasons, explanations, or causes of something, and which reasons, explanations, and causes we choose to consider when we are looking for an answer. This phrasal verb is often used with modal verbs, such as can/can’t, will/won’t, would/wouldn’t, and should/shouldn’t. For example: “We can’t rule out the possibility that Zaya was responsible for this mistake.” This means we need to consider Zaya as a possibility for who caused the mistake.

Before we rule something or someone out, we usually go through an investigative or logical process. For instance: “Why did she decide to move to Dublin? I think we can definitely rule out money because she will be getting paid less at her new job, and the cost of living is higher where she’s going.”

Grammar note: Another possible structure for saying “We can’t rule out the possibility that Zaya was responsible for this mistake” is “We can’t rule out Zaya as a possibility.” Remember, you can rule out something or someone.

Collocations: “can rule out,” “can’t rule out,” “is/was ruled out,” “not ruling it out,” “rule it out,” “rule out an explanation,” “rule out as a possibility,” “rule out the possibility,” “rule [someone] out”

Related words and phrases: “count out,” “dismiss,” “exclude”

Practice: Finish the idea: “We can’t rule out _____ as a possibility.”

run on [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to use something as the source of something’s or someone’s energy

Examples: “Did you know that some cars can run on vegetable oil?”
“Rockets run on jet fuel.”
“Does it run on batteries or electricity?”

Common sentence: “It runs on batteries.” (It needs batteries to work; it uses batteries as the source of its power/energy.)

Today’s cars run on fuel, electricity, or solar power. Cellphones run on batteries. Many toys run on batteries, too. When we say that something “runs on” something else, we mean it uses something as its source of energy. You can get creative (and funny) with this phrase and talk about what gives a person their energy. For example: “He runs on coffee and dreams”; “I run on pizza and soda”; “She runs on love and optimism.” In all of these cases, we mean that these people get their energy from the mentioned sources. As you can see, they can be things such as food and drink, or more abstract things, such as dreams and love.

Grammar note: This phrasal verb is usually used in the present simple (“It runs on batteries”) or the present continuous (“She’s running on a thirst for justice”). However, other tenses are also possible, such as, “Their company thinks that all cars in the future will run on solar power.”

Collocations: “run on (a particular emotion or abstract idea),” “run on (a specific food and/or drink),” “run on a hope and a prayer,” “run on batteries,” “run on electricity,” “run on fumes,” “run on gas/fuel/petrol,” “run on hope,” “run on solar power,” “run on the belief that…,” “run on the idea that…”

Practice: What do you usually run on in the morning? (Get creative!)

shed light on [something]

idiomatic phrase

1.to help explain something; to give information that makes something clearer; to make something easier to understand; to give something more focus
Common variations: “shed a light on something,” “shed some light on something,” “cast light on something,” “throw light on something”

Examples: “The news report helped shed a light on government corruption.”
“Who moved all the furniture? Jane, could you help shed some light on this?”
“This is complex topic, so give me a minute to shed some more light on it.”

Common sentence: “Could you shed some light on this?” (Could you help explain this and make it clearer for me/everyone?)

If you shed light on something, you try to make it clearer for others. You try to provide information that helps explain something. For example: “The presentation shed light on climate change.” This means the presentation provided more information on climate change and made it easier to understand. This phrase is often used in the question form “Could you shed some light on...,” as in “Could you shed some light on this situation?” In this case, the person is asking you to help explain the situation in more detail because you probably know something about it.

Collocations: “help (to) shed light on something,” “shed light on a situation,” “shed light on a subject,” “shed light on a topic,” “shed light on it/this/that,” “shed light on the fact that…,” “shed more light on something”

Practice: What is a news story that you wish media companies would shed more light on?

side with [someone]

idiomatic phrase

1.to support someone’s opinion(s) or action(s); to agree with someone
Opposite: “side against [someone]”

Examples: “I’m siding with the protesters. I think they have a very valid cause.”
“How could you side with him against me? I’m your brother!”
“This is a tough decision. Who are you going to side with?”

Common sentence: “Who are you siding with?” (Who are you going to support and/or agree with in this situation?)

You can side with someone in an argument. For example, if your mother and father are having a debate, you can choose to side with your mother or to side with your father, depending on whom you agree with. Another way to say this is that you are “on someone’s side.” In the situation above, you can say “I’m on my mom’s side in this case,” or “I’m siding with my mom in this case.” You can side with someone’s opinion, beliefs, reasons for doing something, or their actions. The opposite of this phrase is to side against someone, as in “His own brother sided against him.”

Grammar note: This phrase can be used in most tenses, but is often used in the past simple (“She sided with the enemy”), the present simple (“Don’t side with him”), or the present continuous when you make a decision in the moment or you are temporarily picking one side to support (“We’re siding with the company for now”). This phrase is also sometimes used in conditional sentences, as in this third conditional example: “I would have sided with them if they hadn’t fired 200 employees.”

Collocations: “side with management,” “side with [someone] against [someone else],” “side with the employees,” “side with the enemy”

Practice: Finish the idea: “I can’t believe he sided with...”

sign on

phrasal verb (intransitive)

1.to officially join a company, organization, etc. (usually by signing a contract)
Common variation: “sign [someone] on [to do something]”

Examples: “Did Teddy decide to take the job?” “Yeah, he officially signed on today.”
“We’re impressed by your experience, and we want you to sign on with us.”
“Did you hear about Bobby? The team signed him on for two more years.”

Common sentence: “He’s/She’s signed on for another ______ months/years/etc.” (He/She has this much time remaining on their contract.)

If you sign on with a company, you usually sign your name on an official contract. Signing on with a company means that you have an agreement with them, whether permanent or for a specific period of time. This phrasal verb is often used in sports, as in “She signed on with the team for another four seasons.” A company or organization can also sign someone on, as in “They signed me on for a summer position.”

Collocations: “sign on for (a specific period of time),” “sign on with [someone] (a company, organization, team, etc.)”

Related phrase: “bring [someone] on board” (ex: “The company has brought 5 new people on board.”)

Practice: Finish the idea: “They need to sign more people on to...”

2.See the entry for “log in”

sign up

phrasal verb (intransitive)

1.to register for a membership to a club, website, etc.; to register for an event, a social gathering, a volunteering opportunity, etc.; to sign one’s name in order to do something or join something

Examples: “Are you going to sign up for more guitar lessons this year?”
“To attend the presentation, you have to sign up on their website.”
“I really wanted to take that class. Is there still time to sign up?”

Common sentence: “I signed up.” (I officially registered or volunteered.)

“Have you signed up yet?” This means “Have you signed your name to officially join the activity, presentation, course, club, etc. yet?” You can sign up for a gym membership, an email list, or a social gathering, among many other things. Parents often sign their children up for activities. For example: “I signed up my son for taekwondo classes”; “My mom signed me up for summer camp.”

Collocations: “sign up for something,” “sign up for __________ classes,” “sign up for __________ lessons,” “sign up for an account,” “sign up for a membership”

Practice: What was the last thing/membership/program you signed up for?

slip by

phrasal verb (intransitive)

1.to pass without being noticed; to evade notice
Similar, see also: “pass by”

Examples: “He called you lazy. Are you just going to let that slip by?”
“This is a very tense movie. The spy just slipped by the guards.”
“I’ll let it slip by this time, but make sure you’re never late again.”

Common sentence: “I let it slip by.” (I let it pass, either consciously or because I missed it.)

If someone slips by you while you’re standing on the bus, they do their best to pass you without touching you and without being noticed. They might even say, “Excuse me. Can I slip by?” This is similar to the phrase “pass by,” but it has an element of stealth attached to it. Imagine a person who makes themselves as thin and quiet as possible when they are trying to move through a tight space.

This phrase can be used in a variety of contexts. For example: “Many of his jokes slipped by me.” “It’s already 5 o’clock? The day just slipped by.” “I can’t believe that policy slipped by and was included in the final document.” In all of these cases, something passes something or someone. In the third example, just like a spy can quietly slip by a security guard, a policy can slip by an approval process and get into (or, “slip into”) an official document.

Usage note: An opportunity can “slip you by” or “slip by.” This means you do not take advantage of it quickly enough, or you choose to ignore it. For example: “You could have had that position, but you didn’t file an application, so you just let the opportunity slip you by.” (Or, “slip by.”)

Collocations: “let an opportunity slip (you) by,” “let it/him/her slip by,” “slipped by quietly,” “slip by undetected,” “slip by without being noticed”

Practice: Finish the idea: “Excuse me. Could you let me...?”

stand by

phrasal verb (intransitive)

1.to wait in readiness for someone or something; to wait and be ready to help and/or take action; to wait and do nothing

Examples: “He’s going to hurt himself. I can’t just stand by and do nothing.”
“The president will be here in 5 minutes. Please stand by for his official address to the nation.”
“The soldiers are standing by and waiting to receive their orders.”

Common sentence: “Are you just going to stand by and do nothing?” (Are you just going to allow this to happen by not taking any action?)

This phrase is common in official contexts, such as in military and government orders, or during news reports. For example: “The president says the army is ready and standing by”; “Please stand by for further details”; “We now take you to our field reporter, Joanna Mackinson, who is standing by at the site of the accident.” In all of these cases, someone is waiting and doing nothing, but they are ready to act when needed.

This phrase is also often used in the context of scolding someone for not acting during an important moment. Two common sentences with this usage are: “We can’t just stand by and let this happen,” and “Are you going to stand by and do nothing?”

Collocations: “can’t just stand by,” “stand by and do nothing,” “stand by and let something happen,” “stand by for details,” “stand by for further instructions,” “stand by until further notice,” “standing by and ready for action,” “standing by and ready to go”

Related phrases: “be on alert,” “be ready for action”

Practice: Finish the idea: “I can’t believe he just stood by and...” (use the collocations to help you)

2.stand by [someone or something]. (transitive, inseparable) to continue supporting someone or something, especially during a difficult time or when it might not be popular to do so
Similar, see also: “stick with [someone or something]”

Examples: “Thanks for standing by me after all these years.”
“I stood by him when no one else did.”
“Do you still stand by what you said in your speech, or have you changed your perspective?”

Common sentence: “I stand by what I said.” (I am not changing my opinion. I still believe and support what I said.)

We often stand by our friends when they are going through a difficult time, or when they are the objects of criticism, unpopularity, or ridicule. Our friends need us during these times because they might feel scared or anxious. When we stand by them, we offer them continued support. We might not even agree with our friends, and we might think they deserve the criticism they are receiving, but we still choose to stand by them.

You can also stand by something you say or believe, as in example 3 above. This means you still believe and support your own words, beliefs, etc. When we choose to stand by someone or something we say or believe, we can also become objects of criticism, unpopularity, or ridicule.

Collocations: “stand by one’s words/beliefs/opinions,” “stand by [someone], no matter what,” “stand by what one says/believes,” “stand by you/me/etc.”

Related phrases: “be loyal to [someone],” “stick by [someone]”

Practice: Who is someone who has stood by you for a long time?

stay away

phrasal verb (intransitive, inseparable)

1.to avoid someone or something; to not go near someone or something
Common transitive extension: “stay away from [someone or something]”

Examples: “You should stay away from doing any exercise until your shoulder heals.”
“Don’t come near me! Stay away!”
“Stay away from him. He’s bad news.” (He’s someone who causes problems.)

Common sentence: “Stay away from him/her/them.” (Keep your distance from him/her/them. This can be literal because the person is sick, for instance, or advice not to interact or become involved with someone.)

“You should stay away from social media.” This means you should avoid social media. The phrase “stay away” is often used in sentences that give advice, such as in the example above, but it can be used in practical contexts as well. For instance: “I try to stay away from eating dairy because it upsets my stomach”; “I couldn’t stay away from him.” In the second example, perhaps the person was really in love with someone, or they had an unhealthy relationship with someone.

If something is everywhere and/or unavoidable, you can also say “You can’t stay away from it,” or “You can’t stay away from it forever.” You can also “try to stay away” from someone or something, such as drinking, overeating, sweets, watching the news, etc. In all of these cases, we are talking about avoiding or not going near someone or something.

Collocations: “stay away from danger,” “stay away from him/her/them/etc.,” “stay away from strangers,” “stay away from temptation,” “stay away from trouble”

Related phrases: “keep away [from someone or something],” “keep one’s distance [from someone or something],” “stay out of trouble”

Practice: What bad habits do you try to stay away from?

stem from

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to originate from something; to be caused by something; come from

Examples: “A lot of people’s problems stem from unresolved childhood trauma.”
“Where do your beliefs stem from?” “Most of them stem from my father.”
“Empathy stems from the idea that we should try to understand each other better and not be so quick to judge one another.”

Common sentence: “It stems from the fact that…” (It originates from this fact. This is the source of the point I am discussing. For example: “My position on this topic stems from the fact that I have personal experience with this type of situation.”)

Everything has a source, or something it stems from. A flower stem is the long green part you hold between your fingers when you pull or cut a flower from the ground. The stem grows and comes from the ground before it blooms at the very top and becomes a full flower. In the same way, our beliefs can stem from various sources, such as our parents, our schooling, our independent reading, and/or our friends. We don’t typically talk about people stemming from somewhere (as we do with “come from”), but about concrete and abstract things. For example, our problems often stem from something, as do our beliefs and opinions.

Collocations: “stem from (unresolved) trauma,” “stem from a belief in [someone or something],” “stem from childhood,” “stem from one’s beliefs,” “stem from one’s education,” “stem from one’s experience,” “stem from one’s faith,” “stem from one’s parents,” “stem from one’s religion,” “stem from the belief that...,” “stem from the fact that…,” “stem from the idea that…”

Practice: Where do your personal values stem from?

stick with [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to continue using something, especially when you have other options
Common variation: “stick to [something]”

Examples: “I’ll stick with this laptop for another year or so. It still works pretty well.”
“If you’ve always had good experiences with that shoe brand, you should stick with it.”
“His girlfriend wanted him to convert to her religion, but he stuck with his.”

Common sentence: “Stick with what works.” (Continue using or doing what has worked in the past and continues to work in the present. Go with the safe, reliable option.)

If you decide to buy a new phone, and you choose to buy one that is the same brand as the phone you currently use, you are choosing to stick with that brand. This means you are not changing the brand of your phone. When we stick with something, it’s usually because we have had positive experiences with it in the past.

This phrasal verb is very common when we are talking about the brands and types of things we use and/or choose. For example, a waiter at a restaurant that you often visit might ask, “Would you like to try something new tonight, or will you stick with your usual order?”

You can also stick with a type of exercise (“My friend told me to try karate, but I’m going to stick with yoga”), a particular career field (“She has decided to stick with content marketing”), or a particular way of doing things (“I’m going to stick with doing things my way!”).

Sometimes, we stick with things because of habit. For instance: “I’ve stuck with my internet provider for 10 years. I don’t want to deal with changing everything now.” Finally, we stick with things because they have worked for us in the past: “Stick with what works!”

Grammar note: The past simple and past participle forms of “stick” are “stuck.” For example: “She stuck with that car for twelve years.” “They have stuck with that landscaping company for a long time.”

Collocations: “stick with what got you here,” “stick with what works,” “stick with what you know,” “stick with what you like”

Practice: What is a brand that you have stuck with for a long time?

2.stick with [someone]. (transitive, inseparable) to continue being with or supporting someone (a friend, a romantic partner, a business partner, etc.); to stay close to someone

Examples: “If you stick with me during the training session, everything will be okay.”
“We can’t believe that he stuck with her for three years!”
“Stick with the group, and you won’t get lost.”
