Common sentence: “Stick with me.” (Stay close to me physically, or stay close to me and do not abandon me over a period of time.)

“I have stuck with my husband for 20 years.” This means the person has continued being with their husband for 20 years. This is similar to usage 1, but it uses people (or animals and pets) instead of things. The passive form is also common with “stick with,” as in “My brother went on vacation, so I have been stuck with his dog for the past 7 days.”

If someone is very loyal and stays with someone else until the end of a significant period of time, a common usage of this phrase is “stick with someone until the end,” as in “His wife passed away last year. He stuck with her until the end.”

This phrase can also mean that you are simply physically close to someone, as in this example: “Make sure to stick with me when we’re walking through the airport. It’s easy to get lost.” A similar phrase for this situation is “stick close to someone.”

Collocations: “stick with me,” “stick with the group,” “be stuck with [someone],” “stick with [someone] until the end”

Practice: Which of your friends has stuck with you the longest?

3.stick with [something]. (transitive, inseparable) not to quit doing something; to persevere and continue doing something even when it’s difficult; to practice something regularly so you can become better at it

Examples: “This project is so frustrating.” “We’re almost done. Just stick with it.”
“Learning an instrument is hard, but if you stick with it, it’s very rewarding.”
“If you stick with English and push yourself to study every day, I promise you will see significant results in just a few months.”

Common sentence: “Stick with it!” (Stay with it. Continue trying. Do not become discouraged, and do not quit.)

“Stick with it!” is the most common imperative you will hear with this usage. It means to continue doing the activity you are doing and not to stop or quit. The person who is encouraging you probably sees your talent and the hard work you have already done, so they want you to continue moving forward. For example: “This math question is really hard.” “You did the ones before it, right? Stick with it.” You can imagine that this phrase means to stay with the problem longer and continue trying to solve it. Don’t give up! Keep at it! Stick with it! You can do it!

Collocation: “stick with it”

Related phrases: “keep at [something],” “stay with [something]”

Practice: What is something you have stuck with and seen positive results from because of your commitment?

4.stick with [someone]. (transitive, inseparable) to leave an impression on someone that is not easily forgotten; to be remembered

Examples: “I miss my uncle. The image of his warm smile has always stuck with me.”
“After she lost her job, feelings of embarrassment stuck with her for years.”
“You should listen to this. It’s the kind of music that sticks with you.”

Common sentence: “That has always stuck with me.” (That left an impression on me and I still remember it and think about it today.)

Someone’s words, a movie, a song, a particular memory, or anything that leaves a strong impression on you can stick with you. This means it stays with you and is not easily forgotten. For example: “That movie has stuck with me.” This means the movie has stayed in your memories. This is probably because the themes and messages of the movie feel important to you and speak to your heart, mind, and/or spirit. Feelings and emotions can stick with us, too. For example: “The anger she felt toward her father stuck with her for a long time.”

Finally, if you see a positive change in someone’s behaviour, you can say “I hope it sticks,” meaning “I hope he/she continues to act in this way,” or “I hope this person’s new and improved behaviour becomes permanent.”

Grammar note: This phrase can be used in various tenses, but is often used with the present perfect and the past simple. Note the following: “My university experience has really stuck with me”; “The fear of abandonment stuck with him for a long time.”

Collocations: “always stick with [someone],” “really stick with [someone],” “stick with [someone] for a long time”

Related phrases: “leave an impression,” “stay with [someone]”

Practice: What is a book or movie that has stuck with you?

swear by [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to have a strong belief that something is dependable and effective (because one has used it themselves and has seen consistent results/effects)

Examples: “Can you recommend a good perfume?” “Try this one. I swear by it.”
“I think these books might help you. My brother swears by them.”
“In college, we swore by coffee as the best way to wake up in the morning.”

Common sentence: “He/She swears by it.” (He/She uses it regularly and trusts that it is effective.)

“I swear by it!” This is the most common sentence that uses this phrase. It means “I use this all the time, and I promise you that it is effective.” The “it” can be any product, tool, service, or object that the person trusts. For example, a friend might tell you: “My mother swears by honey and ginger tea as a cure for a cold.” However, while these descriptive sentences are certainly possible and do happen in regular conversation, this phrase is typically used in these quick spoken confirmations: “He/She swears by it” or “I swear by it.” You can also use the plural “them” for plural objects, as in “He/She swears by them” or “I swear by them.”

We can also swear by specific physical remedies, foods, drinks, or even restaurants. For example: “Can you recommend a good pizza place?” “I swear by Gino’s Pizzeria.” This means “I have been to Gino’s Pizzeria numerous times, and I am telling you that it is a great pizza place.”

Collocations: “swear by it,” “swear by [something] as a remedy for [something],” “swear by [something] as the best way to…”

Practice: What is a product or company that you swear by?

take [someone or something] by surprise

idiomatic phrase

1.to cause someone or something to feel surprised; to surprise someone or something

Examples: “Don’t let it take you by surprise, but you didn’t win the election.”
“A car took me by surprise and almost hit me on my way to work today.”
“The news of her death took the organization by surprise.”

Common sentence: “It took me by surprise.” (It surprised me. Usually, this refers to news you heard, or a revelation that is relevant to you)

In most cases, you can replace the phrase “take someone or something by surprise” with “surprise someone or something,” and the meaning will be the same. For instance, “Your decision really took the company by surprise” is another way of saying “Your decision really surprised the company.” This is simply another way to say something, and it adds variety to your language. The word “take” gives a more physical feeling to the surprise, like someone grabbing you by your shirt and saying “surprise!”

Collocation: “really take [someone] by surprise”

Related phrase: “stop [someone] in their tracks”

Practice: What is a news story that took you by surprise?

2.to use surprise as a tactical strategy (by the police, the army, etc.); to attack someone without warning; to surprise someone with the intention of intimidating them or hurting them

Examples: “The police took the criminal by surprise.”
“Captain Miller and his team will take the gang by surprise on the east side.”
“Our soldiers didn’t have a chance. The enemy army took us by surprise.”

Common sentence: “They took him/her by surprise.” (They used surprise as part of their strategy to confront him/her.)

If the police plan a secret operation and hope to surprise a gang of criminals, they plan to take them by surprise. For example: “We’ll take the west compound by surprise. They won’t see us coming.” In this situation, the police are using surprise as a tactical strategy. They are using surprise as a weapon. This usage is common in military and law enforcement contexts, but it can be used in contexts that involve regular citizens as well, as in “He took me by surprise and demanded that I give him all of my money.”

Practice: What is the tactical advantage of taking an enemy by surprise?

take care of [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to deal with or handle something; to be responsible for something

Examples: “Who’s going to take care of the flowers for the wedding?”
“Did you take care of your driver’s license renewal like you said you would?”
“Roberta’s taking care of the decorations for the party.”

Common sentence: “I’ll take care of it.” (I’ll do it. I am telling you that I will manage it and be responsible for it.)

If you’re planning a party and assigning different responsibilities to people, the people are responsible for taking care of their tasks. For example, “Dan’s going to take care of the drinks” means that Dan will buy the drinks. If you have a family and your partner says “I’ll take care of dinner tonight,” this means they will take responsibility for preparing dinner.

“I took care of it” and “I’ll take care of it” are very common sentences with this usage. They mean “I handled it” or “I will handle it.” In all of these cases, someone is responsible for managing something.

Collocations: “take care of breakfast/lunch/dinner,” “take care of it,” “take care of the bill,” “take care of the chores/groceries/dishes/laundry/etc.”

Practice: Who usually takes care of the groceries in your home?

2.take care of [someone or something]. (transitive, inseparable) to be responsible for someone’s or something’s well-being; to keep someone or something safe or in good condition

Examples: “Her grandmother had to take care of nine children.”
“Don’t lend her that book. She never takes care of things.”
“I’m babysitting tonight. I have to take care of my neighbour’s kids.”

Common sentence: “He’ll/She’ll take good care of you.” (He/She will make sure you are given everything you need.)

Parents are responsible for taking care of their children. This means they feed them, provide them with clothing and shelter, and give them the emotional support they require. The phrase can be used in any situation where someone is responsible for the well-being, safety, and/or condition of someone or something. For example, a teacher must take care of his/her students while the students are at school. A trainer at a job is responsible for taking care of new employees, and someone who works with clients or customers is responsible for taking care of their clients or customers.

You can also take care of things and places. This means you keep them in good condition. For instance, “He takes care of his car” means “He keeps his car in good condition.” He probably washes it and takes it to the mechanic on a regular basis. A person can also take care of their home, which means they clean it and keep the house in good condition.

Collocations: “take better care of [someone or something],” “take care of children,” “take care of one’s family,” “take care of oneself, “take care of one’s parents,” “take good care of [someone or something],” “take care of the kids,” “take poor care of [someone or something]”

Related phrase: “look after”

Practice: How many children did your grandparents take care of?

3.take care of [a bill/a ticket/a financial responsibility/etc.]. (transitive, inseparable) to pay for something

Examples: “Bianca said she would take care of the bill.”
“Who’s going to take care of the extra fees?”
“The delivery man needs to be paid. Could you take care of it?”

Common sentence: “I’ll take care of it.” (I’ll pay for it in this case.)

“I’ll take care of the bill” means “I will pay for the bill.” If you are at a restaurant and the bill comes to the table, you might just say “I’ll take care of it,” or “I’ll take care of lunch today.” This usage is similar to usage 1.

Collocations: “take care of the [activation/cancellation/replacement/etc.] fee,” “take care of the bill,” “take care of the check,” “take care of the payment,” “take care of the tip,” “take care of the total”

Related phrase: “cover [a bill/a ticket/a financial responsibility]”

Practice: Who took care of the bill the last time you went to a restaurant?

take [something or someone] for granted

idiomatic phrase

1.to not appreciate something or someone properly and assume it or they don’t require gratitude; to not value something or someone properly; to fail to notice or appreciate someone or something that has value

Examples: “They broke up because he took her for granted.”
“A boss should never take their employees for granted.”
“You’re taking your car for granted. You need to take better care of it.”

Common sentence: “Don’t take him/her for granted.” (Don’t assume they will always be there without the proper gratitude and reciprocal care.)

The word “granted” in this idiom means “given,” as in “take someone or something as a given.” This means things and people are freely given and they are expected to do certain things and/or act in certain ways without any expectation of gratitude or appreciation in return.

A romantic partner who expects you to always cook and clean for them, but who never says “thank you” for these things, might be taking you for granted. When we simply expect people to do certain things for us, or when we ignore their physical and emotional needs but still expect them to continue doing things for us, we take them for granted. For example, a friend might tell you the following: “You cooked and cleaned for him, you took care of the kids, and you even bought presents for his parents’ birthdays because he forgot about them, and he still complained and never showed any gratitude. He took you for granted.”

We can take things (both tangible and intangible) for granted as well. For instance: “Don’t take her kindness for granted”’; “You’re taking your car for granted”; “I took my last job for granted.” In all of these cases, the idea of placing value on things and appreciating that value is present.

Usage note: This idiom is often used in the past simple (ex: “I took you for granted”). It is also common to hear it used in warnings and advice. For instance: “You shouldn’t take him for granted”; “Don’t take her for granted”; “You can’t take this opportunity for granted.”

Collocations: “don’t take [someone or something] for granted,” “take an opportunity for granted,” “take it/this/that for granted,” “take one’s job for granted,” “take one’s position for granted,” “take [someone] for granted”

Related phrases: “take advantage of [something or someone],” “take [something or someone] as a given”

Practice: Have you ever felt like someone was taking you for granted?

2.to assume that something is normal, unquestionable, true, or to be expected

Examples: “We take our public transportation system for granted.”
“You have high speed internet. Don’t take it for granted.”
“Don’t take it for granted that you have access to good public education.”

Common sentence: “We take it for granted.” (We simply expect it to be there, to be true, etc.)

Many suburban people take garbage collection and recycling collection services for granted. This means they have become accustomed to them, and they simply expect them to function normally all the time. Suburban home owners also take things like working toilets, electricity, hot water, and indoor heating for granted. This means they expect it because it’s a normal part of their lives. We often take things for granted when they are a frequent and reliable part of our lives. In this way, we perhaps undervalue these things.

To provide another example, people in many developed and developing countries take paved roads for granted. If a road is not smooth, a person might express their displeasure because they simply expect roads to be paved and smooth. Another way to say this is that people “take paved roads as a given.”

If we are debating with someone and we accept that something is true without questioning it, we are taking it for granted. Your debate partner might say something like this: “I think you’re taking for granted that people are rational creatures,” which means “I think you are accepting too easily that people are rational creatures because there is a lot of evidence to suggest this is not the case.” When we make statements in which we simply accept something as true, we are taking that thing for granted.

Collocations: “can’t take it for granted,” “don’t take it for granted,” “shouldn’t take it for granted,” “take everything for granted,” “take it for granted that…,” “take modern life for granted,” “take nothing for granted”

Related phrase: “take [something] as a given”

Practice: What is a modern convenience that you take for granted?

take into account

idiomatic phrase

1.to include in one’s calculation, argument, or decision-making process; to consider someone or something when making a decision
Common variation: “take [something or someone] into account”

Examples: “We need to take into account that our employees might not be happy after we announce these new rules.”
“When purchasing a house, it’s essential to take your salary into account.”
“This public concert proposal does not take weather conditions, traffic, or access to public transportation into account.”

Common sentence: “I’ll take it into account.” (I will include it in my decision-making process. I will take it into consideration.)

When you are making a decision, you need to consider many factors and decide how they will affect the final outcome. For instance: “We want to have a picnic tomorrow, but we need to take the weather into account.” This means we need to consider the weather in our discussion and final decision about the picnic. You can take a person into account, as in this example: “If we invite Marissa to dinner this weekend, we need to take Leo into account. Marissa just broke up with him a couple of days ago.” This means we need to think about Leo and how he would feel if Marissa was at the dinner party.

Grammar note: “Take into account” is often followed by a noun clause, such as a that-clause or a Wh-clause. For example: “The restaurant took into account that not everyone eats meat”; “We have to take into account which project website is best for our purposes.” This phrase can also have a long noun phrase when using the “take something into account” structure, as in “He didn’t take the weight of his luggage into account.” Modal verbs of advice and necessity are also common with this phrase, such as “should take into account,” “have to take into account,” “must take into account,” etc.

Collocations: “take him/her/it into account,” “take into account that…,” “take into account who/what/where/when/why/how/which…”

Related phrase: “take [someone or something] into consideration”

Practice: What factors do you take into account when you are deciding to buy a new phone?

take on [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, separable)

1.to accept a job or responsibility, especially a difficult one
Informal synonym: “tackle [something]”

Examples: “Our team took on a new project last week.”
“I already have a lot of work. I can’t afford to take on any more.”
“You’re taking on too much lately. You need a break.”

Common sentence: “That’s a lot to take on.” (That’s a lot of work to accept and handle. That’s a lot of responsibility.)

This phrase is often used in the context of work, but it can be used in any context where one accepts a lot of responsibility. For example: “You’re doing a master’s degree and working full-time at the same time? That’s a lot to take on.” You can offer to take on a particular job or responsibility as well, as in “I’ll take it on” or “I can take on a little more.” Imagine physically taking work into your hand, placing it onto your back, and carrying it. You can also imagine a pile of responsibilities in front of you that grows as you say “yes” to more work and/or more responsibilities. In both cases, you choose to accept more work and/or more responsibilities.

Collocations: “be a lot to take on,” “be able to take on more,” “can’t afford to take on more,” “not take on more than one can handle,” “take on a lot,” “take on too much”

Practice: Finish the idea: “I don’t have time to take on...”

2.take on [someone]. (transitive, separable) to employ someone; to give someone a job
Similar, see also: “sign on”

Examples: “The company has taken on three new employees this month.”
“We’re taking Derek on starting on Monday.”
“How many more employees can this department afford to take on?”

Common sentence: “They took him/her/them on.” (They gave him/her/them a job. They chose to employ him/her/them.)

“They took me on for the summer” means “They gave me a job for the summer.” Some companies take on extra employees during particular times of the year. For example, in some parts of the world, tax companies take on more employees during tax season, and malls take on more employees during the holidays. You can use this phrase interchangeably with “bring on,” as in “They’re planning to bring on a bunch of new people next week.”

Collocations: “take [someone] on,” “take [someone] on for the summer/the holidays/the fall/etc.,” “take [someone] on part-time/full-time”

Related phrases: “bring [someone] on,” “hire [someone]”

Practice: Has a company ever taken you on for a seasonal job?

3.take on [someone]. (transitive, separable) to fight someone; to play against/versus someone in a sport

Examples: “This weekend, Real Madrid is taking on Barcelona.”
“Hey! Why don’t you take on someone your own size?”
“You think you can take me on? I’d like to see you try.”

Common sentence: “Take on someone your own size.” (Fight someone who is as big as you. Don’t be a bully.)

When discussing sports, the phrase “take on” is a synonym for “play against.” For example, “France took on Croatia in the 2018 World Cup Final” is the same as “France played against Croatia in the 2018 World Cup Final.” This phrase is synonymous with the idea of competition and battle. It can be used in combat sports (“Fighter A is going to take on Fighter B next month”), or to talk about fighting in any scenario. For example, “My brother could take you on” means “My brother could defeat you in a physical fight.”

Grammar note: The verb “take” can be followed by an object to have the same meaning. For example: “Do you think you can take me?” (Do you think you can beat me in a fight?) or “I can take you” (I can beat you in a fight).

Collocations: “take on an opponent,” “take on the challenger,” “take on the champion(s),” “take [someone] on”

Related phrases: “fight against,” “go against,” “play against”

Practice: Name two teams or athletes that recently took on each other.

touch on [something]

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to mention or briefly discuss a particular point or topic

Examples: “I’d like to touch on last month’s sales report.”
“The news report barely touched on the most important part of the story!”
“Could you touch on the allegations of corruption in your department?”

Common sentence: “We touched on a lot of things.” (We discussed a lot of different topics.)

This phrase is often used in professional discussion or writing contexts, but it can be used in any context where you are addressing something. Here is a question a news reporter might ask a public company representative at a press conference: “You touched on the fact that your company lost money last quarter. What is your plan to turn this around?”

This phrase can also be used to talk about books, articles, and essays. For instance: “The book touches on the challenges that immigrants face in America.” This means it briefly addresses this topic. Things that are touched on are not usually explored in detail, but are discussed to some degree. You can indicate that the topic was discussed extensively by saying “It was touched on in detail.”

Collocations: “touch on [something] a little bit,” “touch on [something] briefly,” “touch on [something] extensively,” “touch on [something] in detail,” “touch on the fact that…”

Related phrases: “bring [something] up,” “talk about [something or someone]”

Practice: What topics should parents make sure to touch on when teaching their children about the world?

used to

idiomatic phrase

1.use this phrase when you want to say that something was true in the past, such as a past state, belief, or repeated behaviour, especially to emphasize that something is not true anymore in the present
Do not confuse with, see also: “be used to,” “get used to”

Examples: “We used to love that show when we were kids.”
“I used to believe in the Tooth Fairy.”
“Where did you use to go to school?”

Common sentence: “I used to live there.” (I lived there in the past. I do not live there now.)

Note these examples: “He used to be a veterinarian.” “My mom used to work there.” “I used to want to be a doctor.” “We used to have a dog.” “That store used to be a factory.” “We used to play soccer every day.” “She used to be married.” In all of these cases, something was true in the past, but is no longer true in the present.

Why say “I used to live there” instead of “I lived there”? “Used to” emphasizes that something is not true anymore. In this way, it can have a feeling of remembrance, loss, and/or nostalgia attached to it.

Grammar note: “Used to” only exists in the simple past. For its negative and question forms, remember to remove the “d,” as in “He didn’t use to live here,” and “Where did he use to live?” Finally, always follow “used to” with a base verb, as in “She used to love that movie.”

Usage note: “Used to” and “would” can both be used to talk about past habits and repeated behaviours, but “would” is more common in this context, especially if you are talking about a series of actions that you did on a regular basis. Note the following examples: “When I was a kid, I would always arrive late to school” (This was a habit of mine); “On weekends, I would wake up late, then I would watch TV and play with my friends.” “Used to” is possible in these cases to refer to the fact that this was true in the past, but “would” focuses more on the repetition of the action or behaviour.

Collocations: “used to be,” “used to believe that…,” “used to go,” “used to have,” “used to listen,” “used to live,” “used to love,” “used to play,” “used to think that…,” “used to watch,” “used to work”

Related word: “would” (see usage note)

Practice: Where did you use to go to school?

work on

phrasal verb (transitive, inseparable)

1.to use one’s time, energy, and effort to improve or produce something; to use one’s time, energy, and effort to handle something or someone

Examples: “What are you working on this week?”
“How’s your diet?” “I’m working on it.”
“They’re working hard on improving their public image.”

Common sentence: “I’m working on it.” (I am currently handling it, trying to improve it, or using my time, energy, and effort to try to complete it.)

By using this book, you are working on your English—you are using your time, energy, and effort to improve a skill. You can also use this phrase to mean you are trying to produce something and you are in the middle of using your time, energy, and effort on it. For instance, you can work on a project, a puzzle, a song, a movie, etc. To illustrate this further, note these examples: “Miranda has worked on 3 movies”; “Julian is working on a puzzle with his son.” “We’re working on renovating our kitchen.” Anything that requires your attention and effort to improve, handle, or produce can be worked on.

Usage note: People can work on themselves. This means they try to improve themselves and their habits. A common sentence with this usage is “I’m working on myself right now.” This is a common idea in therapy.

Grammar note: “Work on” can be followed by a gerund (“I’m working on immigrating”), a noun or noun phrase (“She worked on that song”), or a pronoun (“Who worked on it/this/that?”).

Collocations: “work hard on [something],” “work on doing [something] consistently,” “work on improving [something],” “work on oneself”

Practice: What are you working on these days?

PREPOSITION PHRASES

This chapter provides common preposition collocations, meaning common combinations of prepositions and other words. The phrases in this chapter can act in many ways, including as adjectives and adverbs. I have noted these adjectival and adverbial functions below. Both literal and idiomatic examples are included, and all are useful. Make sure to do the practice questions!

as for

prepositional phrase

1.with regard to; in reference to; used for changing the focus of a discussion by introducing a topic that is still related to the previous sentence

Examples: “I had coffee and a muffin for breakfast. As for lunch, I had sushi.”
“Teachers must be present in their classrooms tomorrow morning. As for students, they can choose to study in person or from home.”
“Your mother has forgiven your sister. As for you, you’re in big trouble.”

Common sentence: “As for your question, I don’t have an answer yet.” (With regard to your question, I don’t currently have an answer.)

Use this prepositional phrase to shift the focus of your words. This phrase is typically used to introduce a subject that is related to what has already been said, as in the examples above. Here is another conversational context: “Georgia is waiting for your decision. Also, do you want to go out for lunch later?” “Tell Georgia I will make my decision before the end of the day. As for lunch, yes. I need to get out of this office today.” As you can see, this is a useful phrase when you want to shift the focus of the discussion. See the related phrases for more ways to do this.

Grammar note: This phrase is always used at the beginning of a new sentence and it is always followed by a noun or noun phrase, a comma, and a command, quick answer, or independent clause. Note the punctuation in the examples above.

Collocations: “as for him/her/them,” “as for his/her/their concerns,” “as for his/her/their question(s),” “as for today/tomorrow/yesterday/etc.”

Related phrases: “as far as…goes,” “in/with regard to,” “on the subject of,” “when it comes to [something or someone]”

Practice: Complete the sentence: “Yesterday was great. As for today,...”

at a disadvantage

adjectival or adverbial phrase

1.in a weaker position to succeed
Opposite: “at an advantage” (not as common)

Examples: “His age put him at a disadvantage for the position.”
“She got a red card and put her team at a disadvantage.”
“We’re at a disadvantage. They have a lot more experience.”

Common sentence: “We’re at a disadvantage.” (We are in a weaker position compared to the other side/team/company/etc.)

If someone or something causes you to be in a weaker position, they “put you at a disadvantage.” In a soccer game, if a player receives a red card, they put their team at a disadvantage. You can include statistics in this phrase to specify the degree of the disadvantage. For instance, “They’re at a 10-9 disadvantage,” which means the other team has 10 players while they have 9 players.

Usage note: You can say “We have a disadvantage” instead of “We’re at a disadvantage.”

Collocations: “be at a big/large/small disadvantage,” “be at a disadvantage,” “be at a major/minor disadvantage,” “put [someone] at a disadvantage”

Practice: Think of a time you were at a disadvantage. What happened?

at a glance

adverbial phrase

1.when looked at quickly; with a quick look; with a quick, initial evaluation
Similar, see also: “at first sight”

Examples: “At a glance, the car looks like nothing, but I promise you that it’s reliable.”
“I only saw him at a glance at the event. Was he good-looking?”
“You shouldn’t judge it at a glance. Examine it carefully.”

Common sentence: “I only saw him/her/them/it at a glance.” (I only saw him/her/them/it quickly.)

This phrase is often used at the beginning of a sentence (example 1) or after a proper noun or pronoun (examples 2 and 3). The idea of judging someone or something quickly is the essence of this phrase. A glance is a quick look, so when you judge someone or something “at a glance,” you are judging them with a quick look and not evaluating them in any substantial depth.

Collocations: “at a cursory glance,” “at a quick glance,” “evaluate [something or someone] at a glance,” “identify [something or someone] at a glance,” “judge [something or someone] at a glance,” “see [something or someone] at a glance”

Practice: At a glance, how would you describe the state of your bedroom?

at a loss

adverbial phrase

1.below the profit margin; to sell something but lose money

Examples: “You need to increase the cost. Right now, you’re selling at a loss.”
“They sold their house at a loss. It was in incredibly poor condition.”
“She sold her book at a loss, but it was for a good cause.”

Common sentence: “He/She/They sold it at a loss.” (They lost money. They sold their product below the profit margin.)

If a product sells at a loss, it costs more to make the product than it does to sell it. This means it should cost more if the producer wants to actually make money (or, “make a profit”). The two most common verbs with this phrase are “make” and “sell,” as in “They made that movie at a loss” and “Those dolls sold at a loss.”

Collocations: “build [something] at a loss,” “make [something] at a loss,” “produce [something] at a loss,” “sell [something] at a loss”

Related phrases: “be in the red,” “lose money”

Practice: Have you ever sold something at a loss?

2.adjectival phrase not to know what to say, think, or do next; to be uncertain
Common variations: “at a loss for words,” “at a total/complete loss”

Examples: “Her decision put/left me at a loss for words. I still don’t understand it.”
“I thought I did everything right, but I still failed. I feel at a loss as to what I should do next.”
“I know that was hard to hear. You look like you’re at a loss.”

Common sentence: “I’m at a loss.” (I don’t know what to think, say, or do about this situation, news, etc.)

If a situation leaves you feeling uncertain about your next thought, word, or action, it leaves you at a loss. A person’s words or actions, a major news event, or a really intense experience can leave us at a loss. For example: “When I finished listening to her story, I was at a loss for words.” In this case, the story the person heard was so powerful that it stunned and/or shocked them into silence. If we hear or witness something foolish or incredible, we can also be at a loss. For instance: “You spent $500 on a t-shirt? But…How…I...I’m at a total loss here.”

Collocations: “be at a loss,” “be at a loss as to [what to say/do/etc.],” “feel at a loss,” “leave [someone] at a loss,” “look like you’re at a loss,” “put [someone] at a loss”

Related words: “bemused,” “confused,” “puzzled”

Practice: What is something that left you at a loss for words?

at a moment’s notice

adverbial phrase

1.immediately; with no need for a warning; very quickly

Examples: “They’re really good people. They’ll help you at a moment’s notice.”
“If you need anything, just let me know. I’ll be there at a moment’s notice.”
“Tell me when you’re hungry. I can have dinner ready at a moment’s notice.”

Common sentence: “I can be there at a moment’s notice.” (I can be there almost immediately. Just ask me shortly before you need me.)

This phrase is used to indicate how quickly someone responds to a request. If someone says “I can be there at a moment’s notice,” it means “I can be there very quickly after you ask me.” You need someone to initiate the action. “Notice” refers to how much warning or how much time you give a person to react after you ask them if they can do something. Here is another example: “Don’t trust that phone company. They’ll increase your costs at a moment’s notice.” This means the company won’t give you a lot of warning before they increase your costs.

Usage note: This phrase is often used with the modals “will” and “can,” as in “She’ll be there at a moment’s notice” and “I can be ready at a moment’s notice.”

Collocations: “be ready at a moment’s notice,” “be somewhere at a moment’s notice,” “come at a moment’s notice,” “help at a moment’s notice”

Related phrase: “in an instant”

Practice: Who is someone you would help at a moment’s notice?

at a standstill

adjectival phrase

1.without movement; completely still; stuck; in a position where no one has the advantage

Examples: “I’m going to arrive late today. Traffic is at a standstill.”
“Negotiations between the countries are currently at a standstill.”
“The two teams seem to be at a standstill, as neither side has sustained any offensive pressure.”

Common sentence: “It’s/They’re at a standstill.” (It’s/They’re stuck. There is no movement. No one has an advantage.)

If traffic is at a standstill, it is not moving and no single driver has an advantage over another driver. Common things that can be at a standstill include traffic, negotiations, sports matches, debates, and arguments. This means all sides are in the same position, and they are stuck unless one side concedes their position, or in the case of negotiations and arguments, compromises their position.

Usage note: A common variation of this phrase is “to a standstill,” which is often preceded by the verbs “fight,” “negotiate,” “argue,” or “play,” as in “The teams fought to a standstill,” which means the game ended in a draw. The phrase “in a standstill” is also common, and is usually preceded by the verb “end,” as in “The negotiations ended in a standstill,” which means an agreement was not reached, and neither side wanted to compromise. An argument or negotiation can also “reach a standstill,” which means it arrives at a point where all sides in the negotiation are stuck and do not want to concede or compromise their position.

Collocations: “argue to a standstill,” “be at a standstill,” “end in a standstill,” “fight to a standstill,” “negotiate to a standstill,” “play to standstill,” “reach a standstill”

Practice: When is traffic usually at a standstill in major cities?

at a time

adverbial phrase

1.used for saying how many people or things there are (or are allowed) in one moment, activity, or occasion
Same, see also: “at once,” “at the same time”

Examples: “How many cookies can you fit into your mouth at a time?”
“The bus can transport a maximum of 50 people at a time.”
“Please board the plane one person at a time.”

Common sentence: “One at a time, please.” (Please go in order, one person/thing/question/etc. followed by the next.)

When you eat crackers, if you regularly place three of them into your mouth and then eat them, you eat three crackers at a time. If an elevator can only fit seven people, you can say it can only fit seven people at a time. If a bank doesn’t allow more than 20 people inside its doors, it only allows 20 people at a time. As you can see, this phrase refers to how many things or people there are in one place, moment, activity, or occasion.

We often use this to talk about rules or expectations. For example, if someone finishes giving a presentation and everyone around them starts asking questions at the same time, the presenter might say the following: “I will address your questions one at a time.” This means the presenter will answer the questions in order and only listen to one person before continuing to the next one. They have set the expectations for everyone.

Collocations: “(not) too many at a time,” “(not) too much at a time,” “one/two/three/etc. at a time”

Related word and phrase: “at one time,” “simultaneously”

Practice: How many potato chips (crisps) can you eat at a time?

at all

adverbial phrase

1.in any way or to any degree or quantity; to any degree or quantity greater than zero

Examples: “Did that work?” “Not at all.” (That did nothing.)
“You should call your grandmother. She’s not doing well at all.”
“Who ate all of the popcorn? I had none at all!”

Common sentence: “Not at all.” (This is a standalone response meaning “Not in any way or to any degree or quantity.”)

“I didn’t like that movie at all” means “I liked nothing about that movie.” This phrase is often used in the negative, but it can be used in affirmative sentences as well, such as “Please let me know if you have any questions at all” or “Do you need any help at all?” In these cases, “at all” means “in any way or to any degree or quantity.”

Note the following negative examples for further contexts: “That didn’t help at all”; “That didn’t work at all”; “He was no help at all”; “The job was simple. It was no work at all.”

The phrase “Not at all” is also a response you can give after someone says “Thank you.” This is more common in current and former British English regions. This phrase is also a possible answer to many different scenarios, such as the following: “How was the test? Was it really hard?” “Not at all.” “Not at all” is an emphatic way to say “No” in scenarios such as this.

Collocations: “any at all,” “any help at all,” “any questions at all,” “no effort at all,” “no help at all,” “no work at all,” “none at all,” “not at all”

Practice: Do you own anything that doesn’t work at all anymore?

at all costs

adverbial phrase

1.regardless of the resources required; used when you want to say that something is to be done regardless of the consequences or the damage it may cause

Examples: “We need to finish this project today at all costs.”
“They’ve been winning at all costs lately.”
“At all costs, do not let the boss find out this happened.”

Common sentence: “Win at all costs.” (Find any way to win. Winning is the most important part of this game/competition/etc.)

“Protect the Queen at all costs” means “Use any and all means necessary to protect the Queen regardless of the resources that might be sacrificed to protect her.” A hockey team might want to win at all costs, meaning they are willing to do anything necessary to win.

This phrase is often used in imperatives and when discussing necessity. For instance: “Defeat them at all costs!”; “At all costs, avoid the enemy.”; “We have to do better than them at all costs.” As you can see, there is often an element of competition, battle, or secrecy with this phrase.

Grammar note: While this phrase can come at the beginning of a sentence, it is usually used at the end.

Collocations: “at any cost to myself/yourself/himself/etc.,” “avoid [something or someone] at all costs,” “win at all costs”

Related phrases: “above all else,” “at any cost”

Practice: What is something you would defend or try to protect at all costs?

at all times

adverbial phrase

1.continuously and always; without relaxing your effort or attention for any moment or period of time; without changing or stopping for any reason same in some contexts: “all the time”

Examples: “The prisoner needs to be monitored at all times.”
“Keep your hands where I can see them at all times.”
“You need to be cautious at all times when you’re doing this job.”

Common sentence: “Pay attention at all times.” (Do not relax your level of attention for any moment or period of time. Always pay attention.)

“When I was a kid, my parents knew where I was at all times.” This means my parents always knew where I was. This is the essence of this phrase. If you are referring to or asking for continued attention, then “at all times” is the correct phrase to use. You might see this word on official warnings and instructions, such as the following: “Keep your hands inside the vehicle at all times.” “Keep your helmet on at all times.” “Please keep your luggage with you at all times.” “Keep your seatbelt on at all times.”

Usage note: “At all times” is not a consistent substitute for “all the time” and “always.” For example, saying “I always study in the evening” is not the same as “I study at all times in the evening.” In the first example, the person studies in the evening on a regular basis. This is their usual time to study. However, in the second example, the person studies continuously from the beginning of the evening to the end. In the “always” example, perhaps the person studies for 30 minutes per day, every day. It is unclear. Luckily, “I study at all times in the evening” is not very common at all, as the vast majority of people stick to “I always study in the evening.”

Collocations: “be available at all times,” “be careful at all times,” “keep [something] on/with you at all times,” “pay attention at all times”

Related phrase: “all the time”

Practice: What is something you keep with you at all times, or almost at all times?

at any rate

adverbial phrase

1.in any case or in any event; regardless of anything else; used when you want to change or re-focus the subject, or indicate that what you said or are going to say is true, more precise, or the most important part of your statement
Similar, see also: “anyway”

Examples: “It was too expensive to buy, and at any rate, I didn’t really want it.”
“That’s a big problem. At any rate, what are we going to do about it?”
“Their performance was terrible. I thought so at any rate.”

Common sentence: “I think so at any rate.” (This is my opinion in this case.)

You can put this phrase at the beginning, in the middle, or at the end of a sentence. Just like the word “anyway,” it’s used to add an extra emphasis on a particular part of your statement/thought/etc. For example: “We all agree that this is a really terrible situation. At any rate, what’s our next move?” Here, the person is changing the focus of the conversation. The most important thing they want to say and know is “What’s our next move?” When used in this way, “at any rate” means “I acknowledge everything we have just discussed, but here is what I think is the most important thing.” Here, “at any rate” has the same meaning as “in any case” as a transitional phrase.

Collocations: “(not) want [something] at any rate,” “at any rate, we have to/should/etc.,” “for me/you/etc. at any rate,” “to me/you/etc. at any rate,” “to think so at any rate”

Related phrases: “in any case,” “in any event”

Practice: At any rate, how are you feeling today?

at best

adverbial phrase

1.in the most ideal scenario; in the most ideal circumstances for him/her/it/etc.; in the best possible case
Opposite: “at worst”

Examples: “You think he’s one of the best players in the world? He’s top 30 at best.”
“At best, you can make around $40,000 per year at that company.”
“Let’s be honest. She’s an average writer at best.”

Common sentence: “It’s average at best.” (In the most favourable conditions and in the kindest assessment, it’s not good or bad. It’s average.)

Use this phrase when you want to be honest and a little critical about the limits of something or someone. If you hear a statement about the level of quality of something or someone, but you think the statement rates the thing or person too highly, that’s when you can use “at best.” For example: “You really think that’s his best film? It’s number 5 at best.”

You can also use this phrase to talk about the top limits and most ideal outcomes in a particular case. Note the following examples: “You can make $15 an hour there at best”; “That medication is effective for 4 hours at best”; “That’s a terrible place to work for. At best, you’ll gain some work experience, and at worst, you’ll lose your mind.”

As you can see, when you use “at best,” the focus is on the negative. It means that even the best outcome is still pretty bad.

Collocations: “at most,” “average at best,” “top (rank/number) at best”

Practice: How long can you run for at best?

at face value

adverbial phrase

1.the presented image of something, without asking any questions about it
Common extension: “take [something] at face value”

Examples: “You can’t take what corporations say at face value. They always have a hidden agenda.”
“You can take her words at face value. She’s a very honest person.”
“Her first mistake was taking Max’s promises at face value.”

Common sentence: “Don’t take it at face value.” (Do not simply accept what you have been told or presented. Ask questions because it is possible you are not being told something.)

“Face value” refers to the value printed on something in the financial sector, such as a bond, stock, dollar bill, life insurance policy, etc. However, while it is still used in the financial sector in this way, in everyday language, it is far more commonly used idiomatically.

This phrase is most often used with the verbs “take,” “accept,” and “judge.” If we take someone’s words at face value, it means we take them and accept them without asking any questions. We simply believe them. Here are two examples of this usage: “I accepted their promises at face value. I didn’t think they would lie to me”; “Don’t take his words at face value. He always has a secret agenda.” The face value of something is what you see or what you are presented with. Here is another example: “At face value, this looks like a great deal.” When we take something at face value, it is possible for us to be tricked or lied to.

Collocations: “accept [something or someone] at face value,” “judge [something or someone] at face value,” “take [someone’s] words at face value,” “take [something or someone] at face value,” “take what [someone] says at face value”

Related phrases: “as far as one can see,” “at first sight”

Practice: Whose words do you usually take at face value? (You know this person well and you know you can trust them.)

at fault

adjectival phrase

1.responsible for a bad action or situation; guilty of causing something bad; the thing or person who is to blame

Examples: “The court has ruled that the defendant was not at fault for the damage.”
“The entire house flooded. The inspector said poor plumbing was at fault.”
“You’re not at fault in this situation. Stop blaming yourself.”

Common sentence: “He/She was at fault.” (He/She was the person who did it. He/She was guilty.)

If someone is at fault for doing something, they are the ones you blame for an unpleasant situation. This phrase is often used to discuss accidents and official court cases. For example: “The police haven’t determined who was at fault in the accident.” Here is another possible scenario: “When a relationship ends, it’s usually very difficult to say who is most at fault.” In both situations, we are finding someone to blame. The question “Who is/was at fault?” is common in these cases.

Things can also cause unfortunate circumstances. For instance: “Poor construction was at fault for the building’s collapse”; “A massive storm was at fault for last night’s power outage.”

“At fault” basically answers the question “Who or what caused this situation?”

Collocations: “at fault for (not) [doing something],” “is/are/was (not) at fault”

Related phrase: “be in the wrong,” “be the one/thing to blame”

Practice: Who was at fault in your last verbal argument?

at first sight

adverbial phrase

1.when first seen or discovered
Similar, see also: “at a glance”

Examples: “Do you believe in love at first sight?”
“It’s not wise to judge people at first sight.”
“It seemed great at first sight, but after I took a closer look, I realized there were a lot of problems with it.”

Common sentence: “It was love at first sight.” (They fell in love the first time they met/saw each other. Also: “It was a fairy tale romance.”)

“Love at first sight” is what most people associate with this phrase. This is the idea that you can fall in love with someone the first time you see or meet them. However, “at first sight” can be used in any context that involves seeing or discovering someone or something for the first time. Here are three examples: “The house doesn’t look like much at first sight, but just wait until you see the inside”; “At first sight, it was just like any other school I had ever been to”; “The test was much easier than it appeared at first sight.”

Collocations: “appear [adjective] at first sight,” “love at first sight,” “seem [adjective] at first sight”

Related phrase: “at first glance”

Practice: Do you believe in love at first sight?

at full strength

adjectival or adverbial phrase

1.at maximum strength, full health, or the complete number of people in a group (a team, a work department, an army, etc.)
Opposite: “short-handed” (not having a complete number of people)

Examples: “The doctor told her not to return to work until she was at full strength.”
“Two people are absent. We have to survive without being at full strength.”
“The penalty has expired, and the team is back at full strength.” (Context: a hockey game.)

Common sentence: “He’s/She’s/They’re not at full strength.” (They do not have a complete group, or they are not 100% healthy.)

A soccer team at full strength has 11 players on the field. If you are recovering from an illness, you are not at full strength. Perhaps you are at half strength or only at 30% strength. In both cases, this phrase refers to a person or a group of people not being at maximum strength, either physically or from a numbers perspective.

Collocations: “(not) be at full strength,” “(not) be back at full strength,” “play at full strength”

Related phrases: “return to full strength,” “get back to full strength,” “get to full strength,” “at half strength”

Practice: When do you most often feel like you’re at full strength?

at heart

adverbial phrase

1.the true, core nature of someone; what someone is really like on the inside; someone’s true spirit; the defining feelings a person has in their heart
Do not confuse with, see also: “by heart”

Examples: “I know she seems strict, but she’s a very caring person at heart.”
“He’s a good guy at heart. He just acts childish sometimes.”
“She’s 75 years old, but she’s really young at heart.”

Common sentence: “He’s/She’s/They’re a good person at heart.” (Despite their flaws, their true nature is good.)

Who you are at heart is who you truly are. You can be a kind person at heart, or a vindictive person at heart, for instance. This phrase refers to the core emotions and feelings you hold inside your heart towards the world and the people around you. While this phrase typically refers to someone’s general character, you can use it in specific situations as well. For instance: “He says he’s happy for you, but I can tell that he’s jealous of you at heart.”

Collocations: “a good/bad/etc. person at heart,” “young at heart”

Related phrases: “deep down,” “on the inside”

Practice: What kind of person are you at heart?

at large

adjectival phrase

1.the majority of the group mentioned; most of the group mentioned

Examples: “People at large want to be free to live their lives.”
“The country at large is dissatisfied with the government’s recent acts.”
“The recent tax increases will affect home owners at large.”

Common sentence: “What does this mean for the country at large?” (What does this generally mean for the majority of the country?)

This usage is always preceded by mentioning the particular group of people you are talking about. For example: Canadians at large, the community at large, the population at large, athletes at large, men at large, etc. Use this phrase to speak about groups in general ways. For example: “Americans at large value free speech”; “Our community at large doesn’t feel safe.” The second sentence could be rephrase of “Most of the people in our community don’t feel safe.” You can use this slightly formal phrase to talk generally about any small or large group or unit of people.

Collocations: “citizens at large,” “people at large,” “the community at large,” “the company at large,” “the population at large”

Practice: What is something wanted by your community at large?

2.(referring to dangerous people, criminals, or animals) active in public and not detained by the appropriate officials; free to harm the public or to cause more damage; unrestrained

Examples: “The gunman is still at large, and police are asking the public for help.”
“Please remain inside your homes. A wild lion is at large in the community.”
“The serial killer remained at large for three weeks before he was caught by the police.”

Common sentence: “The man/woman is still at large.” (The dangerous man or woman is still a potential danger to the public, and they have not been found and/or captured by the proper authorities at the time of the report.)

This phrase is most often used in the present simple because it is often used in breaking news reports which refer to someone or something currently dangerous to the public. “At large” usually refers to people, but it can refer to dangerous animals as well. For instance, a news reporter or an official speaking at a press conference might make one of the following statements: “A grizzly bear escaped from the zoo this morning. It is still at large in our community”; “A SWAT team has been called to detain the criminal gang, who are still at large on the east side of the city.”

Collocations: “a criminal at large,” “a man/woman at large,” “be at large,” “remain at large,” “still at large”

Related phrases: “on the run,” “roaming the streets,” “still a danger to the public”

Practice: What would you do if you heard there was a dangerous animal at large in your community?

at least

adverbial phrase

1.at the minimum; not less than a particular number, and maybe more
Common variations: “at the least,” “at the very least”
Same: “no less than”

Examples: “We’ll need at least two weeks to finish this project.”
“It has been raining for at least five hours now.”
“At the very least, you will need $700 to take this trip.”

Common sentence: “I need (a particular number) at least.” (This is the minimum number I need.)

This phrase can come before or after the number mentioned, or at the beginning or the end of a sentence. For example, all of these sentences are possible: “I want at least 5”; “I want 5 at least”; “At least, I want 5.” This is the minimum number you want. You can use this phrase in any context that involves numbers. Here is one more example which shows how you can use this phrase to give a quick answer. “How much more time do you need?” “20 minutes at least.”

Collocations: “a few at least,” “need/want at least [a particular number]”

Practice: For at least how many minutes have you been reading this book?

2.used when you want to focus on something positive despite a bad situation; at the minimum
Common variations: “at the least,” “at the very least”

Examples: “We couldn’t go on our vacation last year because of the pandemic. At least we saved some money.”
“I know you don’t want to have that teacher next year, but at the very least, your two closest friends will be in your class.”
“Our team lost.” “At least you tried.”

Common sentence: “At least now you know.” (Maybe you didn’t get the answer you wanted, or maybe things ended poorly, but now you don’t have to worry about it anymore because knowing the truth is a positive.)

If you tell your friend that you were rejected by your romantic interest, your friend might say, “At least now you know how he/she feels.” This is a good thing! Use this phrase in this way when you want to focus on one positive aspect of a bad situation, and/or to encourage someone to see the positive side of something. For instance: “Sure, the food wasn’t that great, but at least it was cheap.”

Related phrase: “if nothing else”

Practice: Complete the sentence: “It was a bad experience, but at least...”

3.used when you want to place a limit on what you said, or change the focus of what you said
Common variations: “at the least,” “at the very least”
Similar, see also: “anyway,” “at any rate”

Examples: “People’s politics should begin with respect. At least, that’s what I believe.”
“Do you think they’ll win?” “Probably. At least, I want them to.”
“He didn’t see anything, or at the very least, he says he didn’t.”

Common sentence: “At least, I hope so.” (I want my previous statement to be true. I hope it is true.)

Sometimes, we realize we have just said something without fully thinking about it or completely believing it. In these cases, it’s useful for us to focus our words a little more, so people know how we really feel. For instance: “This test will be easy. At least, I hope it will be.” In this sentence, you don’t know if the test will be easy, so you quickly add the second sentence to change or clarify your idea. When used in this way, “at least” often comes at the beginning of a second sentence, but it can be used in other areas as well. For example: “That was a great movie, or at the least, a memorable one.”

Grammar note: In writing, you will find examples of this usage with and without a comma after “at least,” as in “At least I hope so” and “At least, I hope so.” Both forms are acceptable. The comma shows a slight hesitation.

Related phrase: “in any case”

Practice: Use “At least I hope so” after a statement you hope is true.

at length

adverbial phrase

1.in detail; for a long time
Common variation: “at great length”
Similar, see also: “in detail”

Examples: “We discussed our plan at great length.”
“The course explores the history of Victorian literature at length.”
“Lately, your behaviour has been unacceptable. I’d like us to sit down and talk at length about what has been happening with you.”

Common sentence: “We discussed it at length.” (We discussed it in detail.)

This phrase is useful in professional contexts, but can be used in casual situations as well. Here are two examples: “We discussed the future of the company at length”; “They talked about their vacation at length.” In both cases, “at length” refers to a detailed conversation. If you discuss something at length, you discuss it for a long time because you want to explore every area of it, or you want something to be understood or communicated. This phrase is often preceded by verbs such as “debate,” “discuss,” and “talk.”

Collocations: “at great length,” “debate [something] at length,” “discuss [something] at length,” “explore [something] at length,” “go over [something] at length,” “review [something] at length,” “speak/talk [about something/to someone] at length,” “speak/talk at length [about something or someone]”

Related phrases: “in depth,” “in great depth,” “in detail,” “in great detail”

Practice: What is a subject you would like to study at length?

at once

adverbial phrase

1.immediately; as soon and as quickly as possible
Similar, see also: “right away”

Examples: “The teacher asked him a question, and he answered it at once.”
“Go to the principal’s office at once!”
“Why is he with his friends? He’s grounded. Tell him to come home at once.”

Common sentence: “I’ll do it at once.” (I will do it immediately/now.)

This phrase is often used with imperatives, as in “Come here at once,” “Stop what you’re doing at once,” or “Call her at once.” In all of these cases, “at once” means immediately. You can also promise to do something at once (“I’ll be there at once”), or talk about a past immediate decision (“We left the building at once”). Here are two examples using different tenses: “She texted me at once”; “I’m going to leave my house at once.”

Collocations: “come [somewhere] at once,” “do [something] at once,” “go [somewhere] at once,” “tell [someone] [something] at once,” “leave [somewhere] at once”

Related phrases: “as fast as possible,” “as quickly as possible,” “without a second thought”

Practice: Speak a command and end it with “at once.”

2.simultaneously; together
Similar, see also: “at a time,” “at the same time”

Examples: “Only five people can fit at once.”
“Stop! Not all at once!” (Several people are trying to speak simultaneously.)
“I can’t fit everything into my car at once. I’ll need to make two trips.”

Common sentence: “Not all at once.” (Not everyone or everything simultaneously.)

“The store allowed only four people at once.” This means only four people were allowed to be in the store simultaneously. In general, “at a time” and “at the same time” are more common phrases for this usage.

Collocations: “[a particular number] at once,” “(not) all at once,” “(not) everyone at once,” “(not) everything at once”

Related phrase: “at one time”

Practice: How many apps do you usually have open at once?

at one’s discretion

adjectival or adverbial phrase

1.as someone wishes; however/whatever/whichever/whenever/wherever is convenient for someone; it is one’s choice
Similar, see also: “up to you”

Examples: “The amount of money you donate is at your discretion.”
“Tell her she can call me at her discretion.”
“You may use the hotel pool at your discretion.” (Whenever you want.)

Common sentence: “It’s at your discretion.” (It’s your choice/decision.)

Discretion means having the freedom to decide what to do in a particular situation. When you are given a choice or an option that relies on your preference, this choice or option is at your discretion. For example, if you go to a buffet restaurant, the amount of food you eat is at your discretion. If you donate to a charity, the amount of money you give is at your discretion. If you open a bank account and you can take money from it any time you want from a bank machine, bank withdrawal times are at your discretion. In all of these cases, you are using your decision-making freedom.

Note that this phrase is slightly formal and is more often used in official contexts. Saying “It’s up to you” is more common in informal situations.

Usage note: If someone tells you to “use your discretion,” they mean to use your decision-making freedom, but use it carefully or wisely, and in a way that doesn’t announce your decision to everyone.

Collocations: “be at his/her/your/etc. discretion”

Related phrases: “as you please,” “as you wish,” “at your convenience,”
“however/whatever/whichever/whenever/wherever you please,”
“however/whatever/whichever/whenever/wherever you wish,” “up to you,” “use your discretion”

Practice: If it were completely at your discretion and money wasn’t an issue, what kind of job would you choose to have?

at one’s disposal

adjectival or adverbial phrase

1.available and ready to be used; able to be used whenever someone wants, and for whatever purpose someone wants

Examples: “I need to go to the library. Do you have a car at your disposal?”
“He can do whatever he wants. He has billions of dollars at his disposal.”
“The fire station only has two fire trucks at their disposal.”

Common sentence: “I don’t have one at my disposal.” (I don’t have one, or I don’t have one at this moment.)

Billionaires have a lot of money at their disposal. They also have a lot of people at their disposal. Hospitals have doctors and nurses at their disposal. If something or someone is at your disposal, it or they are available and able to be used by you. It’s also possible that you don’t have something at your disposal at a particular moment. For example: “Can we use the map app on your phone to check the directions?” “Sorry, I don’t have my phone at my disposal. I forgot it at home.”

The proximity of an object is another common context of this phrase. For example, if you are on a technical support call, the representative might say, “I’m going to give you a reference number. Do you have a pen or pencil at your disposal?” This means “Is there a pen or pencil near you that you can use?” Finally, someone who is offering to help you with something might say “I am at your disposal,” meaning they are available and ready to help you in any way you wish. As you can see, this slightly formal phrase can be used in a variety of situations.

Collocations: “be at one’s disposal,” “have [something] at one’s disposal”

Related phrase: “at one’s service” (ex: “I am at your service.”)

Practice: What is something you wish you had at your disposal?

at one’s leisure

adjectival or adverbial phrase

1.when one has a convenient time or opportunity; when one has free time; at one’s convenience
Do not confuse with, see also: “at one’s discretion”

Examples: “They’re a bad business. They only help you at their leisure, not when you actually need them.”
“Don’t worry about returning it today. Just give it back at your leisure.”
“The best thing about Netflix is that you can watch things at your leisure.”

Common sentence: “You can do it at your leisure.” (You can do it when it is convenient for you—when you have the time and opportunity.)

If you do something at your leisure, you do it when you have the time to do it, and you do not rush the activity. For example, if a friend lends you a book and they don’t care when you return it to them, they might say “You can give it back at your leisure.” This means you can return it when it is convenient for you—when you have free time and don’t need the book anymore. In this way, the phrase feels similar to “at one’s discretion,” as both focus on personal convenience. However, the focus of “at one’s leisure” is on free time, while the focus of “at one’s discretion” is on any time you want. To clarify this further, if a friend sends you a news article to read, they could say “Read it at your leisure” to mean “Read it when you have free time,” as opposed to “Read it at your discretion,” which means “Read it if and/or when you wish to read it.”

Collocations: “call [someone] at one’s leisure,” “[do something] at one’s leisure,” “read [something] at one’s leisure,” “return [something] at one’s leisure”

Related phrases: “at one’s convenience,” “when one can”

Practice: What is an activity that you enjoy doing at your leisure? This means you do it when you have free time, and you do not rush the activity.

at one’s request

adverbial phrase

1.used for indicating that something is done because someone asks for it

Examples: “I did it at his request.” (I did it because he asked me or told me to do it.)
“Dessert will be served at your request.” (Dessert will be served when you ask for it.)
“We can deliver it at your request.”

Common sentence: “It’s available at your request.” (It is available when you ask for it.)

This is a slightly formal phrase that is common in official contexts and that is particularly useful in professional emails. For example: “At your request, I have attached a list of our services.” When you do something at someone’s request, you do it because they asked for it or perhaps even demanded it.

Collocations: “[do something] at one’s request,” “be ready at one’s request,” “send [something] at one’s request,” “serve [something or someone] at one’s request”

Related phrases: “as per one’s request,” “at one’s discretion,” “on request,” “when(ever) one wants,” “when(ever) one wishes”

Practice: Complete the sentence: “At your request, I...”

at peace

adjectival phrase

1.in a calm and/or peaceful state; free from anxiety, unhappiness, etc.
Common extension: “truly at peace”
Opposite, see also: “at war”

Examples: “The two countries are currently at peace (with one another).”
“I’m glad that’s finished. We can finally feel at peace.”
“The city is not at peace, as violent protests have erupted.”

Common sentence: “I’m at peace.” (I am in a positive mental state, and my personal conditions are satisfactory.)

Imagine a person who is meditating, is in a positive mental state, and is breathing calmly. This is an example of someone who is at peace. When we are at peace, we are calm and we are free from anger, anxiety, and other negative feelings. Your life can be at peace as well if you are free from negative elements. In the context of political relations, two countries can be at peace with one another. A community or a neighbourhood can also be at peace if it is free from crime, danger, and other antagonistic activities.

Collocations: “be (truly) at peace [with someone],” “feel (truly) at peace [with someone]”

Related phrase: “be free from strife,” “be on good terms [with someone]”

Practice: When do you feel most at peace?

2.at peace [with something]. in a state of acceptance; in a state where one does not question a recent decision or reaction, and one does not still feel upset by something that happened

Examples: “Are you at peace with your decision?”
“She is finally at peace with what happened.”
“I can’t believe you just let that happen.” “I’m at peace with it.”

Common sentence: “I’m at peace with it.” (I have accepted it. I do not feel stressed, anxious, guilty, or other negative emotions when I think about it.)

A person can be at peace with a decision. This means they do not feel uncertain, guilty, or anxious about it—they have accepted their decision and feel calm when they think about it. You can say they are satisfied and accepting of the choice they have made. For example: “Ending our friendship wasn’t easy, but I finally feel at peace with it.” This means I have accepted that the friendship is over, and I can think about the decision calmly and with acceptance of it. You can also be at peace with a situation you didn’t cause, as in: “How do you feel about the church’s decision?” “I don’t like it, but I’m at peace with it.”

Collocations: “be at peace [with something],” “feel at peace [with something]”

Related phrase: “make peace [with something],” “come to terms [with something]”

Practice: Are you at peace with all of your life decisions?

3.at peace. dead, especially when one wants to say that a person is not suffering anymore
Common extension: “finally be at peace”
Similar, see also: “pass on”

Examples: “She lived with cancer for 7 years. I’m glad she’s finally at peace.”
“It has been a difficult road, but he will soon be at peace.”
“When his mother died, he was just relieved that she was finally at peace.”

Common sentence: “He’s/She’s/They’re finally at peace.” (They died and they are not suffering anymore.)

This phrase is used to talk about someone’s death. However, it is usually used when their death is a relief to the person’s family and to the person themselves. When a person is at peace, they are not suffering anymore. There is sometimes a feeling of relief when a friend or relative with a condition like cancer dies because they can finally rest and not have to deal with their condition—They can finally be at peace.

Related phrase: “be at rest”

Practice: Have you ever felt relieved that someone you knew was finally at peace?

at random

adverbial phrase

1.randomly; with no warning, preference, order, and/or organization

Examples: “Her ex-boyfriend texted her at random yesterday.”
“The winner will be selected at random.”
“She has started messaging me at random throughout the week.”

Common sentence: “He/She/It was selected at random.” (He/She/It was selected randomly, with no preference shown towards any person or item.)

This phrase is commonly used to talk about how lottery winners are selected. You can also use it in any situation where someone or something is chosen randomly, or in any situation where something happens randomly. Here are two examples: “The teams were selected at random”; “Lately, my computer has been shutting down at random.” As you can see, randomness can refer to how something or someone is chosen, or when something happens.

Collocations: “choose [someone or something] at random,” “do [something] at random,” “select [someone or something] at random,” “start [doing something] at random”

Related phrases: “leave [something] to chance,” “pull a name out of a hat”

Practice: What is something you sometimes do at random (ex: sing, dance)?

at risk

adjectival phrase

1.in a position where something bad could happen; in a vulnerable state
Common extension: “at risk of [something]”

Examples: “This agreement is at risk of cancellation unless your company takes responsibility for their mistakes.” (Also: “at risk of being cancelled.”)
“It seems that he is no longer at risk of contracting the virus.”
“If we don’t update our cyber security, our network will be at higher risk of getting hacked.”

Common sentence: “Are we at risk?” (Are we in a position where something bad might happen to us?)

If you are at risk of something, you are in a vulnerable position—in a place where something unpleasant might happen. For example, a person can be at risk of infection if they cut themselves and don’t clean the wound properly. If you don’t brush your teeth, you are putting yourself at risk of getting cavities or gingivitis. If your computer doesn’t have an antivirus program, it is at risk of being infected by malware.

As you can see, this phrase is often extended into “at risk of,” but it can be used on its own. For example: “A lot of companies in our industry are shutting down because people don’t use these types of products anymore.” “Are we at risk?” In this case, the intended question is “Are we at risk of shutting down?”

Collocations: “at higher risk [of something],” “at lower risk [of something],” “at risk youth/children,” “be at risk of [doing something/something happening],” “put oneself at risk [of doing something/something happening]”

Related phrases: “a risk of” (ex: “There’s a risk of infection”), “in danger”

Practice: What is the best way to ensure your online passwords are not at risk of being discovered?

at the age of

adverbial phrase

1.at a specific age when something occurred; at the time of a particular age
Common variations: “at age (a specific age),” “before/by the age of”

Examples: “He graduated university at the age of 28.”
“I can’t believe you got married at such a young age.” (See related phrases.)
“She got her first job at the age of 15.”

Common sentence: “At the age of (a specific age), I/He/She…” (I did this when I was this age.)

“At the age of 25” is another way of saying “When I am/was 25 years old.” This phrase is usually used to talk about the past. For instance: “She moved out of her parents’ house at the age of 27.” However, it can be used in other tenses as well, as in “If everything goes well, I will have graduated university at the age of 22.” This phrase is often used with the words “by” and “before” as well. For example: “You need to learn to drive by/before the age of 17.”

Usage note: The collocation “at the ripe old age of” is a way of saying “at a very old age.” For example: “She got remarried at the ripe old age of 72.” This collocation is often used humorously.

Grammar note: Do not add the words “years” or “years old” after the numerical age in this phrase. “I read that book at the age of 23.” “I read that book at the age of 23 years old.”

Collocations: “at the ripe old age of [a specific age],” “get married at the age of [a specific age],” “get one’s license at the age of [a specific age],” “graduate at the age of [a specific age],” “leave home at the age of [a specific age],” “move out at the age of [a specific age]”

Related phrases: “at (such) a young age,” “at (such) an advanced age,” “in one’s teens/twenties/thirties/etc.,” “when one was [a specific age]”

Practice: At what age can people get a driver’s license in your country?

at the beginning

adverbial phrase

1.the point where something begins
Common variations: “at the very beginning,” “right at the beginning”
Opposite, see also: “at the end”
Similar: “in the beginning” (See usage note.)

Examples: “They became friends at the very beginning of the school year.”
“At the beginning of the day, I always feel exhausted.”
“Sorry, I wasn’t listening. Do you mind starting again at the beginning?”

Common sentence: “Let’s start at the beginning.” (Let’s start at the beginning point of this story/presentation/debate/etc.)

Because this phrase uses the preposition “at,” it refers to a specific point. In this case, the point in time when something begins, began, or will begin. For example: “At the beginning of an email, you should always greet the receiver.” The variation “right at the beginning” means “immediately at the beginning.” The variation “at the very beginning” means “at point 0,” which means as close to the actual beginning as possible.
