Usage note: This phrase differs slightly from “in the beginning,” which is usually used to show contrast. For example: “In the beginning, we didn’t like each other, but after a couple of weeks, we became friends.” “In the beginning” is also used in creation stories that discuss the beginning of the universe or the beginning of life on earth. For example: “In the beginning, there was only darkness”; “In the beginning, God created the earth.” Even in these usages, you can see that more information is coming which will contrast with these beginnings.

It is important to note that “in the beginning” refers to a range of time near the beginning of something, and “at the beginning” refers to a specific moment. These phrases are often used interchangeably to discuss the beginnings of stories, movies, books, etc. (ex: “at/in the beginning of the movie”). Just remember that “at” refers to a specific point, and “in” refers to a more general period of time. In quick, casual conversation, you can often use either one to discuss the beginnings of stories, movies, etc., but “at” is preferred if to speak about the absolute starting point of a story.

Collocations: “at the beginning of [a specific period],” “at the beginning of the book,” “at the beginning of the day,” “at the beginning of the movie,” “at the beginning of the story,” “at the beginning of the year”

Related phrases: “at the start,” “close to the beginning,” “in the beginning,” “near the beginning”

Practice: What do you usually do at the beginning of your day?

at the end

adverbial phrase

1.the point where something ends (a day, a movie, a conversation, etc.)
Common variations: “at the very end,” “right at the end”
Opposite, see also: “at the beginning”
Do not confuse with, see also: “in the end”

Examples: “She lives at the end of the street.”
“I’m almost at the end of the book.”
“Have you finished the series?” “Not yet, but I’m right at the end.”

Common sentence: “I’m almost at the end.” (I have almost finished the book/movie/series/email/article/game/etc.)

Whether it’s the end of a conversation, a news article, a movie, or anything else, “at the end” refers to the specific point where something finishes. Here are a few examples: “They both work and only see each other at the end of the day”; “At the end of the flight, I felt really dizzy”; “I’m at the very end of the novel. I can’t wait to see how it finishes!” This can also refer to a physical location, as in “The coffee shop is at the end of the street.”

Usage note: “At the end of the day” is an extension of this phrase, and it can refer to the literal end of a person’s day (or their work day, school day, etc.). It can also be used idiomatically to mean “when all things are taken into consideration.” For example: “He’s a terrible employee, but at the end of the day, he’s the boss’ son, so he’s not going to lose his job.” Here is another example: “At the end of the day, you need to decide what is better for you.”

Another idiomatic phrase, “I’m at the end of my rope” means you have no more patience or energy for something. For example: “I’m at the end of my rope with this job! I’m going to quit if things don’t change immediately!”

Collocations: “at the end of [a specific period],” “at the end of the book,” “at the end of the day,” “at the end of the movie,” “at the end of one’s rope,” “at the end of the story,” “at the end of the street,” “at the end of the year”

Related phrases: “close to the end,” “near the end”

Practice: What do you like to do at the end of the day?

at the expense of [something]

adverbial phrase

1.used for saying that something suffers or is harmed because of something else; at the suffering or consequence of something
Common variation: “at [someone’s or something’s] expense”

Examples: “He took the job at the expense of his friendship.” (He lost a friend, or his friendship suffered because he took the job.)
“The new tax plan will benefit the rich at the expense of the poor.”
“Yes, you can take the day off, but it’s going to come at the expense of your paycheck.”

Common sentence: “They’re laughing at your expense.” (They’re laughing at you and think you are a joke. This can be taken seriously or it can be said and received in a humorous way.)

If someone takes a job in another country but loses their boyfriend or girlfriend because of it, they take a new job at the expense of their relationship. This means their relationship is harmed or comes at the cost of the decision to take the new job. This is the idea behind this phrase. You can also use the common variation to talk about the example above in this way: “I took the job at my relationship’s expense.”

You can use this phrase in a self-deprecating way as well. This means you make a joke about yourself. For example: “At the expense of sounding like a fool, I have no idea what you just said.” This means “I know I’m going to sound like a fool when I say this, but I have no idea what you just said.”

Collocations: “at the expense of one’s dignity,” “at the expense of sounding like a fool”

Practice: Has anyone ever laughed at your expense? This means you were the subject of a joke. How did it make you feel?

at the latest

adverbial phrase

1.not later than (a time, a date, etc.); used for referring to a deadline (the time or date when something is due)
Common variation: “at the very latest” (more emphatic)
Opposite: “at the (very) earliest”

Examples: “When are you going to finish that report?” “By Friday at the latest.”
“I’ll call you by 6 o’clock at the very latest.”
“You should arrive by 4:30 at the latest.”

Common sentence: “I’ll let you know by _____ at the latest.” (I will let you know no later than this time.)

This phrase refers to the maximum future time that something is expected to happen, should happen, or is scheduled to happen. It is commonly used in office jobs. Note these common situations: “I’ll let you know by 2 o’clock at the latest”; “We need this done by tomorrow at the latest”; “The meeting will be finished by 11:30 at the latest”; “At the latest, I’ll get you the report by Thursday afternoon.”

Usage note: This phrase usually uses the structure “by a time/date/etc. at the latest.” Note the examples: “Her baby is due by the end of May at the very latest”; “I want to graduate by next fall at the latest.” You can also omit “by” in many cases, especially when you are responding. For example: “When will you be finished?” “7pm at the latest” (or, “At the latest? 7pm”).

The phrase “no later than” (or the more formal “not later than”) is common in these cases as well. For example: “The package should arrive no later than Friday” (or, “by Friday at the latest”).

Collocation: “by (a time/date/season/etc.) at the latest”

Related phrases: “no later than,” “not later than”

Practice: Finish the idea: “On weekends, I wake up at _____ at the latest.”

at the outset

adverbial phrase

1.at the beginning; at the starting point
Similar, see also: “at the beginning”

Examples: “I just want to say this at the outset: We need this project to succeed.”
“At the outset, everything sounded great.”
“If we can’t be honest with each other at the outset, then we probably shouldn’t pursue this relationship any further.”

Common sentence: “We need to know this at the outset.” (We need this information at the beginning—before we continue moving forward.)

This phrase is similar to “at the beginning,” but it’s not regularly used in some of the same contexts. For example, while possible, it’s not common to say “Do you remember what happened at the outset of the book/movie/story/etc.?” Instead, “at the outset” is often used in contexts that could be personal, professional, legal, etc. For instance: “At the outset, people were excited about the baseball game.”

Usage note: The common variation of this phrase, “from the outset,” means that something started and likely continued. For example: “From the outset, they felt they had made a bad decision.” This means they made a bad decision, and they continued feeling that they had made a bad decision. If you’re planning something, it’s common to make your expectations clear from the outset, or to want to know something from the outset. For example: “Before we go any further, I want to make something clear from the outset.” The phrase “from the beginning” has the same usage in this case.

Collocations: “at/from the outset [of something],” “make [something] clear at/from the outset,” “need to know [something] at/from the outset,” “want everyone to know [something] at/from the outset”

Related phrases: “at the beginning,” “from the beginning,” “from the outset”

Practice: At the outset, how did you feel when you bought this book?

at the same time

adverbial phrase

1.simultaneously; together
Common variation: “at the exact same time”
Similar, see also: “at once”

Examples: “Can you pat your head and rub your tummy at the same time?”
“It’s impossible to be in two places at the same time.”
“Xavier can’t handle doing too many things at the same time.”

Common sentence: “I did it at the same time.” (I did one thing while doing another thing simultaneously.)

If you’re a multi-tasker, you can do several things at the same time. This very common phrase can be used in countless contexts. Can you sing your country’s national anthem while jumping up and down at the same time? Is it possible to hold two opposing viewpoints in your mind at the same time? How many pieces of candy can you fit inside your mouth at the same time? If you have a smart phone, it can probably run many apps at the same time. All of these situations refer to doing things simultaneously or together.

Collocations: “[do something] at the same time,” “go at the same time,” “leave at the same time,” “stop at the same time,” “talk at the same time”

Practice: Are you able to focus on multiple tasks at the same time?

2.used when we want to say that something else also needs to be considered (in addition); used when we want to show the complexity of a situation or idea; used when we say something that changes or contradicts what we have previously said

Examples: “I’d love to have a dog, but at the same time, I’d like to travel without worrying about who would take care of my pet.”
“I’m in the mood for pizza. At the same time, I probably shouldn’t eat anything more today.”
“In the job interview, he didn’t want to seem shy, and at the same time, he didn’t want to seem overconfident.”

Common sentence: “At the same time, I’m not really sure.” (I think what I said is probably a good idea, but I’m not 100% certain.)

This phrase is useful when you want to show uncertainty or when you want to highlight the complexity of a situation or decision. For instance: “Nyla wants to date Tim. At the same time, Tim is Nyla’s best friend’s brother, and she doesn’t want to ruin her friendship.” This isn’t an easy decision for Nyla.

Grammar note: This phrase can come at the beginning of a new sentence, but it is also often used in the middle of a sentence after “and” or “but.” See examples 1 and 3 above for this structure.

Related phrases: “be that as it may,” “by the same token,” “even so,” “nevertheless,” “on the other hand”

Practice: What is something you want, but which is complicated by something else? Use “At the same time” to show contrast. For example: “I want more free time. At the same time, I want to make more money.”

at the time

adverbial phrase

1.at the time that is being discussed; during the particular time that is being mentioned
Do not confuse with, see also: “at a time”

Examples: “We went to the same university, but we didn’t know each other at the time.”
“What were you doing at the time of the accident?”
“At the time, no one could predict how much the pandemic would spread.”

Common sentence: “It seemed like a good idea at the time.” (No one knew it was going to be a bad idea. Everyone thought it was a good idea when it was formulated.)

This phrase is most often used in the past. Here are four examples: “I didn’t know about it at the time”; “He wasn’t thinking at the time”; “We hadn’t considered it at the time”; “They had been drinking at the time.” If someone is telling you a story about their past, you might ask them one of these questions: “Where were you living at the time?”; “How old were you at the time?”; “Where were you working at the time?”; “What kind of car did you have at the time?”; “How did you feel at the time?”

Collocations: “at the time of the accident,” “at the time of the crime,” “at the time of the murder,” “did/didn’t [do something] at the time,” “knew [something] at the time,” “was/were (not) [doing something] at the time”

Related phrases: “at that moment,” “at that time,” “during that time of one’s life,” “in the/that moment,” “when one thinks back”

Practice: Think back to 1 hour ago. What were you doing at the time?

at war [with someone or something]

adjectival phrase

1.in a state of war; in a state of conflict
Opposite, see also: “at peace [with someone]”

Examples: “They were at war for more than 10 years.”
“There is a chance that the two countries will soon be at war.”
“They’ve been at war with each other all morning.” (See usage note.)

Common sentence: “They’re at war.” (They’re in a state of armed conflict, or they are in an intense state of conflict or argument.)

When two sides are fighting against one another in armed combat, they are at war. This phrase can also be used idiomatically to mean that two sides are in an intense conflict. For example: “The rebel group is at war with the state government.” Perhaps the rebel group in this case is protesting in the streets or causing damage to government property because they are demanding change and they want to express their displeasure with the government. You can also be at war with yourself if you are attempting to do something or to improve yourself in some way, but you are struggling because of feelings of insecurity or self-sabotaging behaviours. For example: “She’s at war with herself. She could succeed if she believed in herself more.”

Usage note: This phrase can be used in a hyperbolic way to refer to intense arguments. For example: “They’ve been at war in her office all morning.” Also, if you would like to mention why two sides are at war, you can use the word “over,” as in “They’re at war over oil prices,” or “They’re at war over how the company has been treating its employees lately.”

Collocations: “be at war with [someone],” “be at war over [something]”

Related phrases: “be at odds with [someone],” “be prepared for war,” “in conflict,” “ready to fight”

Practice: Do you ever feel like you are at war with yourself?

at will

adverbial phrase

1.used for saying that someone has the ability to do something whenever they want and as much as they want with minimal effort; easily and without significant effort

Examples: “He’s an excellent soccer player. He can score at will.”
“I wish I could travel around the world at will. Unfortunately, I’m not rich.”
“If you had a pool, you could go swimming at will.”

Common sentence: “He/She does it at will.” (He/She does it whenever he/she wants or when he/she is asked to show their ability to others.)

If you can do something at will, you’re probably very talented or in a fortunate position. For example: “You don’t have to ask for permission to use the car. Just take the keys at will.” A very talented student might be able to write a great essay at will. In sports, it sometimes feels like top players can score at will. In all three cases, the freedom and ability to do something whenever a person wants are present—the person just has to decide they want to do something.

If someone asks you to do something that you know how to do, but you can’t do it at any moment, you can answer “I can’t do it at will.” For instance: “Hey, Jack! Show everyone that trick you can do with a ball and a spoon!” “I can’t do it at will. It takes a lot of practice and concentration.”

Finally, if you’re in a position where you are given freedom, you can also use this phrase. For example: “You don’t have to knock on the door every time you come here. You can come and go at will.”

Collocations: “come and go at will,” “do [something] at will,” “score at will,” “stop doing [something] at will”

Related phrases: “on command,” “when one pleases,” “when one wants”

Practice: What is something you can do at will?

at work

adverbial phrase

1.at one’s job; at the place where one works

Examples: “Is your wife home?” “Sorry, she’s at work. May I ask who’s calling?”
“Cleo has always felt weird about using the bathroom at work.”
“Cody isn’t at home. He has been at work all day.”

Common sentence: “I’m at work.” (I am currently at my workplace.)

Usage note: Unlike the word “home,” which can be used in the same way as other adverbs of place such as “here” and “there,” “work” needs the preposition “at.” Note these sentences, all of which are possible: “She’s at work.” “She’s at home.” “She’s home.”

Collocations: “always be at work,” “be at work,” “be at work all day,” “spend (too much) time at work”

Practice: What’s your work schedule? When are you at work?

2.working; to be in the process of working
Common extension: “be at work on [something]”

Examples: “He’s at work on his next movie.”
“I’ve been at work on this project all week.”
“Our team has been hard at work renovating the building.”

Common sentence: “He’s/She’s hard at work.” (He’s/She’s working hard.)

This is an alternate phrase for “working” or “working on,” though it is not used as frequently. For example, “Shane’s at work on his latest article” is equivalent to “Shane’s working on his latest article.”

Grammar note: You can follow this phrase with “on”+a noun or noun phrase, or with a verb+ing. For instance: “We’re at work on a new policy booklet”; “We’re at work writing a new policy booklet.”

Collocations: “be at work on [something],” “be hard at work”

Related phrases: “chip away at [something],” “lose oneself in one’s work,” “be working away on [something]”

Practice: Are you hard at work on anything these days?

by accident

adverbial phrase

1.without intending to do something; not intentionally
Opposite, see also: “on purpose”

Examples: “Oops! I dropped my phone by accident.”
“I think you sent me the wrong email by accident.”
“Are you sure he didn’t do it on purpose?” “No, I think it was by accident.”

Common sentence: “I did it by accident.” (I didn’t intend to it.)

“It did it by accident” means “I didn’t intend to do it.” Something can be an accident, or something can happen by accident. The opposite of doing something by accident is doing something on purpose.

Usage note: Two other ways to say “I did it by accident” are “I didn’t mean to do it,” and “I didn’t do it on purpose.” If we feel really bad about doing something, or we want to emphasize our innocence, we often add “I swear” to these types of sentences. For instance; “It happened by accident. I swear!”; “I swear it happened by accident!”

It is also common to use modals of probability with this phrase, especially “must have” to say you are very certain that something happened by accident. For example: “Where’s your purse?” “On no! I must have left it on the bus by accident!”

Collocations: “call/message [someone] by accident,” “[do something] by accident,” “drop [something] by accident,” “leave [something] by accident”

Related phrases: “not mean to [do something],” “[not do something] on purpose”

Practice: Have you ever left something at a friend’s house by accident?

by all accounts

adverbial phrase

1.according to all of the information that is currently known and/or available; based on what people have said
Common variation: “from all accounts”

Examples: “By all accounts, the community isn’t happy with the new traffic law.”
“Should I buy this pot?” “By all accounts, it’s a good brand, so yes.”
“By all accounts I’ve heard, Sammy is a great boss.”

Common sentence: “By all accounts, it’s supposed to be pretty good.” (Based on everything I have heard, it (the movie, the restaurant, the book, etc.) is supposed to be pretty good.)

An account of something or someone is a statement or general opinion of something or someone. “By all accounts” can therefore be understood as “By all statements and opinions,” or “When we go by all of the information that is available.” Use this phrase when you want to provide a reason for your beliefs, statements, opinions, etc. For example: “By all accounts, it’s going to be a sunny day tomorrow.” This means I have probably checked various weather reports, and all of them have said the same thing.

You can show that you are uncertain of something by saying “by all known accounts,” which leaves room for accounts that are unknown for some reason. You can also show stronger confidence by saying “by all reputable accounts,” which means the sources of the information have a reputation of providing accurate details.

Usage note: “From all accounts” is interchangeable with “by all accounts.”

Collocations: “by all accounts one has heard,” “by all available accounts,” “by all known accounts,” “by all reputable accounts”

Related phrases: “based on what one has heard,” “based on what one knows”

Practice: By all accounts, what is the weather supposed to be like tomorrow?

by all means

adverbial phrase

1.a polite response that gives permission or allows someone to do something
Do not confuse with, see also: “by any means necessary,” “by no means”

Examples: “Could we see the bathroom?” “Yes, by all means!” (Context: Potential home buyers looking at a home.)
“Do you mind if I use this?” “By all means. Here you go.”
“We’d like to see the dessert menu.” “Of course! By all means.”

Common sentence: “By all means.” (Yes. Please do so. I am inviting you or giving you permission.)

This is usually a standalone response, or one that is preceded by “yes.” It is also often followed by an imperative. Here are two examples: “By all means, take a look”; “Is this seat available?” “Yes. By all means, please sit down.” As you can see, saying “by all means” invites someone, allows someone, or gives permission to someone to do something. There is a very open feeling to this phrase, as you are inviting someone to fully accept your “yes.” “By all means” is like saying “Yes, of course. Do what you need or want to do.”

Related phrases: “as you wish,” “be my guest,” “feel free,” “of course”

Practice: In what situations might you hear or use this phrase?

by any means necessary

adverbial phrase

1.by doing whatever is needed to achieve a particular goal or result
Common variation: “by whatever means necessary”
Similar, see also: “at all costs”

Examples: “We need to win by any means necessary.”
“I don’t care how you do it. Finish it this week by any means necessary.”
“You have to survive by any means necessary.”

Common sentence: “By any means necessary.” (This is a response that means “Do what you need to do to achieve the desired result.”)

The noun “means” means “ways.” The phrase “by any means necessary” means to use any ways that are necessary to achieve something. This phrase is usually used with a negative connotation, as “by any means necessary” often means that you have to do something unpleasant, unethical, or immoral to achieve your goal. In this way, it is similar to “at all costs.”

Usage note: You can say “I don’t care what it takes” to mean “I want you to do this by any means necessary.” You can also remove “by” and use this as an imperative: “Use any means necessary.”

Collocations: “do [something] by any means necessary,” “survive by any means necessary,” “win by any means necessary”

Related phrases: “at all costs,” “do whatever it takes,” “no matter the cost”

Practice: If you were homeless, would you try to survive by any means necessary even if it meant stealing from other people?

by chance

adverbial phrase

1.without planning; accidentally or unexpectedly

Examples: “So, just by chance, I saw your sister at the store today.”
“We met by chance at the grocery store.”
“You should go to the party. Who knows? By chance, maybe you’ll meet someone you like.”

Common sentence: “We met by chance.” (We met unexpectedly.)

When something happens by chance, it happens without any planning. Another way to think about this phrase is that it’s similar to “by luck.” However, “chance” refers to the probability of something happening. If you see someone you know in a public place, you might also say, “Wow! What are the chances? I can’t believe you’re here!”

Collocations: “meet by chance,” “run into [someone] by chance,” “see [someone] by chance,” “win by chance”

Related phrases: “by accident,” “by luck”

Practice: What is a useful app or website that you found by chance?

2.perhaps or maybe (polite)
Common extension: “by any chance”

Examples: “Do you have any cash in your wallet by chance?”
“By chance, do you have an extra pencil?”
“Does she by chance need a room?”

Common sentence: “Do you have any extras by chance?” (Do you maybe have any extra ones? I would like one if one is available.)

This usage is for requests, offers, and questions. When you use “by chance” as in the examples above, you are making your inquiry more polite. You are also usually hoping the answer is “yes” because you want something or you want to offer something.

Grammar note: This phrase is not a direct substitution for “perhaps” or “maybe” in declarative sentences. This usage of “by chance” is only possible with requests, offers, and other questions.

Collocations: “have [something] by chance,” “need [something] by chance,” “would like [something] by chance”

Practice: Make a polite question with “by chance.” (Ex: “Do you have the time by chance?”)

by choice

adverbial phrase

1.because one wants to do something, not because one has to; not by force or accident, but intentionally
Similar, see also: “on purpose”

Examples: “No one ever goes there by choice.”
“We live here by choice. No one is forcing us to stay.”
“I didn’t do it accidentally. I did it by choice.”

Common sentence: “I/He/She did it by choice.” (I/He/She did it because they wanted to do it, not because they had to do it.)

This phrase means to do something because you choose to do it. “I married him by choice” means “I chose to marry him because I wanted to, not because I felt pressured to.” People live in places by choice, go to places by choice, and make decisions by choice. For example, if you have the option to take a bus to work but you choose to walk because of the health benefits, you can say “I walk to work by choice.” Outside pressures or feelings of obligation are not part of your decision when you do something by choice.

Collocations: “do [something] by choice,” “go [somewhere] by choice,” “leave [somewhere or someone] by choice,” “live [somewhere] by choice,” “work [somewhere] by choice”

Related phrases: “of one’s own free will,” “of one’s own volition”

Practice: What is something you decided by choice?

by definition

adverbial phrase

1.according to the literal definition; technically
Common variation: “by (the) literal definition”

Examples: “You don’t wear a cape, but by definition, you’re a hero!”
“By definition, this has been the most exciting game I’ve ever seen.”
“Can I get paid?” “You’re a volunteer. By definition, volunteers are unpaid.”

Common sentence: “By definition, yes/no.” (By definition, this is or isn’t the case. For instance: “Do I have to mark these tests?” “By definition, yes. You’re a teacher.”)

We usually use this phrase when we want to clarify or emphasize something. For example: “By definition, Becky is an independent contractor. She is not a full-time employee in our company.” This sentence clarifies and emphasizes Becky’s status at the same time. “By definition” stresses the literal definition of something or someone. Here is one more example: “Dan is a police officer. It doesn’t matter if he’s your friend. By definition, he has to arrest you.”

Related word and phrase: “actually,” “whether you like it or not”

Practice: By definition, do you consider anyone in your life a hero?

by design

adverbial phrase

1.deliberately; as planned; built to work in a specific way; working as intended
Similar, see also: “on purpose”
Opposite, see also: “by accident”

Examples: “Do you think smart phones get slower after 2 years by design?”
“That wasn’t by accident. That was by design.”
“The rich getting richer isn’t an accident—it’s by design. It’s how our economy was built.”

Common sentence: “It’s by design.” (That’s how the product/system/organization/company/etc. is intended to function.)

Before anything is built, it is planned. In the design stages, creators decide how specific things will function. For example, if a car company creates a car with a very loud horn because they want it to be loud, the loud horn is added to the car by design. This phrase is often used to discuss facts, but it is also used to express criticism or cynicism about something, as in example 3 above. Here is another similar example: “Why is my laptop so slow? I’ve only had it for 3 years.” “I think most consumer technology is built to slow down after 2-3 years by design.” This means I think consumer technology companies make their products slow down after 2-3 years on purpose—because they want you to buy a newer model every 2-3 years.

While this phrase is often used to discuss how products and systems are designed to function, it can also be used to talk about personal plans and intentions. For instance: “Did you leave your wallet at my house by accident?” “No, that was by design. I wanted you to find and bring it to me, so I could see you again.”

Collocations: “by nefarious design,” “faulty by design,” “flawed by design,” “(not) meant to last by design,” “strong by design,” “weak by design”

Related phrases: “built to fail,” “built to last,” “planned obsolescence”

Practice: Do you think technology is built to eventually fail by design?

by far

adverbial phrase

1.by a wide margin; by a significant degree or figurative distance

Examples: “This is by far the best pizza I’ve ever had.”
“He is the least popular candidate in this election by far.”
“Mika’s presentation was the most interesting by far.”

Common sentence: “It’s the best one by far.” (It’s the best one by a significant distance/measure/etc. It’s vastly superior to the others.)

This phrase is used to compare and emphasize the great distance in quality, performance, preference, or simply the difference between two people, things, categories, etc. Because of this, it is most often used with comparative and superlative adjectives. For instance: “She’s stronger than her by far” (comparative). “He’s the best player in the league by far” (superlative). “By far” means there is a significant gap in superiority.

Grammar note: For superlatives, you can place “by far” before the adjective or at the end of the statement. Note these examples: “This one is by far the best”; “This one is the best by far.” For comparatives, placing “by far” at the end is preferred. For example: “He’s taller than you by far.”

Usage note: “By far” is also used when we discuss preference. In some cases, comparative and superlative adjectives are not necessary. For example: “This is my favourite restaurant by far!”; “I prefer this one by far.” In these cases, you can also change the placement of “by far,” as in: “This is by far my favourite restaurant,” and “I by far prefer this one.”

Collocations: “better by far,” “one’s favourite by far,” “prefer [something] by far,” “the [superlative adjective] one by far,” “the best (one) by far,” “the worst (one) by far,” “worse by far”

Related phrases: “by a significant degree,” “by a significant margin,” “by and large,” “far and away”

Practice: In your opinion, which food is better than another food by far?

by hand

adverbial phrase

1.done manually by using one’s hand(s) instead of using a machine

Examples: “Did you draw this by hand?”
“Don’t use a computer. I want you to write it by hand with a pen or pencil.”
“My grandmother made this by hand.”

Common sentence: “He/She/They did it by hand.” (He/She/They used their hands to do it. They didn’t use any machines or technological aids.)

This phrase is often used in the context of writing and drawing. However, it can refer to any situation where you do not use technology or other aids to help you do or create something (see example 3). In this case, perhaps your grandmother sewed a hat or made a small clay statue with her hands.

Collocations: “[do something] by hand,” “make [something] by hand”

Practice: Do you know how to make anything by hand?

2.delivered directly to someone without using the post office

Examples: “I’d like you to personally deliver this by hand.”
“I know she got the note because I delivered it by hand.”
“I’m going to give the card to my cousin by hand.”

Common sentence: “I delivered it by hand.” (I delivered it personally and gave it to the person directly. I know the person received it.)

Use this phrase to emphasize when something is personally delivered to someone because you want to make sure they receive it. The two most common verbs with this usage are “deliver” and “give.” If you are the receiver of something, the two most common verbs are “get” and “receive,” as in “I got/received it by hand.”

Collocations: “deliver [something] by hand,” “get [something] by hand,” “give [something] by hand,” “receive [something] by hand”

Practice: What was the last thing you received or delivered by hand?

by heart

adverbial phrase

1.by automatic or instinctive memory; without needing to think about it

Examples: “I know the words to that song by heart.”
“As a kid, did you have to learn the multiplication tables by heart?”
“She knows all of the world’s capital cities by heart.”

Common sentence: “I know it by heart.” (I know it without needing to think about it. I have internalized it and I can recall it quickly and easily.)

If you know something by heart, you know it very well and you don’t have to think about it. For example, if someone asks you “What’s six times six?” and you automatically answer “Thirty-six,” you know the six-times tables by heart. They are committed to your long-term memory and you can recite them automatically. Anything that you have an automatic knowledge of, such as the words to a song, the streets in a city, a secret family recipe, or anything that requires memorization, you know by heart. For example: “I know that poem by heart”; “I can play that song on piano by heart.”

Collocations: “know [something] by heart,” “learn [something] by heart,” “recite [something] by heart,” “sing [something] by heart”

Related phrase: “know [something] like the back of one’s hand”

Practice: What is something you know by heart (a song, a poem, etc.)?

by law

adverbial phrase

1.as stated in official law; by decree of the law; because of the law; in accordance with the legal rules; legally
Common variation: “by the letter of the law”

Examples: “By law, you need to stop at a red light.”
“Dominic can’t go into that bar by law. He’s only 14 years old.”
“By law, I have to report my taxes to the government every year.”

Common sentence: “By law, it’s not allowed.” (It’s not legally permitted.)

Actions are prohibited, permitted, or required by law. This means there is a legal obligation to do (or not do) something in society because of the official legal rules of that society. Here are two examples: “By law, you need to be at least 16 years old to drive in the province”; “You’re not allowed to park there by law.” An interchangeable synonym for this phrase is “legally.”

Collocations: “(not) allow [something] by law,” “(not) allowed by law,” “can’t [do something] by law,” “(not) permit [something] by law,” “(not) permitted by law,” “(not) prohibit [something] by law,” “(not) [prohibited] by law”

Related phrases: “break the law,” “disobey the rules,” “follow the law,” “follow the rules”

Practice: By law, how old do you have to be to drink alcohol in your country? (Or, by law, are you allowed to drink alcohol?)

by leaps and bounds

adverbial phrase

1.with incredible rapidity; to an incredible and easily noticeable degree

Examples: “The city has been expanding by leaps and bounds over the past 3 years.”
“Ruby’s English skills have improved by leaps and bounds.”
“Their company is growing by leaps and bounds right now.”

Common sentence: “It has improved by leaps and bounds.” (It has improved with incredible rapidity and to an easily noticeable degree.)

“Leap” and “bound” are synonyms for the word “jump.” If something happens by leaps and bounds, it happens quickly—to a degree that is very noticeable. This means there are incredible changes in the state of someone or something, so it seems like it or they “jump” from one state to another state. This phrase usually has a positive usage, which is why we usually use verbs like “grow,” “improve,” and “expand” with it. For example: “His singing skills have improved by leaps and bounds”; “Their sales numbers have grown by leaps and bounds.” Because the focus is on how different someone or something is when compared to the past, the verb “change” is also common, as in “His negative attitude is changing by leaps and bounds.”

Negative sentences are also possible with this phrase, though they are not nearly as common. Here are two possible examples: “Their recent performances have disappointed people by leaps and bounds”; “His condition is deteriorating by leaps and bounds.”

Grammar note: The present perfect is very common with this phrase, though it can be used in any tense. For instance: “The city’s road conditions are improving by leaps and bounds”; “If you take this class, your knowledge of the subject is going to increase by leaps and bounds.”

Collocations: “expand by leaps and bounds,” “get better by leaps and bounds,” “grow by leaps and bounds,” “improve by leaps and bounds,” “increase by leaps and bounds,” “progress by leaps and bounds”

Related phrases: “to an impressive degree,” “to an incredible degree”

Practice: Has a city in your area expanded or grown by leaps and bounds?

by mistake

adverbial phrase

1.without meaning to do something; unintentionally; because of a mistake
Similar, see also: “by accident”
Opposite, see also: “on purpose”

Examples: “Oops! I wrote my name on the wrong line by mistake.”
“Did you call the wrong number by mistake?”
“He forgot I was allergic to dairy and ordered ice cream by mistake.”

Common sentence: “He/She/They did it by mistake.” (He/She/They made a mistake. He/She/They didn’t do it intentionally.)

We make mistakes when we are not paying careful attention to something or when we are not fully present in the moment. This phrase is almost the same as “by accident,” as both phrases refer to doing something without the intention of doing it. For example: “Sometimes, I call my son his brother’s name by mistake.” In this case, the person probably wasn’t very present in the moment and paying attention to their words, so they called one of their sons his brother’s name. Here is another example of not paying close attention: “David gave me the wrong phone number by mistake.”

Collocations: “call [someone] by mistake,” “[do something] by mistake,” “forget [something] by mistake,” “give [something to someone] by mistake,” “leave [something] by mistake,” “take [something] by mistake”

Related phrases: “mess up,” “not mean to [do something],” “without meaning to [do something]”

Practice: Have you ever called or messaged the wrong person by mistake?

by no means

adverbial phrase

1.not in any way; not at all; used when you want to emphasize a negation
Common variation: “not by any means”

Examples: “By no means should you trust him.” (You shouldn’t trust him in any way.)
“By no means do I think he’s a bad person.” (I don’t think he’s bad.)
“She’s not an expert on this subject. Not by any means.”

Common sentence: “By no means am I saying that…(+independent clause)” (I am not saying this in any way.)

This formal phrase is useful in professional and official contexts. Note the inversion of the subject and auxiliary verb in examples 1 and 2. This is necessary if “by no means” comes at the beginning of a sentence. Here are two sentences with the same meaning but two different structures: “I am by no means saying that he’s right”; “By no means am I saying that he’s right.” In both cases, a negation is being emphasized.

Grammar note: In tenses that have an auxiliary verb and a main verb, as in the present continuous and present perfect, if you begin the sentence with a subject, place “by no means” between the auxiliary verb and the main verb. Here are two examples: “She is by no means asking you to do it,” which means she isn’t asking you to do it, and “We have by no means broken any laws,” which means we haven’t broken any laws—not in any way.

Collocations: “by no means should I/we/etc.,” “by no means am I saying that [+independent clause],” “by no means do I think that [+independent clause]”

Related phrase: “not in any way, shape, or form”

Practice: Finish the idea: “By no means do I think that...”

by the book

adverbial phrase

1.by strictly following the rules, instructions, or procedures, as they are written

Examples: “Dana is the type of boss who likes doing things by the book.”
“The police have solved the case by going by the book.”
“If you always go by the book, you’ll never be surprised by anything.”

Common sentence: “Let’s go by the book.” (Let’s do things according to the official instructions, rules, policies, etc.)

Every professional company has a book of official procedures. We can consider this “the book” in this phrase. If you go by the book, you follow the official rules and you do not leave any room for exceptions or doing things differently. The most common verb with this phrase is “go,” as in “We should go by the book.” You can also “do things by the book,” as in “Shawn never does things by the book.” In this case, we mean that Shawn does his job how he wants, not by following the official procedures.

Usually, if you don’t want to get into trouble with your boss, it is a good idea to go by the book. However, there are exceptional circumstances when it is a good idea not to go by the book. In any case, we choose to follow or not to follow a particular system.

Collocations: “(not) do things by the book,” “(not) go by the book”

Related phrases: “break the rules,” “follow the rules,” “take/use a systematic approach”

Practice: Do you always do things by the book at work/school?

by then

adverbial phrase

1.by the particular time that is being discussed; before or close to the particular time that is being discussed

Examples: “Can I call you at 6?” “Sure, I’ll be done work by then.”
“Natalie apologized, but by then, it was already too late.”
“Why didn’t you tell me this when you saw me yesterday?” “I hadn’t known by then.” (To refer to a specific time, you can also say “at that time.”)

Common sentence: “Will you be done by then?” (Will you be finished by that time?)

If you do things by a particular time, you do them before or close to that time. The phrase “by then” refers to a particular time in this case. For example: “I had wanted to finish the project by Friday, but I didn’t finish it by then.” Here is another situation: “Clarissa didn’t start writing her essay until 10pm. By then, she was too tired to write anything decent.”

Collocations: “done by then,” “finished by then,” “over by then,” “start by then,” “too late by then”

Related phrases: “about/around then,” “before then,” “by that time”

Practice: Finish the idea: “By then, I’ll be...” (+location or verb+ing)

for all intents and purposes

adverbial phrase

1.in every important way; for all practical purposes; in all ways that matter

Examples: “For all intents and purposes, the two brands are exactly the same.”
“The police have closed the case for all intents and purposes.”
“The movie was, for all intents and purposes, an exact retelling of the book.”

Common sentence: “For all intents and purposes, I agree.” (I might disagree with some aspects, but in every way that matters, I agree.)

Use this phrase to say “practically speaking.” To be more specific, use this phrase when you want to say that even though something is not exactly true or the same, it is close enough to being true or the same. This phrase is often used to say that something can be used in a particular situation even though it’s not the same as something else. For example: “For all intents and purposes, a notebook is just as effective at killing flies as a fly swatter.” In example 2, “for all intents and purposes” is being used to say “Even though there might be some things that haven’t been answered about the case, the police have done everything they could, and the case has been closed.”

Related phrases: “on the whole,” “practically speaking”

Practice: For all intents and purposes, what’s the best way to improve a skill?

for better or worse

adverbial phrase

1.regardless of what happens; accepting the consequences whether they’re good or bad

Examples: “For better or worse, he’s the new CEO of the organization.”
“They’ve been married for 15 years for better or worse.”
“For better or worse, local officials decided to leave the controversial monument in the centre of the city.”

Common sentence: “That’s it, for better or worse.” (We have made the decision, and now we have to deal with the consequences, good or bad.)

When you make a decision that you know has positive and negative outcomes, you make that decision for better or worse. It is common in many marriage ceremonies to say you will stay with your partner for better or worse. This means you will stay with them regardless of the potential outcomes. There is a bittersweet (and sometimes anxious or hesitant) feeling to this phrase because it acknowledges that most things in life have a good side and a bad side.

Collocations: “be married for better or worse,” “decide [something] for better or worse,” “make a decision for better or worse”

Related phrases: “no matter the consequences/outcome,” “regardless of the consequences/outcome”

Practice: For better or worse, who is the head of your country’s government?

for good

adverbial phrase

1.forever and/or permanently
Similar, see also: “once and for all”

Examples: “We need to eliminate this policy for good.”
“Do you think it’s possible to end discrimination for good?”
“Gary quit his job. He’s gone for good.”

Common sentence: “It’s/They’re gone for good.” (It’s/They’re gone forever. It/They will never return.)

This is a versatile phrase, as it can be used with a variety of parts of speech. Here are three examples: “That building is there for good”; “She wants to stay for good”; “The government has voted. This will be our capital city for good.” If something is permanent or forever, it is for good. There can be a sense of dramatic importance to this informal phrase. If you finish paying for a car or a house, for example, you can say “Now it’s mine for good.”

Collocations: “done for good,” “here/there for good,” “finished for good,” “gone for good,” “leave for good,” “together for good”

Related phrases: “forever and always,” “forever and ever,” “here/there to stay,” “now and forever”

Practice: Is there a job you would like to do for good?

for good measure

adverbial phrase

1.in addition to other things; more than is necessary to make something better or more complete; adding a little extra of something because one wants to

Examples: “Here, take a pamphlet. Actually, take two for good measure. One for you and one for a friend.”
“Add some paprika, and if you want, some more salt for good measure.”
“I’ll take that one. And give me that one too for good measure.”

Common sentence: “Take one more for good measure.” (Take an extra one just because you can, or just in case you need it.)

Use this phrase to say something is added to something else, not because it is necessarily needed, but because you want to add it, or because it makes something better or more complete. For example: “I finished watching that series you recommended, and I watched the ‘Making Of’ special for good measure.” In this case, the person watched the ‘Making Of’ special about the series they watched because they enjoyed the series and wanted more of it. Watching the special made the experience more complete. To provide another context, in combat sports, if a fighter such as a boxer or mixed martial artist gives their opponent an extra punch, an announcer might say “And he gives him one more for good measure!”

Collocations: “add [something] for good measure,” “an extra one for good measure,” “one more for good measure,” “take [something] for good measure”

Related phrases: “in addition to,” “on top of [something else]”

Practice: On a long trip, what should you take with you for good measure?

for now

adverbial phrase

1.for the moment; temporarily; for the current period of time until the next event happens or until there are more developments
Similar, see also: “for the time being,” “in the meantime”

Examples: “I need a few more minutes. Could you just wait here for now?”
“Do you want something to drink?” “No, thanks. I’m fine for now.”
“For now, the employees are satisfied with their work conditions.”

Common sentence: “I’m okay/fine/good for now.” (I do not require anything at the moment. I am content with the present state of things.)

Something that is the case “for now” can change in the future. “For now” refers to the present moment, which is always temporary. You can ask someone to do something or to wait somewhere “for now” because what they are waiting for is not ready yet. This can be a doctor’s appointment, a visit to the bank, or anything that requires them to wait for something to happen.

You can also be satisfied with something for now, but future events might change that. Here are two more examples: “I’m happy with my phone for now. It can still run all of my favourite apps” (This will likely change in the future); “They’re getting along for now” (They might not in the future).

Here are a few more common uses: “That’s it for now”; “We’re done for now”; “That’s enough for now”; “I’m good for now”; “It’s fine for now.”

Related phrase: “for the moment”

Practice: Finish the idea: “For now,...” (+declarative or imperative)

for once

adverbial phrase

1.in this case, as opposed to what usually happens; sometimes said when you want someone to do something they don’t usually do, but that you wish they did more often
Common extensions: “for once in one’s life,” “just for once”

Examples: “Clean your room for once!”
“It would be great if she would listen to me for once.”
“I’m happy that you look happy for once.”

Common sentence: “Listen for once.” (Listen to me in this case. I wish you listened more.)

This phrase is often used in imperatives, as in “Listen to me for once,” “Clean your room for once,” or “Smile for once.” In all of these cases, the implied meaning is that these things don’t usually happen. When you ask someone to do something for once, you want them to do it in this particular situation, and you wish they did it more often. In short, “for once” can also mean “one time.”

The common extension “for once in your life” makes this phrase even more dramatic. For instance: “For once in your life, do the right thing!”

Collocations: “be happy for once,” “[do something] for once,” “help for once,” “listen [to someone] for once,” “smile for once”

Related phrase: “just this once”

Practice: Finish the idea: “For once, I wish you would...”

for the most part

adverbial phrase

1.mostly; for the majority of the case; overall; in general

Examples: “For the most part, they had a great fishing trip.”
“We get along well for the most part.”
“I had a good time for the most part.”

Common sentence: “I liked it for the most part.” (Overall, I liked it. This can refer to an experience you had, a book you read, a class you took, etc.)

Use this phrase when you want to say that something is usually the case, or that something is mostly true. For example: “For the most part, kids prefer to play with others than by themselves.” In this example, kids preferring to play with others is usually the case. Here is another example: “It was a good movie for the most part.” Overall, it was a good movie.

Grammar note: Place this phrase at the beginning or at the end of a sentence. Note the punctuation above.

Collocations: “be good for the most part,” “be satisfied for the most part,” “believe [someone or something] for the most part,” “have a good time for the most part,” “like [something] for the most part”

Related phrases: “all things considered,” “all things taken into account,” “everything taken into account,” “on the whole”

Practice: For the most part, is society going in a positive direction?

for the record

adjectival or adverbial phrase

1.for the official knowledge of the public; used when one wants to state their official position, story, or opinion on something
Opposite: “off the record” (not meant for public knowledge)

Examples: “I just want to say for the record that I never supported him.”
“For the record, my favourite sport has always been soccer.”
“Are you willing to say that for the record?”

Common sentence: “For the record, I had no idea.” (I want to officially state that I didn’t know anything about this situation.)

“The record” in this case refers to an official record or document with public statements. In an official capacity, this phrase is used in a court of law. For instance, “Would you please state your name for the record?” is a common question a court official asks a witness, a defendant, or the accused. However, this phrase is also used in casual speech, either seriously or humorously. It is usually used at the beginning of a sentence as if to say “Listen to me. This is what I actually think.”

As mentioned, it can be used humorously, as in “For the record, I’ve always enjoyed your cooking.” You might say this after someone else has criticized your friend’s cooking in a light-hearted manner. If you say something for the record, you don’t mind who knows it because it is your official public position.

Usage note: The related phrase “on the record” is also common. Something can be on the record, be left on the record, or be put on the record. “On record” and “on the record” can often be used interchangeably. A person can also “go on record.” For example: “I’d like to go on record to state that I do not support this decision.”

When we don’t want our words to be public knowledge, we can use the phrase “off the record.” For example: “Off the record, I never thought this was a good idea.” In this case, I always thought the idea was bad, but I don’t want you to report my words to anyone.

Collocation: “state something for the record”

Related phrase: “be on (the) record,” “go on (the) record,” “leave [something] on (the) record,” “off the record,” “put [something] on (the) record,” “set the record straight”

Practice: What is an opinion you are comfortable stating for the record?

for the sake of

prepositional phrase

1.for the purpose of something, or for the benefit of someone or something
Common variation: “for [someone’s] sake,” “for [something’s] sake”

Examples: “For the sake of your health, you should reduce your sugar intake.”
“Tasha took the job for the sake of her children.”
“For the sake of safety, please maintain an appropriate physical distance.”

Common sentence: “For the sake of argument, let’s say you’re right.” (For the purpose of our discussion, let’s imagine your argument is correct. This is usually followed by questions that are used to test the argument.)

The context of a sentence will tell you if it’s for the purpose or the benefit of something or someone. For example: “He did it for the sake of his marriage” means he did it for the benefit of his marriage. In this case, purpose wouldn’t make sense. Here is an example that refers to purpose: “For the sake of being honest, I want to tell you that I was jealous of you.”

“For the sake of argument,” or “For argument’s sake” are two of the most common collocations with this phrase.

Usage note: Here are the two sentences in the explanation above used with the common variations of this phrase: “He did it for his marriage’s sake”; “For honesty’s sake, I want to tell you that I was jealous of you.”

Collocations: “for the sake of argument,” “for the sake of one’s children,” “for the sake of one’s health,” “for the sake of one’s sanity,” “for the sake of safety”

Practice: What do you do for the sake of your health?

for the time being

adverbial phrase

1.temporarily; for now; for the current period of time until the next event happens or until there are more developments; used when one asks someone to do something while they are waiting for something else
Similar, see also: “for now”

Examples: “For the time being, we are just friends.” (This might change in the future.)
“The doctor will see you in 30 minutes. Please wait here for the time being.”
“The patient is resting in her hospital bed for the time being.”

Common sentence: “Wait here for the time being.” (Wait here for now.)

This phrase is synonymous with “for now.” When you do something for the time being, you do it temporarily as you wait for something to happen, to develop, or to become more possible. In some cases, we don’t know if the situation will change in the future. For example: “For the time being, relations between the two countries are peaceful.” This means things could change in the future, but for now, there is peace between the two countries. Example 1 above also shows this usage.

Collocations: “do nothing for the time being,” “wait [somewhere] for the time being”

Related phrases: “in the meantime,” “while one waits”

Practice: For the time being, are you satisfied with your annual salary?

from the ground up

adverbial phrase

1.from the beginning stages until the advanced or current stages

Examples: “They rebuilt the program from the ground up.”
“I’m a beginner. I want to learn everything from the ground up.”
“The two of them built this company from the ground up.”

Common sentence: “They rebuilt it from the ground up.” (They rebuilt it from the beginning; they built it again from nothing.)

When you design a building, you start from the ground and build upward. This is the essence of this phrase. You can build something, learn something, or know something from the ground up. For instance, a computer programmer can develop a new application from the ground up.

If you know something from the ground up, you know it very well. For example: “He knows the history of that company from the ground up.” This means he has a thorough knowledge of the company—from its beginnings until now.

Collocations: “build [something] from the ground up,” “know [something] from the ground up,” “learn [something] from the ground up,” “rebuild [something] from the ground up”

Related phrases: “from head to toe,” “from nothing,” “from scratch”

Practice: What is something you have had to learn from the ground up?

in a sense

adverbial phrase

1.from one perspective or point of view; used when one wants to say that something is partially accurate, especially when one looks at it from a certain perspective (and there are other perspectives/options available)
Common variation: “in [a particular sense],” “in one sense”
Similar, see also: “in a way”

Examples: “Do you mean he’s right?” “In a sense, but not exactly.”
“In a professional sense, she’s a typical lawyer.”
“In a sense, we were like brothers.”

Common sentence: “That’s true in a sense.” (That is true from one point of view. “In a sense” is often interchanged with “in a way” in this sentence.)

Use this phrase to focus on a particular way of thinking or understanding something. “In a sense” refers to a point of view. For example: “In a sense, she’s a perfect mother.” This means that depending on how you define a perfect mother, she is a perfect mother from one point of view.

You can focus this phrase more by adding the type of sense or perspective you mean, as in example 2 above. Here is another example of this usage: “It wasn’t a smart decision in an economic sense.” This means it was not a smart decision from an economic perspective.

Usage note: This phrase is very similar to “in a way,” and is often used interchangeably with it. The subtle difference is that “in a sense” refers to a particular perspective, while “in a way” refers to “in a certain fashion” or “in a certain style.” The difference is subtle, which is why they’re often used interchangeably, as they can be in examples 1 and 3 above. Phrases like “in a political sense” are not interchangeable with “in a political way,” however.

Collocations: “good in a sense,” “right in a sense,” “true in a sense,” “wrong in a sense”

Related phrases: “from one perspective,” “from a certain point of view”

Practice: In a sense, are children the most important people in the world?

in a way

adverbial phrase

1.in a certain style or fashion; in some way; true in some respects
Common variations: “in one way,” “in some way,” “in some ways”
Similar, see also: “in a sense”

Examples: “In a way, you’ve helped us more than anyone else.”
“In a way, this was the best possible outcome.”
“Elvis was the first real music celebrity in a way.”

Common sentence: “In a way, you’re right.” (This is similar to “In a sense, you’re right,” which means you are right from a particular perspective or way of understanding things. It’s believable that you’re right.)

If you want to soften the force of a statement, this can be a useful phrase. For example, if you don’t want to commit to saying “Elvis was the first real music celebrity,” but you want to say that in a particular style he actually was, you can add “in a way” as in example 3 above. This phrase is usually interchangeable with “in a sense.” (Read the usage note for “in a sense” for more details on the subtleties between the two phrases.)

Usage note: “In some way” is often used for desires to be part of something, as in “I’d like to help in some way”; “She wants to contribute in some way”; or “We wish we could be a part of the concert in some way.”

Collocations: “bad in a way,” “good in a way,” “in a certain way,” “in a very real way,” “right in a way,” “true in a way,” “wrong in a way”

Related phrase: “to an extent”

Practice: In a way, which decade has been the most significant?

in addition

adverbial phrase

1.also; as well; besides; used when one wants to add another relevant item to the topic that is being discussed
Common extension: “in addition to [something or someone]”

Examples: “They have a strong wi-fi network. In addition, internet data is unlimited.”
“In addition to all of those features, the car has a remote start option.”
“We will discuss the environmental impact of this decision. In addition, we will propose some solutions to potential problems.”

Common sentence: “Is there anything in addition to that?” (Is there anything else added to that? Is there anything else I need to know about?)

This phrase comes at the beginning of a new sentence and relates to something already discussed. The common extension “in addition to” can come in the middle of a sentence as well. Here are two examples of this usage: “Naomi enjoys doing pilates in addition to judo”; “There is a one-time service charge in addition to the cost of the phone.”

This phrase is commonly used in professional and academic contexts, especially in writing. For example: “Dear Mr. Burns, I have attached a detailed list of our services. In addition, I would like to offer you a free tour of our facilities to help you feel more comfortable with your decision.”

Related phrases: “along with (this/that),” “on top of that”

Practice: In addition to education, what are the benefits of school?

in between

adverbial or prepositional phrase

1.in the time, interval, or space that separates two or more things

Examples: “I go to the gym on Mondays and Fridays, and sometimes in between.”
“A spork is neither a spoon nor a fork. It’s in between.”
“They’re not friends and they’re not lovers. They’re somewhere in between.”

Common sentence: “I’m somewhere in between.” (I’m somewhere in the middle of the categories we are discussing. This could refer to your political alignment or if you are between two opinions on something.)

In speaking, “in between” and “between” are often used interchangeably. However, “in between” is more common when it comes at the end of a sentence and is not followed by an object, as in the examples above. For every other case where there is an object, the preposition “between” is correct because it already means that something is in the space of at least two things. Here are some examples: “I dropped my phone between the wall and the couch”; “He’s between jobs right now”; “What do you do between classes?” As noted, in speaking, many English speakers use “in between” in these cases as well even though it is redundant.

Grammar note: To be safe, use “between” when it is followed by an object, and “in between” when it is at the end of a sentence. You can also use the hyphenated form, “in-between,” as a noun or adjective. For instance: “I know this job isn’t the one you want. It’s just an in-between” (noun). “That’s not the final stop. That’s just an in-between station” (adjective).

Collocations: “check in between,” “look in between,” “someone in between,” “something in between,” “some time in between,” “somewhere in between”

Related phrases: “between a rock and a hard place,” “between friends,” “between jobs,” “between two places,” “in the middle of [something or someone],” “stuck between two places/people/choices/etc.”

Practice: If you travel to work or school, what do you do in the time between leaving your home and arriving at work or school?

in case

adverbial phrase

1.used as a precaution to prepare for a potential scenario or event; in the event that something happens
Common variations: “in case of [something],” “just in case”

Examples: “Bring a bottle of water in case you get thirsty.”
“We’ll ask them again just in case.” (We want to double check to be certain, or because maybe the answer has changed.)
“I’ll wait here in case you need me.”

Common sentence: “Bring/Take it just in case.” (Bring/Take it with you as a precaution. You might need it.)

If you are the type of person who likes to be prepared for multiple scenarios, you probably do a lot of things “just in case.” Here are three more scenarios: “We should bring our umbrellas in case it rains”; “Here is my phone number in case you need to call me”; “In case you have forgotten the rules of the game, here is the instruction manual.” In all of these situations, we do things to prepare for the possibility of something happening.

Usage note: “Just in case” is more delicate than “in case,” and is also commonly used as a standalone phrase. For example: “Why did you give me this?” “Just in case.” The first person might then ask “Just in case of what?”

Grammar note: The variation “in case of” is often used on official signs. For instance: “In case of fire, break glass.” “Press button in case of emergency.” In these two cases, the meanings are “Break the glass in the event of a fire” and “Press this button in the event of an emergency.” Said another way, follow these steps if the stated situations happen.

Collocations: “bring [something] (just) in case,” “[do something] (just) in case,” “(just) in case one forgets,” “(just) in case one needs [something],” “take [something] (just) in case”

Related phrases: “as a precaution,” “as a precautionary measure,” “in the event of [something],” “should something happen”

Practice: If you go out on a hot day, what should you bring just in case?

in charge

adjectival phrase

1.in the position of leadership, command, or responsibility; used to talk about the person who oversees something or someone (or a group of people)
Common extension: “in charge of [something or someone]”

Examples: “Who’s in charge here?” (Who’s the boss here?)
“Sumita, you’ll be in charge of checking everyone’s IDs at the door.”
“It wasn’t his fault. He wasn’t in charge at the time.”

Common sentence: “Who’s in charge?” (Who is the boss? Who is the person leading this or responsible for overseeing this?)

If someone is in charge of a group of employees, they are the person responsible for the employees. If someone walks into an event that is disorganized, they might ask “Who’s is charge of this?” because they want to speak to the person who is responsible for the poor event. You can be in charge of particular jobs or tasks. For instance: “Christian is in charge of distributing the information pamphlets”; “I’d like to be in charge of the drinks for tomorrow’s party.”

Usage note: In formal usage, a person can be someone’s charge. For example, “The boy is my charge” means that I am responsible for the boy. I am likely an important authority figure and/or caretaker in his life. While this usage does not occur with a high degree of frequency, it is possible you might hear it in some older period English films.

Grammar note: Follow the phrase “in charge of” with a noun (“food”), noun phrase (“the marketing department”), pronoun (“them”), or gerund (“advertising”).

Collocations: “in charge of a company,” “in charge of a department,” “in charge of [doing something],” “in charge of overseeing [something]”

Related phrases: “be responsible for [something or someone],” “be tasked with [something]”

Practice: Have you ever been in charge of something or of a group of people?

in common

adverbial phrase

1.sharing a particular characteristic, interest, belief, etc. with someone or something; having the same characteristic as someone or something
Common extensions: “have something in common [with someone or something],” “share something in common [with someone or something]”

Examples: “They broke up because they had nothing in common [with each other].”
“Lucinda also grew up without a mother. We have that in common.”
“Large corporations all share one thing in common: They want to make as much money as possible.”

Common sentence: “We have nothing in common.” (We do not have any similar interests, values, etc.)

If you are friends with someone, you probably have/share some things in common with them. For instance: “She’s my best friend. We have a love of animals in common.” This means we both love animals. You can have physical things in common, such as hair colour, height, etc. For example: “We’re both tall, so we have that in common.” You can also compare two things and discuss how they are alike or different. For example: “Those two tables have a lot in common, but one is much more expensive.”

Usage note: “Have” is the most common verb that is used with this phrase, but “share” is also used. For example: “We have a lot in common!”; “Those books share almost nothing in common.” Quantifiers are very often used with this phrase as well. For example: “We have a lot/a little/nothing/much/very little in common.”

Collocations: “have a lot in common,” “have nothing in common,” “have one thing in common,” “have something in common,” “have very little in common”

Related phrases: “in the same manner,” “to the same degree”

Practice: What do you have in common with members of your family?

in depth

adverbial phrase

1.covering many or all important aspects of a topic; in significant detail
Similar, see also: “in detail”

Examples: “Henry has studied the history of Western philosophy in depth.”
“In next session’s literature class, we will discuss this book in great depth.”
“We have already covered this topic in depth, so there is no need to go back to it.” (We do not have to talk about it again.)

Common sentence: “We talked about it in depth.” (We talked about the majority or all of the most important aspects of the topic.)

When we think of depth, we usually think of a body of water and how far down it goes before our feet touch the bottom. This is a good way to think about this phrase: The deeper you study a topic, the closer you get to the bottom of it. Therefore, if you have a deep understanding or a deep knowledge of a topic, you can discuss it in depth. For instance: “Marita can talk about the history of Italian filmmaking in significant depth. She has studied Italian filmmaking for more than 20 years.”

Usage note: This phrase is similar to and often interchangeable with “in detail,” which focuses on a careful and thorough understanding of something. However, in some situations, one phrase is better than the other. For example, if someone asks you to review a long legal document so that you can understand every aspect of it, it is better to say “Study this in detail” than “Study this in depth.” If you study it in depth, you will still have a strong understanding of the most important parts of the document, but maybe you will not be able to explain every single little aspect of it.

To provide another context, when discussing a painter recreating a classic painting, we cannot say that he or she recreated the painting in depth; it is much more common to say he or she recreated it in detail.

Grammar note: Turn this phrase into an adjective by adding a hyphen. For example: “Sunhee has in-depth knowledge of Korean cooking techniques.”

Collocations: “discuss [something] in depth,” “know [something] in depth,” “study [something] in depth,” “talk about [something] in depth,” “understand [something] in depth”

Related phrases: “have an in-depth knowledge/understanding [of something],” “do a deep dive [on something]”

Practice: What is a subject you would like to understand in great depth?

in detail

adverbial phrase

1.carefully and thoroughly; completely to ensure that nothing is missed and everything is understood; in a way that explains every aspect of something, whether very significant or slightly significant
Similar, see also: “in depth”

Examples: “I want you to review this policy in detail and then explain it to me.”
“The essay answer was poor. The student did not support his position in any significant detail.”
“We discussed it for several hours in painstaking detail.”

Common sentence: “We talked about it in detail.” (We discussed every aspect carefully and thoroughly.)

If you discuss something in detail, you discuss every part of it, and you do it carefully so that the subject is thoroughly understood. You can review a company’s terms and conditions in detail, talk about your childhood in detail, or study a book in detail. A friend can tell you about a recent positive or negative experience in detail. In all of these cases, you make sure to cover every aspect of the subject so you understand it completely and from a variety of perspectives. An artist can also create or recreate something in (great) detail. For example: “She recreated the painting in fantastic detail.” See also: example 3 above.

Usage note: Adjectives are often placed in the middle of this phrase to clarify the type of detail that is used. See the collocations below for some of the most common forms.

The collocation “in painstaking detail” is one of the most common phrases and means to discuss or do something carefully and laboriously. This phrase emphasizes the idea of doing something or talking about something in a disciplined manner without jumping from one point to the next, but by going one small step at a time. It can be difficult and require a lot of personal discipline not to get bored or sloppy. Note the example: “We reviewed the proposal in painstaking detail. We didn’t miss anything.”

Collocations: “discuss in detail,” “in awe-inspiring detail,” “in great detail,” “in incredible detail,” “in painstaking detail,” “in significant detail,” “look at [something] in detail,” “review in detail,” “talk about in detail”

Related phrase: “from top to bottom,” “go into detail [about something],” “cover every aspect [of something]”

Practice: What is something you can discuss in great detail?

in due course

adverbial phrase, formal

1.in the usual or expected amount of time; when the time is right, and not before then

Examples: “Do not stress about it. The baby will come in due course.”
“Please be patient. It will be your turn in due course.”
“In due course, the election results will be revealed.”

Common sentence: “All in due course.” (This will happen when it is supposed to happen or when it usually happens. Just wait because there is nothing you can do to quicken the process at the moment.)

This formal phrase means that something will happen when it is supposed to happen, or when it usually happens. Patience is often associated with this phrase, as it is often used in sentences that require someone to wait for something. For instance: “We apologize for the delay, but rest assured that your package will arrive in due course.” “Course” in this phrase refers to the course or movement of time and events. If something happens “in due course,” it happens when it is due (or meant) to happen.

Collocations: “arrive in due course,” “be ready in due course,” “come in due course,” “happen in due course,” “know [something] in due course,” “reveal [something] in due course”

Related phrases: “in due time,” “in the usual course of events,” “take its course”

Practice: What is something that comes in due course?

in essence

adverbial phrase

1.essentially; basically; in the most general and fundamental way; used to focus a statement on the most important part of what one is saying

Examples: “In essence, we need to think of a backup plan, or this could end really badly for all of us.”
“Mike is, in essence, the most important person on this team.”
“I know this sounds complicated, but in essence, it’s quite simple.”

Common sentence: “In essence, you’re right.” (You are right in the most general and fundamental way, although you might not be 100% right because things are not that simple.)

The essence of something is its spirit—its basic nature. This is the feeling of this phrase. For example: “In essence, Kris is a trustworthy person.” This means that Kris’ true nature is that she is trustworthy. Use this phrase to focus on the main or most important part of what you are saying. For instance: “What you are telling me, in essence, is that our business is going to fail. Is that right?” When using “in essence,” we want to simplify a topic to its most basic and honest nature.

Usage note: If something is “of the essence,” it is of great importance. The most popular sentence with this phrase is “Time is of the essence,” which means we need to hurry because time is very important in this case.

Collocations: “in essence, one believes,” “in essence, one is,” “in essence, one should,” “in essence, one thinks”

Related phrases: “at the end of the day,” “for all intents and purposes,” “when all is said and done,” “when you get right down to it”

Practice: In essence, how would you describe your personal beliefs?

in fact

adverbial phrase

1.actually; in actuality; what is actually the case; used for giving supporting information about something, or to clarify something
Common variations: “in actual fact,” “in point of fact”

Examples: “Sushi is my favourite food. In fact, I eat it at least once per week.”
“These shoes look nice. They would look really good on you in fact.”
“Were you in fact at home during the time of the incident?”

Common sentence: “That’s true in fact.” (That is actually true. This is commonly said to support or confirm what someone has said.)

Use this phrase and its variations to give more detailed information about something. For instance: “Phillip is incredibly helpful.
