In fact, he has been volunteering at the homeless shelter for the past 3 months.” Here, we are providing a factual example of how Phillip is an incredibly helpful person. You can also use “in fact” and its variations to provide a contrast to what has been said before. For example: “We thought the product would be successful. In actual fact, our sales numbers were abysmal.”

Usage note: This phrase is most often used at the beginning of a new sentence, but it can be used at the end or in the middle of a sentence, as shown in examples 2 and 3 above. The variations, “in actual fact” and “in point of fact” are much more common and sound more natural at the beginning of a new sentence. Here are two examples: “He thought he would easily win the competition. In actual fact, he was eliminated in the first round”; “Getting a personal trainer has been the best decision I’ve ever made. In point of fact, I’m healthier than I’ve ever been.”

Collocations: “correct in fact,” “right in fact,” “true in fact,” “wrong in fact”

Related phrases: “as a matter of fact,” “if truth be told,” “in actuality,” “in truth,” “to tell [someone] the truth”

Practice: Finish the idea: “The capital of France isn’t Lyon. In fact,...”

in favour of [something or someone]

prepositional phrase

1.in support of something or someone; on the side of something or someone; for something or someone; to the benefit of something or someone
Common extension: “all in favour of [something or someone]”
Opposite: “against [something or someone]”

Examples: “We’re all in favour of cancelling the meeting.”
“The opposition party is not in favour of increasing public taxes.”
“Are you against or in favour of the death penalty?

Common sentence: “I’m in favour of it.” (I support it. If we are discussing our preferences in this situation, this is the side I prefer.)

This slightly formal phrase is common when discussing preferences. For example: “Janet is in favour of remodeling the office.” This phrase can also mean that something is to one’s benefit. For example: “The judge ruled in favour of the defendant.”

Usage notes: When you ask people to vote on a decision, you can say “All in favour?” and wait for those who agree with you to say “yes.” A common variation of this is “All in favour say ‘I,’” followed by people saying “I” if they support or agree with you.

“Do you support” is another way of saying “Are you in favour of.” For instance, “Are you in favour of strict immigration laws?” is another way of saying “Do you support strict immigration laws?” “In favour of” is often used when discussing important public issues such as capital punishment, abortion, immigration, drug laws, etc.

Collocations: “in favour of capital punishment,” “in favour of democracy,” “in favour of legal abortion,” “in favour of stricter/stronger regulations”

Related phrases: “back [someone or something],” “be behind [someone or something],” “be on [someone’s] side”

Practice: Are you in favour of decriminalizing drugs?

2.to choose something or someone instead of something else; to do one thing to support something else; to the advantage of something or someone

Examples: “Jane quit her job in favour of starting her own business.”
“He got rid of his Android phone in favour of an iPhone.”
“I’m going to get rid of my car in favour of a bike.”

Common sentence: “It was in favour of him/her.” (It was for his/her benefit or advantage.)

This slightly formal usage is not as common as usage 1, but it is possible and you might see it in news articles or hear it on podcasts or in movies. When you do something in favour of something else, the second thing is given precedence or an advantage.

Related phrases: “for [someone’s or something’s] benefit,” “for the benefit of [someone or something]”

Practice: Would you change jobs in favour of more money and stress?

in general

adverb phrase

1.generally; overall; in the overall case
Similar, see also: “for the most part”

Examples: “In general, people are more aware of social injustices these days.”
“I agree with that point, but in general, I don’t support your position.”
“After the latest program update, the user experience is better in general.”

Common sentence: “In general, yes/no.” (When I think about the overall situation, then yes/no. Use this when you don’t want to fully commit to a yes or no answer. Ex: “Do you support strict drug laws?” “In general, no.” In this case, there is room for supporting some aspects of laws against drugs.)

When you are speaking about something in general, you are speaking about its whole or overall nature, not its specific pieces. We all have general feelings about things, and we are able to give our general assessment of a person, thing, or situation. Here are two examples: “In general, Lisa has been doing a great job”; “The country in general is a better place to live in today than it was 3 years ago.” Just because things are positive in general doesn’t mean we should ignore the negatives, but sometimes, it can be helpful to look at the general picture to see the positives, and vice versa.

Usage note: “In general” is usually used at the beginning of a sentence, but can be used in other parts (as in examples 2 and 3). Here are some other examples: “I’m happy in general”; “In general, she’s a good leader”; “The streets feel safer, so in general, the government’s plan has worked.”

Collocations: “bad in general,” “good in general,” “negative in general,” “not in general,” “positive in general”

Related phrases: “everything taken into account,” “in the grand scheme of things,” “on the whole”

Practice: In general, how have you been feeling lately?

in light of

prepositional phrase

1.taking into account; considering; given; in view of

Examples: “In light of safety concerns, we have decided to cancel the parade.”
“We need to reassess our approach in light of recent events.”
“In light of recent findings, the court has reversed its decision.”

Common sentence: “In light of recent events,...” (Because of or considering recent events. This is a common way to start a sentence when we want to state that something has changed or been decided because of something significant that has recently happened.)

Light improves our ability to see something. When you think of this phrase, imagine a light shining on the topic that is being discussed. “In light of” is usually used when something new or recent happens or becomes known (or, when it “comes to light”), but it can be used in a more general way. Here are two examples: “In light of wanting to improve her health, Sydney joined a local basketball league”; “You can’t just discuss this painting by itself. You need to discuss it in light of the painter’s other works.” The first sentence can be re-written as “Considering that Sydney wanted to improve her health, she joined a local basketball league.” The second sentence means you need to consider or take into account the painter’s other works.

Usage note: Something can “come to light,” which means that new information is discovered or becomes known. Note the examples: “New information has come to light”; “It has come to light that the construction company did not finish the job properly”; “We need to be careful. This document can’t come to light.” (It can’t be discovered by others.)

Collocations: “in light of new information,” “in light of public concerns,” “in light of recent events,” “in light of recent findings,” “in light of safety concerns” “in light of the news”

Related phrases: “on account of,” “taking [something] into consideration”

Practice: Have you ever changed your opinion in light of new information?

in line with

prepositional phrase

1.in agreement/accord with; in conformity with; the same as or similar to
Common extension: “more in line with [something or someone]”
Common variation: “get in line with [something or someone]”

Examples: “His vision for the company wasn’t in line with hers.”
“I’m sorry, but this contract isn’t in line with what we discussed.”
“My opinion is in line with yours.”

Common sentence: “It’s in line with my expectations.” (It’s in accord, agreement, or conformity with my expectations.)

When you are in line with someone, you are in agreement or in conformity with them. Note the examples: “Sorry, Tom, but I’m in line with Neil on this issue”; “I voted for him because his politics are more in line with my beliefs.” When you think of this phrase, imagine multiple lines/queues of beliefs, ideas, opinions, people, etc., and you choosing one of the lines instead of the others. Note that in example 2 above, a document can be in line with or not in line with what you discussed with someone. Something can also be in line with or not in line with your hopes, expectations, wishes, etc. For instance: “The apartment looked great in pictures, but when I visited it in person, it wasn’t in line with my expectations” (or, “It wasn’t in line with what I expected”).

To provide one more context, a person’s current behaviour or mood can be in line with their past behaviour or mood. For instance: “Dan’s been really quiet lately. His behaviour hasn’t been in line with how he usually acts.”

Usage note: If you want someone to agree or conform to something, you can use the phrase “get in line with,” as in “You need to get in line with the company rules.”

Collocations: “in line with one’s beliefs,” “in line with one’s expectations,” “in line with one’s opinion,” “in line with one’s politics,” “in line with what one discussed”

Related phrases: “similar to,” “the same as [something or someone]”

Practice: Are your beliefs in line with other members of your family?

in order

adjectival or adverbial phrase

1.organized (in an ordered sequence or in a generally clean and tidy way)
Common variation: “get [something] in order”
Do not confuse with, see also: “in order to”

Examples: “The books are in alphabetical order.” (adjective, modifies “the books”)
“You need to get your room in order.” (adjective, modifies “your room”)
“Please line up in order.” (adverb phrase, modifies the verb “line up”)

Common sentence: “Put it/them in order.” (Tidy it/them or put it/them in an organized sequence. This could refer to organizing documents on your computer, or to organizing books from A to Z on a bookshelf, for instance.)

“Order” refers to organization. Something can be in order or be done in order. If something is in order, it is organized or tidy. If you do something in order, you do it in an ordered sequence. If you need to get something in order, you need to organize it. Here are two examples of this usage: “You need to get your life in order”; “I got my house in order over the weekend.” The first example is a bit dramatic, but it advises someone to organize and improve their messy life. The second example means I cleaned my house over the weekend, and my house is now clean and tidy. If you watch a series of movies in their proper sequence (Superman, Superman 2, Superman 3…), then you watch the movies “in order.”

Usage note: You can specify the type of order things are organized in. For example: “in alphabetical order,” “in numerical order,” “in ascending order,” “in descending order.” You can also specify the type of order by using the phrase “in order from…to.” Here are two examples: “Please line up in order from tallest to shortest”; “The books are in order from A to Z.”

Collocations: “do things in order,” “do [something] in order,” “get [something] in order,” “get things in order,” “in proper order,” “put [something] in order,” “put things in order”

Related phrase: “from A to Z”

Practice: Is your bedroom currently in order? (Meaning: is it neat and tidy?)

in order to

idiomatic phrase

1.so that it is possible to; for the purpose of
Do not confuse with, see also: “in order”

Examples: “We moved here in order to have a better life.”
“In order to get that job, you need at least 2 years of experience.”
“He sold his car in order to have enough money to make a down payment on a house.”

Common sentence: “In order to do that, you need to…” (If you want to do that, you need to do the following.)

If you do something in order to make someone happy, the purpose of your action is someone’s happiness. “In order to” refers to an effect. For example: “In order to travel, you need a passport.” If you have a passport (the cause of travel), you will be able to travel (the effect of having a passport). Another way to understand the usage of this phrase is by asking the question “Why?” after the clause which doesn’t have “in order to.” For instance: “You need a passport.” “Why?” “In order to travel.” Here is another example of this: “She moved back to Brazil.” “Why?” “In order to be closer to her family.”

Collocations: “in order to be successful,” “in order to please [someone],” “in order to win/lose”

Related phrase: “for the purpose of”

Practice: What does a person need to do in order to be successful?

in other words

adverbial phrase

1.said or interpreted in another way

Examples: “He thought the presentation was too slow. In other words, it wasn’t good.”
“I only told him I was worried about his health.” “In other words, you called him fat.”
“The boss expects us to act more professional. In other words, we need to stop joking around so much at the office.”

Common sentence: “In other words, you want me to…” (Said another way, you want me to do something that will be uncomfortable or difficult for me. This usage can also be used to clarify what someone, such as your boss, wants you to do.)

Use this phrase to interpret someone’s words or a situation in another way. Usually, the reinterpretation is much more direct. For instance: “Your mother is not happy with the state of your room. In other words, clean it today or you will not be allowed to see your friends this weekend.” You can use this phrase in a subsequent sentence that you say yourself, or as a response or follow-up statement to what someone else says. For example: “She thought it was strange that you gave her a dozen red roses.” “In other words, she’s not interested in me romantically.” “Bingo!”

Usage note: You can use this phrase to clarify what someone has told you, or to make sure you understand a particular task that has been given to you by someone. For example: “I want you to find other cleaning companies and to tell me how much they charge for their services.” “In other words, you want me to find a cheaper option than the one we have now.” “That’s right.”

Related phrase: “said another way”

Practice: Finish the idea: “Their couch is old. In other words,...”

in person

adverbial phrase

1.in physical presence; to be physically present somewhere, as opposed to being on the television, the radio, or the internet

Examples: “She’s a great singer! I saw her in person several years ago.”
“It looks like an amazing place in pictures, but it’s even better in person.”
“Could we meet in person next week?”

Common sentence: “It’s even better in person.” (It’s more impressive when you actually see it directly in front of you than when you see it in a video or in a picture. This could refer to an experience, a concert, a place, etc.)

If you meet someone in person, you are both bodily (physically) present at the meeting. You can use this phrase to talk about going to concerts and seeing a band or singer in person, as in example 1 above. (You can say “I saw her live” in this case as well.) You can also specify whether you would like to have a work meeting with someone online or in person, as in example 3 above. Example 2 is very common as well, as we often compare how things look in pictures and videos to how they look in person.

Usage note: Use the hyphenated form, “in-person,” as an adjective. For example: “We had an in-person meeting.”

Another common way to say that something looks better in person than in pictures is “Pictures don’t do it justice.”

Collocations: “meet [someone] in person,” “see [someone or something] in person,” “visit [someone or something] in person”

Related phrases: “face to face”

Practice: Have you ever met or seen a celebrity in person?

in real time

adverbial phrase

1.during the actual time that something happens (an event, a process, etc.) (most often used during news broadcasts); instantaneously

Examples: “We are broadcasting the protest in real time. The images you are seeing are happening right now.”
“Their online support is very good. You can chat with someone in real time.”
“We now go live to Sid, who will update us on the situation in real time.”

Common sentence: “It updates in real time.” (It updates instantaneously. This likely refers to a game, a website, or an app.)

If something happens in real time, it happens instantaneously and during the actual time of an event. For example, video footage of a protest that is happening at the moment of recording is happening in real time because it is happening now, and the video is being shared instantaneously with those who are watching it on a television or computer.

News can be reported in real time. Computers can download updates in real time. You can talk with an online customer service representative in real time. A video game can generate a new environment in real time. As you can see, this phrase is often closely connected with technology, but it can be used in other contexts as well. For example: “Learning how to play soccer is very simple, but it’s much more intense when you’re playing in real time.”

Usage note: Use the hyphenated “real-time” for the adjective form. For example: “The app downloads real-time updates when they become available.”

Collocations: “chat in real time,” “report in real time,” “talk with [someone] in real time,” “update in real time”

Practice: When is it important to receive real-time updates of a news story?

in regard to

adverbial phrase

1.regarding; concerning; used to introduce or focus on a topic
Common variation: “with regard to”

Examples: “I’d like to speak with you in regard to your most recent email.”
“In regard to your question, the answer is no.”
“Do you have anything to add in regard to this debate?”

Common sentence: “I am writing in regard to…” (This is the topic I am writing to you about. This structure is commonly used in emails.)

This is a useful phrase in professional and academic contexts, especially in writing. If you have an office job where you need to send emails, this phrase is incredibly common. Here is one possible usage: “Dear Monica, I am writing in regard to your experience with our software.” As noted in the definition, you use “in regard to” to introduce or focus on a topic. Here is another example: “In regard to your recent request, I would be happy to help!”

Usage note: Avoid the common mistake of writing “in regards to,” but know that many people use it. You wouldn’t say “with attentions to,” so you shouldn’t say “in regards to.” The correct phrase is “in regard to.”

Collocations: “call [someone] in regard to,” “contact [someone] in regard to,” “email [someone] in regard to,” “write [to someone] in regard to”

Related words and phrases: “as regards,” “concerning,” “regarding,” “with respect to”

Practice: Finish the idea: “I would like to speak to someone in regard to...”

in spite of

adverbial phrase

1.despite; regardless of; in defiance of

Examples: “In spite of their problems, they stayed together for 30 years.”
“It’s a good car in spite of its age.”
“Sean bought a boat in spite of Kate’s wishes.”

Common sentence: “In spite of everything,...” (Despite everything that happened. Follow this with your opinion or feelings on the matter.)

This phrase means you do or do not do something regardless of a particular situation or person. In other words, the situation or person aren’t important because they are ignored or defied by the subject. For example: “In spite of the bad news, I had a pretty good week.” This means that even though there was bad news, I still had a good week in defiance of or regardless of the bad news. Here is a slightly more complex example: “In spite of everything, I wouldn’t have changed a thing.” This means that regardless of everything that happened, I wouldn’t have changed anything—I accept the good and the bad that happened because it was such a memorable experience.

Grammar note: Follow “in spite of” with a noun (“curiosity”), noun phrase (“the terrible weather”), gerund (“failing”), or object pronoun (“him/her”).

Collocations: “in spite of everything,” “in spite of everything one has been through,” “in spite of the difficulties,” “in spite of him/her/them/etc.,” “in spite of one’s age,” “in spite of one’s wishes,” “in spite of the weather”

Related phrases: “in defiance of,” “in the face of,” “regardless of”

Practice: In spite of everything, how do you feel about your city/town?

in terms of

adverbial phrase

1.as it pertains to; as it relates to; in relation to; with regard to; used to focus on a particular aspect of a topic you are discussing, or from which point of view you are discussing it
Similar, see also: “in a sense” (see usage note)

Examples: “Greg thinks of everything in terms of money.”
“You have to think about this decision in terms of personal security.”
“In terms of our business relationship, our side has been quite satisfied.”

Common sentence: “Don’t think about it terms of…” (Do not think about it from the perspective of money, personal discomfort, difficulty, right and wrong, how it will impact you personally, etc.)

This phrase focuses on how things relate or pertain to something. For example: “Don’t think about this decision in terms of money. Think about it in terms of improving your child’s future.” This means not to think about the decision and how it relates or pertains to money, but to think about how it relates or pertains to your child’s future. Here is another example: “How are you doing in terms of your physical health?” This question is asking how you are doing, but it is specifically interested in your physical health. In this way, this phrase also focuses the perspective of a statement.

Usage note: A variation of this phrase, “in (adjective) terms,” allows you to be more specific about which aspect or point of view you are discussing. For example, you can discuss something in economic terms, in political terms, in financial terms, in philosophical terms, in historical terms, etc. Here is an example of this usage: “The philosopher Socrates had a significant impact on the minds of the people around him, not just in philosophical terms, but in political terms as well.” See the entry on “in a sense” for related usage (“in a philosophical sense,” “in a political sense,” etc.).

Collocations: “in terms of believability,” “in terms of economics,” “in terms of money,” “in terms of politics,” “in terms of public safety”

Practice: How are you doing in terms of physical health?

in the end

adverbial phrase

1.in the final moment of decision or in the final result; in the final estimation or outcome; after everything/all; eventually; after everything is considered or decided, or after everything happens
Do not confuse with: “at the end” (see usage note)
See also: “at the beginning”

Examples: “People will always demand your time and energy, but in the end, you have to decide what is best for yourself.”
“We thought about going to see a movie, but in the end, we decided to stay at home because of the weather.”
“We had many paint colours to choose from. We chose grey in the end.”

Common sentence: “It turned out well in the end.” (After everything happened or was decided, the final result was positive. This could refer to almost anything, such as an event, a project, an essay, etc.)

This phrase is often used to discuss final decisions, opinions, happenings, results, or estimations. In short, final outcomes. Here are two examples: “I wanted to play Monopoly, but my sister wanted to play chess. In the end, we played chess” (final decision); “In the end, he was the best friend I had ever had” (in the final result or personal estimation). Two very common sentences with this phrase are “It is/was (not) worth it in the end” and “It doesn’t/didn’t matter in the end.” If something is “worth it,” it is beneficial or positive enough that you are willing to spend a certain amount of time, money, energy, etc., on it.

Usage note: This phrase is sometimes confused with “at the end.” Note the differences in meaning and usage:

“in the end” = after everything happens or is decided; in the final outcome or estimation

“at the end” = at the final point or moment of something; in the final moments

Note these examples for “at the end”: “The main character dies at the end (of the book/movie/story/etc.)”; “I quit my job at the end of high school”; “They live at the end of the street”; “We’re almost at the end of the episode. There are only 5 minutes left.”

In some cases, both are possible but there is a subtle difference in meaning. For example, imagine you are discussing the death of a character in a book or film. The sentences “He dies at the end” and “He dies in the end” have slightly different meanings. “He dies at the end” means he dies in the final moments of the story—not at the beginning or in the middle, but at the end. “He dies in the end” means he dies after everything happens. This is the final outcome for the character. There were other possible outcomes, but this is the one the author chose in the end—after considering all available options.

Collocations: “be worth it in the end,” “decide to [do something] in the end,” “not matter in the end,” “turn out well/poorly in the end”

Related phrases: “after all of that,” “after everything,” “at last”

Practice: In the end, what did you decide to do last night?

in the first place

adverbial phrase

1.used to say that something was true or should have been done at the starting point of a situation; originally
Similar, see also: “at/from the beginning,” “at/from the outset”

Examples: “Why didn’t you tell me that in the first place?” (I wish you had told me that at the beginning of this situation/conversation/etc.)
“I didn’t want to do it in the first place.” (I never wanted to do it.)
“We should have done that in the first place!” (This is the first thing we should have done.)

Common sentence: “I wish I had known that in the first place.” (I wish I had had this information when we started this, when this situation began, etc. Grammar side note: Use the past perfect (had+past participle) for past wishes, as in “I wish you had told me that in the first place.”)

As you can see from the examples above, this phrase is often used to look into the past and express some level of dissatisfaction or annoyance. However, this isn’t its only application. Here are two neutral examples: “In the first place, we need to decide who is going to work on this”; “In the first place, we haven’t been fair to our partners.” In these contexts, “in the first place” is simply used as a replacement for the word “first.” When used in this way, the phrase is usually put at the beginning of a sentence.

Collocations: “decide [something] in the first place,” “do [something] in the first place,” “say [something] in the first place,” “talk about [something] in the first place,” “tell [someone something] in the first place,” “want [something] in the first place”

Related phrases: “before [something] gets going/started,” “from the start”

Practice: Why did you decide to study with this book in the first place?

in the meantime

adverbial phrase

1.in the period between two events, decisions, etc.; meanwhile
Common informal variation: “in the meantime and in between time”
Similar, see also: “for the time being”

Examples: “Don’t worry about me. I’ll just wait here and check my email in the meantime.” (Context: waiting for someone to see you.)
“Lunch will be ready in 20 minutes. I’ll wash the dishes in the meantime.”
“Sorry, I need another 15 minutes to get my kids ready for bed. In the meantime, feel free to relax on the couch and watch some TV.”

Common sentence: “What are you going to do in the meantime?” (What are you going to do while you wait for this event, process, person, etc.?)

“While one waits” is a good way to explain and understand this idiomatic phrase. The “meantime” in this phrase refers to the time between two things. For example: “We won’t know the results until tomorrow. In the meantime, we can just hope for the best.” In this situation, we are waiting for the results of something (a test, a medical examination, etc.). While we wait for the results, the only thing we can do is hope for the best possible outcome between now and then.

Usage note: The extension “in the meantime and in between time” is only used in casual speech. For instance: “I’ll be waiting for you in the meantime and in between time.” People sometimes use this extension to mean “at some point in the middle.” The most common sentence with this usage is “I’ll see you in the meantime and in between time,” which means I will see you between now and some point in the future.

Collocations: “[do something] in the meantime,” “wait somewhere in the meantime”

Related phrases: “for now,” “while one waits”

Practice: Finish the idea: “I’ll be ready in 5 minutes. In the meantime,...”

in time

adverbial phrase

1.before or at a specific time; early enough; sufficiently early
Common extension: “just in time”
Common variations: “in time for [something],” “in time to [do something]”
Do not confuse with, see also: “on time”

Examples: “I need to leave for a few minutes, but I should be back in time for dessert.”
“Joanna! You’ve arrived just in time for Robert’s speech!”
“Did I arrive in time?” “For what?” “For the fireworks.” “No, they’re finished.”

Common sentence: “Just in time!” (Something happened or someone arrived at the exact moment I wanted it/them or needed it/them.)

You arrive in time for something, to experience or to do something. Here are three examples: “We arrived in time to see the couple exchange their wedding vows” (We missed the beginning of the wedding); “I need to use the bathroom. I’ll be back in time for the start of the movie” (I will return early enough so I do not miss the start of the movie); “They were late to the game, but they arrived just in time to see the winning goal” (They missed the majority of the game, but arrived early enough to see the winning goal).

Usage note: If you finish something “just in time,” you finish it shortly before or exactly when it is needed. You can also arrive exactly when you are needed for something. For instance: “Derek! Just in time! This customer has a question that only you can answer.” The sentence “You’re just in time!” is also common and means “You have arrived at the right moment!” Perhaps the person who says this wants or needs you for something, or you have arrived at the moment something important is going to begin or happen. For instance, if your friend invited you to watch a televised baseball game at their house and you arrive 2 minutes before the start of the game, your friend could say “You’re just in time!” (for the start of the game)

Collocations: “(just) in time for breakfast/lunch/dinner,” “(just) in time for the beginning/the start,” “(just) in time to do/see/experience [something],” “arrive (just) in time,” “make it (just) in time”

Related phrases: “(just) in the nick of time,” “almost too late,” “not a moment too soon,” “under the wire”

Practice: How early should you arrive at a movie theatre to ensure that you are in time for the start of the movie?

on a roll

adjectival phrase

1.having a sequence of good luck, positive results, correct answers, forward momentum, etc.; used when someone is having continued success and is likely to continue having success in the future
Similar, see also: “on fire”

Examples: “You’re on a roll. You’ve answered 20 questions correctly in a row.”
“Sorry, but I can’t meet with you today. I’m on a roll with this work project right now, and I just want to keep going.”
“Wow. She’s on a roll. She has scored 6 consecutive baskets!” (Context: a basketball game.)

Common sentence: “I’m/He’s/She’s on a roll.” (I’m/He’s/She’s having consistent success right now and it is likely that this success will continue.)

When you are on a roll, you do not want to stop doing what you are doing because you have positive momentum. If someone goes to a casino and wins many games of blackjack (a card game) consecutively (or, “in a row”), they might say “I’m on a roll!” In this case, the person should probably quit when they have a certain level of success, but there are times when you just want to keep going. For example, if you are working on something, such as a personal project, and you are making good progress on it, you feel good while doing it, and you do not want to stop doing it, you are on a roll (or “rolling). With that in mind, this phrase has two main meanings:

I) having a sequence of individual successes (such as scoring consecutive baskets in a basketball game or winning consecutive games)

II) having a period of time when you are making significant progress on something and you do not want to stop working on it

Usage note: A person who is “on a roll” is also often “in the zone,” which refers to a mental (or perhaps spiritual) state in which you are at the peak of your performance and you feel like you are difficult or impossible to stop. Athletes, writers, painters, and anyone who is engaged in a creative or personally satisfying activity can reach or be “in the zone.” This means they are fully engaged with their work/activity/etc.

Collocations: “be on a roll,” “stay on a roll”

Related phrases: “have a string of good luck,” “have everything work out for [someone],” “in the zone,” “on a hot streak,” “on a lucky streak”

Practice: When was the last time you felt like you were on a roll?

on account of

prepositional phrase

1.because of; due to; used when you want to introduce the cause of or the reason for something

Examples: “The football game was cancelled on account of rain.”
“On account of public complaints, we have decided to delay the event.”
“The photographer went to the store on account of needing a new camera.”

Common sentence: “On account of the weather.” (Because of the weather. This is a quick response to someone asking why something was cancelled.)

This slightly formal phrase is used as a replacement for “because of,” “as a result of,” and “due to,” usually in official contexts, as in example 2 above. Here are three more examples: “On account of your contributions to the company, we are willing to offer you a generous severance package”; “We left the store on account of feeling disrespected by the staff”; “On account of your unique situation, the court has decided to drop the charges.”

Collocations: “on account of certain conditions,” “on account of the weather,” “on account of one’s situation”

Related phrases: “as a result of,” “in view of,” “owing to,” “thanks to”

Practice: Have you ever cancelled your plans on account of the weather?

on behalf of

prepositional phrase

1. as a representative of; speaking for [someone, a group, a company, etc.]
Common variation: “on one’s behalf”

Examples: “On behalf of everyone here, I want to welcome you to our family.”
“I think what Brent means is…” “I don’t need anyone to speak on my behalf.”
“Marko couldn’t be here today, so I will lead the meeting on his behalf.”

Common sentence: “I’m calling on behalf of [the name of a person or company].” (I’m calling as a representative of this person or company.)

You usually do or say something on behalf of someone or a group of people. This phrase is often used with the verbs “be” and “speak.” Here are two examples: “He wasn’t there, so I spoke on behalf of him”; “I am here on behalf of my brother.” The phrase “on behalf of” is often changed to “on one’s behalf.” The prior examples can also be said like this: “He wasn’t there, so I spoke on his behalf.” “I am here on my brother’s behalf.” In all of these cases, someone is representing someone and speaking for someone.

Sometimes, people get sick or are not able to be present at an event, so someone must represent them, usually in an official capacity. Employees also work for and represent their companies. Here are two examples of the mentioned contexts: “Dina isn’t feeling well this morning, so I’m here on her behalf”; “Good morning, sir. I am calling you on behalf of Green Financial Services. Do you have a minute to speak with me today?”

Usage note: When someone is making a public speech that is honouring someone or a group of people, the phrase “On behalf of everyone here” is very common. For example: “On behalf of everyone here, I would like to say ‘thank you’ for everything you have done for this community.” This is common at weddings, birthday parties, ceremonies, etc.

Collocations: “be [somewhere] on [someone’s] behalf,” “on behalf of all [group name: mothers, employees, etc.],” “on behalf of everyone,” “on behalf of the community,” “speak on behalf of [someone]” “speak on [someone’s] behalf”

Related phrases: “in place of [someone] or [a group of people],” “speak for [someone] or [a group of people]”

Practice: Have you ever had to speak on someone’s behalf? (Ex: “Once, I called the phone company on behalf of my parents.”)

on board

adjectival phrase

1.supportive of a particular cause, objective, plan, project, etc.
Common extension: “on board with [something or someone]”

Examples: “If we’re going to do this, I need everyone on board.”
“He seems uncertain about this. Are you sure he’s on board?”
“This is a good idea, but we need management on board with it too.”

Common sentence: “I’m on board.” (I support this and believe in this. You can depend on my support and participation in this cause/project/etc.)

Just like you can be on board a ship, a train, or an aircraft with a group of people, you can be on board a particular cause, an objective, or a plan with a group of people. This means you all share the same goals and feelings about something. For example: “I think this is an interesting proposal, but I don’t think the accounting department will be on board with it.” Perhaps the accounting department will think the proposal is too expensive.

To understand this phrase better, imagine that you are on a ship and that everyone on the ship has the same goals and feelings about something. The people who are on the ship are “on board,” and those who are not on the ship are “not on board” for their own reasons.

Usage note: If someone is not on board with something, someone could try to persuade them to get on board. Note the following examples: “Allie has been really stubborn about not wanting to support this project. I haven’t been able to get her on board.” “You’re closer to Ethan than I am. Do you think you can get him on board? We need unanimous support for this proposal.”

Collocations: “be on board,” “come on board,” “get on board [with something or someone]”

Related phrases: “be in support of [something or someone],” “come out in support of [something or someone]”

Practice: Are you on board with the majority of your government’s decisions?

on call

adjectival phrase

1.ready to be called to work at any time, especially in emergencies; ready to respond when one is needed in a work situation (regardless of where one is)

Examples: “I’m on call tonight, so it’s better if I stay home in case the office needs me and I have to run to my computer.”
“It must be hard being a doctor. They’re always on call.”
“I’ll come with you for coffee, but I’m on call this afternoon, so I might have to leave at any moment.”

Common sentence: “I’m on call today/tonight/etc.” (I have to be ready to work at any time. This is often said when a person with this type of job wants to tell you they can not attend a social gathering due to their job.)

Many tech support and medical jobs are on call. This means that people respond when they are needed. If no one needs them, they either do other work, or they wait to be called. Some people who work on call still go out with their friends, but with the knowledge that they might be called to work at any time.

Usage note: If you are on call and your company calls you because they need you, your company calls you in. In the passive form, you are called in or you get called in by your company. Note the examples: “Tricia had to leave immediately because she got called in by the hospital.” “Hi David. I’m sorry to bother you, but I have to call you in. We have a situation.”

Collocations: “be on call,” “be on call today/this afternoon/tonight/etc.”

Related phrases: “call [someone] in,” “get called in”

Practice: Do you know anyone who has worked on call?

on cue

adverbial phrase

1.when expected; during the appropriate or expected moment
Common extensions: “as if on cue,” “right on cue”

Examples: “She got dressed, then right on cue, she said she needed to use the toilet.”
“As if on cue, my phone rang just as we entered the funeral home.”
“Watch this. Lina is going to pretend she has no money and then she’ll ask me for 20 dollars.” “Sarah, I forgot my wallet at home. Can I borrow 20 dollars?” “What did I tell you? Right on cue.”

Common sentence: “Right on cue.” (Exactly when I expected this to happen, or when it was appropriate for this to happen. Perhaps you were talking about someone, and the moment you said the person’s name, they entered the room. “Speak of the devil” is common in this situation, too.)

This phrase is usually used in a humorous way. If someone has a funny or annoying habit that is triggered in particular situations, their habit happens on cue. The idea of cues comes from theatre, where actors have to wait for certain words to be said or actions to be completed before they can continue with the next part of the performance. In this way, something that happens on cue happens when you expect it to happen or when it typically happens.

In example 1 above, the little girl often decides she needs to go to the toilet after she gets dressed to leave the house. This means she will have to take off her shoes and jacket, go to the bathroom, then get dressed again before she leaves the house. Her getting dressed to go out is the cue (or signal) for her to say she needs to use the bathroom. The entire situation is like a performance you have seen before, so you know what to expect.

Usage note: The common extension “right on cue” is often used as a standalone response to something funny that happens. It emphasizes that something happens at exactly the moment you expect it to.

Like all versions of this phrase, “as if on cue” can be used at the beginning, in the middle, or at the end of a sentence. It signifies that something happens in a way that feels like it was deliberate or part of a performance. Here are the three positions: “As if on cue, he started crying”; “The rain, as if on cue, started falling just as we exited the building”; “He laughed at her joke as if on cue.”

Collocations: “do [something] (right) on cue,” “fall (right) on cue,” “happen (right) on cue,” “speak (right) on cue”

Related phrases: “as expected,” “right on time”

Practice: Do you know anyone who often tells the same story as if on cue?

on demand

adverbial phrase

1.whenever one requests or wants something; when needed or asked for

Examples: “That hotel has great reviews. They have all-day room service on demand.”
“Certain drugs shouldn’t be available on demand at the pharmacy.”
“The best thing about YouTube is that everything is available on demand.”

Common sentence: “It’s available on demand.” (It’s available whenever you ask for it. You can demand it and expect it at any time you wish.)

This phrase is often used to talk about services. For example: “Netflix offers video on demand.” However, it can be used in any context where you are able to ask for something or have something whenever you want. For instance: “I love going to all-you-can-eat buffets. It’s food on demand!”

Usage note: Use the hyphenated “on-demand” to use this phrase as an adjective. “They have an on-demand delivery service. You can order something and have it at your door in 45 minutes.”

Collocations: “audio on demand,” “available on demand,” “deliver on demand,” “have [something] on demand,” “music on demand,” “payable on demand,” “ship on demand,” “video on demand”

Practice: Do you subscribe to any on-demand streaming services?

on duty

adjectival phrase

1.engaged in the act of work, or responsible for a task that was assigned; working during one’s assigned working hours (most often used by doctors, nurses, police officers, and security guards)
Opposite: “off duty”

Examples: “Hi, can I call you back? I can’t talk right now. I’m on duty.”
“Are you ever off duty? I feel like you work all the time.”
“She had only been on duty for 15 minutes when the accident happened.”

Common sentence: “He’s/She’s on duty.” (He/She is actively working. It is not a good time to contact him/her for casual conversation.)

While there are many serious jobs, the idea of being on duty is most often reserved for doctors, nurses, police officers, and security guards. If one of these workers is on duty, they are working during their assigned working hours. If they are off duty, they are not working. Note the example: “I’m on duty at the hospital. I’ll be off duty in around 30 minutes.” This means this nurse or doctor will be finished work in around 30 minutes.

Usage notes: Use the hyphenated forms “on-duty” and “off-duty” to use these phrases as adjectives. For example: “He got arrested by an off-duty police officer.”

This phrase can also be separated to specify the type of duty or job a person is currently responsible for. This usage is very versatile and can be used by anyone, whether they’re at work or not. First, here are a couple of work examples: “I’m on guard duty until 4:30pm”; “Jeffords, I’m going to put you on traffic duty today” (Jeffords will have to give parking and/or speeding tickets to drivers who break parking and/or speeding laws). Here are three informal examples: “Sorry, I can’t go out tonight. I’m on baby duty” (I am responsible for taking care of my baby tonight); “The party is tonight. Could you be on drink duty?” (Could you be the one responsible for providing drinks for the party?); “It’s your turn to be on toilet duty this week. I cleaned it last week” (You are responsible for cleaning the toilet this week).

Collocations: “be off duty at (a particular time),” “be on duty until (a particular time)”

Related phrases: “off the clock,” “on the clock”

Practice: Who is normally on laundry duty at your home?

on edge

adjectival phrase

1.anxious and unable to relax; worried; used to describe someone who is tense, nervous, and/or sensitive to any changes in their environment
Common extensions: “a bit on edge,” “a little on edge,” “totally on edge”
Opposite, see also: “at peace”

Examples: “The whole world has been on edge since the start of the pandemic.”
“You look a little on edge.” “I just found out my mom is in the hospital.”
“Today’s news has really put me on edge.”

Common sentence: “I’ve been a bit on edge lately.” (Lately, I have felt anxious and unable to relax.)

If you feel anxious, tense, nervous, or worried, you are on edge. The physical signs of being on edge could include an increased heart rate, nervous behaviours (such as quickly tapping your foot on the ground), and tense muscles. A person who is on edge is unable to relax. This is not a positive state, but sometimes it is unavoidable if we receive some scary news about a family member, or if we feel that we are in danger.

Usage note: You can be on edge about something. For instance: “She is on edge about her driving exam.”

Collocations: “be on edge,” “appear to be on edge,” “feel on edge,” “leave [someone] feeling on edge,” “leave [someone] on edge,” “look on edge,” “put [someone] on edge”

Related phrases: “wound tightly,” “climbing up the walls”

Practice: If you are on edge, what is a good way to calm down?

on end

adverbial phrase

1.without stopping or ending; continuously without any interruptions; non-stop
Common variation: “without end”

Examples: “We talked for hours on end.”
“It has been raining for days on end.”
“Sometimes, he would not talk to me for weeks on end.”

Common sentence: “We/They talked for hours on end.” (We/They talked for several hours without stopping; we/they talked for a long time.)

If you do something on end, you do it continuously without any stops or interruptions. For example: “He likes to complain on end about the government.” “On end” can follow a verb (“argue on end”) or a noun (“satisfaction on end”), but it is most often used with plural time nouns, such as “hours,” “days,” “weeks,” “years,” etc. The three examples above show this usage. “Non-stop” and “without end” are good synonyms for this phrase.

Usage note: Note these noun examples: “Seeing them again brought me joy on end” (or, “non-stop joy”). “We enjoyed drinks on end last night” (or, “drinks without end”). This is the least common usage of this phrase, and “non-stop + noun” is usually preferred in these cases. Verb + non-stop is also common (“party non-stop,” “dance non-stop,” etc.).

Collocations: “for days on end,” “for hours on end,” “for weeks on end,” “for years on end,” “party on end,” “talk on end,” “work on end”

Related phrases: “all day every day,” “from morning until night,” “non-stop,” “until the sun goes down”

Practice: What is something you can do on end without getting bored?

on fire

adjectival phrase, slang

1.producing incredibly positive results and sustaining positive momentum for a period of time; being incredibly successful at what one is doing for a period of time
Common extension: “on fire right now”
Similar, see also: “on a roll”

Examples: “That’s his fourth goal in fifteen minutes! He’s on fire! No one can stop him!”
“That game has sold over 2 million copies this year. It’s on fire right now.”
“Roberta has convicted 3 criminals this month. She’s on fire at the moment.”

Common sentence: “He’s/She’s/They’re on fire right now.” (He’s/She’s/They’re producing incredibly positive results right now, and it does not feel like anyone can stop him/her/them.)

Someone who is on fire is difficult to stop and easy to notice. See example 1 above for this usage. However, it’s not only people who can be on fire. A company can be on fire (“They made a lot of money last month, and their popularity is increasing. They’re on fire”). An industry or market can be on fire (“The real estate market is on fire. You can make a lot of money by selling houses right now”). Even a consumer item can be on fire (“That t-shirt’s on fire right now. Everyone is buying one”). If someone or something is producing incredibly positive results and it does not feel like anyone or anything can stop them, they are on fire. In the case of celebrities, consumer items, and trends, someone or something that is successful and extremely popular during a particular period of time can also be on fire.

Finally, when someone or something is on fire, the expectation is that the state is temporary, as it is impossible to sustain a superlative level of performance, success, and/or popularity for an infinite amount of time. For example: “She was on fire in 2019, but not as many people talk about her music today.”

Usage notes: You can use this phrase to give someone positive validation or encouragement by saying “You’re on fire right now!” If someone makes a series of funny jokes, you can also use this comment, or use it sarcastically if someone makes a series of mistakes or foolish blunders.

The adjective “hot” can also be used in the same idiomatic way in most cases. For example: “The real estate market is hot right now.” “That t-shirt is hot this month.” In addition, the word “fire” can be used colloquially to mean that something is of really high quality. For example: “This song is fire!”; “That movie was fire!”; “Wow! Your outfit is fire today!” Note that this usage is different than something or someone who is “on fire.”

Finally, there is an older usage for “on fire.” If someone is “on fire,” they are full of passion, excitement, and enthusiasm. For example: “He’s on fire with passion”; “Did you hear her speech? She was on fire in front of that microphone.” This usage is not as popular today, but is still used.

Collocations: “be on fire with excitement,” “be on fire with enthusiasm,” “be on fire with passion”

Related words and phrases: “hot,” “on a hot streak”

Practice: Name a company or product that is on fire right now.

on hand

adjectival phrase

1.presently and immediately available; used for saying that one does or does not have an item or object with them at the time of speaking

Examples: “You should always have a piece of ID on hand.”
“Do you have a pen I could borrow?” “Sorry, I don’t have one on hand.”
“She needed a nice dress for the conference, and she didn’t have time to go home or to go to the store to buy one. Luckily, I had one on hand.”

Common sentence: “I don’t have one on hand.” (I do not have one that is immediately available to me.)

If you have something on hand, it is close to you and able to be taken, bought, or used immediately. You can ask a shop owner if they have something specific on hand, meaning that they have it available for purchase (ex: “Sorry, we don’t have any on hand. Come back later”).

Collocations: “have [something] on hand,” “not have [something] on hand at the moment”

Related phrases: “in stock,” “on one’s person,” “within reach”

Practice: What is something you usually have on hand?

on hold

adjectival or adverbial phrase

1.in a state of interruption during a phone call, or while one waits to be transferred to another line, person, and/or department

Examples: “Could I put you on hold for a minute, please?”
“I’ve been on hold for over 25 minutes.”
“I need to put you on hold while I speak to a member of my team. Thank you for your patience.”

Common sentence: “I’m on hold.” (I am waiting for the person on the other end of the phoneline to return, or I am waiting to be transferred.)

If you ever call a company, especially for customer support, it is likely you will be put on hold at some point. This means the person you are speaking to needs to explore your situation or needs to input a lot of information on to their computer system. If you are on hold, you can not hear the other person. This is also common when your call is being transferred to another department and/or person in the company.

Usage note: The verb “hold” (by itself) is very common in this context as well. Note the examples: “Please hold”; “Thank you for holding”; “Could you hold for a minute?” The last example is usually said as “Could I put you on hold for a minute?”

Collocations: “be on hold,” “place [someone] on hold,” “put [someone] on hold”

Practice: What is the longest you have ever been on hold?

2.paused indefinitely; in a state of interruption or delay

Examples: “The wedding has been put on hold until November.”
“Our vacation plans are currently on hold.”
“The parade is on hold until the roads are cleared.”

Common sentence: “It’s on hold.” (It is delayed or paused at the moment. I probably do not know when it will continue.)

If you delay or pause your plans, you put your plans on hold. Often, we do not know when this state will end, but sometime we can clarify when we expect it to end by using “until.” For example: “Your final grades are on hold until the school finishes its investigation.” If you tell someone that something is on hold, they might ask “Why?” or “Until when?” You might have to put vacation plans on hold because of a family emergency, an international incident, or something else.

Usage note: Something can also be “on hold” if someone is reserving it for you and not allowing others to take it. For instance: “We can put the item on hold for you for 48 hours. After that, we will sell it to someone else.”

Collocations: “be on hold,” “put [something] on hold”

Practice: Have you ever had to put a party or other event on hold?

on purpose

adverbial phrase

1.intentionally; deliberately; to decide to do something and do it
Opposites, see also: “by accident,” “by mistake”

Examples: “Ouch! Did you just hit me on purpose?”
“I’m sorry! I didn’t do it on purpose! I swear!”
“She did it on purpose.”

Common sentence: “He/She did it on purpose.” (He/She meant to do that. It was not an accident.)

If you do something on purpose, you mean or intend to do it—you decide to do it and then actually do it. This is the opposite of doing something by accident. Note the conversation: “Did you do that on purpose?” “No, I did it by accident.” People often question the motives of others, and the idea of doing something on purpose is usually associated with choosing to do something which causes harm. Note the collocations for more examples.

Usage note: The verb “mean” is synonymous with doing things on purpose. For example: “I didn’t mean to do it” (I didn’t do it on purpose); “Did you mean to hurt your brother?” (Did you hurt your brother on purpose?).

Collocations: “break [something] on purpose,” “drop [something] on purpose,” “hurt [someone] on purpose,” “hit [someone] on purpose,” “lie to [someone] on purpose,” “take [something] on purpose”

Related phrases: “because one could,” “with the intention of [doing something]”

Practice: Have you ever broken something on purpose?

on the contrary

adverbial phrase

1.the opposite of what one has said; used to say that something is true in opposition to what has already been said

Examples: “He’s not ready.” “On the contrary, he’s been ready for the past 10 minutes.”
“Don’t you hate salads?” “On the contrary, I eat a salad every day.”
“Many people think their company is losing money. On the contrary, they just had their most profitable year ever.”

Common sentence: “On the contrary.” (This is used as a standalone response to say “Actually, the opposite is true.” This response must be followed with a statement the provides the opposite side of the situation.)

You can use this phrase as a response to someone, as in examples 1 and 2 above. You can also use it as part of your own statement, as in example 3. When used in this way, the phrase can create a dramatic effect. Here is another example of this usage: “Everyone here thinks I don’t have the experience for this job. On the contrary, I have been doing this type of work for over 7 years.”

Usage note: “On the contrary” can be used as a standalone response before providing more information. For instance: “Are you tired?” “On the contrary! I feel quite refreshed.” The phrase “quite the contrary” is also common in this case. A common extension is “Quite the contrary, actually.” This is a formal phrase, but when used properly, it can add a sense of gravity to your statement. Note the following example: “Why did he do that? Was it because of hatred?” “Quite the contrary, actually. He did it because of love.”

Related phrases: “just the opposite,” “not so,” “quite the contrary”

Practice: Finish the idea: “She’s not in a bad mood. On the contrary!...”

on/at/in the front line

adverbial phrase

1.doing the hardest and/or most important work; usually used to talk about soldiers, or workers such as doctors, nurses, teachers, and police officers who interact with the public directly
Common variation: “on the front lines”

Examples: “Nurses are on the front line every day, trying to keep people healthy.”
“Medical supplies are being delivered to soldiers on the front line.”
“Dara works on the front line at McDonald’s. She talks to customers all day.”

Common sentence: “I’m/You’re on the front line.” (I/You do the hardest and most important work in the company. I/You deal with clients, customers, and/or the public in general directly.)

If you are on the front line, you are interacting directly with customers, clients, or people in general. In times of war, the front line refers to soldiers who are first or “at the front.” In the case of teachers, they are on the front line because they deal directly with students, compared to administrators, who (usually) do not. The prepositions at, on, and in are often used interchangeably with this phrase, as in “I work at/on/in the front line.”

Usage note: The adjective form of this phrase is “frontline,” as in “She’s a frontline worker” or “He used to be a frontline employee.” You can also say that a person who is “in the front line” is “the first line of defense” or “in the line of fire.” People in the front line are vulnerable because they cannot hide behind anyone. They are the first people the public deals with.

Collocations: “a frontline employee,” “a frontline worker,” “be on the front line,” “work on the front line”

Related phrases: “be in the line of fire,” “be the first line of defense”

Practice: Have you ever had a job where you had to work on the front line?

on the go

adjectival or adverbial phrase

1.constantly active; busy and restless
Similar, see also: “on the run” (meaning 2)

Examples: “She’s always on the go.”
“You need to get on the go.”
“I like to stay on the go.”

Common sentence: “He’s/She’s always on the go.” (He’s/She’s always doing something. He/She is a very active person in his/her life and/or work.)

A person who is on the go is always in motion. A common context for this phrase is when you decide to eat something or to buy something to eat while you are travelling. For example: “I’ll eat on the go”; “I’ll get something to eat on the go.” In the case of buying food, this is connected to ordering something for takeout, or “to go.” Here is a common social exchange with this usage: “Will that be for here or to go?” “I’ll take it to go, please.” (Context: a restaurant.)

Usage note: In addition to being on the go, you can also get on the go. If you say “Let’s get on the go,” you mean “Let’s move. Let’s go!” In example 2 above, the person is saying that someone needs to be more physically active. You can also remain or stay on the go. If you are someone who likes to stay active or to always be doing something, you like to stay on the go, as in example 3 above.

Collocations: “be (always) on the go,” “get on the go,” “stay on the go”

Related phrases: “on the move,” “on the run”

Practice: Are you usually on the go, or do you have a relaxed lifestyle?

on the line

adjectival phrase

1.at risk; used when something is wagered in a bet, when something can be won or lost, or when something is at risk

Examples: “You need to fix this. Your job is on the line!”
“The first person to solve the puzzle wins. $200 are on the line.”
“What do I get if I win? You need to put something on the line, too.”

Common sentence: “I’ve got nothing on the line.” (I am not risking anything. This is not a dangerous situation for me. This could be used in a situation where you are involved in a competition just for pleasure.)

If you put something on the line, you put it at risk. You might keep it or lose it depending on the outcome of a situation. In war, soldiers put their lives on the line. If you make a bet with someone, you put something (a personal item or a specific amount of money) on the line. In a competition with a cash prize, the cash prize is what is on the line, as in example 2. This means it is able to be won or lost.

In example 1, a boss is telling an employee that the employee made a big mistake that could result in them losing their job. If they fix the mistake, they will keep their job. Their job is at risk, in danger, or on the line.

A person might say “You have nothing on the line” about someone who makes a decision that could affect others, but not themselves, or who states an unempathetic opinion. This person could be criticized in the following way: “That’s easy for you to say. You’ve got nothing on the line.” This means the person is not risking anything because they are in a comfortable or advantageous position.

Usage notes: There are two other common meanings for this phrase:

I) to be on a call with someone (“I’m on the line with my mother”)

II) to be on the border of two categories (“He’s on the line between madness and genius”)

Collocations: “lay it on the line,” “money on the line,” “on the line between [something and something],” “put [something] on the line,” “reputation on the line”

Related phrases: “in danger,” “out on a limb”

Practice: Has your job ever been on the line? What happened?

on the one hand…on the other hand

adverbial phrases

1.used to contrast two things, positions, topics, arguments, opinions, situations, beliefs, etc.
Common variation: “On one hand…On the other hand”

Examples: “On the one hand, he’s a great worker. On the other hand, he’s a bully.”
“On the one hand, they make great products. On the other hand, they pay their workers incredibly low wages.”
“On the one hand, I want to support you. On the other hand, you’re making it really difficult to do that because of how you’re acting.”

Common sentence: “On the other hand,...” (Used to contrast something that someone else has said. For example: “We have been together for 5 years. He has been a good boyfriend”; “On the other hand, he just cheated on you and you should break up with him.”)

“On the one hand” introduces one side of a topic, and “on the other hand” provides a contrast. This does not always mean that the two sides are opposites or that one is negative and one is positive. For example: “On the one hand, I really want a hamburger for lunch today. On the other hand, I just had a hamburger for dinner yesterday.” This person is conflicted about their situation. “On the one hand” and “on the other hand” are about perspective and considering different sides of things.

Usage note: “On the other hand” can be used without “on the one hand.” Note the situation: “I have $200 and I’d like to buy some new clothes. On the other hand, I should probably just use the money to buy groceries.”

Related phrases: “in contrast,” “in one respect…in another respect,” “in one way…in another way,” “looked at one way…looked at another way”

Practice: Use this phrase to talk about something you want or need. (Ex: “On the one hand, I need new shoes. On the other hand, I want new skates.”)

on the run

adjectival phrase

1.in a position where one is trying to escape or hide, especially from authorities
Common extension: “on the run from [someone or something]”

Examples: “He’s on the run from the police.”
“They escaped prison 2 weeks ago and have been on the run ever since.”
“They’re on the run from a terrorist organization.”

Common sentence: “He’s/She’s on the run.” (He/She is trying to escape or hide from someone or something.)

If a person is on the run, they do not wish to be caught or found. While this phrase is often associated with criminals who are trying to escape the police, as in examples 1 and 2, it can also be used to describe someone who is trying to escape from a dangerous situation or a dangerous group, as in example 3. A person can be on the run from war, danger, etc. This is a reality for some people in the world, and it is also something you will often hear in news reports, books, and films.

Collocations: “have [someone] on the run,” “on the run from authorities,” “on the run from danger,” “on the run from the law,” “on the run from the police,” “on the run from [someone],” “on the run from terrorism,” “on the run from war”

Related phrases: “in retreat,” “on the loose,” “running away”

Practice: Where would you go if you were on the run from the police?

2.adverbial phrase while one is hurrying, rushing, or busy
Similar, see also: “on the go”

Examples: “I ate lunch on the run.”
“He’s always on the run.”
“I’ll call you on the run.” (I will call you while I am engaged in my activities.)

Common sentence: “I’ll eat it on the run.” (I will eat it while I am travelling between places by car, public transportation, or on foot, or while I am working or engaging in another activity.)

This usage is synonymous with “on the go.” It is common to talk about eating something or calling someone on the run. When we do something on the run, we do it while we are engaged in another activity, such as travelling from one place to another. “On the go” is slightly more common.

Collocations: “call [someone] on the run,” “eat [something] on the run,” “have breakfast/lunch/dinner on the run”

Related phrase: “do [something] in the middle of an activity/event/etc.”

Practice: Do you ever eat on the run?

on the spot

adverbial phrase

1.in the moment; spontaneously and immediately; often used when someone is placed in a position where they must contribute an opinion, or perform in an expected way in front of someone without any preparation

Examples: “The boss asked me to recite my presentation on the spot.”
“I don’t like being put on the spot.”
“He started singing on the spot.”

Common sentence: “Don’t put me on the spot.” (Do not ask me to answer this or perform this in the moment.)

If someone puts you on the spot, they put you in a position where they expect you to respond to something or to perform something immediately. Note the following exchange: “You’re the coordinator of the department. Can you tell me how many times Tina has arrived late to work this year?” “Not on the spot, but I can find the information if you give me a few moments.” In this case, the coordinator’s boss put the coordinator on the spot. He/She asked for information to be given at the moment of speaking.

To better understand this phrase, imagine that the person who is on the spot has a single light shining on them, like an actor on a stage. If someone puts you on the spot, they push you into a position where everyone can see you and where you are expected to say or do something. Most people do not enjoy being put on the spot. In fact, one of the most common statements with this phrase is “I hate to put you on the spot, but…” In this case, the person speaking knows they are putting the listener in a bad position, but they still want to ask them to say or do something.

Collocations: “answer on the spot,” “ask a question on the spot,” “be on the spot,” “be put on the spot,” “perform on the spot,” “sing on the spot”

Related phrases: “be in the spotlight,” “here and now,” “then and there”

Practice: Are you comfortable being put on the spot?

on time

adverbial phrase

1.arriving or happening at the correct or expected time; not late; on schedule
Do not confuse with, see also: “in time”

Examples: “She never arrives on time. She’s always late.”
“We stopped being friends because he was never on time for anything.”
“The concert didn’t start on time because the drummer was late.”

Common sentence: “Please arrive on time.” (Please arrive at the time I expect you to arrive.)

If you arrive neither late nor early for something, you arrive on time. Trains, buses, and other modes of public transportation typically run on specific schedules. If a train arrives at a station when it is supposed to arrive, it arrives on time. If it doesn’t, someone might say “The train has arrived early,” or “The train is late.” Most companies expect their employees to arrive and start work on time. If you do not start your job when you are expected to start it, your boss might say: “You’re late. You need to start on time tomorrow. Got it?”

Usage note: This is different than arriving “in time,” which means you either arrive early or exactly when something is expected to happen. “In time” is also often followed by a prepositional phrase that starts with “for” to indicate what the person arrived in time for. For example: “She arrived in time for the speech.” This means she did not miss the speech, but arrived sufficiently early enough to hear it.

Collocations: “arrive on time,” “be on time,” “begin on time,” “come on time,” “end on time,” “finish on time,” “start on time”

Related phrases: “in good time,” “on schedule,” “on the dot”

Practice: Do you typically start work on time, or are you sometimes late?

on top of [something]

prepositional phrase

1.dealing with something (a task or situation) successfully, often when it is difficult; being fully engaged with and successfully handling one’s work or responsibilities

Examples: “Could you do the dishes?” “Sure, I’m on top of it.”
“Don’t worry about it. Our team is on top of everything.”
“I need you to be on top of this. We can’t make any mistakes, and we need everything finished by Monday.”

Common sentence: “I’m on top of it.” (I am fully engaged with it and am handling it successfully. You can also use this when someone asks for help with something, and you accept the responsibility for a particular task.)

If you are on top of something, you are dealing with it successfully because your attention is focused and you are doing what is expected. This phrase is often used in professional and academic contexts. For instance, a student can be on top of his/her/their homework, and an employee can be on top of a work. At home, a person can be (or not be) on top of the housework, the bills, or anything else that requires regular attention to maintain a house, an apartment, etc. You can be (or not be) on top of any task or situation that requires you to be focused and doing something.

Usage note: If someone asks you to do a specific task or to help them with something in particular, you can respond with “I’m on top of it” or “I’m on it” to accept the responsibility and to show your enthusiasm, confidence, and competence.

Collocations: “be on top of [something],” “be on top of a project,” “be on top of one’s homework,” “be on top of one’s work,” “be on top of this/that,” “stay on top of something”

Related phrase: “on the ball”

Practice: Are you usually on top of your responsibilities at home?

on track

adjectival phrase

1.progressing in a way that will likely result in success, or following a course of action that will achieve a particular result; on schedule
Common extension: “on track to [do something]”
Opposite: “off track”

Examples: “I’m currently on track to graduate by September.”
“We need to get back on track.”
“The government’s plan is still on track, and the program should be successfully implemented this year.”

Common sentence: “Let’s get back on track.” (Let us return to a good schedule, or let us return the discussion to the original topic.)

This phrase has two meanings. The first one is similar to “on schedule.” If you are on track, you are progressing in a way that will likely result in success, or you are progressing at the rate that is expected. The phrase can be used by itself in these case. Note three examples for more context: “The project is on track”; “We are still on track”; “Our plan is not on track.”

“On track” can also mean that something is progressing in a way or is following a course of action that will achieve a particular result. This can be positive or negative, and it can refer to something happening at a particular time. Here are three different examples of this usage: “He’s on track to fail the class”; “My application is on track to be accepted by the summer”; “The movie is currently on track for a September release date.” As you can see, things can be on track to do something, or on track for something.

Usage note: If someone tells you to “get on track,” they want you to start doing what you need to do to achieve a particular result. If someone tells you to “get back on track,” they want you to return to a previous course of action or to a desired schedule because something has caused you to “get/go off track.” Note the following context: “The project got/went off track last week. We need to get it back on track.”

A person can also get off track (or, “lose their train of thought”) when they’re speaking, which means they start discussing something unrelated to their original point. For example: “Sorry, I got off track for a minute. My point is…”

Collocations: “(not) be on track,” “get back on track,” “get on track”

Related phrases: “on course,” “on pace,” “on schedule”

Practice: Do you know anyone who often goes off track during conversation?

out of line

adjectival phrase

1.inappropriate and/or unacceptable; used when someone says or does something insubordinate or impertinent
Common extension: “way out of line”

Examples: “The teacher’s remarks were out of line.”
“I apologize for my actions. I was out of line.”
“You shouldn’t be in charge.” “That’s out of line. Go take a break.”

Common sentence: “That’s out of line.” (That is too much. You/He/She/They should not have done or said that.)

Your words or your actions can be out of line. This means they cross a metaphorical line, and do not conform with or follow a particular rule or social propriety. Someone who is out of line says or does something that is not received well by others because it does not follow the expectations of a company, a society, an individual, etc. Note three more examples: “Your words were incredibly out of line”; “She checked your personal emails? That’s out of line”; “He always says things that are out of line.”

Usage note: Note the use of these closely Related phrases: “You crossed a line”; “You were over the line”; “Your words were uncalled for”; “You were out of order.” Finally, the emphasizing adverb “way” is common with this phrase, as in “That’s way out of line.” (Or, “That’s very out of line.”)

Collocations: “be out of line,” “speak out of line”

Related phrases: “cross the line,” “out of order,” “over the line,” “uncalled for”

Practice: Are you comfortable telling someone that they have said something out of line? What actions are out of line in public?

up to

prepositional phrase

1.referring to a maximum value; used to state that something is close to or equal to a certain value or a certain point of elevation

Examples: “This building can fit up to 325 people.”
“Our house was flooded last week. There was water up to my ankles.”
“This car can go up to 200 miles per hour.”

Common sentence: “It goes/fits up to...” (This is the maximum speed of the vehicle, or the maximum number of people that can fit in this space.)

What is the maximum number of people who can fit in the store? “Up to 50 people can fit in the store.” You can use any types of numbers with this phrase, including seconds, hours, or monetary values. For example: “You can make up to $100,000 per year working for that company.” This phrase can also refer to a certain level of elevation, as in example 2.

Collocations: “add up to,” “come up to,” “fit up to,” “go up to”

Practice: How long can you hold your breath? (Ex: “Up to X seconds.”)

2.be up to [something]. to be engaged in an activity; to be engaged in secretive activity

Examples: “She’s not up to much these days. She’s just enjoying her retirement.”
“What are you up to this weekend?” (What are you doing this weekend?)
“The kids are really quiet. They must be up to something.” (They’re doing something secretive.)

Common sentence: “What are you up to?” (What are you doing?—at the moment, or in your life in general.)

If you are up to something, you are doing something. This phrase is most often used in the question, “What are you up to?” which means “What are you doing?” A secondary meaning for this phrase is that someone is doing or planning something secretive. Some common sentences with this usage are: “I think he’s up to something”; “She’s definitely up to something”; “Do you think she’s up to something?”

Grammar note: “Be up to” is often followed by quantifiers such as “much,” “a lot,” or “not much,” as in “I’m not up to much these days” or “They’ve been up to a lot lately.”

Collocations: “not up to much,” “up to no good,” “up to [something]”

Practice: What are you up to tomorrow? (What are your plans tomorrow?)

STANDALONE RESPONSES
