These are all common responses and interjections you can use in the contexts specified under each entry. Memorizing set sentences and responses is one of the best ways to increase your vocabulary and your speaking confidence and fluency in any language. Practice these responses by yourself or with a partner to boost your speaking skills.

Most of the entries in this chapter do not include common sentences, as the standalone responses usually are the common sentences. To practice these responses, read the examples, memorize the ones that stand out to you, and think of situations that would force you to respond with these sentences.

“As if.”

1.used to express that something sounds highly unlikely or is actually untrue

Examples: “She’s the best swimmer in the world.” “As if! She’s not even close.”
“Julia said it was your fault.” “As if! You’re going to believe Julia?”
“These jeans are worth $200.” “As if! Have you felt the material?”

This is a quick interjection that is often said emphatically. It is like a strong instant negative reaction because you cannot believe the person speaking to you has actually said what they have said. In short, you are saying “That’s a lie” or “I highly doubt it.”

Usage note: “As if” is often used as a standalone response, but it can also be used in the middle of sentences. Note the examples: “He talks to people as if he were the king of England”; “The house was incredibly quiet. It felt as if everyone had been sleeping.” In these cases, it is synonymous with “like,” but it uses conditional grammar structures.

You can also use “as if” in the second part of a sentence or as a response to show contrast and strong personal feelings about a situation. For instance: “My wife bought four new plates—as if we needed more plates.” In this case, you are not happy that your wife bought four new plates because you feel you do not need them. Here is one more example: “The boss said he would fix the problem.” “As if our boss has ever fixed anything.” In both of the examples above, the person saying “as if” is skeptical and maybe even sarcastic. They do not believe that something is necessary or true.

Related responses: “I highly doubt it.” “Yeah, right.”

“Come again?”

1.“Could you repeat that?” Used when one did not hear or understand someone properly, or when one wants to confirm they heard something properly (because it sounds strange or unbelievable)

Examples: “Your grandmother is going camping this weekend.” “Come again?”
“Are you listening to me?” “Hmm? Come again? Sorry, I was distracted.”
“He wants to sell the company.” “Wait, what? Come again?”

This common response is often used when you were not paying attention properly to someone, you did not hear someone well, or when someone tells you something strange or unbelievable. It is an informal way to ask someone to repeat something. It can be rude if the person who is speaking to you has spent a long time trying to explain something, but you have not been paying attention to them.

Usage note: “Could you run that by me one more time?” is another common question with similar usage. “Wait, what?” is used to show shock or surprise. It is sometimes said quickly and lazily, and can sound like “weiwa?”

Related responses: “Run that by me one more time.” “Wait, what?”

“Come on.”

1.used for telling someone to hurry, or to start moving

Examples: “Come on! We’re going to miss the bus!”
“We need to leave. Come on.”
“Come on. The performance starts in an hour.”

If you tell someone to “come on,” you are telling them to start moving or to move quicker. In the case of moving quicker, the imperative “Hurry up” is also common. Depending on your tone, “Come on” sounds a little more encouraging and less commanding. “Hurry up” usually sounds commanding.

Related responses: “Hurry up.” “Let’s go.” “Let’s move.”

2.used for encouraging someone to make a greater effort, to stop being sad or upset, or to show one’s frustration with someone’s words or actions

Examples: “You think I’m ugly!” “Come on, Nik. That’s not what I said.” (frustration)
“You can find another job, Jason. Come on. It’s not so bad.” (encouragement)
“Come on, Dorothy! You can do it! You’re almost there!” (encouragement)

Common sentence: “Come on. It’s not that bad.” (Look at this situation in a different way. It could be worse.)

In all of the examples above, “Come on” encourages some form of movement, whether physical or mental. As you can see, depending on the context, you can express your frustration with someone or something (“Come on! Why isn’t this stupid phone working?!”), or encouragement, as in examples 2 and 3 above. When you say “Come on,” your mind wants to shake the other person in some way and change their behaviour or their perspective. It’s like saying “Hey! Wake up.”

Related responses: “Look on the bright side.” “Really?!” (frustration)

3.used for showing one’s surprise or disbelief

Examples: “Come on! Are you serious?”
“There’s no way that’s true. Come on.”
“Come on. Really? You’re joking, right?” (Or, “You’re kidding, right?”)

Common sentence: “Come on. Really?” (Are you serious? I do not believe what you are telling me.)

When you use “Come on” in this way, you are surprised or in disbelief. It’s like you are encouraging the other person to continue speaking, hoping they will tell you they are joking or trying to trick you–because what they are saying is hard for you to believe or accept. “Come on. Really?” and “Come on. Seriously?” are very common responses in these situations.

Usage note: You can also say “Come on. Really?” or “Come on. Seriously?” when you are frustrated with what someone has said or done. Note the following exchange: “I lost your tablet.” “Come on. Seriously?”

Related responses: “Are you joking/kidding?” “No way.” “Say what?”

“Do tell.”

1.“Tell me more.” Used when you want someone to give you more information about a topic they have just introduced, typically because the information really interests you or the information is gossip

Examples: “Jake and Samantha broke up.” “Really? Do tell.”
“I just told the boss I wanted more money.” “Oh! Do tell. How did it go?”
“We had an amazing date last night.” “That sounds exciting! Do tell.”

Most people are interested in hearing interesting stories about other people. The phrase “Do tell” is an imperative that encourages someone to tell you more details about a topic they have introduced. This response is very common in situations where gossip is involved, as in example 1. However, if any topic interests you, you can use this phrase. Just remember that it is not typically used in serious conversations, but is a light-hearted (meaning amusing or entertaining) invitation for someone to tell you more details about a topic they have introduced.

Related response: “Don’t spare any/the details.” (Tell me as many details as possible.)

“For sure.”

1.“Definitely.” “Certainly.” “Without a doubt.” Used when one is certain of something, or when one can definitely do something
Common variation: “That’s for sure.”

Examples: “Can you help me to clean the bathroom tomorrow?” “For sure.”
“They’re going to win today.” “Oh, for sure!”
“Can she really lift 250 pounds?” “Yes. For sure. I’ve seen her do it.”

You can say “yes” to a person’s request with this response. For example: “Could you help me with this?” “For sure. Just give me a second.” “For sure” means “definitely” or “without doubt.” If something is for sure, it is 100% certain. You can also show that you are extremely confident about a particular outcome, as in example 2 above.

Usage note: Say “That’s for sure” when you want to strongly agree with something someone has said. This is a way of saying “That is 100% true.” For instance: “This was the best vacation we’ve ever had.” “That’s for sure.”

Other quick words and phrases are often used around “for sure.” Here are some examples of how you can respond positively to a request: “Yeah, for sure”; “For sure. No problem”; “For sure. Don’t worry about it.”

Related responses: “One hundred percent.” “No doubt about it.” “Without a doubt.”

“Here you go”; “There you go.”

1.used when you give, send, or throw something to someone
Common variation: “Here you are”; “There you are.”

Examples: “Could you please pass me a tissue?” “Sure. Here you go.”
“I’ll also take a pack of gum.” “No problem. There you go. Anything else?”
“Do you need a pen? I’ve got an extra one in my bag. Here you go!”

Say “Here you go” when you give something to someone and place it in front of them or into their hand. The use of “Here you go” or “There you go” depends on physical and mental distance from the object. If you are in the process of giving something to someone, meaning your hand is in motion, you will probably say “Here you go.” A server at a restaurant who is placing your meal onto the table will most likely say “Here you go,” as will a bank teller who slides money from their side of the counter to your side.

Use “There you go” the moment you feel you have “pushed” the object away from you toward the other person. If your friend asks you to send a picture via text message, you can send the picture and say “Here you go” or “There you go” depending on how close you feel to the picture. Do you imagine yourself with the picture in your hand (“Here you go”) or do you feel that the picture is now “away” from you (“There you go”)?

Usage note: “Here we go” is used when an event or course of action is about to begin. For example: “Is everyone ready to start playing? Okay! Here we go!” You can also use this when you are not directly involved in a game or event, but are simply watching it, listening to it, etc. For instance: “The political debate is about to begin in 3, 2, 1—here we go.”

“Here we go” is also used when someone starts doing or saying something that they have a habit of doing. Typically, this is a habit that we think is annoying, or something that we expect the person to say or do. For example: “You wouldn’t act this way if your mother had never…” “Here we go! You always have to bring my mother into every argument.”

Finally, “There you go” can be used synonymously with “Of course” when something happens that we expect. It is used to show our resignation or excitement towards what has happened. Note the two examples: “My brother just took the money and ran away.” “There you go. We shouldn’t be surprised. He always does this”; “I finished in first place!” “There you go! That’s great! I knew you could do it!”

“I got it”; “Gotcha.”

1.“I understand what you are telling me.” “I understand you.”
Common abbreviated variation: “Got it.”

Examples: “Finally, you need to put this here, and then you’re done. Got it?” “I got it.”
“The delivery will arrive between 6 and 7 o’clock.” “Gotcha.”
“A gerund looks like a verb but acts like a noun.” “Oh! I got it now.”

Grammatically, this is the informal way to say “I have it,” “I have acquired it,” or “I have got it.” In informal situations, when someone explains something to you, you can say “I got it” to mean that you understand it. You can also say “Got it” without the subject. A person who is trying to show you how to do something or explain something to you might ask “Got it?” or “You got it?” to check your understanding. When they do this, you can answer with a simple yes or no, or say “I got it,” “Got it,” or “Gotcha.” The popular negative usage of this phrase is “I don’t got it” or “Don’t got it.”

“I gotcha” or “Gotcha” are also popular in casual situations. They are shorter colloquial pronunciations of “I have got you,” and are other ways to say “I understand you.”

Usage note: You can also say “I get it” in the present form to mean “I understand it” or “I understand what you are explaining to me or showing me.” The negative of this is “I don’t get it” (or, “I didn’t get it” to say you did not understand something). “I get it” and “I got it” are often used interchangeably even though they look and sound like they are using different tenses. (Remember that “I got it” is actually the grammatically incorrect form of “I have got it,” which is also present.)

Related responses: “Crystal clear.” “I get what you’re saying.” “Sounds good.” “That makes sense.”

2.“I got it.” used to say one will handle a situation or accept responsibility for a task, typically as a volunteer
Similar, see also: “I’m on it,” “on top of [something]”

Examples: “Oh no! The oven is still on.” “Don’t worry. I got it.” (I will turn it off.)
“Can you do this for me?” “Of course! I got it.”
“Don’t worry about the apartment while you’re away. I got it.”

This phrase reassures someone that they do not have to worry about something because you are accepting responsibility for it. For instance, if your neighbours go on vacation and ask you to check their mail while they are away, you can say “No problem. I got it.”

Usage note: Use the related phrase “I got this” to tell others that you are confident about your ability to handle a particular situation, task, or responsibility. You can also encourage someone and show them that you believe in their abilities by saying “You got this.” For example: “You shouldn’t feel nervous about this test. You have studied all week. You got this.”

Related responses: “I got this.” “I’m on it.” “I’m on top of it.”

“I know, right?”

1.“That is true.” “I agree with what has just been said.” Used for showing that one agrees with someone
Common abbreviated variation: “Right?”
Canadian variation: “I know, eh?” (“eh” is pronounced “ei,” as in “hey”)

Examples: “This pizza is so delicious!” “I know, right?”
“Today’s weather is absolutely brutal.” “I know, right?”
“She needs to stop listening to him.” “I know, right?”

This quick response shows that you agree with what has just been said. To understand this response a little better, in example 1, “I know, right?” could be substituted with “Yes, it is” in regard to the deliciousness of the pizza. Example 2 could be substituted with “Yes, it is,” and example 3 could be substituted with “Yes, she does.” “I know, right?” shows emphatic agreement. You can use it to agree with a negative statement as well. For example: “People shouldn’t work 5 days a week.” “I know, right?”

Usage note: You can also simply say “Right?” for the same effect. For example: “That was such an amazing show.” “Right?!” In both cases, “I know, right?” and “Right?” you are happy that someone has expressed your thoughts or feelings before you did.

Related responses: “Ain’t that the truth?” (American for “Is that not the truth?”) “I was just going to say that.” “I was thinking the same thing.” “You got that right.”

“I think so.”

1.“I think that is the case.” “I think that is true.” Used when one is relatively confident that something is (or is not) true or the case
Opposite: “I don’t think so.”

Examples: “Didn’t they get married last year?” “I think so.”
“Do you think these shoes will fit him?” “I don’t think so.”
“Will the room be painted before Friday?” “I think so.”

This is a common response to yes/no questions when you are not 100% certain of the answer, but you have enough knowledge or information to think (or believe) that something is (or is not) true. Here is another example of this usage: “Isn’t Sydney the capital of Australia?” “I don’t think so. I always forget the actual capital, though” (The capital of Australia is Canberra). Here is another context: “Are the cookies ready?” “I think so. I’ll check the oven.” As you can see, if you are relatively confident or if you believe that something is (or is not) true, you can use “I think so” (or, “I don’t think so”).

Related responses: “I believe so.” “I hope so.” “He/She/It should be.”

“I’m on it.”

1.used for indicating that one will handle or be responsible for a particular task or situation
Common abbreviated variation: “On it!”
Similar, see also: “on top of [something]”

Examples: “Who’s going to write the final report for our team?” “Don’t worry. I’m on it.”
“Could someone turn down the air conditioning in here?” “On it!”
“We’re behind schedule on this project, so I need you to get on it.” “No problem, boss. I’m on it.”

Use this response when someone directly asks you if you can handle a particular task or responsibility. For instance: “Could you go to the store to buy some carrots?” “No problem. I’m on it.” When someone says they’re “on it,” they typically act almost immediately. In this case, the person probably went to buy the carrots shortly after promising to do so. When someone says “I’m on it,” it is similar to them saying “You can trust me to do this.” Someone who is “on it” acts independently and feels confident that they can accomplish a particular task or handle a particular situation or responsibility.

This response can also be used in a group situation. For example, if you are planning a party and you ask your friends to help you, you might ask “Who can bring potato chips?” The person who responds “I’m on it” (or, simply, “On it!”) is offering to be responsible for the potato chips.

Usage note: You can also “get on it,” as in example 3 above. This means you take or accept responsibility for handling something. Here are 3 more common sentences with “get on it”: “I’ll get on it as soon as I can”; “I can get on it starting tomorrow” (I am busy today, but I can become involved in this starting tomorrow); “I just got on it today. I need more time.”

Related responses: “I’ll handle it.” “I’ll get on it.” “I’m on top of it.”

“Kind of”; “Sort of.”

1.“In some ways.” “In some respects.” “Not exactly, but partially.” Used for saying that something is partially true or the case
Common colloquial pronunciations: “Kinda”; “Sorta.”
Similar, see also: “in a sense,” “in a way”

Examples: “You look a little sad. Didn’t you have a good time?” “Kind of.”
“Were you disappointed by the movie?” “Yeah. Sort of.”
“I thought the two of you were related.” “We are. Kind of.”

You can be kind of disappointed. A wall can be sort of green. A car can be kind of old. In all of these cases, the idea is that things are not exactly true or the case, but that there is some truth to them. If a friend asks you to stay late at a party, you can say “Actually, I’m kind of (or ‘sort of’) tired.” This means you are partially tired and you probably want to go home.

Usage note: “Sort of” and “kind of” can be used interchangeably. In addition to using these phrases with adjectives and verbs (“I kind of want that”), you can use them with nouns. For example: “She’s kind of a philosopher.” This means she is not exactly a philosopher, but perhaps she sounds like a philosopher sometimes. If you bake a cake that looks bad, someone can say “It’s sort of a cake.”

The phrases “some sort of” or “some kind of” are also common. This means that someone or something is a particular type of something, but you are not sure about their exact status. For instance: “He’s some kind of/sort of teacher.” This means you know he is a teacher, but you do not know which subject he teaches, or you are not sure what his teaching credentials are.

Finally, you can use this phrase sarcastically or to make a situation feel less serious. For example, you can tell a person “That’s kind of wrong” when you mean “That is absolutely wrong.” If someone says “You’re sort of out of time,” they usually mean “You have no more time remaining.” Using “sort of” in this way softens the news, or it can be used sarcastically as well.

Collocations: (all “kind of” uses can be replaced with “sort of”) “kind of helped,” “kind of it,” “kind of poor,” “kind of rich,” “kind of right,” “kind of tired,” “kind of true,” “kind of wrong,” “some kind of answer,” “some kind of danger,” “some kind of response”

Related phrases: “a type of,” “in a manner of speaking”

“More or less.”

1.approximately; about; close to; to a certain extent or degree
Similar, see also: “Kind of”; “Sort of.” “Pretty much.”

Examples: “Did your job interview go well?” “More or less.”
“Are they finished working on that?” “More or less.”
“Can he survive in a Portuguese-speaking environment?” “More or less.”

Sometimes, we want to use imprecise language. If something is not exactly true or the case, but it is close to being true or the case, it is more or less true or the case. A person can say “I’m more or less done.” This means they have done as much as they can and probably don’t intend to do more. You can use this as a standalone response when you want to say that something is close to being true, or is approximately true. Note the examples above.

Usage note: “More or less” is slightly more precise or closer to the truth than “kind of”/”sort of.”

Collocations: “more or less done/finished,” “more or less the case,” “more or less ready,” “more or less true”

Related phrase: “give or take (a few days, an hour, a few minutes, etc.)”

“No way!”

1.“I don’t believe it!”; used to express shock, surprise, disbelief, and/or an impossibility

Examples: “Lucas was the one who did it.” “No way!”
“She paid over $100 for that shirt.” “No way!”
“Her parents kicked her out of the house.” “No way!”

This is an emphatic response to show that you are shocked, surprised, or in disbelief because of what someone has just told you. It is similar to saying “Wow! I don’t believe it.” Another way to think of this response is “There is zero possibility that what you have just told me is true.” With this in mind, you can also use “no way” in the middle of a sentence, especially with the structure “There’s no way,” as in the following examples: “There is no way they can win” (They can’t win); “There was no way he had good intentions” (He didn’t have good intentions in his heart when he did that); “There is no way she’s telling the truth” (I strongly believe she is not telling the truth).

Usage note: You can also use “no way” to mean there is no path, no course, or no road to arrive at a certain location or outcome. This is typically used with “There is no way” or subject + have/has + no way. Note the examples and structures: “There is no way for them to win” (It is impossible for them to win); “There is no way into the city” (All the roads into the city are inaccessible); “We have no way to contact her” (We cannot contact her because we do not have her contact information, or maybe she is somewhere without mail service or a wi-fi signal). Note the possible structures here:

no way + to + base verb (“no way to know”)

no way + for + noun or pronoun + to + base verb (“no way for him to go”)

no way + preposition (“no way into the program”)

Collocations: “no way into [somewhere or something],” “no way to enter,” “no way to escape,” “no way to get in,” “no way to go,” “no way to know,” “no way to win”

Related phrase: “no chance,” “not a chance”

2.an emphatic way to say “No” to a command or request

Examples: “Can I borrow $500?” “No way. You still owe me $100.”
“Clean this room right now!” “No way! I didn’t make this mess!”
“He wants you to apologize.” “No way. I didn’t do anything wrong.”

If someone asks you or tells you to do something, and you have a strong reason for saying “No,” you can say “No way,” as in the examples above. In these cases, you strongly refuse to do something. Maybe it is impossible for you to do it, but it is more likely you simply do not want to complete the request or command for personal reasons, or because you are stubborn.

Related phrase: “no chance,” “not a chance”

“Not a chance.”

1.used for saying there is no possibility of something happening or being true
Common variations: “no chance,” “no chance in hell,” “not a chance in hell”
Similar, see also: “No way.”

Examples: “Do you think he knows the answer?” “Not a chance.”
“Will you forgive him?” “Not a chance.”
“Are we going to get there in time?” “Not a chance.”

Use this phrase when you feel strongly about the impossibility of something. In this way, it is a stronger way to say “No” to someone. Two common contexts for this are denying requests (“Could I borrow your phone?” “Not a chance”), and speculating about the possibility of something, as in example 1 above. “No chance” and “No way” can be used in the same way.

Usage note: To make this phrase stronger, use the colloquial variation “no chance in hell.” Note the example: “Are you going to call him back?” “No chance in hell.” In this case, you feel very strongly about the negation.

Related phrases: “No way, no how.” “Not (even) if you paid me.” “Not in a million years.”

“Not at all.”

1.not to any extent or degree; “No.”

Examples: “Do you have a good relationship with him?” “Not at all.”
“Is he allergic to anything?” “Not at all.”
“Have you been practicing your French lately?” “Not at all.”

Use this phrase when you want to emphasize a negative case, as in the examples above. If “not at all” had a numerical equivalent, it would be zero. For instance: “Do you like horror movies?” “Not at all.” This means I do not like horror movies to any extent or degree—I receive zero enjoyment and/or have zero interest in horror movies.

You can also use “not at all” in the middle of sentences, typically before adjectives. For example: “I am not at all satisfied”; “Phillip was not at all disappointed with the outcome.” In these cases, however, it is more common to wrap “not at all” around an adjective or longer phrase, as in the following examples: “I am not satisfied at all”; “Phillip was not disappointed with the movie at all.”

Usage note: As a substitute for “You’re welcome,” this phrase is also similar to “No problem” and “Don’t worry about it.” Note the examples: “Thanks again for paying for dinner.” “Not at all”; “Thank you for your help.” “Not at all.”

Finally, it is common to emphasize the negative quality of something with the phrase “not good at all.” For example: “This song isn’t good at all.”

Collocations: “not bad at all,” “not good at all,” “not satisfied at all”

Related response: “Not in any way, shape, or form.”

“Not by a long shot.”

1.not by a wide margin; used for indicating that something is far from finished, true, or the case

Examples: “Wait. I thought you grew up here.” “Not by a long shot. I grew up on the other side of the country.”
“So, you’re saying she’s not the best candidate?” “Not by a long shot.”
“Are we done yet?” “Not by a long shot.”

While the phrase “by a long shot” can be used in affirmative cases, it is most often used in the negative, and as a standalone response. “Not by a long shot” means that something has a long way to go, and that the target is very far away. This can be the truth (example 1), the case (example 2), or simply the level of incompletion (example 3). When you use this phrase, think of a massive distance between what someone says and what is actually the case. In example 1, you can also simply imagine a physical distance.

Usage note: As mentioned, “by a long shot” can be used in affirmative sentences. This is most common in some variation of the sentence “It’s a long shot.” Note these three examples: “It’s a long shot, but it’s possible”; “They can still win, but it’s going to be a long shot”; “I asked for an extra meal for free, but they said no. I knew it was a long shot, but I had to try.”

Related responses: “In no way, shape, or form.” “No way, no how.” “Not in any way, shape, or form.” “Nothing could be further from the truth.”

“Of course.”

1.used to show emphatic agreement, or to give emphatic permission; absolutely; definitely; “Yes. Obviously.”
Opposite: “Of course not.”

Examples: “May I sit here?” “Yes. Of course.”
“Do you think this plan will work?” “Of course.”
“Could I borrow your pen for a second?” “Yeah. Of course.”

Use this phrase when it is not a problem for you to give permission to someone, or when you want to show emphatic agreement or confidence, as in example 2. Another way to think about this phrase is to say “That is not even a question!” Basically, if the state or outcome of something seems obvious to you, you can use “of course.”

Related phrases: “without a doubt,” “without question”

2.used for saying that something is obvious, or to introduce an obvious or expected point; obviously; “That is clear.”

Examples: “We will need at least three more people for this.” “Of course.”
“Everyone will need to arrive at least 10 minutes early.” “Of course.”
“Of course, dinner will only be served once the bride and groom arrive.”

Examples 1 and 2 are similar to the first meaning above. However, note that you can use “of course” as a standalone response or as part of a sentence, as in example 3. Here are a few more examples of this usage: “We will of course pay for our own tickets”; “Of course I’ll do it!”; “Yes, I can help you. Of course, I will need a few minutes to arrange some things first.”

Related phrases: “without a doubt,” “without question”

“Pretty much.”

1.used to say that something is approximately or basically the case; close to; “Basically.”
Common casual extensions: “Pretty much. Yeah.” “Yeah. Pretty much.”
Similar, see also: “More or less.”

Examples: “Are you saying you don’t like him?” “Yeah. Pretty much.”
“Their business failed because their prices were too high.” “Pretty much.”
“Has she not done it because she doesn’t care?” “Pretty much. Yeah.”

Use this phrase to say that something is approximately or basically the case. Note another example: “Are you ready to leave?” “Pretty much.” If something is close to being the case, but is not 100% the case, you can use “pretty much” to describe this situation. For these reasons, it is interchangeable with “more or less.”

Usage note: In addition to being a standalone response, “pretty much” can be used in the middle of sentences. It can be followed by an adjective, preposition, noun, pronoun, or noun phrase. Here are some examples: “I’m pretty much done”; “We’re pretty much in”; “That is pretty much the case”; “There are more details to the story, but that’s pretty much it”; “Arcade Fire are pretty much the best band in the world.”

Collocations: “pretty much done,” “pretty much it,” “pretty much true”

Related phrase: “just about”

“Right on!”

slang

1.used for showing strong approval, support, or encouragement

Examples: “They have approved my application.” “Right on! I knew they would!”
“She’s going to quit her job tomorrow.” “Right on. They don’t deserve her.”
“They sold their farm for twice the price.” “Right on!”

This is a colloquial way to say “That’s great” or “I approve of this.” It is typically used when speaking to friends or people you are familiar and comfortable with.

Usage note: This phrase can also be used to mean “exactly.” Note the example: “Guess how much this t-shirt costs.” “I don’t know. $25?” “Right on!” You can also use the common variation “right on the money.” In addition to guesses about numbers, both phrases can be used to say that someone’s assessment of a situation is accurate. For instance: “You said the reasons for the company’s changes were due to not having enough employees. You were right on (the money) when you said that.”

Collocation: “right on the money” (see usage note for meaning and context)

Related response: “All right!”

“So what?”

1.used for saying that something is not a big deal and that it should not be treated with suspicion, contempt, or objection
Common variation: “So?”

Examples: “She had fish with red wine!” “So what? Maybe she really likes red wine.”
“He isn’t coming to the wedding.” “So what? He can do what he wants.”
“Rena spent all of her money on shoes.” “So what?”

If you do not see or understand why something is significant, you can say “So what?” We use this response when we think that something does not matter or when we think it is not important. In short, it is not a big deal. We can also use it when we do not think something is relevant to us. This response is also often shortened to “So?” Note the example: “Teddy wore a white shirt today.” “So?”

Usage note: While we often use this response to say “That doesn’t sound important or significant to me,” we can also use it to say “I don’t understand why that’s important. Could you tell me more?” The above example about Teddy wearing a white shirt could have this usage.

We can also use this response when we want others to think that we do not care about a situation—even when we do. In some cases, we use “So?” or “So what?” as a defensive response. For instance: “Dana, you haven’t taken a shower in 7 days.” “So what?!” Dana is basically saying “Why do you care? It’s my life, and I can make my own decisions.” She doesn’t see the problem. In this case, we use “So what?” defensively when we hear criticisms about ourselves or others that we don’t want to hear, but maybe need to hear.

Related responses: “I don’t see the problem/issue.” “What’s the big deal?” “What’s your point?” “Who cares?” “Why do you care?” “Why does it matter?”

“Tell me about it.”

1.used for showing complete agreement with someone; “Absolutely”
Similar, see also: “You can say that again.” “You’re telling me.”

Examples: “Life was so much simpler when we were kids.” “Yeah. Tell me about it.”
“Trisha looks much happier these days.” “Tell me about it! I thought I was the only one who noticed.”
“The weather this weekend has been incredible.” “Tell me about it!”

When someone says something that creates a strong desire in you to say “Yes!” to agree with them, you can say “Tell me about it.” This phrase is used for both, positive and negative situations. When we use it for negative situations, we typically say it in an unhappy or sarcastic tone. Here is an example showing this: “Terry is a horrible boss.” “Yeah. Tell me about it.”

Usage note: We do not usually want people to tell us more about the situation when we use this phrase, but we can continue the conversation with it if we would like. For instance: “The team played incredibly well today.” “Tell me about it. Did you know they hired a physical trainer for all the players?” “No. Really?”

Related responses: “Ain’t that the truth?” “I know what you mean.” “No doubt about it.” “That’s for sure.”

“Why not?”

1.used for questioning why something is not permitted, true, or possible, or to show approval or agreement of a proposed course of action
Common colloquial variation: “Why the heck not?” “Why the hell not?”

Examples: “You can’t do that.” “Why not?” (Why is this not permitted?)
“What do you think? Should we do it?” “Sure. Why not? Let’s do it.”
“Do you want to go out with us tonight?” “You know what? Why not? I’ve spent too much time indoors lately.”

When someone wants us or asks us to do something, the answer “Why not?” can have a few different sentiments. We use “Why not?” when we are tired and do not want to argue strongly in the opposite direction. We can also use it emphatically to show our agreement or approval of something. Finally, we use “Why not?” when we are curious and when we want to see what will happen if we follow the proposed course of action. For example: “I want you to visit the art gallery with me. I promise you’ll like it. What do you say?” “Why not? It sounds interesting.”

Usage note: You can use “Why not + base verb” to make a suggestion. Here are three examples: “Why not have dinner with your parents tonight?”; “Why not call him in the morning?”; “Why not get a new one?” This is synonymous with “Why don’t you/we/they/etc. + base verb?”

Related responses: “I can’t see any reason not to.” “I don’t see why not.” “What’s the worst that could happen?” “You’ve twisted my arm.”

“You can say that again.”

1.used for showing complete agreement with someone; “Absolutely”
See also: “Tell me about it.” “You’re telling me.”

Examples: “This is an awful situation for everybody.” “You can say that again.”
“Antonio isn’t in a good mood today.” “You can say that again.”
“Choi Min-jeong is an incredible skater.” “You can say that again.”

This response is synonymous with “Tell me about it.” It shows strong agreement because you are inviting the person to say the same thing again. Of course, you do not really want the other person to repeat their sentence. You simply mean “Yes, that is true and I completely agree with you.”

Related responses: “Ain’t that the truth?” “I know what you mean.” “No doubt about it.” “That’s for sure.”

“You’re kidding”; “You’re joking.”

1.used for showing disbelief, surprise, shock, or skepticism toward what someone has said or done
Similar, see also: “No way.”

Examples: “My 7-year-old daughter ate 3 hamburgers for breakfast.” “You’re kidding.”
“I got this for free.” “You’re joking.”
“They won the lottery!” “No way! You’re kidding!”

If someone is kidding or joking, they are not being serious. The response “You’re kidding” or “You’re joking” is used when we are not sure if someone is telling us the truth, either because they have a history of sarcasm and trickery, or because their news is difficult to believe. We can express shock, disbelief, surprise, or skepticism with this response.

Usage note: “Are you joking?” and “Are you kidding?” are very common responses as well.

Related responses: “Are you joking?” “Are you kidding?” “Are you kidding me?” “No kidding.” “You’re kidding me.”

“You’re telling me.”

1.used for telling someone that you are already aware of something and/or that you are in agreement with them
Similar, see also: “Tell me about it.” “You can say that again.”

Examples: “I hate this rainy weather.” “You’re telling me. I got wet during my break.”
“Wow. That looks really nice on you.” “You’re telling me! I look great!”
“This movie is too long.” “You’re telling me. I’m ready to fall asleep.”

Use this informal response to tell someone that you agree with what they have just said because you have personal experience with or awareness of it as well. For instance: “This computer is so slow.” “You’re telling me. It took me 15 minutes to check my email.”

Related responses: “Ain’t that the truth?” “I know what you mean.” “No doubt about it.” “That’s for sure.”

FILLERS AND TRANSITIONS

The words and phrases below are all extremely common in English conversations all over the world. They are used to begin or to fill a gap in a conversation, to provide relevant information, or to change the topic. Learning the language in this chapter will make a significant impact on the fluency of your speech, and on the confidence you exhibit in conversation.

Practice these fillers and transitions by reading the example sentences and thinking of your own contexts and original sentences.

“Anyway” (”at any rate,” “in any case,” “in any event”)

1.used for changing the topic or returning to a previous topic

Examples: “My weekend was good. Thanks for asking. Anyway, are you busy today?”
“I need to buy some groceries later. Anyway, you were talking about your mom visiting you tomorrow?”
“I can’t wait to get home and take a hot bubble bath.” “Okay. Anyway…”

Common sentence: “Anyway, what were we talking about before?”

If someone says something that makes you feel uncomfortable, awkward, bored, or uninterested, you can shift the focus of the conversation by simply saying “Anyway.” If you use the word as a standalone response, the person or people whom you are speaking to should understand that you want to change the topic. Example 3 above shows this usage. You can also use “anyway” to change the topic, as in example 1, or to return to a previous topic, as in example 2.

In all of the cases above, “anyway” is used to shift the focus of the conversation from one thing to another. Here is one more example of changing the focus of the conversation: “Julian! Nice to see you! Anyway, can I help you with anything?”

2.used for indicating that you want or need to end the conversation

Examples: “Anyway, it was nice to see you. Good luck with everything!”
“Anyway, I need to get back to work, so I’ll talk to you another time, okay?”
“Anyway, the store closes in 15 minutes, so I should go. Take care.”

Common sentence: “Anyway, I should get going.” (I should go.)

If you do not know how to elegantly end a conversation, use “anyway” followed by your reason for ending the conversation, or by saying something polite, as in example 1. Here are a few other common ways to end a conversation with “anyway”: “Anyway, it was nice to see you”; “Anyway, I have to go”; “Anyway, I really should get going”; “Anyway, I’m going to go.”

Usage note: “Well” can be used in a similar way and replace “Anyway” in all of the examples above.

3.used for indicating or asking about the real reason for or cause of something

Examples: “Why did you do that, anyway?”
“Why didn’t you call me, anyway?”
“Their breakup was for the best. They never got along, anyway.”

Common sentence: “Where is he, anyway?”

“Where is Leo, anyway?” This question wants to know about Leo’s current location. When you use “anyway” in this way, use it at the end of a sentence. You can use it at the start as well, but if you do, it sounds like you are changing the focus of the topic. It still makes sense and it works, but it does not serve the exact same function.

Example 3 shows this usage in a declarative sentence. Here are some other common situations with this particular usage: “I didn’t want it anyway”; “I’m glad we didn’t go. I wanted to save money anyway”; “The government is always going to do what they want anyway”; “It’s a good thing that I came with you to the store. I needed to buy a few things anyway.”

4.used for introducing a statement that supports what has already been said, or to show that a statement is true despite what has been said before

Examples: “I saw a new bike on sale for $80 at the store today, and I needed a new one to get to work this summer anyway, so I bought it.” (support)
“Edgar thinks the information is false. Anyway, I don’t think we can trust him based on his history of lying.” (strong contrast)
“I need to get into better shape, and anyway, I already have a gym membership, so I’m going to start going to the gym 3 times a week.” (support)

Common sentence: “Anyway, I don’t think that’s true.” (Regardless of what has been said before this, I do not think it is true.)

You can use “anyway” to show support (examples 1 and 3) or strong contrast (example 2). If you remember that “anyway” is another way to say “in any case” or “at any rate,” this usage is easier to remember. In the end, you are still using “anyway” to shift the focus of the conversation—to concentrate on a specific part of what you wish to say. In the case of showing strong contrast, think of it as using “regardless.”

“As I was saying”

1.used for returning the conversation to what the speaker was previously saying; “To return to what I said”
Common variation: “Like I was saying,...” (ungrammatical, but used)

Examples: “Sorry about that interruption, everyone. As I was saying, we need several volunteers to work on Saturday.”
“As I was saying before we took our break, our company had a very successful third quarter.”
“How much did you pay for it?” “As I was saying, I got it on special a couple of days ago, and to answer your question, it cost me $45.”

Common sentence: “As I was saying before I was so rudely interrupted,...” (This is usually a light-hearted usage that can be spoken among friends.)

Use this phrase to return the conversation to what you were previously talking about. This is a common transition after an interruption, as in example 1. You can use it in informal and formal situations. If the flow of ideas in a conversation is stopped or temporarily paused for any reason, and you want to return the conversation to its original focus, you can use this transitional phrase.

Related responses: “As I have already mentioned,...” “As I just said,...” “Getting back on track,...”

“By the way”

1.incidentally; used for adding something new or tangentially related to the conversation, especially something that one has just thought of

Examples: “That was a great game. Thanks for paying for the popcorn, by the way.”
“I’m going to the gym tomorrow. Oh! By the way, I got those running shoes you recommended, and I love them!”
“We had a nice time at my cousin’s birthday. By the way, how was your job interview?”

Common sentence: “By the way, did you ever…?” (Did you resolve or do what we had previously discussed? I want to ask you now while it is on my mind.)

If you think of something to add in the course of a conversation, you can introduce it with “by the way.” It is usually related to the current discussion, but it can be something completely new, as in example 3. Here is another example: “By the way, did you read the email I sent you this morning?” In this case, you and this person were already having a conversation about something else. When you use “by the way,” it’s because you have just remembered something you wanted to say, or you wanted to say in the middle of the conversation. See the related phrases for other ways to think about this phrase.

Usage note: Use “by the way” at the beginning or the end of a sentence. It is more common to use it at the beginning if you are in the middle of a conversation with minimal pauses. You can also use it in the middle of a sentence if you are just making a side statement in the middle of your conversation. Here are three more examples: “Where did you get that, by the way?”; “By the way, did you ever find out who stole your backpack?”; “I spoke with Tamina, who, by the way, recently got a new job, and she said she didn’t have any problems with our decision.”

Related phrases: “Before I forget,” “While it’s on my mind,” “While it’s still fresh in my mind”

“If you ask me”; “The way I look at it”; “The way I see it”

1.used for emphasizing that one is going to give one’s opinion

Examples: “If you ask me, they should have done this sooner.”
“The way I look at it, social media is mostly a waste of time.”
“If you ask my dad, he’ll tell you that life was better 30 years ago.”

Common sentence: “If you ask me, he’s/she’s/they’re right.” (In my opinion, he/she/they are right.)

These phrases gives your listener(s) a sign that you are going to give your opinion on something. Of course, it is often clear that we are giving our opinion or perspective on something without phrases like “I think” or “In my opinion,” but it is still common for people to use phrases such as these to prepare listeners for what comes next. While the phrases “If you ask me,” “The way I look at it,” and “The way I see it” are often used in the first person, they can be used to talk about other people’s opinions and perspectives, as in example 3.

Usage note: Use “Not if you ask me,” “Not the way I look at it,” or “Not the way I see it” as standalone responses when someone says something you disagree with. For example: “This park is fantastic.” “Not if you ask me. Just look at the grass. It’s all brown and yellow.”

Related phrases: “in my opinion,” “personally speaking”

“Let’s see”

1.used when one wants to think carefully about something, try to remember something, or to allow for more time to think about something
Common variation: “Let me see”

Examples: “How many people did you know at the community centre?” “Let’s see, there was Andy, Eric, Alisha…”
“The best book I’ve ever read? That’s a tough one. Hmm. Let me see.”
“Who do you think is the best candidate for this job?” “Well, let’s see, that depends on what qualities you’re looking for.”

Common sentence: “Let’s see. We need...” (Used when you are trying to plan or organize something, and you are discussing it for the first time. What actions need to be taken? What things do we need to get?)

Use this filler phrase when you need more time to think about something or to remember something. It is similar to saying “Give me a moment or two to think about it.” Here are two more examples: “Let’s see, how did we get there last time?”; “Let’s see, how much will this cost with tax?” In both of these cases, the person who says “Let’s see” means they are trying to see with their mind’s eye—to think carefully about something or to try to remember something.

Usage note: This phrase is often preceded by the words “now” and “well.” For example: “Now, let’s see, where did I put my glasses?”; “Well, let’s see, what do you think the best way is to do this?”

Finally, the usage described here is not similar to “Wait and see” or “Let’s see what happens,” which simply mean that patience is required, or that no action is possible, recommended, or necessary.

Related responses: “Give me a minute.” “Let me think.”

“Look at it this way”

1.used for encouraging others to see something from a different perspective
Common variations: “Look at it another way.” “Think of it this way.”

Examples: “I really don’t want to do this.” “Look at it this way: The sooner you start it, the sooner you’ll finish it.”
“I can’t believe the company promoted him.” “Well, look at it this way: The company could have given the job to Jake, which would have been worse.”
“Before you say anything, look at it this way—There were no good options in this scenario. We had to pick the lesser of two evils.”

Common sentence: “Look at it this way: It could have been worse.” (The situation was bad, but it could have been worse.)

Use this transitional phrase to ask someone to change their point of view. This is often used to make people see a situation in a more positive way. Here is one more example: “That was one of the worst vacations I’ve ever had.” “Look at this way: At least you got a good story out of it.”

Grammar note: In writing, because this phrase prepares the listener for more information, it is often followed by a colon (:) or an em-dash (—).

Related phrases: “Look at it like this,” “Look on the bright side”

“So”

1.used for beginning a new sentence or conversation, or for changing the subject

Examples: “So, what did you think of the city tour?”
“So, did you watch the game last night?”
“So, what do we do now?”

Common sentence: “So, how did it go?” (How was the presentation/job interview/exam/doctor’s appointment/etc. I am curious about the outcome and I hope it went well.)

“So” has many uses, but for the purposes of practical conversation, it is most useful as an introductory particle. This means it is used at the beginning of a new sentence. It warms up the listener and lets them know you are going to speak.

You can think of “so” as a reliable sentence starter when you do not want to be too direct with someone. “So, how was your vacation?” is a warmer and more familiar conversation starter than “How was your vacation?” This makes “so” particularly useful with acquaintances, colleagues, and people we are not as comfortable with or familiar with. However, it is also common among friends. See the common sentence for a popular case of this.

“So” can also be used to fill in gaps in a conversation or to change the subject. For example: “Oh no. We’re late.” “So, what are we going to do now?”; “The meeting is over. Thank you for your time.” “Thanks for the info, Dave. That was really useful. So, does anyone want to grab lunch with me?” This usage of “so” is slightly more polite than “anyway” in the same situation, as “anyway” tries to disregard what has come before in this case.

Usage note: “So” is often followed by questions, but it can be followed by declarative sentences as well. Note the following examples: “So, you decided to attend the event after all”; “So, I heard your date went well”; “So, your brother texted me today”; “So, I went to a hockey game last night.”

Related phrases: “Now then,...” “Say,...” “You know,...”

“Well”

Used for filling gaps and pauses in speech. This includes:

1.expressing doubt, curiosity, anger, or surprise
Common extension: “Well now”

Examples: “Well, I’m not so sure about that.”
“Well, you should have thought of that sooner!”
“Well, well, well, what do we have here?”

Common sentence: “Well, that’s interesting.” (I just saw or heard something unexpected or interesting. I am expressing my surprise or curiosity, or I am simply stating a fact.)

“Well” is doubled or tripled to show surprise. This is particularly useful when you catch someone doing something they should not be doing, or something they do not want you to know they are doing. In all of the cases above, however, “well” is used to fill a gap in the conversation. Along with “so” and “anyway,” it is the word people use most to transition from one idea to another in everyday conversation.

Usage note: “Well now” can replace “Well” in all of the examples above. In the case of “Well, well, well, what do we have here?” you can say, “Well now, what do we have here?”

2.asking a question, especially when one wants more details or an explanation
See also: “So”

Examples: “Well, what did she say?”
“Well, who is the person responsible for this mess?”
“Well, how can we make this situation better?”

Common sentence: “Well, isn’t this nice?” (I think this is nice, either truly or sarcastically depending on the situation. Others will probably expect me to say more after this, but they might also feel like I am waiting for them to say something about the topic.)

If someone pauses in the middle of telling you a story, you can say “Well, what happened next?” You can use “well” before a question that asks for more details because you are curious. This is similar to usage 1. You can also use “well” before you ask for an explanation, as in example 2. Finally, you can use “well” before questions in which you want to know the next step of something, as in example 3.

Usage note: If you are just starting a conversation with someone, it is still much more common and natural to use “so” instead of “well.” Note the examples: “So, where were you born?” “So, what are your plans for today?” You can use “well” if you are in the middle of a conversation and these questions are relevant to what you have been discussing and you are curious or trying to get more information about someone or something, but not as the starting point of a conversation. With this usage, “well” is also sometimes said with a question intonation, as in the following example: “Well? What do you think of the soup?”

3.responding when someone expects something to be the case, or when someone expects the answer to something to be “yes,” but the answer is actually “no”

Examples: “Did you send the email?” “Well, not exactly.”
“So, did you buy the cookies?” “Well, I really wanted to, but I forgot.”
“You brought the equipment, didn’t you?” “Well, that’s an interesting story…”

Common sentence: “Well, not exactly.” (A response to someone. You are saying that what they hoped would be the case is not actually the case.)

In these cases, “well” is being used to create time and space for the speaker. They know the person whom they are speaking to will not like their answer, so they are just trying to delay their answer by filling the space with another word. This usage of well can be quite funny if you stretch the length of “well.” Note the example: “Did you do what I asked?” “Weeeeeeeeeelllllll…” “You didn’t do it?!” “Weeeeell, no. I’m sorry.”

4.introducing an answer to a question

Examples: “Well, that’s a very good question.”
“Well, the truth is I haven’t done it yet because I haven’t had any free time.”
“What have you been doing all this time?” “Well, I’ve actually been working on an important personal project.”

Common sentence: “Well, I’m glad you asked.” (I am happy you asked this question because I know the answer, or I am excited to discuss this topic.)

This usage “warms up” the conversation and is another case of “well” acting as a filler word before you say what you really want to say, or what is relevant to the conversation.

Usage note: “So” would also work in all of the examples above.

5.filling space in the middle of a sentence because one needs more time to think about what they want to say

Examples: “I talked to him, and, well, I didn’t get the answer that I wanted.”
“Your answer was a little…well…too aggressive.”
“I don’t know why we have to…well…work so many extra hours.”

Common sentence: “That’s…well…difficult to say.” (I do not have a good answer for this question/situation/etc.)

If you need more time to think about what you want to say, either because you are struggling to find the right words, or you do not want to be too hurtful to the person whom you are speaking to, “well” in the middle of a conversation can be a useful filler. Here is another example: “I’m not sure if, well, they’ll actually listen to me.”

6.ending a conversation, presentation, etc.
Similar, see also: “Anyway”

Examples: “Well, I think we’ll stop there for today. Have a great day, everybody!”
“Well, that’s all for now. Thanks for your time and attention.”
“Well, I’ve got to go. See you tomorrow!”

Common sentence: “Well, I should probably get going.” (I should probably go. I do not want to be late for my next event.)

If you are not sure how to end a conversation, starting with “well” (or “anyway”) is a good idea. Since “well” is a filler word that prepares the listener for more information, it is a good way to get someone’s attention and to indicate that you want or need to end the conversation. It is a shorter and “sharper” way to indicate that you want to end a conversation. “Anyway” is a bit gentler and more relaxed, though you can make “well” sound more relaxed by extending its pronunciation (”Weeeell, I should probably go”).

OTHER

The phrases below do not neatly fit into the previous, so I have included them here. They are as common and useful as the other phrases in this book. Make sure to do the practice questions to become comfortable with the language in this chapter.

according to

prepositional phrase

1.as said or written by [someone]; as said or written in [a book, essay, etc.]

Examples: “According to my weather app, it’s going to rain tomorrow.”
“Did he commit the crime?” “Not according to official reports.”
“According to the official company policy, she should be reprimanded.”

Common sentence: “According to who?” (Who said that, or where did you get that information? I am skeptical of your statement. Note that “who” is ungrammatical here and should be “whom,” but “who” is what is used and what sounds most natural to the vast majority of listeners.)

Use this phrase when you want to point to the source of your information. Who said it, or where was it stated? This is useful in speaking and in writing. University professors will want to know where you acquired your information for an essay, for instance. Note the example: “According to the stoic philosopher Lucius Seneca, all cruelty comes from weakness.”

In a conversation, you can use the negative form of this phrase to counter what someone else has said by referring to a source of information which provides contrary information. For example: “It’s 6 o’clock.” “Not according to my phone.”

Collocations: “according to official reports,” “according to various sources”

Related phrases: “as noted by,” “as said by,” “if one is to be believed”

Practice: According to your weather app or your nearest source of weather information, what will the temperature be tomorrow?

2.following something or someone as a guide; in conformity with someone or something; “in accordance with”

Examples: “We did it according to the instructions in the manual.”
“He doesn’t do things according to the rules.”
“You should have done it according to the boss’ preferences.”

Common sentence: “Play according to the rules.” (Play in a way that follows and respects the rules. Do not use illegal tactics.)

This usage often refers to a set of rules, principles, instructions, or policies that are written somewhere, but it can refer to a person as well. If you follow someone or something as a guide when you are doing something, you are doing it according to that thing or person. Here is one more example: “If we don’t do this according to the client’s specifications, we won’t get paid.” In short, there is accord (or harmony) between the action and someone or something’s expectations.

Related phrases: “consistent with,” “in agreement with,” “play by the rules”

Practice: Do you always try to play sports or games according to the rules?

as long as

conjunction

1.for a particular duration; for a period of time
Common variation: “for as long as”

Examples: “I’ll remember you as long as I live.”
“She hid for as long as she could.”
“I haven’t known Fred as long as you have.”

Common sentence: “I lasted as long as I could.” (I tried my best to continue the activity until I could not do it anymore.)

This usage strictly refers to a length of time. Some common extensions of this phrase include “as long as I live,” “as long as I do,” “as long as I can,” and “as long as I’ve known you.” This usage can be used to make a comparison, as in example 3, or to specify a particular duration or period or time, as in examples 1 and 2. A popular appearance of this phrase is in wedding vows. After a series of promises regarding fidelity and honesty, the phrase “as long as you both shall live” is stated at the end by the wedding officiant, meaning the promises made will be kept for the duration of the couple’s lives.

Collocations: “as long as one can,” “as long as we/you both shall live”

Practice: Finish the idea: “I can hold my breath for as long as...”

2.on the condition that; provided that; “if”

Examples: “You can come with me as long as you don’t ask any questions.”
“As long as you finish your homework, you can do whatever you like.”
“We were allowed to play outside as long as we had eaten dinner first.”

Common sentence: “As long as you’re telling the truth.” (On the condition that you are telling the truth, I will agree to your wishes.)

This usage introduces a condition that must be satisfied before the cause is activated. Here is one more example: “You can take my car tonight as long as you fill up the gas tank.” Here, the person who wants to borrow the car must fill up the gas tank to use the car. Finally, a popular appearance of this phrase is in the Backstreet Boys song “As long as you love me.”

This phrase is similar to saying “if” or “only if.”

Practice: Finish the idea: “You can go to the party as long as...”

3.for a maximum of a particular period of time

Examples: “The meeting might be as long as 2 hours.”
“The clinic said the wait time for an appointment could be as long as 3 months.”
“Do you think he’ll talk for as long as last time?”

Common sentence: “It will take as long as it takes.” (I do not know how long it will take to do this. It will be finished when it is finished regardless of length of time that is required.)

This usage is often found with verbs of uncertainty, such as “might” and “could,” as in examples 1 and 2, or with the verb “will,” as in example 3.

Usage note: Another way to say “It won’t take as long as 2 hours” is “It won’t take longer than 2 hours.” In both cases, you are referring to the maximum amount of time that something will require. The second form (“not longer than”) is more common in these cases.

Related phrase: “all of (a particular period of time)”

What is the maximum amount of time it takes you to travel from your house to the nearest market or grocery store? (Ex: “It can take as long as...”)

far be it from me [to do something]

idiomatic phrase

1.used for saying that one does not intend to criticize or disagree with something or someone even though it might seem that way; used for saying it is not one’s place to say or do something, but then saying or doing that thing regardless

Examples: “Far be it from me to tell you what to do, but I think you’re making a huge mistake.”
“Far be it from me to interfere with your personal life, but you seem a bit sad and lonely.”
“Far be it from me to complain, but this hotel room is incredibly small.”

Common sentence: “Far be it from me to tell anyone what to do, but…” (I know it is not my place to tell anyone what to do, but…)

Use this slightly formal phrase to say “I know it is not my place to say or do this, but…” In common usage, this phrase is always followed by to + base verb. This phrase can be heard in some official settings, such as in a court of law. For example: “Far be it from me to criticize the army, but what sergeant Henry did was wrong.”

Collocations: “far be it from me to complain,” “far be it from me to criticize,” “far be it from me to tell anyone what to do”

Related phrase: “not be one’s place [to do/say something]”

Practice: Finish the idea: “Far be it from me to complain, but...”

regardless of

prepositional phrase

1.used for saying that something is not affected or influenced by something else; without regard for; despite
Similar, see also: “in spite of”

Examples: “Regardless of your feelings toward me, you have to admit this is a good idea.” (This is a good idea. Your feelings should not influence this fact.)
“I will do it regardless of your approval.” (My decision is not dependent on your approval.)
“Regardless of our past, I want you to know that I will treat you fairly.” (Our past relationship will not affect how I treat you in the present.)

Common sentence: “Regardless of anything, I want you to know that…” (Forgetting everything else, I want you to know that…)

Use this phrase when you want to say that something does not have an impact on something else. Here are three more examples to illustrate this: “Regardless of its length, it’s a good movie” (The movie’s length does not affect its overall quality); “I’ll do it regardless of anything” (Nothing will prevent me from doing this); “Regardless of the odds, they succeeded” (The odds were against them, but they succeeded regardless).

Collocations: “regardless of how many/much,” “regardless of one’s feelings,” “regardless of one’s past,” “regardless of the risk/danger/consequences, etc.,” “regardless of whether or not [something happens]”

Related phrases: “irrespective of,” “no matter,” “setting aside [something]”

Practice: What is something you want to do one day regardless of the cost?

so much for

idiomatic phrase

1.used for saying that something is no longer true, useful, helpful, or possible (because something has failed to make one feel this way or for this to actually be the case)

Examples: “Bret and Julie got divorced? So much for everlasting love.”
“He betrayed his country. So much for patriotism!”
“Well, that didn’t work. So much for that idea.”

Common sentence: “So much for that idea.” (That idea does not or did not work. We can discard it as an option for our current problem/situation/etc.)

Use this phrase to express your disappointment or contempt that something has failed. The related phrase “There goes” is a useful synonym for understanding “so much for.” Note the two examples: “Someone hit Mario’s car in the parking lot. So much for his new car”; “Someone hit Mario’s car in the parking lot. There goes his new car.” In both cases, Mario’s new car is no longer useful. Imagine yourself throwing away the object that comes after “so much for.” It is no longer true, useful, helpful, or possible. As you can see in examples 1 and 2, this can also refer to abstract ideas such as love, loyalty, honesty, etc.

Usage note: “So much for” is often followed by a noun (“So much for justice”) or noun phrase (“So much for my life’s work”), but it can be followed by a gerund as well. Note the example: “So much for believing in the innate goodness of humanity.” As you can see, you can use this structure to sound sarcastic, cynical, and/or dramatic.

Collocations: “so much for honesty/loyalty/love/justice/etc.,” “so much for that,” “so much for that idea”

Related phrase: “There goes [something]”

Practice: Think of a time a plan didn’t work out for you. Use the phrase “So much for...” to express what failed. Use a verb+ing in this case as well. (Ex: “So much for visiting my brother this weekend.” In this case, I had planned to visit my brother, but maybe my car stopped working, and this prevented me from visiting him.)

About the Author

Alex Makar is a Polish-Canadian immigrant who has been teaching English in and out of classrooms since 2007. In 2009, he decided it would be a good idea to show his face to millions of people on YouTube and on engvid.com. He has not regretted this decision.

These days, Alex resides in the province of Quebec with his wife and two daughters. When he’s not answering English language questions or writing books, Alex enjoys reading, watching movies, playing board games, collecting nerdy t-shirts, pretending to maintain a regular exercise routine, listening to music, and learning from his kids.

300 Practical English Words and Phrases is Alex’s third book. If you enjoyed it, make sure to get his first two books, 100 Practical English Phrasal Verbs and 200 Practical English Idioms. Alex says they are also quite useful!

For more English learning resources, including free video lessons, go to:
www.englishalex.com
